Piers Vitebsky's stud y of religion and psychology in tribal India focusses upon a unique form of dialogue between the living and the dead , co nducted throu gh the medium of a shaman in trance. T he dead so metimes nurture their livin g descendants, yet at o th er tim es th ey inflict up on th em the very illnesses from whi ch th ey died. T hrou gh intimate dialo gue, the So ra use the occasion of death to ex plore their closest emoti o nal a tta chments in all their a mbi va lence . Dr Vitebsky a na lyses th e actors' words and relation ships over seve ral years and develops a typo logy of moods a mo ng the dead a nd of kinds of memo ry amo ng the livin g. In compa rin g Sora sham a ni sm with the treatment of bereavement in psychoana lysis and psyc hothe rapy, he hi gblights a co ntras t in their assumptions which has fa rreaching co nseq uences for the social a nd professional sco pe of the two kinds of practice.

Preface
] lived for four years on the Indian subcontinent between 1976 and 1983 . This book is based on eighteen months which I spent among the Sora, mostly during 1976-7 and 1979, and the ethnographic present refers mostly to the second of these periods. I also made brief return visits in 1984 and 1992 . I am indebted for substantial financial support at various times to the Social Science Research Counci l of the UK for an initial postgraduate studentship; to Girton College, Cambridge, for a Margaret Smith Research Fellowship; to the Perrott-Warwick Fund for Psychical Research; and to my parents. I also received supplementary assistance from the Governing Body of the School of Oriental and African Studies; the Emslie Horniman Fund of the Royal Anthropological Institute; and the South Asia Language and Area Center of the University of Chicago. Many people have read part or all of the manuscript and many more have given me hospitality or encouraged me in other ways. J am grateful to Pramod Kumar Behera, Andre Beteille, Jean Briggs, Audrey Cantlie, Michael Carrithers, Elisabeth Chaussin, Tony Cohen, Daniel de Coppet, Susan Drucker-Brown, Dorothy Emmet, John Forrester, Doris and Meyer Fortes, Christoph von Fiirer-Haimendorf, Jean-Claude Galey, Ernest Gellner, Kathleen Gough, Roberte Hamayon, Sarah Harrison, Olivier and Arlette Herrenschmidt, Mark Hobart, Caroline Humphrey, Ron Inden, N. S. Kumaraswamy, Celia and Edmund Leach, Godfrey Lienhardt, Julius Lipner, Roland Littlewood, Geoffrey Lloyd , Alan Macfarlane, T. N. Madan, Laxman Mahapatra, Sitakant Mahapatra, Adrian Mayer, Rodney Needham, Charlie Nuckolls, Gananath Obeyesekere, Dorinda Outram, Robert Paine, Michel Panoff, Dave Parkin , Johnny Parry, Prakash Pashpureddy, Bala Patnaik , D. P. Pattanayak, James Peacock , Terence



xviii Prefa ce
Penelhum, C hri st ian Petit , C. A. Prasad, Declan Quigley, Prasada Rao , Audrey Richards , Steve Rose n, Malcolm Ruel, A nn Salmond, Rupert Sheldrake, Milton Singer, Bhupinder Sin gh, Surajit Sinh a, David and Patricia Sta mpe, Hilary Standing, Marilyn Stra thern , J . S. Uberoi , Roy Wagner, Michael Yo rke, Norman Z id e; to two a nonymous reviewers, o ne Indian , one American; to Mel Otis, Katie Andersen and other C hri sti a n mi ssionaries of various denomina tion s in Serango, Gunupur and Parla khemundi (Parla kimidi) ; to Bryce Boyer, Danny Freeman and the members of the Anthropology-Psychoan a lysis Interdisciplinary Colloquium of the American Psychoanalytic Association, who devoted their December 1988 meeting in New York entirely to the discu ssion of thi s manuscript; and to my wife Sally and c hildren Patrick and Catherine, who came into my life after I had left the Sora but who gave me much bette r reason than I had had before to reflect on mortality. Most of all, my debt is to the numerous Sora who after initial doubts welcomed me into their homes and their lives and cared for me during long periods when I was heavily dependent on them . This book is about their relationships with each other and, given the intimate nature of the ma tters discussed, it seemed best to protect the anonymity of the main characters even though some of these are among my greatest Sora friends and benefactors. There is another book to be written about my own relationship with these people; but I hope that enough will emerge in the pages which follow to go some way towards explaining why my time among the Sora was a powerful formative period in my life, the lessons of which I am only gradually beginning to understand. I can do no more than dedicate this book to the Sora whom I knew, their ancestors and their descendants, along with my own family . I worked exclusively in the Sora language. I deliberately did not take any written sources on the language with me into the field and it was not until quite late that I had an opportunity to learn something of the Oriya, Telugu and Hindi which are partly known by some Soras. This approach gave me a very slow start since I arrived in Soraland quite inarticulate. But it also gave me a much richer reward in the later stages of my fieldwork. For someone who enjoys languages, there can be few more fascina ting experiences than working out the semantics of a language from the constant exploration of consistencies and variations in actual usage. Sora culture is an oral one. Villages, families and even some persons have their own distinctive dialects or ways of speaking: for example, it was often said that I had clearly learned to speak Sora in the company ofInama because of my extreme emphasis on glottal stops. Not surprisingly, there is no standard orthography among
PreFace XIX
those who have occasion to write the lang uage down (whet her in the Oriya, Telugu or inte rn at ion al phonetic script) , namely lin guists, missionaries and some So ra s themselves if they write le tters home while working in Assa m. T hough phon'ologists mi ght wish for many more diacritics than I have given here and the use of the s/lIl'a, for the sa ke of other readers (whom I very much want to remember certa in Sora terms) I have used only Ii (pronounced ' ny' as in Span ish) and I , similar to the Russian bl or the T urki sh back vowel. I have also distinguished ng from ngg, correspo nding respectively to the sounds in the E ngli sh words 'singer' and ' linge r'. ' Word s from other Indian languages a re spelt according to generally accepted standard s. The dia logues quoted are verbatill1 transla tions of tape reco rdin gs and are laid out like the script ofa drama. Elsewhere a speaker's actual words, or the literal translation of a Sora term , a re given in inverted commas. Where I supply my own paraphrase of a speaker's words I make this clear. Square brackets are used for stage directions or other material supplied by me to make the meaning clearer. In dialogues and figures throughout the book names and remarks of dead speakers are printed in italics. Personal names are stressed as follows: two-syllabled usually as in Sindi ; threesyllabled on the third syllable, as in Mengalll , Indiri , Jamano (never on the second syllable, as one might be tempted to do on the analogy orItalian or Spanish). To remember this, one has only to think of people calling to each other from one hillside to another, their voices whooping upward: 'E! Mengalu!' 'E! Pirino!' Sora life has a strong sense of gender, though sexual roles are less sharply polarised than in much of India: men can fetch water for the house, while women can plough with buffalos. Where no specific person is mea nt , I have usually avoided clumsy English circumlocutions such as ' he or she' and used the pronouns 'he ' for laypersons and 'she' for shamans, since the most important shamans are usually women. My 1992 visit, made while this book was going to press, showed that Sora society is changing rapidly. The children with whom I played earlier are now young adults and many are becoming Baptists. For those young people, there are no more dialogues with the dead and the ethnographic present of this book which I was still able to share with those over thirty is already something which belongs to an older generation.



PART I
SONUM: THE CONTINUATION OF CONSCIOUSNESS AFTER DEATH

The encounter
The Sora are an aboriginal ' tribe' who live in the forested hills around the borders of Orissa and Andhra Pradesh, towards the eastern seaboard of India (figure 1.1; see also chapter 2). In every Sora village, almost every day, living people conduct dialogues with the dead , who speak to them through the mouth ofa shaman in trance . The following is a typical example, which will reappear in context in chapter 7 (lines 251-62). A little girl who died recently has returned to speak with her young mother. Her mother is too overcome with grief to respond and the talking is done on her behalf by an older woman, the child's aunt. Here and in dialogues throughout the book, names and remarks of dead speakers are in italics.
Text 1.1 Dialogue between a dead little girl and her living aunt

Living aunt:

[arriving from the Underworld, faintly] Molher, where are my nose-rings?
[answering for the girl's mother] They must have burned up in the pyre, darling, we looked but couldn't find them. I don't know whether they jumped to one side or what. [petulantly] Why aren 'l you showing me my nose-rings? They were so tiny. If I'd found them of course I'd show them to you. [a pause; the aunt continues] Oh my love, my darling, don't cause your own illness in others. Can you say that your mother and father didn't sacrifice for you? They didn't turn their backs or refuse to help you, did they? Think of all those pigs, all those chickens, goats, buffalos, my lovely child . Didn' t your father say, 'Let's

My Mlimmy doesn 'I care enough aboulme. [returns to the Underworld] This book is about Sora people's awareness of their own mortality and of the mortality of those around them. At first sight, 'mortality' suggests the organic and fleshly na ture of humans, our predisposition to fall ill , to decay and to create our own replacements through sexuality. But the Sora will also force us to ask what it is that dies and what it means to be dead . The interpretation which Tshall offer is that for them, death is not a negation or absence of life. Rather, 'life' and 'death ' a re both phases ofa person 's total existence and close relations between persons are maintained across the line dividing these two states.
Dialog ues belll'eell Ih e lil'ing alld Ih e dead 5
In di a logues between livin g and dea d , speake rs persuade, cajole, tea se, remind , deceive, plead with each othe r. Dialogues represent a mutu al quest for awareness abo ut the other perso n's state of mind . At the same time, they a re the niedium through which each person 's being is co nsta ntly moulded. Livin g and dead peop le ca use each other to do thin gs through dialogues at the same time as they are themselves changed by these encounters. Each person is an agent, but at the same time is acted upon a nd does not simply return to his or her previous sta te. Since thi s kind of mutual moulding or perso ns passes throu gh their loss and separation from each other, thi s book is also about the special place which death has for the So ra in their fee lin gs of love and resentment , grief and anger. Finally, since life and death are set within the pa ssage of time and against the birth of new generations, this book is also about social co ntinuity and hope. What is a dead person? A perso n who di es becomes a SOllllll1 (pronounced with a long 0).' This word designates both an entity and a state. There is no article in the Sora language; but following Sora usage one can sa y of a dead person either that he has 'become a [particular] sonum' or that he has ' become sonum' (in both cases sonum-en gar/dIe). Virtually all illnesses and deaths a re said by the Sora to be caused in some way by sonums. In attacking the living, the dead seek to transfer to them certain experiences which they themselves underwent at the moment of their deaths. They do this by 'ea ting the soul' (puradan jum-) of the living victim in order to absorb him , thereby causing in him a kind of symptom or of dea th which is analogous to that which was undergone by the attacker himself (cf. the last few lines of text 1.1, above). However, the dead do not only attack the living and harm them: they also nourish and protect them. It is the interplay of these two contradictory attitudes, respectively aggressive and nurturing, which lies at the heart of Sora thinking a bout their dead. Sora hold their dialogues sometimes in the open by a path, stream or cremation ground, sometimes in the shadowy interior of the house . The shaman, who is frequently though not always a woman, sits at the centre of a group of people. Sora say that her soul leaves her body and goes to a separate domestic life, with husband and children, in the Underworld (kll1orai desa). While she is in a dissociated state of trance, her body is available for a succession of the dead who speak, one at a time, through her mouth . According to the mood of the dialogue and the degree of their involvement, their living interlocutors may squat on their haunches and huddle intently around her arguing vehemently with the dead, weep and embrace her, or else come and go at the edge of the proceedings and interpose a careless remark. A sequence of dialogues can last up to several



6 Dialog lles lI'ith the dead
hours and range from casual gossip to ext remes of emo tion which I cou ld never learn to observe while remaining unmoved. But dialogues with the dead are not a ll tears: they often include moments of good humour and of obscene joking amidst uproariou s la ughter. A dialogue is a lways associated with the performance or promise of a sacrifi ce by the living to the dead . Though in one sense the dead have power, at the same time they exist in a state of emotional a nd material deprivation. Dialogues are thus not only a communication but also a feed in g. They are held on certa in occasions: at the divination to find out the cause of a patient's illness; in some of the subsequent hea lings; at certain seasonal harvest rites; and at the various stages of each person's funeral rites. In a large village of 500 people a dialogue may take place from about five times a week to about ten times a day, according to seaso n. Dialogues contain a quest for a verdict or for an increase in certainty. During divinations, living people seek the cause of a patient's illness; where a healing requires a trance, they seek confirmation that their sacrifice has been successful; while at a funeral they seek understanding about the cause of the victim's death . Whether on behalf of a recently decea sed person or of a currently ill one, dialogues with the dead are largely discussions of the
Shamans in trance sit side by side at a funeral, surrounded by mourners .
Dialoglles betll'eell th e li villg alld th e dead 7
motivation and operation of these attempts to eat the livin g. Through the offering ofa sacrificial anima l as a subst itute (apalladll) for a patient, they are a lso a means of forestalling the co nsu mmation of these attempts . T he people who take part in a dialogue are genera ll y a wider or narrower circle of relatives. Under these circum stances, various groupings a nd regroupings among the li ving and the dead find themselves in constantly recurring contact: family conversat ion s, jokes and qua rrels continue after some of their participants have crossed the dividing lin e between life and death. It will become increasingly clear in later chapters how the occasions which precipitate these dialogues are not simply co ncerned with reso lving a situation of the moment. Rather, these co nversation s are ep isodes in lon g- running discussions which may bear ultimately on quite different and much la rger matters.
An outline of the argument
I have orga ni sed this book around a se ri es of dialogues between the livin g and the dead . These dia logues involve characters whom the reader will meet in chapter 2 and whose relationships we shall then follow through the book, both while all of these persons are alive and after some of them have died and become sonums. Part I gives a general outline of Sora ideas of the person in life and death . Part II analyses a funeral dialogue in which the living discuss with a recently-dead person the mea ning of his dea th and how it will affect their relationship with each other in the future . Part III analyses the dialogue at a healing rite, in which a family converses with nineteen dead perso ns who have been close to them at different times in the pa st. Each dead person is now seen to be in a distinct cosmological position along the trajectory of his or her ove rall existence. By analysing the evolution of moods in both the living and the dead , I show how a person's private feelings are linked to the unfolding texture of continuing society over a broad sweep of time. Finally, I compare the metaphysics and practices of Sora shamanism with those underlying contemporary psychotherapy and psychoanalysis, and relate each of these to the societies which they serve. To study how a group of unfamiliar people talk about some of their deepest feelings is not to diminish these feelings in our own eyes, but to try to make them at least a little more accessible. Anywhere in the world, regardless of their metaphysics, we may expect people to love and resent each other, the bereaved to grieve and the sick to fear for themselves. But another language or culture is not simply a filter which we can remove in order to understand other people's experiences as our own. Concepts of



8 Dialogues Ivith the dead
emotion '[serve] complex communication, moral and cu ltura l purposes rather than si mply [being] labels for intern al states whose nature or essence is presumed to be universa l' (Lutz 1988 : 5). Nonetheless, havin g been obliged to express myself day in day out in the Sora lan guage and hav ing become emotiona ll y involved with the people aro und me, I believe that we can begin to experience as familiar the apparently stra nge things said in these dialogues. Part ly, this is because we can understa nd so methin g of their systems of classification, in particular their cla ssification of sonl1ms: emotion, to quote Lutz again, is a bout 'seeing things in certain ways' (1988: 2 17). These ways of seeing a re patterned within culture and ca n be analysed. However, empathy does not depend on understanding the mea nin gs a nd relationshjps of concepts alone. Though my analysis will grow out of an unfamiliar cosmology in which, for example, babies are made by a female blacksmith in the Sun, in the end it can be valid only to the extent that it can help us to reflect on our own mortality and our own feelin gs of love and anger, loss and continuity. In transcribing tapes with the aid of notes and my memory, I shall describe tones of voice and styles of delivery as 'ungracious', 'frantic' , 'tender' or 'sarcastic' and in later chapters shall move towards a typology of such moods. This analysis of the emotional tone of dialogues will depend not simply on what was said at the time, but on my understanding of the connections between words and other gestures, actions and events, as well as of the place of these words in the sequences of episodes which make up the relationships of the persons concerned over many years. This is the understanding which Carrithers calls narrativity , the understanding of 'complex nets of ever-new deeds and changing attitudes' by which humans 'perceive any given action not only as a response to the immediate circumstances or current imputed mental state of an interlocutor or of oneself but also as part of an unfolding story' (Carrithers 1990: 269).
Just as we come to know the characters in this book only through the unfolding of theil' relations with other characters, so the empathy the anthropologist or the reader may achjeve with the feelings of the Sora, however limited, is based on an awareness of this narrative . It is therefore far richer than whatever is implied by any notion of empathy with a supposed mental state of the moment , such as 'love' or 'anger'. Michelle Rosaldo suggested that what distinguishes feeling from thought is 'the engagement of the actor's self, ... the apprehension that "I am involved'" (M . Z. Rosaldo 1984: 143). Even at the distance of being a reader, this involvement is with other persons and implies a sense of time. In

empat hi sing with ot hers, we make a n im aginative leap which in volves seein g ourselves in anot her person's situ ation , a sItuation wh ich represents a moment in a sequence. Thus J take feelings to be interpersona l and socia l as well as in terna l and private; and I sha ll conclude, partly with the help of id eas of ca usa lity which are so forcefu l fo r the Sora themselves, that they have, not just an immed iate present, but also a past and a future. Rather than seein g dialogues as bein g abo ut life and death , then , I shall interpret them as being about relationships betweeil persons over time. Death is only a phase of a person's tota l conscious existe nce. The Sora person who begins to emerge from chapter 3 onwards is therefore I~~t the sta rting point of So ra metaphysics, but a hard -won and always provIsional achievem ent. It is not the same kind of bein g as the sharply bounded ent ity commonly ima gined in the West, which as it grows in ma turity reaches a height of individuation and is then subject to sudden a nnihilation (or transportation to an inaccessible, tran scendent rea lm) . Rather, the longer the Sora person lives the more he becomes involved with other perso ns , as his relationships with those others bea r upon his own being in more and more complex ways . Instead of being a bounded entity, the person can also be interpreted in this light as having a core, or focus of concentration, which at its outer edges diffuses into other persons. Persons partake ofeach other's destiny and are understood , even defined , largely in terms of each other. Virtually everything significant in a person's fate, his viability as well as his vulnerability , is interpreted through his involvement with other persons living or dead. The Sora thus appear to see a complex web of agency between persons, in which agents, even while they act on others, are themselves changed by the interaction. Within this web of mutual agency, death plays a central role . The word 'sonum' represents the most enigmatic stage of personhood, both for the Sora and for their interpreter. I do not try to tran slate this word until late in the book, but mea nwhile set the word free in order to see how it behaves. The essence of loss on bereavement is not annihilation of the loved one, but separation from the loved one. People who remain attached to each other are pulled apart into separate realms of existence, which the Sora represent as separate kinds of space, or rather, as separate modes within the same space. The common landscape which is occupied by both the living a nd the dead emphasises the ontological gulf which exjsts between them: even when one visits that very point on the landscape where one knows that a dead person resides, one no longer interacts with them in the same way as one did
when that person was alive. Sonums are enigmatic not only because they are inaccessible. The



10 Dialogues IPith the dead
problem of loss is one ofseparation com bin ed with, or even conceived as, a con tinuin g interaction which is somehow qualitatively tran sform ed . Afte r death, the consciousness becomes a powerful but cont rad ictory causal principle, nourishing its livin g kin through the soul -force it puts into their grow in g crops, but at the sa me time precipitating illness and death amo ng them. This id ea of causality is not simple and its implications will be developed throughout the book. In one aspect, the dead give to the livin g their continued sustenance and their very ex istence, yet in another th ey diminish their being as they impinge on them and consume them. The Sora express thi s causal relationship through an elaborate classifica tion of sonums. Chapter 4 prese nts the fundamental contrast between the two cross-cutting aspects of a dead person. On the one hand , he is an Ancestor-Sonum, retaining his role within the lineage and something of hi s personality and nurturing his descendants . On the other hand , he is a member of one of several collectivities which I call 'Experience' sonums. This contains many people who have died in a simi lar way to himself. In this latter aspect, his personality and lineage role are submerged in favour of an emphasis on the experience of pain which he has suffered and on the aggressiveness with which he tries to perpetuate this suffering among those he has left behind . Thus, to put it at its simplest, someone killed by a leopard becomes a member of the collectivity called Leopard-Sonum and then sends leopards to attack his descendants in order to cause their deaths in turn. Different categories of sonum are located in different features of the landscape. As a living person moves around this landscape, he may encounter sonums and become involved with them. But this happens, not at random , but as a development of his long-term relationships with various dead persons. Though his encounters with sonums may cause illness, they do not constitute a medical history so much as a history of his states of mind in relation to other persons. An illness in a living person is a reflection ofa mood or attitude in a dead person, and the rite which heals a sick person is also a rite to acknowledge the claims of the sonum who has attacked him. The living person's awareness of the dead is an integral part of the symptoms and of the definition of his illnesses, so that his medical history amounts to an important part of his biography. It is the shaman who enables this awareness to be developed through verbal articulacy. By her techniques of trance and formalised verse, she creates the setting for living and dead to confront each other and to speak, each for themselves, in ordinary language. Word-play constitutes the main Sora art-form and words for speaking are often used where one might

In part II ('Responding to a new death'), I ana lyse the dIalogue and the sung verse at a man's funeral. Chapter 5 shows how a group of persons use dialogue at a n inquest to bring themselves lllto a state of shared consciousness with the deceased. This consciousness encompasses a paradoxical mixture offeelings. Thinking about the dec.eased is linked to an awareness of his suffering, a suffering whIch contlllues lllto the present. On the one hand , the living share his suffering out of compassion (abasu)'/I/J). But at the sa me time they fear (batollg- ) the dead person whom they love, since he will later become an aggressor himself and repeat the form of hi s
death on them. For the dead man himself, the funeral is a crucial rite of transformation. In chapter 6, the hving Ancestor-Men impersonate his Ancestors a.nd struggle to 'redeem' him through so ng from the Experience sonum whIch represents the form of his death , in order to lead him into their own company. This redemption of the deceased is not fully successful at first and in the early years he frequently reverts to the form of his Experience and repeats his death on others . The transformation is partly cosmologica l, as the Ancestors move him from one location on the landscape to another; partly ontological, as they turn him from one kind of sonum into another; and partly social, as he is led from the anti-society of his fello:" Experience-victims into the company of his patrilineal Ancestors. ThIS transformation from Experience-member to Ancestor passes through the transitional category of Earth-Sonum, the imagery of which centres on wholeness and retention of form. Earth-Sonums are located in springs and the dead who reside at each site contribute to the soul-force of the crops which grow around that site. Earth-Sonum is an Experience, causing illness and dea th oBut it does so through swelling of the body, the blockage of its orifices and the prevention of childbirth. This very same imagery when applied to the kin-group allows Earth-Sonum to function also as a rallying point for the redeemed dead, since by this same imagery of wholeness It reunites the dispersed members of a lineage segment. Part III (,Operating the calculus of all previous deaths') opens out the discussion to a broader time-scale. Every sonum, at any given moment, has its own cosmological location and its own relationship to each of its mourners. Chapter 7 presents a dialogue in which members of one family converse with the sonums of nineteen of their dead and the state of each one



12 Dialogues Illith th e dead
of these dead persons is revealed. Throughout the remaining chapters, I progressively develop the sociological dimension of the dead person's evo lution . Which one among the range of available alternative Eart hSonum sites he resides in, reflects his status as a future Ancestor for various kin -groups whom he thereby endorses or rejects. The ambiguous status of married women is particularly intense , caught as they are bearing ch ild ren for their husband's lineage against the interests of their father's lineages. Crucia l cases of inheritance can hinge on whether a married woman sett les after death in the Earth site of her hu sba nd 's or her father's Ancestors . Throughout these chapters, I exam ine how the Living and the dead combine to work through their uncertainties abo ut reside nce and affi liation , in dialogues which show that such 'structura l' conflicts are closely linked to long-term shifts in the psychological process of mourning. Two clu sters of relationships are drawn out of this dialogue for closer study at the beginning of chapter 8, which constitutes the heart of the book. In the first case, a dead girl vigorously affirms her devotion to her surviving husband against all the devices which her hostile brothers can summon up to tear them apart. The second case shows a web of relationships which links suicide and attempted suicide among the living to a chain of marriage, childbirth and perverse changes of residence among the dead . This web implicates numerous living persons as they struggle to assert their claims to disputed property and to manage their lives. These two case studies reveal a tangled intertwining of lineage, property, inheritance, love, jea lousy and anger, in which the conflicts of interest involved ensure that living interlocutors will not always hear from the dead what they want to hear. Some of the sonums who speak are almost devoid of personality and leave their living interlocutors indifferent, while others, like the little girl at the beginning of the book, have great emotional power over those whom they address and cause them extreme distress. This chapter analyses the emotional tone of each encounter between dead sonum and living mourner. The analysis correlates unsettled moods in a dead person with a tendency to revert readily to his Experience mode, since the Sora see him as still unhappily trapped in the Experience of his own death; while as he becomes more settled, he will be more inclined to remain in his Ancestor mode. Thus by asking which sonums remain unredeemed and why, we see that there is a correspondence between a dead person's cosmological position and his state of mind . In interpreting this , I build here on the earlier discussion in chapter 4 of how a living person's medical history represents a history of his relationships with others who are now dead. A sonum's effect on you depends on that sonum's own state of mind , but it also equally reflects how you feel a bout the deceased . Sora sonums
Dialoglles between th e fillin g and the dead 13
make you who you a re through inheritance; yet at the same time they invade your house, raid your body and then go back to their hid eouts to ea t and sleep with other so nums. They wander at large, at their own volition , yet they are at"so shared between a number of living interlocutors and ch a nge their nature over time only through the med ium of dia logue with groupS of these persons. Our interpretation of what a sonum is, is intim ate ly bound up with how we try to translate the word into our own lan gua ge. Phenomena in some way resembling sonums are encountered throughout the world. One possible approach to their interpre tation might be first to translate the word into a widely accepted E nglish eq uivalent and then to discuss the workings of the present particular examp le in comparison with others from elsewhere. Among such equivalents, co mmonly used ones include 'spirit' , 'God' or 'god', ' deity' and 'power'. However, the use in anthropology of such terms may block the reader's und ersta nding by prejudging the nature of what one has set out to investigate. A term such as 'spirit' seems to presuppose some degree of consensus and yet at the same time is treated as a ' black box' immune to internal analysis . Owing to the inbuilt materialist bias of much of this work , this consensus is one not so much of understanding as of that kind of distancing which leads to the more intractible formulations of the supposed dichotomy between 'primitive' and 'rational' thinking. Even within theology, where terms like 'spirit' and 'god' are more readily comprehended , there are difficulties. 'God' with a capital 'G' is closely tied to the idea of monotheism and can perhaps be used only when a similar idea is presupposed for the reli gion under discussion , as was done for instance by Evans-Pritchard for the Nuer (1956) or on a worldwide scale by Schmidt (1926- 55); while 'god' with a small 'g' implies a possible multiplicity of such gods so that by contrast to 'God ' we risk suggesting their falsehood or inferiority. For many modern readers faced with the concept so num , such glosses do not support the et hnography in any sense which makes them feel that they could with equal ease have been born to live out their life as a member of Sora society , pervaded as it is day in day out, for instance, by 'spirits' . For such a reader , spirits are something eccentric and marginal and probably are not even 'real'. As Leach asked, speaking no doubt for many others, ' how should we interpret ethnographical statements about palpable untruth? ... Why do all these people believe in something which is untrue?' (1967: 44). If the reader is told that for the Sora, sonums are the main agents and even principle of causality in human affairs, how can he understand this in any way which does not distance him irretrievably from those people's experience? At this stage of the book I propose a gloss for 'sonum' which engages



14 Dialogues Il'ith the dead
with certain key properties which have emerged from our analysis. One of these is the way in which sonum s make a journey through a time which is not homogenous but is shaped by people's feelings. A further property is the association of a sonum with the biographies of living persons, since a sonum is a relational concept: the state of mind ofa sonum affects the state of mind (and body) of the living person. The sequential , causal relation ship between these states suggests that we are dealing with an aspect of memory. The reason why the redemption of the deceased is a slow and painful process is that the living person is unable to free himself from the memory of what things were like before this transforma tion. An illness then appears as a resurgence of this old memory. A healing, called in Sora a 'banishing-rite', seeks to repress this old memory and uses dialogue to confirm its more recen t form. For the rest of the book I translate the word 'sonum' as 'a Memory', with a capital 'M' , as distinct from the ordinary use of the word 'memory' . The consonances and dissonances of putting these two terms side by side bring out clearly why sonums can seem at the same time so close to my feelings yet so foreign to my metaphysics. A sonum has the locus of its existence outside the minds of any of the people whom it affects. A Western 'memory', on the other hand , is still seen as located firmly in the mind even when it is said to be 'eating up' its rememberer. A Sora Memory of a dead person remains outside the mind of the rememberer, who is at least partly a confluence made up of such Memories rather than an entity who could contain them. The use of the term 'Memory' instead of'sonum' for the remainder of the book will again and again throw into relief this contrast in metaphysics. A sonum is my Memory of someone else, so that my present state is affected not only by my own past state, but also by that of another person. So in addition to the onward movement of time there is also a transferral of perspective, a shift from the subjective viewpoint of the deceased to that of his successor. Chapter 8 concludes by pinpointing the mechanism of this transferral in the grammar of the Sora verb, as a dying person shifts his perspective from being the victim of someone else's aggressive action to being the perpetrator of his own . The Experience mode of being dead makes an analogy between persons based on shared suffering and is associated with transitive verbs. The living person embarks on his path towards death in the passive: he is 'grabbed', 'seized', 'raped' , 'eaten up'. He emerges some time after death as a grammatically active, causal agent who perpetrates on others the force of those same verbs whose action he suffered earlier. He is now doing the 'grabbing' and 'seizing' . Having been the patient of someone else's violence, he has become the agent of his own. The
Dialogues betll'eel/ the fillil/g al/d the dead 15
process of public divination , with its shared con sciou sness, unmas ks the age ncy of the attackin g sonum which is otherwise hidden by the form of the Sora pa ssive verb . Only through divination does it becom e possible, not only to sa y thal the patient is being attacked , but to say by whom. It is thi s whi ch ex terio rises the pati ent's inn er fee lings and ma kes them part of the 'objective' world outside.
This viciou s cycle is broken only when the dead person 's redemption becomes complete and he becomes an Ancestor who does not revert to his Experience mode. In his Ancestor mode , he sets up a different kind of analogy with a livin g person . This is expressed by a verb in the middle voice, whereby he creates an identifica tion between himself and the new ba by who is to be his namesake. Here, there is a transmission without aggression and the repetition is one not of action but of being, not ofa form of suffering but of social identity.
The Sora person who passes through various stages of being alive and dead is thus not an entity to which memory-traces cling on the outside like barnacles . He emerges not so much as having a firm core or an essence, as being a changeable confluence of attributes and traces of memories and events, a coming together of other people's perceptions and actions as much as of his own.
Just as the person came into being through his cumulative interaction with others, so I use my translation of a sonum as a Memory in order to interpret the sonum's gradual decline and dissolution as a process of being forgotten. In chapter 9 we see the person dissolving again after death into his various constituents, of which his Experience and Ancestor aspects have been only the first to emerge clearly. The dead person's choice between rival Earth-Sonum sites is an essential stage in the disputed transmission of his material property; for a woman, in addition, it is a final statement about the validity of her marriage. One by one, the qualities or attributes of the deceased fall away from him and are returned to the living, who then have less and less reason to talk with him. The last constituent to be returned is the person 's name, which returns to one child among his descendants in a festival which echoes the format of the funeral but inverts its imagery by suffusing it with joy. There remains a final, vestigial residue of the deceased which continues its trajectory ever onwards, away from the awareness of the living. Eventually, this attenuated Memory dies a second death in the Underworld, at which point it becomes a butterfly beyond the reach of any communication with the living. This occurs around the time when the deceased is passing out of living memory, a Memory who has no-one left alive to remember him.



16 Dialogues with the dead
Chapter 10 concludes the book by asking how we can best understand Sora ideas of death, loss and continuity by looking at our own ways of remembering, grieving and looking forward to the future . Within the overall diversity of modern industria l society, there is a growing dissatisfaction with what many people see as an increasingly alienated relationship to death, as religion flows out of people's lives and leaves the field largely to
psychology. Though Sora insights into the human co ndition are profound, I argue that to adapt them for use in contemporary psychotherapy is more problematic than we might wish: though they are good answers, they do not correspond directly to the same questions. The psychological effect of dialogues with the dead is deeply rooted in the total patterning of Sora society, in which the person grows and falls away again, all in term s of other persons. Consciousness is not the starting point of a Sora perso n's interaction with other persons but a result, as though one did not have full consciousness without this interaction . The lives of the Sora people whom we have met show how the Sora practice of funeral and dialogue cannot assuage every grief. However, it does provide a framework of structural time which nurses the evolution ofa mourner's feelings and relates this to a public acknowledgement of these feelings, along with the feelings of many others. This acknowledgement is possible because of the exteriority of the Sora Memory, which is not contained within the diffuse living person but is stored in features of the common landscape. Psychotherapy and psychoanalysis lack the Sora concepts of communal space and time, as well as the interaction with exterior Memories which exist outside their rememberer. The theoretical foundation of most modern secular bereavement counselling within these idioms lies in a seminal paper by Freud called ' Mourning and melancholia ' (1957[1917]). Freud's paper yields a model of the process of bereavement which is parallel to that implicit in Sora thinking, yet one which inverts the fundamental Sora presupposition about the continued autonomous, exterior existence of Memories. The psychological and social consequences turn out to be more far-reaching than the mere fact of logical inversion would suggest. If the dead person does not 'really' exist, then to treat with him beyond a certain time must be judged pathological. Yet for the Sora, the deceased does continue to exist. While they use the categories of Ancestor and Experience to distinguish the more from the less desirable, there is nothing 'pathological' about the Experiences. These are mapped across the common landscape and the process of conventionalisation represented by the Experiences reminds the mourner that the loss which to him seems unique
Dialogues betweel1 the Iivil1g alld th e dead 17
and terrible, is ana logo us in very specific ways to numerous other losses which have happened before and will happen aga in . By placing the sufferer in the midst of a public divination, the gro up converts hi s private perception info a common one and locates the sou rce of his suffering outs id e the circl e which they all share. The very fact that other livin g rememberers have slightly different personal memories of the deceased only confirms the reality of his ex iste nce as the point at which their separate memories all overlap . The experiencing subject, the individua l perceiver, is subordinated at every turn to somet hin g grea ter than himself. This is co llective and, through the manner in which it encompasses dispute, is ultimately co nsensual. Where Freud and bereavement co un se lling offer us a mere psychology of death, the Sora offer a total interpretation of death. Their view of death is larger because for them death itse lf is not a negation of life but a continuation of it with a change in the quality of fac'e-to-face interaction. This is why the dead are so articulate. Once one party to a relationship dies , certain inhibitions are lifted and the unspeakable can become the spoken. But when we seek the source of what it is which is spoken, we cannot a ttribute this solely to the individual speakers or to the shamans through whom their messages pass. We are driven back to the community as a whole. In an attempt to clarify the shaman's role, I compare her not only to the psychoanalyst but also to Dostoyevsky as an author of the 'polyphonic novel', in which the characters speak, supposedly independently of the author, with a 'pllll'ality of voices' . In some essentials the comparison turns out to be close; but it also shows that the shaman has a different relationship to space and time, while the contradictory voices within and around her are those of litigants ra ther than, as in Dostoyevsky, of ideologies. The speakers have a fa r higher degree of autonomy than do the characters of Dostoyevsky, and the sa me living and dead persons communicate with each other on different occasions through a range of different shamans. The complex web of perso ns' lives means that Soras can be seen as the authors of both their own and of each other's personhood . They discuss their feelings, not so much through nouns for inner states of emotion such as 'love' or 'anger', as through verbs which link states of consciousness such as Memories . These verbs represent the dynamic strand of interpersonal relations among both the dead and the living. What one might like to call inner feelings are brought to the very edge of the Sora person, to the point at which they encounter other persons and a re dialogised.



18 Dialogues Ivith the dead
A note on 'shamans' and other intermediaries T he Sora word which I have translated as 'shaman' is kurall. T he re are two kinds of shaman, the ' funeral' (sallatung) kind , and the 'divination' (tedulIg) kind 'vvho also perform healings. Funeral shamans are more ' im portant' (muda) and are largely women , while divining and hea lin g shamans, who are more ' lowly' (osellg) , are somet imes women but often also men. In a large village there may be two or three of the former and a dozen 01: more of the latter. Divinations and most hea ling rites are a sma ll affair and a shama n perform s a lone. But at fun eral s, which a re more momentous occasions held in stages over three years, several may sit together. In one village with a strong line of succession Thave seen four young peo ple (three women and one ma n) who sat sid e by side with a n old, nearly blind woman who was their teacher. Each acted independently as the mouthpiece for a different stream of dead people while their living interlocutors moved from one to another of them amidst the hubbub. The dead , just like the living, sometimes also conducted their own subsidiary conversations as the shamans through whom they spoke turned their heads away from the living
towards each other. On the morning of a journey to the Underworld the shaman fasts , though she may drink alcohol and smoke tobacco. An assistant often lights a lamp which will be kept burning throughout the shaman's journey in the darkness below. The shaman sits down with her eyes closed and her legs stretched straight out in front of her, a posture not used at any other time since one usually sits on the floor squatting. Then , perhaps beating a steady pulse with a stick or knife, or else swishing grain around a winnowing fan , she sings a song calling on a succession of former shamans who a re now dead and are called her rauda so nums . The journey which she is about to undertake is an impossible one for ordinary people who will eventually make it once only, when they die, and with no hope of returning to their bodies. The earth and the Unde rworld are linked by a huge tree, down which the shaman must clamber. Sora also say that the path includes dizzyi ng precipices on the way down to the ' murky-sun country, cockcrow-light country (mongol-yung desa. tartar-im desa)' . In order to make this journey, the shaman's soul ' becomes' a monkey . And so, in a ~n~ or quave ring voice according to her age, she sings words like the followll1g 111 a special tune to her predecessors (text 1.2):

After some minutes of this singing, her voice peters out and her head flops down on to her breast . Her soul has now reached her husband and children in the Underworld . Bystanders, who have proba bly paid no pa rticular attention to the invocation , now rush forward in a commotion to unclench her body which has locked itself rigid . They bend her knees and lay her legs down straight again on the ground, bend her elbows, unclench her fingers and then return them , limp , to rest on her knees. Even in frail little old ladies, this clenching can defy two or three strong men for some seco nds. When her body has been loosened , the shaman sits motionless for a while. Then with a sharp intake of breath her ha nd passes over her mouth and the sonum of her immediate predecessor, her closest rauda , begins to si ng her own tune in reply and to speak as mistress of ceremonies. The ra ud a then leads on a succession of the dead , each of whom demands a drink on arrival a nd maybe a smoke too. The dialogue now begins . Shamans of either sex acquire their ability to perform by marrying a special kind ofsonum, called tlda , in the Underworld (see chapter 3). Men, if they are to become shamans in either tradition , generally acquire their vocation in adult life; but women start to become shamans at a very early age, usually around seven or eight years old . In the dreams of the future shaman, rauda sonums come and escort her down to the Underworld . The pa th is terrifying, as little girls have told me, but the sonums are kind and reassuring . At first these dreams come only occasionally, but later they a re



20 Dialoglles with the dead
repeated eve ry ni ght and come to lose their fearsome quality. Around puberty the girl marries a so num who is the so n in the Underworld of a livin g shaman known to her. This shaman is ge ne rally , thou gh not a lways, a patrilinea l relative. (Similarly , a male shaman marries a living predecesso r's so num -da ughter.) She now has a framed rectangular wall-pa inting made a nd consecrated as a 'house' for him to vi sit her in return. It is this marria ge a nd co nsecration which will give her the ability to enter trance. For the first few years she will not do thi s alone, but while sitting alongside the older, more ex perienced shaman whose so num-son she ha s marri ed . Down in the Underworld the shaman ha s a full domestic life with a spouse and growin g children, indepe ndent of any marria ge which she (or he) may have above ground. This mea ns that when she goes to sleep she resumes her other life down below just whe re she left off the la st tim e she was there, in the same way as we all do on waking up in this world. Sora say that trance involves the same absence of the soul from the body as dreaming, except that whether dreaming or in trance, the experience of a shaman has a coherence and continuity which are lacking in the dreams of the rest of us. It is this consistency which ena bles trance to be purposeful, in

In terms of a compara tive typology of shamani sm vel'sus spirit possession (e.g. de He usch 198 1) the kUl'an is per haps hard to place in that whil e her so ul is sa id to travel during trance to the Underworld , she does not send back messages about her own doings there but simply acts as a cha nnel through which the dead speak for th emse lves. In th e revised edition of hi s epo nymous survey of 'shamani sm' , E liade devoted some space to th e So ra (1964: 421 - 7, usin g Elwin (1955) as hi s source). In talking a bout the IWl'an 's journey to the Underworld , the Sora do indeed use ima gery , such as a ladder or tree linking cosmological leve ls, which corresponds closely to that widely documented by Eliade and considered by him to be among the cla ssic defining features of 'shamanism' (E li ade 1964). However, the interest of the Sora lies not in the typological status of their practice, intriguing though this may be. In this book I shall focus not on the shaman but on her clients, not on her own experience but on the way she helps lay persons to live their own lives . In doing this, I shall reverse a widespread approach which leads Eliade, for example, to see 'shamanism' as a 'mysticism', ' at the disposal of a particular elite' (1964: 8). Given the shaman's need for an audience even to unclench her body and the fact that a trance takes place only in order to summon the ancestors of that same audience, her experience can have meaning only to the extent that it is in some sense shared by laymen. The format of dialogue ensures that lay people share in the shaman's activity but that they do so from their own perspective, which is a complementary one. Moreover, in making the extension from dream to trance, the Sora are showing us quite clearly that for them specialisation is a question of degree: the shaman's activity and experience are a specialised form of those of any other member of the society. In cha pter 4 and throughout this book, I shall link this question of degrees of specialisation to the ordinary conversational tone of dialogues with the dead. For the moment , I suggest that we think of the kUl'an's practice not as ' shamanism' but as 's hamanship '. I take this term from Atkinson, who defines it as ' the art or skill of performing as a shaman' (1989 : 17). Writing of the Wana of Sulawesi, she uses this term in the context of competition between performers who 'must strive to achieve ... recognition of their shamanic claims' (Atkinson 1989: 17). Though the Sora recognise better and worse shamans (one shaman I knew was known for the rest of his life as 'Oops!' [Gege] because a crab he was hiding for a conjuring trick popped out of his pocket before its cue), their vocation is



22 Dialogues Il'ith the dead
not seriou sly in doubt. But there is another helpful resonance to the E ngli sh word 's hamanship '. L ike craftsmanship or musicianship , this is a ta lent or inclinat ion as much as a n act ivity a nd is spread variously a mong pe rso ns, who practise it to varying degrees . Many people know and can even reproduce the words of a shaman's invocation but are simply unable to make them work as performances, that is, to act out their implications fully. Most people a re ab le to perform various rites, for example to protect cattle or trees from so rcery, and every man ca n make minor offerings to his own patrilineal ancestors. Villages genera lly also have a hereditary male buy a, who conducts harvest festival s for the village Kidtung, a creator so num and villa ge guardian (this corresponds broadly to a widespread Indian pattern of a so ber pries thood co ntrasted with a performance based o n trance, e.g. Dumont 19 59; Berrema n 1964; Babb 1975). There is also a hereditary siga, the village funeral pyre-li ghter. Though such peo ple do not marry in the Underworld or go into trance, they all receive their abilities by succession and with the aid of the sonums of their predecessors. Anyone performing any of these actions is at that time called kuran, the noun which I have already glossed in its pal' excellence vocational sense as 'shaman '. Most importantly for my own fieldwork, each patrilineage has several men and women called 'Ancestor-Men' (ida i-mal') and 'Ancestor-Women' (idai-boj) . Ancestor-Women are women who were born into the lineage a nd still live in it, and are therefore either unma rried girls or else widows or divorcees who have returned from elsewhere. It is they who strip, wa sh and dress a corpse, male or female. Whatever the relationship, 'now there's no embarrassment' (nami garoj tid) . It is they who pour water on the ashes to 'cool' the deceased when the pyre ha s died down ; and it is one of these Ancestor-Women who keeps the la mp burning to light the funeral shaman's way in the Underworld . Ancestor-Men are also members of the linea ge, me n of suitable talent and temperament, who for several hours sing, dance a nd mime the various stages and procedures of the funeral. Like the shamans in trance, Ancestor-Women and Ancestor-Men fast , consuming only alcohol a nd tobacco for one day (during the 'stone-planting' sta ge ofa funeral) or three days (during the kmja, the a nnual festival of the dead and harvest-commemoration) . In this wa y, somewhat like the shaman, they move 'close' (a 'dam) to the sonums: 'they become the Ancestors, so to speak' (idaienji gadtlteji amrid). At the end, before they can safely eat any of the sacrificed meat, they likewise pass through a careful 'making healthy' (ab -suka-) rite to bring them back among the living. I was grateful for the existence of the role of Ancestor-Man as a half-way
Dialoglles betll'een the fil'ing and the dead 23
stage towards Full shamanship. D urin g my first year in the fi eld , I had fanta sies a bout becoming a trainee shaman, Fa ntasies whi ch I gradua ll y ca me to see as na ive. T he problem wa s not so much that sha man s were unwilling. So,:a have ways of keeping o ther known ethn ic groups at arm's length, but my presence there was so unprecede nted that anyt hin g mi ght pe rhaps have been possible. Shama ns themselves did eventua ll y encourage me to work as their assistant rather than sim ply to observe and record. (My friend Kantino saw another way for me to get involved: 'Why don 't you marry the shama n - it would be a perfect match. She' ll go into tra nce, you' ll write it down! ' [Kuranbojen orl/llga - tamte. Anin m erte, amen idoltem. My inabili ty to become a full sha man was due to a combination of my own difficulties with language and faith , as well as to my la ck of loca l co ntext. Fo r instance, sha mans receive a lm ost a ll their teachin g from ra ud as in dreams: my own drea ms only rare ly gave me fra gments of this ex peri ence and never allowed me to make the necessa ry inces tuou s marriage with a hi gh-caste k~atriya cousin (see chapter 3) . But as soon as I returned for my seco nd period of fieldwork after a n absence of two years, I fell immed ia tely a nd eas ily into the role of an Ancestor-Man for the linea ge of Jamano a nd Mengalu (both introduced in chapter 2) a nd was one of the three or four men , including M engalu himself, who fasted, danced and sang at the karja and at severa l stone-plantings for peo ple whom I had known when they were alive. Much of the detailed material a nalysed in this book is based on my participation in this role. Like any Sora, my experiences were made possible, and my own biography developed , by talking to the dead as well as to the living.



2
The Sora people
A 'tribe' in Hindu India
The Sora (also sometimes written So:ra:, Saora, Savara, etc.) number around 452,000 1 and are officially designated a 'scheduled tribe'. This is a heterogeneous administrative category derived from a combination of nineteenth-century ethnology and the pigeon-holing imperative of the census. It is used for around 40 million people in India (38 million in 1971: Raza and Ahmad 1990: 6) who are anomalous for reasons of language or occupation, or else marginal geographically or economically. Many, though not all, of these 'tribals' are assumed to be aboriginal and to predate the arrival in India of Aryan and Dravidian populations; they are also widely thought to be somehow not fully Hindu . The Sora fit most, if not all, of these criteria . They have long been at the outer edge of various kingdoms and other administrative divisions. They live largely by shifting cultivation (slash-and-burn agriculture) and relate to the world of markets as purveyors of shifting-cultivation crops and wild forest produce. Above all, their linguistic connections lie ultimately with southeast Asia. Linguists classify the Sora language as belonging to the Munda group, which includes some other Indian 'tribal' languages such as Mundari, Ho, Santal and Bondo; while they consider this group itself to be a branch of the Austroasiatic family which includes Mon-Khmer languages such as Cambodian, as well as many of the languages of the interior of the Malay peninsula and of the Montagnard peoples of Vietnam (Pinnow 1959; Zide 1966).2 Though there are historical uncertainties, this suggests that such peoples form an ancient stratum of the population across tropical Asia who in each country have been surrounded and dispossessed by larger, settled rice-growing popula tions. Certainly, some Sora faces look southeast Asian and the flavour of Sora
The Sora people 25
culture often seems reminiscent of the linguistically related peopl es described by Howell (1984) , Benjamin (1967) or Co ndomina s (1965, 1974). Yet at the sa me time, contact with surrounding popula tions is longsta nding and comp lex a nd many aspects of So ra culture a lso seem close to village H indui sm as described by Whitehead (1 92 1), Babb (197 5) or Herren schmidt (1989). As one walks or cycles out of the mountainous So ra heart land into the plains beyond, it is imposs ible to say quite where the fl avour of Sora culture end s. The ethnic environment of the Sora is cos mopolitan . To the northea st live the 'Hindu' Oriya population who speak a language of the Indo-Aryan family and to the south live the 'Hindu' Telugu , belonging to the Dravidian family. To the northwest live the Khond or Kond who , though they speak a Dravidian language, are also considered ' tribal' and whose culture is in many ways similar to Sora. The Sora sometimes think of themselves as adiv(lsi, a pan-Indian term for ' tribal ' which sometimes has radical political overtones, though my impression is that they learned this term in the tea gardens of Assam, where they sometimes go to earn cash and work alongside members of other central Indian 'tribes'. The Sora in this study also refer to themselves as 'Hindu' (indu) in opposition to the enclaves of missionised Christian (kir/un) Sora in their midst. The Sora perceive themselves as falling into several main divisions . The Sora groups in the plains, such as the Sarda (,clean') and Kapu (,cultivator') Sora, live by various occupations and resemble their casteHindu neighbours more or less closely. These groups remain virtually unstudied . The Sora with whom I lived call themselves the Lanjia Sora and are also known variously as Lamba-Lanjia , Jedu ('wild'), Mone, and Arsi (,monkey'). The approximate distribution of the Lanjia Sora is shown on figure 2.1 (though this entire region is heavily infiltrated by a number of castes and representatives of government agencies). The word 'Sora' in this book refers to these Lanjia Sora, who by any criterion can be considered the most isolated and 'tribal' of any Sora group. They straddle the mountainous border between Ganjam District and Koraput District in Orissa , an area to this day made inhospitable to visitors by virulent malaria . I lived mostly in the Sora villages within a few miles of Puttasing, a settlement on the Koraput side which contains some assorted officials but is inhabited mainly by Pano (Dom) , a Christianised group of Oriya Harijans who live throughout tribal Orissa by trading with the tribals and lending them money at high interest. Lanjia Sora territory comprises a series of mountains rising to 3,000 ft. It is bounded to the north by the Khond hills and on the other three sides by



26 Dialogues lVith the dead
plains o r rive r valleys. A t the foot of the hills lie the lowns ofGunlipur a nd Parlakhemundi (Parlakimidi), co ntaining a diversity of Oriya and Telu gu casles as well as Sarda a nd Kap u Soras. For lo ng the seats ofTelliga rajas, the towns now act as admini st ra tive headq uarters a nd the main commercia l focusses o f the area . To the So ra they are a world of police and cl erks armed with guns a nd bteracy , of metal-workers, bu s-sta nds, cemen t hou ses and loud speakers blaring film music over bazaars where the flare of kerosene lamps a fter dark falls on piles of plastic trinkets and bales of cloth brightl y co lo ured with sy nthet ic dyes. This is a world which most So ra find both rep ul sive a nd fa scinating, but overall deeply intimidating. The pa th out of Gunupur towards the Lanjia Sora hills leads through fields of paddy a nd irri gated vegetables by the river Vamsadhara and past a village of Sard a Sora s. From these one cro sses the desolate fla t area where Gunupur cuts its firewood. Once fores ted, thi s is now furrowed with deeply
