Abo ut six miles on, after a little climbing through a fairly bare land scape, one wa lks into laltar (in Sora: Ya ltab), a village conta inin g Soras who accord in g to perspective may be ca lled ei ther Sarda or La nji a and who live side by sid e with a number of administrative officers and se ttlers of various castes. Thi s is the scene of the weekly market through which a large quantity of Sora produce leaves the hills beyond , bound for the plains. After a further six miles, up two steep passes and past severa l wholly Lanjia Sora villages, one comes to Puttasing (in Sora : G udangsin g, ' Level Village') , a sma ller sett lemen t almost exclusively of some 400 Pa no a long with a few Pa ik traders and some gove rnment employees, but no Soras. Here, surrounded by a small er area of eroded firewood copp ice (the Sora go far into the forest for their wood), a re the la st sma ll police stat ion , dispensary , post office, a nd primary school. A simila r picture hold s for the route from Parlakimidi via Gumma to the gove rnment outpost of Serango (in Sora: Serung). This is more developed as there is a surfaced road along which buses ply, thou gh the road to Puttasing is due to be surfaced likewise. There are other similar outposts on all sides along paths reaching past weekly markets at the foot of hills almost into the centre of Sora territory . Between these outposts the mountainous hea rtland , populated exclusively by Soras, is not more than a few miles across . During the years I have known the area, non-Sora immigration into these outposts appears to have increased noticeably. As one draws away from Gunupur, certain contrasts appear between the Sora and the peoples of the plain. Many of the Oriya and Telugu castes, and even the Sarda Soras, have comparatively long-limbed, dark, often almost ebony-skinned bodies and their men ha ve a strong beard growth and hairy legs 'like a bear' (saluan amrid) . By contrast, the Lanjia Sora have shorter bodies, sometimes with a light reddi sh-brown skin, rounder faces, often with distinctly southeast Asian fea tures, no bea rd growth and very little body hair. Among both men and women, the chest is usually bare when they are among their own kind. For men , the ankle-length lungi, dhoti or trousers of the plains give way to a cream-coloured homespun co tton loincloth exposing thin muscular thighs and buttocks or increasingly these days, to market-bought khaki shorts. Whereas women in the plains wear saris, often brightly coloured, Sora women wear a knee-length wraparound skirt, which is either homespun in cream or market-bought in black. Instead of the vivid colours of plastic and nylon, virtually all artefacts in Sora homes have the colour of the earth, stone or dried vegetation from which they were made. Sora women wea r none of the masca ra or other cosmetics of the pla ins, though the faces of both women



28 Dialogues with the dead
and to a lesser exten t men have perm a nent ta ltoos , dyed with the juice of an indigo -coloured berry. The bri ghtest colours are those of their jewellery in bra ss, silver, aluminium, gold and co loured glass, all made in the plains by Hindu castes usin g a technology of fire which remains a mystery to the Sora; brass waterpots, though only in wealthier homes; and the red patterned bands in vegetable dye, used deliberately sparin gly on the borders of homespun loincloths and skirts. Even in the markets the Sora choose cloth of kha ki , black, whi te or da rk bl ue and the merchan ts ca tel' for them accordingly . The Sora comme nt on the limited use of bright colour in their earth and vegetation-tinted world: red (je) is the colour of blood and fire, and a dream of the red borders on clothing presages bloodshed or a violent death . Freer use of bright colours is associated with the plains: to wear flowers or coloured cloth on one's person is to invite an attack by Ruga-boj ('Smallpox-Woman'), a sonum of the plains whom they identify with the goddess Durga or Thakurani. I was first inspired to study the Sora through reading Elwin's account of them in his book The religion of an Indian tribe (1955). It was this work which made the Sora widely known among both anthropologists and students of comparative religion (cf. Eliade 1964). The importance of religion in Sora life is obvious to any observer and Sora rites and 'spirits' already feature prominently in the earlier accounts by Fawcett (1888), Thurston (1909) and Sitapati (1938-43). Elwin's account of Sora religion has since been widely paraphrased in India (in Bose (1972) and in many popular magazine articles). It is clear that Elwin was fascinated and moved by the Sora . His book is long and at first sight appears exhaustive, but is ultimately disappointing. Dumont (1959: 60- 74) and Turner (1967) each wrote substantial review articles. Both lamented the accumulation of description which, since it lacks order, tantalises and ultimately overwhelms the reader. Though they each responded to the exciting nature of Elwin's material, each with a different emphasis pinpointed the source of his own disappointment. Dumont tried to puzzle out the place of the Sora within India as a whole, while Turner tried to find a connection between Sora rites and social structure. Dumont has presented his overview of India in his book Homo hierarchicus (1980 [1966]). This portrays India as a whole made up of subordinate parts (the various 'castes'), tightly interlocked in a mutual dependence which is at one and the same time functional (via their specialised occupations), political (through the system's organising principle of hierarchy) and above all religious, in that the high status of the
The Sora people 29
Brahmin depends on an intrinsic, hereditary ritual purity, and the lower statu s of othe rs on a corresponding degree of uncl ea nness. Thou gh thi s model ha s not been supplanted by a nythin g of equal weight, it raises many problems and has bee n widely criticised . For exa mple Parry (1974 , 1979) drew atten tion to a wid espread co untervailin g principle of ega litariani sm; and while the model is apparently designed to account for low castes, it is unable to accommod a te the tribal s, who constitute an important , distinctive and highly varied section of the Indian population . Dumont pays scant attention to tribal et hnography and hi s main discussion of it, coincidentally, is probably in his review (19 59: 60- 74) of Elwin's book. He was frustrated at his inability to place the Sora clearly within his hierarchical and monolithic model of India. On the one hand , he concludes that the Sora are 'autonomous' (Dumont 1959: 61) and ' not Hindus' because they 'do not imitate the Hindus in the recognition of impurity. They do not submit directly to the scheme of Hindu values' (Dumont 1959: 61). Yet on the other hand , they cannot be considered 'a bsolutely alien to Hinduism' since 'the relation of the SaOl-as [sic] with their spirits is achieved through marriage of their shamans with some of the Hindu dead, considered of higher rank and greater power than their own' (Dumont 1959: 66). I do not propose to discuss the Soras' relation to Hinduism at length in this book, reserving tlus for a later work.) Rather than finding an inside-outside model of tribal-Hindu relations, the reader who knows other parts of India will notice a variety of ways in which the Sora sometimes ignore ideas and images from mainstream Indian culture, sometimes take them over and recombine them in original ways. Beneath the immediate phenomenon of dialogues with the dead we seem to be witnessing another ancient and continuing dialogue between the Sora and their wider Indian environment. From Dumont's perspective, looking from the summit downwards, there would be no place for this kind of debate since the ethnographic situation of the moment could be little more than a failed model. But what Dumont presents amounts to only one indigenous model , that of the Brahmins, and it is only one actor's viewpoint among several possible ones. For the Sora, this limitation would be ironic since they prefer if anything to play with certain ksatriya values . This kind of play is open-ended and invites continuous re-evaluation of its object. The Sora do not try to 'inlitate' the outside world but respond to it with a more or less self-conscious commentary which sometimes amounts to parody, as with Pubic-Haired Sompa, the ambiguously Sora-Hindu foundress of shamanism who will appear in the next chapter.



30 Dialogues 11'ith the dead
For Turner the Africanist, the interest of the Sora wa s quite different. He was frustrated at Elwin's lack of sociological data which would have allowed him to grasp the relationship of the rites descr ibed to the Sora patrilineage. In his introduction, Elwin had written baldly: 'The most remarkable thing about the orga nisation of Saora society is its lack of organization' (Elwin 19 55: 50), and the remainin g 500 pa ges, rather oddly, discuss a religion based on ancestors with virtually no reference to kinship. There is simply no concept of the lineage and little explicit data with which the reader could construct such a concept. Nonetheless, by detective work from Elwin's raw descriptions Turner was able to draw some inferences about the uneasy position of married women between lineages and also about the foundation of shamanship in fantasy incest (Turner 1967: 192-4). These interpretations, as far as he was able to take them, will generally be borne out in the present book. Turner also sensed the possible solidarity, cutting across the lineage, of groups of cross-cousins (1967: 193, though cross-cousins do not as he suggested form residential groupS).4 There is another serious problem in Elwin's book which was not noted by reviewers but is central to the concern of the present work . Despite his own Anglican background (Elwin 1964) and his undeniable theological and literary sensitivity, Elwin translates 'sonum' indiscriminately as 'god', 'deity' and 'spirit' (Elwin 1955 : e.g. xxi, xxiii), a casualness which led Turner at one point into a fruitless speculation about the differences between 'spirits' and 'gods' as agents of affliction (Turner 1967: 186).5 As much as the absence of sociological perspective, it is this which renders The religion of an Indian tribe an ultimately baffling account of a religion. What is the point of sonums? The reader emerges from Elwin's account unable to understand what a sonum is, or why for the Sora they exist and are tolerated. The picture given is a gloomy one in which the dead oppress the living unremittingly . I shall argue that the two questions of sociology and theology are not separate: a sonum's power is derived at the same time both from its theological attributes and from its position in social organisation. Seen in this way, sonums also playa constructive role. So far as the fact of death will allow - a profoundly theological consideration - they give to the living as much as they take from them. Historically, the evidence suggests that the Sora have been squeezed into their present enclave from a much wider area. Many villages along the coast from the Mahanadi valley well into Andhra Pradesh have Sora names but few if any 'Sora' inhabitants. The Sora did not appear to practise human sacrifice and so did not attract the righteous British campaigns which were waged against the neighbouring Khond (Campbell 1864; Macpherson
The Sora people 31
1865; Padel 1987). Nonetheless th e Puttasing area was brought und er government rule in 1864-6 by an expedit ion ary force which executed and transported Sora resistance leaders a nd esta bllshed a permanent Pollce presence (Se na pati and Sa hu 1966: 72) . Thereafter, the area has been sLlspended in permanent marginality between various distant cen tres (Elwin 1955 : preface; Bell 1945) . F or much of the colonial period, this entire region lay at the far north ernmost tip of the vast Madras Presidency. But the Sora live on the very boundary between the north and sOLlth Indian culture areas and this is now appropriately reflected in their division between two of India's modern states. If they go down the mountain one way, they enter the Indo-Aryan world of the Oriya -speaking state of Orissa ; if they go down the other side , they find themselves in the Telugu-speaking, Dravidian state of Andhra Pradesh. 6 Even within the state of Orissa which now encompasses most of their territory, the Sora people live on either side of the border between the di stricts of Ganjam and Koraput . On the Ganjam side they were ruled in the British period through Oriya middlemen of Paik (Warrior) caste and ksatriya varna; and on the Koraput side through Sora village Headmen (gomang , also meaning 'rich man'), who were appointed by the British and collected revenue for the raja of Jeypore . Since the 1950s, the office of Headman has been officially suspended (though in practice it remains powerful) and the area governed through local councils '(pancayat). The Soras' awareness of the outside world is considerable, though uneven (my friends were convinced that I could not safely invite them to my country since I would be unable to restrain my fellow-countrymen from eating them). The Sora have been pushed to the edges of a world, the other parts of which have a greater bureaucratic, economic and cultural complexity than they have themselves . They interact with this world in highly specific and limited ways. The Sora see themselves as surrounded by a range of life forms, from monkeys, pythons and plants to rajas and policemen. Each of these has its own jati (race or species). But there are possibilities of merging and equivalence between these. Animals' souls are a substitute for human souls in sacrifice; dead Soras become part of the soul of their living descendants'crops; shamans in trance become monkeys when they clamber down to the Underworld; the Lanjia (also Arsi , 'monkey') Sora as a whole are monkeys because they are a jungle people whose men wear loincloths with a long tail; and Sora shamans marry ksatriya sonums in the Underworld and themselves become ksatriya after death . In the same way, the lineage is exogamous and Lanjia Soras in the hills also sometimes marry other kinds of Sora from the plains; while terms



32 Dialog ues Illith the dead
like Jedu and Sarda are not absolute but relative , implying respectively uphill and downhill , of the jungle and of the cultivated landsca pe. Since around the 1920s many young Sora have gone to pick tea in Assam , some 600- 900 miles away , usually for a year a t a time, and thi s is also a common way of escaping for a while from an embarra ssin g situation at home. In recent years they have also worked on road building projects in the North -Ea stern Frontier Agency (N E FA) area . Here and in Assam they meet members of many other tribes , learn some Hindi as a lingua./i'anca and live, according to their own account, in a strange, casteless frontier society. Mengalu , whom we shall meet below, was bombed during the last war while working as a labourer for the British in Burma. This migrant labour helps to integrate the Sora into the Indian cash economy . So too does their extensive sale of produce to local Panos and traders of other castes who come up from the plains to the weekly markets . Sora villa ges and households have trading rela tionships and long-running debts with specific Panos, who also exert effective control over the pancayats and act as interpreters and go-betweens in the Soras' dealings with government employees. These relationships are characterised by mutual insult and distaste, at the same time as having something of a jajmGni flavour of mutual dependence. The Sora incur debts especially for the buffalos which they sacrifice to their dead and which they say cannot be bred in the Sora hills. The moneylenders manage these debts at high rates of interest in such a way that they can rarely be paid off, while the Sora sell their produce at low prices in a climate of intimidation which is often crudely physical. In addition, while local traders manage their Sora partners shrewdly with a long-term perspective, some government officers who are sent against their will to this remote area on 'punishment posting' extort huge sums during their short stay on a hit-and-run basis. Sora wealth is based on a manipulation of these outside forces which is often played defensively, as well as on the ownership of the region's limited paddy land, which yields much higher profits than the slash-and-burn plots on which most of the population rely.7 The Headmen and their close lineal relatives control much of this paddy land and other Soras often criticise them for the exploitation of their fellow-villagers, most of whom are their own relatives. But a strong Headman's clique is also able to resist the intense pressures of outsiders seeking political control and financial gain within the communities (I have discussed the topics in these paragraphs at length in Vitebsky 1978a, 1978b). Clearly, the Soras' political position with regard to neighbouring ethnic
The Sora people 33
groupS is not a strong one. But Sora c ulture is not a stripped -down culture of pove rt y, in which shamanism is Iitlle more than a compensation for helplessness; nor does it prese nt a picture of deviance and squ alor, as in F reeman 's vivid account (1979) of low life a mong low ca stes elsewhere in Ori ssa. Our fo cus on dialog ues with the dea d will lead us to see in la ter chapters how th e Sora use the politics of ethnicity in order to give to the livin g the power to compel sonums to revea l the vital relationships of causality i;l the most intimate areas of their own lives. But the ima ge ry of Brahmanical purity , so prominent in sanskriti sa tion models, features here only slightly: instead , the Sora look to forms of power appropriate to ksatriyas . In the most obvious sense, this is the brute power of the police and the military , deriving their authority from raja s and the state. The shaman's rauda-helpers are ' policemen' who can handcuff other sonums and lead them to a dialogue. But this is also power in the more insidiously coercive sense of literacy , the power to command by naming and writing. This power belongs not only to the police system but also to the techniques of the komo (Oriya karan, a caste of Kayastha scribes who are likewise ksatriya). When the rauda-helper arrives, she reassures the shaman's clients that whichever sonum is causing the trouble, 'I'll write his name, I'll inscribe his name.' Once 1 attended a divination for a sick baby in which the shaman's rauda-helper announced from the Underworld that an tlda sonum wanted the baby to bear his own name in addition to an ancestral name, as is usual. Thjs was a strange Oriya name and when the trance was over nobody could remember what it was - including the shaman , on the grounds that his soul had been absent while the rauda was speaking. After waiting a while to see what they would do , 1 suddenly 'remembered' the name by glancing down at my notes and the shaman said , teasingly, that they knew they could rely on me.
Main personalities
Just as the contrast between the Sora and others is one of degree, so the journey from the plains into the Sora heartland reveals a transition which is gradual but nonetheless perceptible. One has a sense both ofcontinuity and of entering another world. Towards the Sora heartland a distinctive landscape emerges of steep stony hills interspersed with more level areas in the valleys, some of pocket-handkerchief size, others large plains running into each other in shallow steps over one or two miles. The hills are covered with remnants of forest, patches of which are cut down for shifting cultivation, while the valley floors have been terraced into paddy-fields which sometimes climb



34 Dialog ues lvi/Ii /Iie dead
hig h up out of the va ll ey and reach into clefts in the hill s behind meticulou sly co nstructed stone retainin g wall s up to forty feet hi g h. From a high vantage point at certain seasons th e landscape resemb les glaciers of paddy or straw-coloured stubble flowin g round th e base of green wooded mountain s. The So ra draw a living from thi s landscape by huntin g, ga therin g, shifting a nd settled cultivation. Sora villages are located mostly a long the line where a fores ted slope meets the more leve l paddy-land , and a lways near a water supply. They are ge nera ll y ha if a mile or a mile apart, and may co nta in from approximately 50 to 500 people. Villages on valley floors a re la rge r than those on hill s, thou gh the latter a re a lmost always simil ar ly sited by some sma ll area of terraced paddy-fields. The locat ion a nd style of villages varies co nsiderab ly. I lived successively in three villages, with frequent visits to others. My first village, Rebjingtal , was a large village of about 400 people in eig ht lineages , facing a broad expanse of paddy terraces steppi ng downwards from the village. This village commands the gap at which the path coming up from Gunupur finally sp ills on to the plateau which gives access to the heartland of Sora territory. Just beh ind Rebjingtallies Puttasing, the settlement of ad mini strators and Pano traders, so that the village is extremely accessible to settlers and travellers. The Headma n lineage is large and strong, with their houses close by the road. They have tightly woven but double-edged political and economic relations with the traders of Puttasing. Further off the road and slightly uphill are the less powerful, sometimes poor , lin eages. Their rel ations with the Headman group are often disadvantageo us to them, but in other ways, since they a re less preyed upon by casual passers- by, the atmos phere in these parts seems more relaxed. The second village, which I shall not na me, lies high on a still -forested hilltop . Access is only via steep and thorny paths. There is very little paddy land up there a nd the population is small (about 100, in three lineages) with tattered clothing and a rough air. Other villages consider this village to be lethally infested with sorcery and my friends in Rebjingta l begged me for my own sake not to move there or if I must, at least not to take with me tapes containing their voices. It turned out that sorcery was indeed virulent and that it was related to occasional notorious outbreaks of murder. I suspected that these could also be linked to the acute shortage of land which leads to the area's highest rate of mig ration to Assam , as well as to several early conversions to Baptism. The case studies in the present work are taken from the third village, which I shall call Alinsing ('Wine-Village' : to simplify the place- name I
Th e Sora people 35
have writte n this '-sin g' in stea d of'-slIIg' ). T hi s is a large vi ll age on one side ofa va lley (l oor, with a rich , powerful Headma n and some twelve or fifteen lineages, according to one's reckoning. It includ es a section ca lled ' Lower Vill age' (lqj/a-Gorjallg) and an offs hoot about half a mil e away on the other sid e of the valley , here ca ll ed Tongseng ('Dancing Party'). Tongseng wa s form ed several generation s ago when a man of the Headman lin eage set up hou se there. His desce ndants have now multiplied to so me twenty-five hou seho lds, led by Jamano , a wealthy old man of firm cha racte r. M a ny of the events ana lysed in thi s book will reveal the growin g distance between the people of Tongseng a nd the main part of their own lineage back in Alinsing. The surro undings of Alinsing, Lower Village a nd Tongse ng are shown in chapter 4 in fi gure 4.3 a nd the combined population of these villages is around 800. In the midday heat a village is often deserted, apart from an occas io nal very old person, as everyone else has joined up into work parties and go ne off with a ll their children to the jungle or fields. In the evening and early morning, by contrast, the village is full of life. In the darkness before daw n, while the women pound grain and tend bubbling pots of millet or sorghum porridge and the children are still sprawled across the floor in sleep, the men are already walking around, wrapped in bl a nkets and calling to each other. Every m a n then sets out on a tour of the forest to collect the wine which ha s dripped out overnight from his toddy trees into a suspended pot. The sap of the alin (fishtail palm, Caryo/a U/'ens) is kept flowing by incisions made at the inflorescence of the tree . It is fermented by airborne yeasts to produce a foamin g toddy which is rich in vitamin s a nd mildly alcoholic. Except in the rainy season (July- September) when the trees stop yielding, this is harvested two or three times a day. A man's trees may be widely scattered and the nurturing of the trees a nd the gathering and drinking of wine in regul a r informal groups is the ma in focus of socia I and political life. Wine is also widely drunk by women and sometimes children .s The drinking site is a circle offlat sto nes set up as seats around a heart h . The location is at the same time sheltered and open: it may be in a clearing, or on a n outcrop backing on to a cliff a nd with a commanding view. The first man to arrive will light the fire, squinting through the acrid new smoke. Around you in the distance you can hea r the sound of bamboo flutes , all playin g, at the sa me pitch , variants of the same pentatonic melody and crossing and re-crossing each other up and down the scale. A flute comes closer and a ma n bursts in on you with a grin: 'Ai! [an ex pression of surprise] Are you thereT, and a clay pot of wine crooked over his arm on a rope of creeper bark. One by one as the men appear, they pour their wine



36 Dialogues Il'ilh Ihe dead
into a large pot set over the fire. People adjust the wood, skim off froth and in sects with a leaf and test the temperalure , shoutin g 'Warm it up some more!' or ' Quick , take it om'. When the drink is at just the right lukewarm temperature, one of the men dips in a gourd lad le and passes it to his neighbour on the right who drinks it, refills it and passes it on again. Someone may produce a snack of sa lt crystals, dried meat, or chilli. As more arrive, the conversation becomes more animated :
- Ai! Is that what he sa id? - Who did? - My shit! - Why? - No, I didn't!
There is no indirect speech construction, so that gossip comes in a form which purports to be a l'erbalil11 report: "'Why won't you come?", I said. " Because you didn't give me the grain you owed me" , she said . "Grain my shit! Didn't I give you two basketfuls the other day?" I said .. .' Opinions are moulded, areas of consensus are formed , plans are made for the day's work, for the organisation of rites and the direction of political strategies. By now the sun has risen and as the pot empties, the men set off to the village in single file , carrying additional pots for their women to drink at home and still talking vehemently . The file always stops for a moment before reaching the village: 'aiiumen dele - let's have a pee.' The circle will meet again at dusk and sometimes at midday. Women passing by will join in a circle but will not be regular attenders. Women 's occasions to talk in large groups without men are when they are collecting water at wells and springs morning and evening. A drinking circle may contain two men or a dozen. Each village has several of these, the composition of which shifts according to changes in friendship, convenience of access or fecundity of toddy trees. The core of the group will generally be a set of brothers. Other lineage members (also called 'brother') and sometimes in-laws and cousins from another lineage may also join. Any of these may split off and join up again throughout the season. Anyone passing by, even from a distant village, will be invited to join, as the standard question on a path 'Where are you going?' is answered by 'For a drink - come along.'9 A drinking circle is thus a group of men with strong patrilineal implications but sufficiently open-ended to acknowledge intimacy with any other category of person. Actual political relations encompass large
The Sora people 37
inequa lities in land, labour and productivity as well as in connections within the community and outsid e. The idea l of equa l sharin g as an expression of intim acy runs deep through the workings of any social group, whether th e actua l economic and politica l power between person s is equa l or not. Meat from sacrifices is di stributed among the lineage or branch involved, and parcels are sent to those who were unable to attend; further portions are given to visiting in-laws and cous ins and find their way as snacks to the drinking circles . Wild game, however sma ll , is shared among the entire hunting party. A find of grubs or ants is taken home to the family or out to the even ing's drinking circle. Not to do this is to be ranglw , 'greedy', or a 'carefu lly-lookin g person' , g(jg(jlllar , judgements of so litude and self-absorption which can shade into accusat ions of sorcery. Of co urse, people are torn every day between this obliga lion to share and their desire to keep things for themse lves. Thus in the parched and sweltering st illness before the monsoon, the forest loses its leaves a nd the toddy trees start to go dry or turn bitter (often , it is said, through the jealous sorcery of those whose trees have already stopped yielding). The la rger drinking circles break up and those whose trees are still productive save the precious juice only for their closest friends. Now hopeful passers-by ask about wine and are told 'We aren't getting any either - our trees have been bewitched', or 'There was a little but it's just run out - it wasn't any good anyway.' People met on the path who ask 'Where are you going?' are fobbed off with the uninviting answer 'To empty my bowels.' I remember once during this season how I set out up a mountain in the midday heat with just one other man, and how Dangdang waved us off in front of a gro up of people, saying 'See you tomorrow .' Fifteen minutes la ter, at the summit, he crossed our path with a chuckle bearing a small potful of wine which the three of us then drank with selfish enjoyment. The acceptance of such adroit and necessa ry social hypocrisy is counterbalanced by a darker awareness of the difficulty of knowing what others ' really ' (jadi) think and feel. One feels 'suspicion' (dalgad) when on the lookout for bukai, Ion 101'0 and .folda (,deceit' or 'trickery'). In certa in contexts, one's loyalties will lie with a lineage or other grouping in an obvious and uncomplicated way. However, loyalty is often not so simple. All men , and most women unless they have married in from very far away, have known each other all their lives. People are linked by such a variety of cross-cutting ties, both structural and emotional, that the alignments of oppositions and friendships shift rapidly and kaleidoscopically . There is a keen sense that in the midst of intimacy the most important psychic processes in others , namely how they feel about us, are hidden from us: ' I



38 Dialogues IlIilh Ihe dead
speak nice-tasting words, but how can you know what's inl11 Ysoul?', as one friend put it to me . Thus, the outward display of affection can signify either itse lf o r its opposite - or most likely, some opaque mixture of the two. The potential for this uncerta inty tends to increase with closeness. It is among brothers, who li ve in and out of each ot her's houses a nd who will share their fa ther's property equally , tha t so rce ry is most likely. Perhaps the least ambivalent relati o nship is a good marriage of lon g stand in g, where a wife has moved sufficie ntl y close to her husba nd that her brothe rs are no lon ger a ble to tug her away from him . The descendants of a woman's husband and of her brothe r or sister ca ll each other 'cousin ' (marollgger, feminin e marol/set) , a nd for three generations they are also supposed to address each other as ' broth er ' and 'sister' and avoid interm a rri age. If a woman's husband and brothers overcome the mutua l jea lousy im plicit in their re lationship , then their children may indeed help eac h other and for a
while enjoy some of the close ness of siblingship without the rivalry of real siblings.
In writing now I am torn between the desire to tell the reader about all sorts of people I have known, and the need to develop a systema tic and specific exposition of how I came to understand these people's dialogues with their dead . I should have liked to talk about Inama (himself now dead), the first Sora to invite me for a drink and then take the risk of letting me into his home. La ter on, when I had already moved to my second village, I caught hepatitis a nd disappeared to a distant hospital without being able to tell anyone where I had gone. On the night I returned to Soraland three months later, it was to Inama tha t I went first. I shall never forget how he and his brother Lakkia hugged me for long minutes, then said that I was rumoured to have been murdered in a quarrel over a woman in some remote village. Inama said that he had never believed I was dead and had once dreamed that he saw me coming along the path and had actually got up in the night to have a look. On another occasion he to ld me how he had dreamed that I had gone down to Gunupur town barefoot and wearing my Sora earrings and loincloth , that I had been ridiculed there by the merchants and officials, and that he had woken weeping 'out of pity' for my humiliation.
I should have liked, also , to be able to dwell on my memory of Lakkia bathing his dangerously sick child in a pot of warm water with intense tenderness in his eyes and in every movement of his ha nds; and to talk of the many forthright young women holding out against domineering fathers or brothers for the men of their choice - ofDoimani, a young woman who as a

chil d had cl imbed ove r everyt hin g and everyone ' like a monkey ', accord ing to [nama , and who as a woman had suc h spirit that for a long time no boy dared to ask her to marry him ; and of her fri end Gosandi , a littl e girl of about eig ht with a husky voice and a coo l a ppraising ga ze through the smoke of he r ciga r, who was trainin g to be a funeral shaman. I remembe r M ejeru , an eld er ly and skinny man, bald with a frin ge at the back a nd a cau lifiower nose, a grea t dancer, sin ger a nd joker. One day on a hillside we came across some wild fruit which had been gathered by some o ld women and then left in baskets unattended. Mejeru placed some large stones in the ba skets undern ea th the fruit a nd wa ited gigglin g behind a tree for the puzzled look o n the women's faces when they returned and tried to lift their baskets. I remember these o ld women and many like them with their wrinkled bod ies a nd gross , throwaway humour ; a nd Sirdoro, a n o ld Headman who in the 1940s had ensl a ved fifty of hi s fellow villagers for debt and who used to be carried a lon g the stony mountain pa th s on a litter like a petty raja , a man who by a ll accounts mu st have been ex tremely cruel ('he was begotte n by a leopard o r a viper ') but who appeared to me now only as an ineffectua l old rogue (,gradually he mellowed: it's in hi s so ul but he has n't the power '). I remember too how Inama and his brothers sent their children to live with me when I first had a house of my own beca use ' it's not good to live a lone'; and I remember other children throughout my stay who would seek me out when I was sitting somewhere in solitude and ask me if my soul was sad because of the people I'd left behind at home. I was comforted by this a nd by the constant solicitude over cuts and thorns in my feet and enq uiries about whether I rea lly did not mind certain kinds of food; a nd by the open invitations which a llowed me eventually to dispense with running a house of my own and to walk anywhere across Sora territory (where there are no shops or cafes) without baggage, money (which people would not accept) or a ny plan for the nig ht. I shall not, however, be able to do justice to most of these people here. Given that my argument will often be in terms of such intan gibles as mood, nua nce a nd tone of voice, I ha ve decid ed to build it firml y on a limited number of specific cases, eve nts and texts . I have been furth er constrained by the loss a t the very end of my fieldwork of a large number of tapes which were in a bag stolen at a Madras bus-stand (and no doubt immedi ately overlaid with film music for cut-price resale). Accordingly, I shall build this book aro und the lives of three men in Alinsing whom I knew well a nd whose affa irs intertwine and are documented in detail in the surviving ta pes. These are Menga ltl , Sagalo and 1amano (all pseudonyms , a ll stressed

Mel/galu
Mengalu is a lanky middl e-aged man of the Headman lineage with close-cropped grey hair, lips which purse outwards when he speaks and an alert, impish look in his eyes . His style is old -fashioned and he always wears a tattered loincloth , never shorts. He is a perverse, crazy, wry, witty man, clever with words and ges tures. He cares deeply about correct performance

a nd with hi s memory, hi s improvisa tory flair and hi s humour he is the natural lea der of the dancing and chantin g whether for hi s own lineage or for the entire village. Mengalu is a t once the life and so ul of the party a nd a loner, capa bl e ofspending days at a time in so litude on the hill side , herdin g a few cows or co llecting roots. He is also ambitious, but in a disorganised way which ha s never allowed him to prosper. He has four wives in two houses, with six surviving children , but the family wears no go ld and have a somewhat down-at-heel air. He is also unfortunate. About ten years before I arrived (i. e. in the late I 960s), he gave way to an enviou s impulse in an act for which he has been paying ever since. His father's brothers, Urung and Poke, had acquired some land jointly. When Poke died without heirs his other land was shared between Mengalu's and Raduno's line, but thi s land was inherited by Raduno alone on the grounds that hi s father had bee n the only other partner in it. However, Mengalu was trying to build himself up through polygamy and needed more land . Besides this , he had several sons who would need to inherit his land while Raduno had only two daughters . It appears that Mengalu hired a professional sorcery-assassin from another village to kill Raduno and his dependants by the standard technique of burying a pot containing their discarded toothbrushing sticks, excrement, soil from their footprints, etc., along with the remains of a tortured black chicken trussed up in thread and a head of rice similarly twisted and bound . Such a pot is supposedly buried at night on a hillside to the accompaniment of sung dialogues (kata) and a pantomime enacted by the professional assassin and his clients (both exclusively male roles and called 'sorceryman', tonaj-mar) and remains effective so long as it is not dug up. But Mengalu was indiscreet, rumours spread and Raduno intimidated the assassin into confessing and digging up the pot, which was displayed in the village with great publicity and embarrassment for Mengalu. This is one of the cases, perhaps rare, in which sorcery is actually done by a sorcerer rather than merely imagined by the victims, and I personally believe that Mengalu has never done it again . But though he is not the only such suspect in the village , we shall see that there is scarcely a death in Alinsing over which the possibility of his sorcery is not at least aired. Ironically, Mengalu and Raduno are now the best of friends again. Partly, though their personalities are very different, they were friends in childhood and still feel a liking for each other. Rad uno , who is several years younger, has a fond memory from his childhood of how Mengalu returned from Burma and washed him with the first soap ever seen in the area. But also , a degree of cooperation is inevitable among closely related lineage



42 Dialog ues with the dead
brothers. Be longin g as they do to the sa me branch of their lin eage, they have inherited the cu lt ofa virtually ide ntical ran ge of ancesto rs; they work sid e by side in field s whi ch are a shared inheritance from their grandfather; and they live near to each other, borrowin g tools , food and labour while their childre n ofte n play together. However , it is precisely because as here the principl e of equal inheritance amo ng so ns is so often subverted by economic and demographic accident that sorcery is most common among close lineage brothers.
Saga/o
Sagalo is a sweet and gentle yo uth whose father has been dead for some years. In early 1977 hi s 'orpha n-house' (kundra-s/I1g) a lso conta ined hi s widowed mother (a sister of Mengalu) who has not rema rri ed, hi s unmarried elder siste r a nd his little brothe r Mando . They are a poor family in a sma ll and poor linea ge (actually the Buya linea ge, custodians o f the village Kidtung so num and supposedly the original settlers) an d so are without padd y- fields or substantial political influence. When I first knew him , Sagalo was preparing to perform a funeral stone-planting for his third-generation cousin (marongger) , a ma n from Rungrungba village called Sunia. Such a funeral is supplementary to the one which will a lready have been performed in Rungrungba by Sunia 's own lineage, a nd is by no means obligatory (cf. chapter 3) . But Sunia had been a man of influence and though the connection was an old one, Sagalo was anxious to reta in it, despite the expense of the funeral. It was open to Menga lu to be a close ally of this fatherless family since he was a mother's brother living in the same village. But in 1977 this was not the case. Sagalo's little brother M a ndo had bee n suffering for a year from a painful di sease which twisted his body and shortly after my arrival he died . The following morning I a ttended the inquest (abgijte), in which the ghost of the deceased is questioned about the circumstances and causes of his death . It was the first time I had attended an inquest since knowing enough of the la nguage and the people to follow something of wha t was going on. Listening now to the tape-reco rding, I see from preliminary conversations among bysta nders that it was already inev itable th a t the trance should give a verdict ofMengalu's sorcery (for this kind of process in detail , see chapter 5). The rea so n given for the sorcery was tha t Sagalo had sed uced the girlfriend of Mengalu's son Sargia. In reve nge Mengalu was supposed to have performed so rcery against him . But since the so ul of the youth Sagalo was strong and resilient, the sorcery ricochetted off it on to the more delicate one of the little boy and killed him instead .
The Sora people 43
During the inquest the grief of the boy 's mot her and sister was combined with a towerin g rage aga inst Menga lu (who chose to be absent, Ju st as he will do during ano ther inquest in chapter 5). Saga lo sat silent and , I a m sure, wretched with remorse . So the stone-pla nting originally pla nned as a diploma tic co urtesy for a distant cross-cousin beca me a dou ble funeral and something a lto ge ther more tragic as two stones were planted and tw~ buffalos sacrificed, one for the distant, elde rl y Sunl a and one for Saga lo s
beloved little brother Mando. By the time I returned in ea rl y 1979, Saga lo's life had improved. The disputed girlfriend had married elsew here outside the village and Saga ~ o had married Panderi , a woman from Lower Village. They seemed deeply 111 love a nd very happy together. She was a strong-minded woman fr om a well-placed family . Her brothers had arra nged a suitabl e formal marriage (pangsal) for her with someone else a nd had sea led the contract by dl'lnklllg the suitor's wine. But Panderi refused the proposed groom and bought herself out of the contract by paying him the necessary fine out of her own resources . After living with another ma n for a while, she had eventu ally come to Sagalo by the poor person's form of marriage, mutua l free choice or ' love ma rri age' (dari). Panderi 's brothers objected to this ma tch on the grounds of his poverty and reinforced their case by pointing to his di sta nt relationship, that of a third-generation cross-co usin (here, a descendant of a sister of their lineage), with whom marriage should not be allowed . But the third generation is the last point at which a cross-cousin relationship is recognised. As with Sunia, who stood in an identical relationship to Sagalo, it is somewhat open by this stage how far one cultivates or ignores the connection. Panderi defied her brothers and the birth in 1978 of her baby boy, Sagalo's lineal descendant, seemed to indicate the final defea t of the still rumbling opposition of her brothe rs. By this time too, only two years after Mando's dea th , good relations with Mengalu had been restored . Mengalu was a frequent visitor to his sister's hou se a nd helped her and his young nephew in various ways in their dea lings with the world . This apparently com fortable resolution of ea rlier hostilities will be dramatically tested in chapters 7 and 8, as we see the impact on all concerned ofPanderi's sudden tragic death.

Jamano is a dignified , upright , elderly ma n. He has clearly once bee n very ha ndsome, as is his son, Ranatang, who resembles him closely. Jamano has had several wives who have now died, but also retains severa l of ILis sisters and grown-up dau ghte rs, women who either do not marry or who reverse the usual virilocal pattern a nd bring poor husba nds home as their dependants . In either case, such women bear children who do not belong to ot her men's lineages but who swe ll the ra nks of the women's own nata l lineage . Women's motives for doing this a re mi xed: so me are unwilling to leave the co mfo rt a nd co mpa ny of a ho use with many women (thi s is the main reason , too , why a man' s second wife is often welcomed by the first) , but others a re held pri so ne r by a dominant male. 3
The formation of the Sora person
Gradual transformations in the journey from birth to death and beyond In the last chapter I presented a group of my friends on their landscape. This chapter will begin to explore the Soras' own account of their interpersonal relationships by examining their understanding of how a person is formed. The idea of formation or shaping is expressed by an imagery of craftsmanship which surrounds the start of a child's life. Western psychologies tend to presuppose an intrinsic essence of the person which then 'grows', 'develops', 'unfolds' or 'matures' as it is 'socialised' into the company of others. But despite the existence of Sora words for human consciousness at various stages of being alive and dead, what we shall see here and in later chapters is not simply a person who is a unitary entity which embarks on a process of development during which it confronts other, similar entities. Rather, the person appears more as a confluence whjch comes together only on the basis of its relationship to other beings who are themselves equally changing. Just as persons and other entities radiate something of themselves out to others, so they seem to be hard to bring and hold together: their constituent elements tend to drift away again and go towards the formation of other entities. The Sora account presupposes that some part or aspect of the person persists after an event called 'death' (k-er-enid, the noun from kenid-, 'die') and that it then assumes a powerful effect upon the living. The dead are thus causal agents, but what they exert cannot simply be called an 'influence', since they themselves are also changed by their encounters with the living. During the course of his existence the person is considered to pass through
several stages. Though some rites of passage are prominent, no chang~ is immediate and definitive. Rather, the new state takes hold only gradually against the persistence of the previous state. I shall outline these changes
The formalion of Ihe Sora person 47
briefly under the following head in gs: birth and childhood ; adolescence and marriage ; and from firsl dea th on ea rth to second dea th in the Underworld.
Birth alld childhood
Birth is the outcome of a comp licated process in which Sora say that the baby is formed by the sonum of the Sun (UYllllg-Sl/l1 , 'S un -S onum') and then grad ua ll y sepa rated from the mother's body (see below). The birth itself is only the first step towards separation and the dependence on the umbilica l cord is echoed through breast-feeding which la sts for around three years. Soon after birth, a patrilineal ancestor of the same sex causes a fever(aslI) in the baby and in a subsequent divination promises the baby hi s or her name. The Ancestor-Men tie a fin ger- ring, sa id to have been saved from that ancestor's funeral, round the baby's wrist (ab~ii-ell -, ' [cau sat ive particleJ-tie-ring'). The ancestor und er takes to protect the baby from attacks by other sonums and in return receives a sacrifice of a chick en . This is a promi se of a buffalo at a big name-giving ceremony (ab -iillll-Oll , '[causative particleJ-name-child') to be staged two or three years later when the child is fully weaned . At that point the ancestor will definitively give his name to the child and the Ancestor-Men will untie the ring. This is also around the age at which the child becomes enough ofa person to talk , and thus to appear as a sonum at its own funeral. Ifin the meantime the baby dies, as so many do, it does not receive a full funeral. The ancestor then seeks out ahother baby among her or his descendants to whom to give the name, since this must reappear somewhere among the living. Further stages in the evolution of the child's personhood are expressed in various ways over the following years . A few days after birth its hair is shaved (apart from a little tuft at the back) ' for the Sun', and its head will be kept shaved or short throughout childhood. As a toddler, its nose and ears will be pierced (pad-) for rings which, at least for girls' ear-rings, will continually be increased in number and size throughout their lives. Before adolescence, someone will tattoo the child's face (lang-lang-bob, 'p rickprick-head'). Boys in particular are giving this up, since it is an embarrassment in their increasing contact with non-Soras . But among older people one can still hear remarks like 'How come she died so suddenly? I saw her only the other day and her tattoo was still clear.'
Adolescel/ce alld marriage
Around this time, girls cease shaving their heads so that their hair begins to grow to the full, woman's length. Only shamans and their high-caste Ilda-spouses observe any menstrual taboos. Boys nowadays also cease



48 Dialogues with th e dead
shavin g their heads. Many old er men remain shorn for life exce pt for the tuft a t the back which they say is a Telu gu Bra hmin style, but most younger men adopt the conve ntional 'Oriya' or ' Hindi -film ' hairc ut o f the plain s. Sora men have very little fa cia l hair and wh a t little there is, th ey find di stasteful and pull out (puin -) with tweezers. Wh en I firs t lived amon g them , I stopped shaving for a while but was then repea tedly asked to shave. Late r, whe n I und erstood enoug h, I learn ed that a ha iry face is suita bl e only for bears, leopard s, Oriya s and T elu g us, in other word s that I was being asked to become more Sora. Tee nagers form friendships and sexual relationships wid ely , though the power of 'embarrassment' (garoj) imposes some discretion. Sexual rela tions with linea ge members are forbidden, but there is an ambi guity about c ross-cousins (fa ther's sister 's or mother's brothe r's children) and pat'allel cou sin s outside the lineage (i.e. mother's sister's children). All of these are ca lled /liar-anger, male; mar-ol/sel, female, and often addressed as ' brother' and 'sister' . People often say that these too are forbidden up to the third generation , but in practice there are many such liaisons and marriages , especially as the connection fades further into the past (for example, Sagalo has married his third -generation cross-cousin). Young people form and develop liaisons especially during reciprocal work parties (aI/sir) , when men and women may suddenly down tools and chase and tickle (gatarsi-) each other, and at the funeral stone-plantings (gum'), which bring together groups from different villages to drink and dance. Most people have a history of several affairs (dari) before they settle with the partner with whom they raise a family. Setting up house together is recognised as a declaration of intended permanency but in the early years there are many breakups. Most marriages originate in this free-choice way. Dari marriage is not marked by any ceremony and need involve no dowry or bride-price. But the desire of a girl's parents or brothers to interfere or hold on to her can be intense, especially if the family is wealthy. This tendency is compounded by the simultaneous availability of a pattern of arranged marriage (pang-sal, ' taking wine'), involving bride-price and favoured by the better-off. There is no word meaning ' marriage ' as such , covering both these ways in which a man and a woman may come to live together. Panderi's history (see the previous chapter and especially chapter 7) is typical in counterposing her brothers' intention of an arranged marriage with her variollS affairs and eventual dari marriage to Sagalo . The same patterns can be seen in 1amano's attempts, recounted at the end of the last chapter, to control his sisters' love-lives. Even where it is marked by a ce remony, marriage has a tentative or

provi sional na ture which will be a pparent throu ghout thi s book. It is stri kin g that it is the only ceremo ny in the life-cycle in whic h sha ma ns a nd so num s uSll a ll y play no pa rt. The marria ge becomes progressive ly more sta ble only with the birth and surviva l of children, especially ma le childre n. From first tieath 011 earth to secollti death ill the Ulldenvo"'t/
As soon as someone dies, whatever the hour of day or night, the women of the house start to lament. If available, guns are fired . An orchestra of drums and oboes is assembled to play the 'death beat' (kerenid-deb) . All the men of the lineage within earshot abandon whatever they are doing and gather together to chop down a tree and build a pyre on the lineage's cremation ground. Meanwhile the Ancestor-Women strip the body, wash it in 'cooling' turmeric water and dress it in good , clean clothes. A man from the village's pyre-lighter (siga) lineage lights and tends the pyre. The following morning, the Ancestor-Women pour water on the ashes 'to cool the soul' . Then they bury the ashes on the crema tion-site while the funeral shaman leads the dead person's soul into that person's house. There she enters trance and his soul passes into her body and is interrogated by bystanders about the circumsta nces and causes of hi s death. This is the first of two inquests (described in chapter 8) . Some weeks or months later (usually after the harvest , since this is expensive), the heirs carry out the ma in step in the funeral sequence. They sacrifice buffalos for the deceased to eat and plough with in the Underworld ; they also plant an upright memorial stone at the lineage's stone-planting site, to join the many stones stacked up there, leaning against each other, from previous funerals. On that day the living stage a second inquest in which they again discuss the circumstances of his death with the deceased . This is analysed in detail in

cha pte r 5. Durin g the followin g three yea rs the deceased is commemora ted collectively at ce rta in season s alon g with othe r rece ntl y-dead people . These occasions a re al so a gri c ultura l fes tiva ls a nd the sac rifices a nd tra nces are staged collectively by the whole villa ge : the lajab (a rice-tran splanting commemora tion) and , more spectacularl y, the karja (h a rvest commemoration a nd a nnu a l fes tival o f the dea d , described in cha pte r 9) . T he ka rj a is staged in Feb rua ry or Ma rch whe n all outstandin g ston e-pl a ntin gs a re complete, at the first full moon after the main grain harvests. A fter a period , the dead person is ready to ha nd on hi s na me to a new baby. Thi s often ha ppens soon after the stone-p lantin g but it ma y some times be delayed for seve ral generations, that is for fifty years or even a hundred. The reason s are partly demographic and partly to do with the evolution of the dead person 's own supposed ex perience a nd a ttitudes after death. At dialogues held during these various stages of the fun eral seque nce, the deceased reveals details about hi s condition in the realm of the dead . It is this condition, and the changes which take place within it , which determine how the deceased will affect the living . After having named a child, the ancestor gradually appears less and less during trances until finally the living learn from other dead spea kers that he has died again and received a further stone-planting in the Underworld . Nothing more is known about him after this , though it is said that he has turned into a butterfly.
S un-Woman as blacksmith and embryologist: heat, form and the development of consciousness A consciousness is called puradan ('soul') when alive, Iwlm(/n (' ghost') for the few days or weeks between death and stone-planting, and from the stone-planting onwards, sonum . By chapter 9 it will be clear that the transmission of an ancestor' s name to a ba by is only one of several elements which show that though this is not a reincarnation, there is a powerful cyclical aspect to the identity of the Sora person . Sora thinking about the origins of the person is concerned with the acquisition of form rather than with creation ex nillilo. This goes with a metaphysic in which the new is seen as a re-formation and partial repetition. There appears to be no noun meaning ' form' in general. The associated verbs, however, all of which are applied to babies as well as to craftmanship, clearly presuppose the prior existence and the mutability of the material which is now taking form:

T he Sora view of the formation of a child takes place within thi s technologica l im age ry. The confined spa ce (kampllng , mea ning both ' womb ' and 'stomac h') within which the foetu s is form ed out of blood is a lso a bla cksmith 's mould (kul1Ipi , a cog nate word?) in whi ch Sun -Sonum (UYlIng-sl/n) casts the fo etus out of molten metal. Who is the Sun? In her dominant aspect as Sun -Woman (Uyung- boj) , the Sun is a Telugu woman of low caste. She al so command s a number of underlin gs who are bla cksmith s, python s and T elu gu cattle-herders (see chapte r 4). H er focal image is of me talworkin g. On appropriate occa sions, a sha man in trance sin gin g in th e Sun's persona will address a patient as gadi-on lien pOlIJpom-on IJen , 'my moulded child my forged child ', and many nearsynonyms. There is thus a homology between the mother's womb and the Sun 's forge and it is under these still-molten conditions that the baby will begin to be separated from the mother. Heat is the precondition for plasticity or the moulding of something amorphous into something which has form . But though heat allows formation and separation, under continuing conditions of heat the separated form cannot be held stable: form which has been acquired must be retained through the cessation of heat. It is for this reason that a newborn baby fresh from the Sun's forge is protected and its form confirmed by cooling rites. These include the use of hard (i.e. cooled) metal objects and the application of cooling 'medicines' (regal11 , especially turmeric, sangsang) to the umbilical stump of the baby and the vagina of the mother. The shaving of the baby' s head a few days later reinforces thi s definition of its boundary. lfthese procedures are not done adequately the baby 's very existence as a separate entity is threatened with dissolution. The first stage in the formation of a new person is thus a long and delicate process. The fact that it has a high failure rate (through miscarriage, stillbirth and infant mortality) is accounted for by the Sora through the imagery of the process itself. A child may suffer a failure of separation at any stage in its formation up to around the time of the naming ceremony and sometimes even far beyond (I have heard this motif used in a lament sung for the death of a teenager). If this happens, the cI'lild ha s been re-absorbed into the Sun , who sent a python to swallow it up. The Sora do not use the expression , but it is tempting to say that the child is returned to

the meltin g-pot. Thou gh the child once born can be attack ed by any oth er sonum too , the only possible cau se of mi scarria ges and failures to conceive is the Sun when she withhold s or reverses her form a tive fun c tion . The corresponding healin gs a re hi ghl y specific and refer to named as pec ts of Sun -Woman and her python (th ese are included in th e li st of sonUll1 S in appendix I). F or example , in th e ca se of an infe rtile woman , the python may be inside her , swallowin g th e foe tu s almost before it is conceived (Ural-sll11 , ' Bud -Drop-Sonum '); in miscarria ges , a fter swallowing the fo etu s the python vomits it prematurely (Mo-gaj-slIl1 , ' Eclipse-MoonSonum ') instead of at the properly-timed , viable moment of normal birth; after the birth, the python may drain the mother's breast of milk and starve the baby by puttin g its own tail in stead of the nipple into the baby's mouth (Ajora-slI11 , ' Stream-Sonum'). Each of these aspects of the Sun ha s its own story or other rationale, some of which will be discussed in chapter 4. The Sora can say nothing explicitly about the origin of a human consciousness. This consciousness is called purodall ('soul ') and seems to be formed in the womb along with the body. In one sense, a child 's soul , like its body, grows and becomes stronger with time . This is why little Mando succumbed to the crossfire ofMengalu's sorcery after it had bounced off the more robust soul of his teenage brother Sagalo (chapter 2). Consciousness is essentially fluid and in one sense it takes on form and identity by filling a bounded space. I could find no single Sora word covering all English senses of ,boundary' (cf. usal , 'skin'; maneng, 'edge') , but it is clear that a person's boundary is important, at least when it is conceptualised in terms of the person's body. In living animals, humans and even plants, soul animates the body. It is contained in the blood and is said to be like the shadow or photograph of the body, in other words, it takes the same form and occupies the same space as the body. Absence of form is expressed by the word Iw~ja , used as an adjective or adverb, and meaning not only 'without form' but also '(at) random; without reason, discrimination or specification; aimless(-Iy)', etc. Space is essentially ka'ja, except where some significance is read into it. Then it provides a framework within which beings can take form. However, Sora do not seem to conceive of personhood exclusively or even mainly in terms ofa bounded entity. At the same time as talking about the blood which occupies the whole of the body, Sora also say that soul is concentrated at the centre of the person, in the liver. (Some people also say that the ' heart' and 'brain' play some role, which they are unable to specify. These organs are known from the butchering of animals). Thus sickness is caused when a sonum starts to 'eat' the victim's soul , and it is said of a sick
Tli e Iof/lWliOIl of Ihe So ra persoll 53
pa ti ent both th a t he ' ha s no blood ' (a lIIiiial1len agasa) and that the a ttac kin g sonum is ea tin g hi s liver. So the preoccupa tion with the mainte nance of a person 's bound a ry may a lso be see n as a conce rn with th e difficulty of holding a n entity 's con stituent elements at a centre or fo ca l po int. In this se nse , we ma y tran sla te ka'ja not so mu ch as ' vvith o ut a clea r outline' but rath er as 'not in a state offocu s or conce ntration in the middle'. Thi s is so especially whe n one think s, not of the person ' s body but of hi s will , motivation or a gency. Sora d escribe the impul se ofsonums as a force, power or ene rgy (renabli, from rabli-, to be abl e) and say that it can be conducted and stored in space. When I took Sora friends to a city , they had no difficulty in understandin g electric lights, accumulators a nd telephones - to th e extent that these operate in the same way a s sonum , whi ch is seen as similarly dynamic and subject to stora ge in containers, tran smi ss ion alon g threads and path s, and leapin g across gaps . But in Sora practice, consciousness is far more than electricity . The impetus which it represent s ca nnot be switched off since it has its own will and agency with which , as sonum , it engages and affects the living. A consciousness becomes sonum after death and stone-planting. Now it no longer animates a body as soul, nor does it retain the same relatively high degree of individuation: it is less clear 'who' each sonum is, as categories ofsonums merge and refract according to a logic which does not apply to the living. But consciousness is still subject to focussing and concentration. Conspicuous features of the landscape, such as rocks, trees or heavenly bodies, are the residences of certain sonums (chapter 4). Shamans may a lso contain sonums in sealed pots , wall -paintings and their own bodies during trance;and they may conduct them across 'formless' space along threads and the ladders used to link the sun, earth and Underworld. But the relationship of soul to sonum is not simply that the one will change into the other after death . There is also a reciprocal web of mutual dependence. Sonums are nourished by soul, whether of the living humans whom they 'eat' when they attack them or of the domestic animals, grain and alcohol offered to them during sacrifices as a substitute (apanadu) for a sick patient. Living Sora in turn are also nourished by soul. This is not the soul of sacrificed animals (which is consumed by sonums, leaving their flesh behind - conveniently - for the living) . Rather, it is the soul of grain. Something of the consciousness of their ancestors is infused back into the grain grown by their descendants on the land which those ancestors used to cultivate. The way in which Sora say that this takes place will be discussed extensively in later chapters. Having crossed the dividing line from death and re-entered living (ameng) plants, this consciousness derived from



54 Dialog lles IPith the dead
sonums is again called pumdall ('soul'). Thus the mutual dependence of the consciousness of the living and of th e dead is rooted in a basic image of kinship which entails a notion not only of returning, but also of rec iprocal nouri shm e nt.
Naming the baby: childbearing and the husband's lineage There is a conflict between the theory of embryo formation and the ideology of patrilineal kinship. It is true that the Sora do sometimes use 'seed' (jabmof) and ' blood' (l11iiial11) to refer to shared kinship. But these words are used only loosely to refer to the idea of some sort of kinship: to say that two people are of the 'same' seed or blood can refer to anything from membership of a lineage to membership of the Sora people as opposed to Oriyas or white people. Tnama and his brothers, when they placed more porridge than I could manage in my bowl , would reassure me that they would finish offwhat I left uneaten from the same bowl, since we were of'the same blood, the same seed (abo 'nin a millam, abo 'nin ajabmof)'. A baby is made from fusion of semen and vaginal fluid; these are called respectively asong-kadand asong-t{j, meaning literally 'exuviae of the penis' and 'exuviae of the vagina'. The word asong on its own is the common word (probably originally a euphemism) for faeces and it is used to make the terms for secretions from other parts of the body and from certain other processes, as follows:
asong-l11u nose-exuviae i.e . nasal mucus asong-mad eye-exuviae sleep-dust asong-Iud ear-exuviae ear-wax asong-kad penis-exuviae semen asong-t{j vagina-exuviae vaginal fluid asong-set! wine-exuviae sediment, lees asong-Iang iron-ex uviae slag
These suggest that asong should be glossed as 'fluid or semi-fluid (i.e. formless) exuviae given off and relinquished by a bounded entity or at the outer edge of a concentra tion' . The child is thus made of substances which during the process of conception and birth derive, in an equivalent manner, from both mother and father. Though in conceiving, the mother 'takesfruit -child' (pallg-gur-on-), the root kud-, 'beget, give birth', is used equally of both parents, while the noun formed from it, kerud-kud, is used as a more or less precise synonym of the patrilineage. In other words, these images do not in themselves permit the unambiguous separa tion of male and female roles in making a baby.
The forlllatioll of th e Sora persoll 55
We ca n rela te this to the tenta tive na ture of the early stages of a marriage. In providing a mould for their sex ual fluids, the mother is the custod ian of something which will become sepa rate from both parents, but in different ways. A husband's patrilineal claim to his wife is weak and becomes stronge r only with the growt h of their children. As a father , he will simil ar ly need to separate his baby from hi s wife's body . His grain grows on plots which he cultivates and it contains the so ul of hi s lineal ancestors who inhabit those sites. His baby is at first nourished exclusively with milk derived from its mother's body. But after weaning it will never again drink milk , human or animal. Instead the child will feed on porridge mad e from the father 's g rain , thus building up its commitment to his ancestors. It will be remembered that weanin g, significa ntly , coincides rou ghly with th e baby's permanent acquisition around the age of three ofa name belonging to one of its father's ancestors. For the child, this is straightforward and it will grow up unambiguously a member of its father's lineage (except where the mother is living with her parents and the baby is effectively fatherless, in which case it will join its mother's paternal lineage). The mother's lineage membership, however, can remain ambiguous indefinitely. After marriage a woman gradually transfers her loyalties to her husband's ancestors in a way which matches the change in her source of nourishment . Her own father and brothers counterbalance this gradual transference of her loyalty, especially where a marriage is made against their wishes. This is either realised or implied in a number of ways, and will emerge in a dramatic and tragic way for Sagalo and his wife Panderi in chapter 7. For example, throughout her married life a woman continues to manage independently her own personal wealth (keruru, containing jewellery, clothes or grain) which is derived entirely From her father's family. Her grain is kept separately from her husband's grain and can never be cooked and eaten by the household , all of whose food is provided by the husband . Meanwhile the wife can lend her grain out at interest and may even lend it (though in this case interest-free [rea]) to her husband. Her father's family may give her Further substantial amounts after harvests and whenever she visits them. Eventually she may be able to buy her own paddy land Which she and her husband will work together along with his fields, though the crop will be hers alone to dispose of. A woman's keruru is inherited by her children. If she has only daughters, they receive everything; if she has sons as well , they take the land and grain while her daughters receive her jewellery and personal effects. Thus we can see how some ofa woman's keruru is derived ultimately from her own mother's family in a process which crosses from lineage to lineage every time its



56 Dialog lles with the dead
holder marries. We shall see below how women's names are transmitted along exactly the sa me traj ectory . This cont rast s sharpl y with the way in which me n's na mes and belon gin gs are tran smitted indefinitely down the sa me lin eage. T he amb iguity o f a wife's position between two lin eages emerges exp licitly in funerals. A man is given a funera l by hi s lineage a nd from then on is en um erated by their A ncestor-Men, a long with all their other ma le ancestors, und er the headin g 'fa thers, brothers' (w(lngji, bui'iangji, cf. chapter 6, text 6. 1). Unmarried wome n, like men , receive only one sto ne-plantin g, from the lineage into which they were born . A married woman, howeve r, receives two. The first is performed by her husband . But in addition , the lineage into whi ch she was born will then come in a so lemn procession to her husba nd 's crema tion-site and ' ta ke the bones' (pang~jang, i. e. take some of the ashes) to their own cremation-site where they will stage a second, full-scale stone-planting of their own and incorporate her into their own Ancestors (see chapter 8, after the dea th of Panderi). Thus, married women are enumerated twice, by two sets of Ances tor-Men, in identical funerals conducted in duplicate by both their brothers' and their husbands' lineages. In both these funerals they are listed under the significantly undifferentiated heading ' mothers, sisters' (yangji, bujmaiji, cf. chapter 6, lines 213, 225) . So even though wives receive their first funeral from their'husband's lineage and become part of their ancestor cult, they do so in a sense which is laden with paradox since the lineage into which they were born has not relinquished them in its turn . Thus , if a wife dies in childbirth, especially during her first pregnancy, the diagnosis is often that her own natal ancestors have reclaimed her, out of resentment because she is bea ring children for others whose food she now feeds on (examples among Mengalu's relatives will be given in chapter 6). Perhaps the clearest expression of the resistance to her marriage by a woman's natal lineage is the nature of the shaman's own marriage to an I1da sonum in the Underworld. A shaman has two kinds of sonum-familiars. The first are the sonums of her predecessors, previous Sora shamans who are now dead . These are called rauda and are the subject of the invocation given in text 1.2. Usually the most recent rauda was the shaman's own teacher while alive and it is she who speaks during trances while escorting the dead from the Underworld. But these are not the sonums whom shamans marry. The latter, called tlda, belong to the high Paik caste. The Paik were the local caste to whom administration was entrusted in the British period, and their ksatriya varna includes not only rajas, but many local traders and policeman. Ildas have
