This is a relationship within which sex ua l relations or marriage are widely disapproved among the living, In chapter I', I argued that the shaman's experience is only a specia li sed or intensified form of the experience of any la y person, I believe this is true here, too, and that the shaman's cross-cousin marriage represents the fu lfilment ofa culturally powerful fantasy about marriage between brother a nd sister. Consid er the myth of origin of shamanism, The invocat ion which the shaman was sin ging in chapter I (text 1,2) later went on to (race her predecessors right back to the elder of the two sisters who originally founded the two shamanic traditions. These are, respectively, Pubic-Haired Sompa (Kuru-tij Sompa) founder of the funeral tradition; and MascaraEyed Ridi (Sanid-mad Ridi), who founded the tradition of divination and hea ling, I have recorded the story many times and give here a paraphrase:
Text 3,1 The story of Pubic-Haired Sompa
There were two sisters, Pubic-Haired Sompa and Mascara-Eyed Ridi, Ridi was the younger and appears in the story only as a shadowy counterpart of Sompa, Some say they were Sora, some that they were raji, that is, ksalriya, Sompa had so much pubic ha ir thal she had to keep it coiled up under her skirt, hence her full epithet in song: ' Pubic-Ha ired Sompa, Sompa of the coiled pubic tresses' (Kurulij SOlllpa. Saekul' SOlllpa), Some say her groin and thighs were covered with hair 'like a bear' , When they had grown up they sought love rs but no-one among their own people wanted to make love to them (or her: most of the details of the story seem to centre on Sompa), either because they were repelled by the pubic hair or else because they were embarrassed at the elaborate gold jewellery and fine clothes they wore, makin g them 'like raji-women like rich women' (raji-boj allll'id gOlllallg-boj allll'id) , So they were feeling extremely lustful but unfulfilled and the veins in their heads were throbbing 'bi/ bl/ bl/' with sexual desire, Later they abandoned their gold a nd fine clothes, which were then destroyed by termites, and they wore ordinary clothes . In their wanderings in search of lovers they came to a village in the plains inhabited by Sarda Soras, who are paradoxically both despised and felt to be of higher status, and who practise cross-cousin marriage. There there was a dance. The style of drumming and dancing, which differs from that of our Soras, is described: the dance involves a wide-stepping movement, at which Sompa's pubic tresses spilled out and so she aga in failed to secure a lover. The sisters returned home to their brothers, saying 'Hey! brothers! the veins in our heads a re bursting: will the sun drown will the moon drown if we make love to you?' So they slept with their own brothers and their souls were cooled. Later when they found that they were pregnant, they also slept once with their brothers' two Sarda servants, saying 'Now you've slept with us, say it's your child.' Thus they 'p ut the responsibility on' (abdusa/eji) th e two Sardas, Eventually the two girls died and after their deaths, they caused other unmarried girls to fall into trance, Whatever the sisters' ethnic origin may have been originally, they are now I'aji.



60 Dialogues witll tile dead
In th e myth, the public ad mi ssion of sex ual interco urse is linked to the cross-cousin -marrying Sarda servants. But this is only a cover for the essen tial and deeply satisfy ing act of interco urse with the girl s' own brothers , the act which a lso caused their pregnancies. It is not hard from this to see the shama n's i1d a -hu sba nd as representing her own brother and the lineage of their ksatriya hu sba nd s in the Underworld as represent in g the shama ns' own lineages above ground . The implications of this are different for male and female shamans, a nd thi s is reflected in the relative empha sis put on Sompa and Ridi. All shamans trace their descent through a lin e of predecessors and teachers which culminates, according to their tradition, in one or other of these sisters. Mascara -Eyed Ridi is the founder of the lesse r (oseng) tradition of diagnosis and hea lin g, many of whose practitioners a re men . These deal each with hi s own speciali st repertoire ofsonums and may be co nsulted by clients from quite far away. Though divination requires trance, many healings for minor sonums do not , so that not all these men use trance at all. Men learn only as adults. Thus shamanism does not come to a boy in childhood or adolescence nor does it place any strain later upon his marriage, which in any case a lways includes the possibility of polygyny even in this world. Pubic-Haired Sompa, on the other hand, stands at the head of the 'main' or 'important' (muda) tradition , that of the 'funeral' (sanatung) shamans. These shamans work jointly for all the lineages of their own village but for no-o ne else. They are almost exclusively women and where a man takes over for a generation this is supposedly beca use the sonum familiars were unable to find a suitable woman . One example of this is Uda , the shaman through whom the sonul11s will speak in chapter 5. A woman usually marries in the Underworld before puberty , at a formative age a good ten years before she ma rries among the living. This is especially the case in Sompa's funeral tradition , on which the myth puts most of its emphasis. When she later marries above ground, both the woman and her living husband feel the tension . If she is in the divinationand-healing tradition of Ridi, she often continues to practise after marriage; but if she is in the more intense funeral tradition her husband usually finds it unbearable to share her with a dream-husband of elevated status (there is no polyandry anyway amon g the living) , and exerts a strong pressure on her to give up. If on marrying she moves to another village she is obliged to give up in any case, since she is in 'in-law country' (kuiial' desa) and there is a constraint of ,embarrassment' (garo)) on performing funerals for her husband's kin and acting as a mouthpiece for his ancestors. Several
Tile forlllation o/,tlle 50/'{/ persoll 61
of the fema le funeral shamans known to me had never married above gro und , and some of the others had conspic uou sly unstable or peculiar marriages. A few seemed to be transvestites or hermaphrod ites. I wa s a lso told that in some villages female funeral shama ns do not marry at a ll. T hus, women who are sexua ll y active within marriage are genera ll y excl ud ed from the funeral tradition. A woman who does marry will take up the work aga in only in old age, especia ll y after her husband dies, in wh ich case she may eit her ret urn to her natal village or stay on in her husband's village. So apart from a few adult men of a ll ages, the crucia l sequence of funeral rites, on which so much of So ra life depend s, is largely in the hand s of unmarried teenage girls and old women . The latter a re either sisters of the me n with whom they reside, or else wives whose loyalty to their natal lineage has faded to the point where they have effect ively been assimilated to the stat us of sisters in their hu sba nd 's linea ge. Lineage affiliation for women is very much a question of how far brothers let their sisters go to other men and how far their sisters are ab le to ma ke this move. The ultimate indication of where any woman's loyalty has finally come to rest, is where she bestows her name in a future ge neration . Every person has at least one 'proper' (sa 'kai) name besides various nicknames (goibel'i11111 , often affectionately rude, e.g. Gat'gal', 'Cadger'; Asongl'oi, 'Fly-Shit'). Though new proper names can be coined and lidas sometimes give their own names to babies , all old names will sooner or later be given to one baby among the ran ge of the previous holder's heirs. For reasons of demography , one ba by may sometimes bear the names of several ancestors and it will be shown in later chapters how in a dispute over inheritance more than one baby may claim the name of the sa me ancestor. The pa ttern of transmission is different for male and female names. Since the possession of an ancestor's name is an important part of one's total person and sometimes also a determinant of material inheritance, there are many irregularities and disputes, some of which will be examined in part Ill. Normally a dea d man will give his name to a direct patrilineal descendant and a man who dies without sons will give his name to a descendant of hi s brother. This means that a lineage ha s a common reservoir of male names held, as it were, in trust by ancestors and handed back continually as new sons are born. The simplest model of this, since so many grandparents die before seeing their grandchildren, is A, son of B, so n of A , son of B. I A girl child usually receives the name of a woman who has married in to the lineage, typically the name of her own father's mother. But since that woman in turn will have brought her name from the lineage in which she

was born , the lineage has no reservoir of fema le names since these a re transferred across lin eages every time its holder marries. This is how the movement of a woman's name is the same in principle as that of her personal wealth , discussed earlier. In each case, there are implica tion s for the retrospective assessme nt of the woman's own perso nhood as well as for the evolving personhood of her livin g children.
Destiny a nd susceptibility to other persons Earlier I said that nothing seems to be said or known about the origin ofa child's consciousness. One may postulate in the Sora person a certain core which is subject to the imagery of blacksmithing or craftsmanship: someone accused of bad behaviour can say 'It's not my fault: Sun-Woman forged me like that [i.e. in the womb)' (Hen dusa tid: Uyung-bojen edte pomingte). Yet this idea is oflimited scope. Already at tllis stage, formation in the womb is susceptible to other sonums and persons beyond the parents: pregnant women avoid certain places, actions or foods, and especially contact with a recent death by a highly contagious sonum. Over and above any anxiety to protect themselves, they do this to prevent the event from becoming a part of the child in the womb and affecting its ultimate fate. It is widely said that everyo ne's fate is already decided a t the moment of birth, but that we can have no means of knowing this. Sun-Woman, of course, knows: 'Thi s one will be killed by a leopard, that one by a fall , that one with a cough .. .'. Some say that she has built it into the baby's formation as a predisposition to a certain fate ; others, that the sonums themselves race to the scene of a birth to stake a claim to the baby (Hen ate, gamteji, ''' mine'' , they say') . This suggests that at conception there may be no intrinsic prior relationship between the foetus and the form of its eventual destiny or even its personality. Just as it begins to receive a name soon after birth, so somewhere during gestation and birth it encounters the first experience or event on the path leading through the varied experiences of life to its particular death . The discussion above of the sources of its nourishment gives a preliminary idea of how a baby becomes involved at a very early stage with



64 Dialogues lI'i,II ,lie dead
a variety of other agents. Even the mammalian function of breastfeeding is already drawn into a tension between parental lineages, while one of the baby's very first experiences of'illness' underlines a relationshjp with one of its father's ancestors. This relationship is acknowledged by attaching the ancestor's name to the baby, who then receives a measure of protection from hi s intended namesake (ai1tmnamar) against other sonums. As the child grows older, the range of its obligations, friendships, loves and hates will become ever more complex . The way Sora talk about this shows tha t from birth, and maybe even as early as at conception , the ba by's consciousness is moulded in response to a kind of experience which is at variance with any model of inheritance based on ideas of the patrilineage. There are various words for inheritance implying a form of transmission which, even though we shall see how these are sometimes disputed, at least in principle follows the contours of the linea ge. To ' inherit' material property is dane -, while physical characteristics are said to pad- , 'pierce, penetrate, pass through' from one person to another, i.e . across boundaries or from one focal centre of personhood to another. This is the usual word for stitching leaves, cloth , etc., and is commonly used also for the transmission of facial resemblance and 'physical' characteristics generally (e.g. chapter 7, line 231). It implies an orderly social, we might even say quasi-biological, heredity. However, there are other aspects of a person which are also transmitted down the generations and to which the word pad- does not apply. These aspects are unhappy ones connected with kinds of illness and death: previous victims return to cause similar dea ths among those whom they have left behind. These kinds of experience, when they are transmitted , are said to gorod-, 'wa nder, leap, move contagiously'. But at the same time, the dead transmit their own illnesses to those closest to them: friends, acquaintances and lovers, and particularly descendants. A briefexample may be given to illustrate how heredity, influences on the foetus, influences at the birth and subsequent adult experiences are all brought to bear as possibilities in interpreting a single person's life:
A certain man is widely cited to illustrate the workings of contagion : he sometimes has violent raging fits in which he beats his wife, brother and others. This is usually said to be because as an adult he helped to carry the corpse of a murder victim before the decontamination ('blocking') rites against a murder were performed (cf. chapter 7, lines 208-11) . The victim's unchannelled (ka'ja) blood entered him and now causes these fits. Out of obstinacy, this man still refuses to undergo the rite and is expected to die a violent death himself. However, I later heard, contrary
Tile forl7/a'ion of' ,lie Sora person 65
to this , that he had also been a sad istic child and had once beaten a buffalo to death and given hi s younger brother a ser ious head injury with a sto ne. Meanwhile his aged mother, defensively , is at pains to insist (I) tha t there was no such violence among hi s forebears; alld (2) tha t she had avo id ed all such influences during her pregnancy; alld (3) that she had rubbed him well with turmeric after his birth to coo l him.
The Sora thu s exp lain a person's mortality, vulnerability and much of hi s character in terms of his relationships to others, but especially to those who are now dead. Some of these susta in the person, while others try to pull the person's consciousness away from his centre in order to 'eat' (jlllll - ) it. To the extent that the person is defined by these others, he is thereby given hi s very being. Yet at the sa me time he is diminished to the extent that they impinge on him in a certain overwhelming and consuming way. Effectively nothing in a person's fate which can be talked about at all, is interpreted only as innate: the events of his illness and death are always interpreted in terms of his involvement with other persons. There is a distinction between transmission from the dead as generational succession and the transmission of bad experiences. This distinction throws upon the latter much of the burden in the explanation of the unhappiness inherent in human frailty and lies at the heart of the complex Sora classification of sonums. It underlies the funeral's slow struggle to eliminate that bad experience and restore the deceased to an idiom of pure Ancestorhood. The irony, which will emerge in the following chapters, is that the bad-experience aspect is also an unavoidable part of the person's total social identity since this, too, is based on his interaction with other persons.



4
Interpreting and persuading the dead
Ancestor and Experience: mapping two distinct modes of being dead
AIlcestOl'-SOllIlI1lS (I1/(/ the Experiellce SOlll111lS
In their classification of sonums, the Sora make a fundamental distinction. They distinguish between those aspects of the dead whereby they pass on a social identity, from those in which they inflict the forms of their own deaths. In the first case, the dead protect and nourish their descendants , in the second they consume and absorb them. The use of the generic term sonum always implies the possibility of specifying which sonum is meant. A sick person asks , 'Which sonum is stroking me? (iten a sonul11 sll11ling?), and a recently-dead person is asked 'Which sonum took you? (ilen a sonul11 pangal11le?).' The question 'which sonum?' may yield an answer simultaneously in two kinds of terms . The first is a set of specific persons, for example ' the sonum of my father' , ' the sonum of my grandmother'. These are called idai-slln : 'Ancestor-sonum ', or often just idai, ' Ancestor'. The other is a set of ca tegories which are both cosmological and related partly through medical symptoms to forms of death . These have names like 'Sun-Sonum', 'Leopard-Sonum' or 'Convulsion-Sonum' (respectively Uyung-sll11 . KlI1a-SlIl1 and Kani-sll11). There is no collective word in Sora for the various categories of this set. Sora conceive of their action in terms of incidents and events during the course of people's lives. I shall therefore gloss them collectively throughout this book as 'Experience' sonums (with a capital 'E'). Where sonums are discussed as causes of illness and death (both of which can generally have no other kind of cause), the full explanation of such events requires that these two sets of terms be combined in order to explain both the motive for the attack and its form. A person may say something
Illt erpretillg alld persuadillg tlte dead 67
like 'It was my fa ther, acting through Sun -So num .' T hus the dead little girl who spoke at the beginnin g of chapte r I (tex t 1.1) was killed by a previou s victim of Madu-slln ('Leprosy-Son um '). T hrou gh the form of her own dea th , she has become associated with thi s sonum in her turn a nd is now li kely to attack her livin g relatives in thi s sa me way. The interpl ay betwee n the Ancestor and the Expe ri ence aspects ofsonum is com pl ex. However, throu ghout all the paradoxes and resolution s of lhis interp la y, this conceptual distinct ion rema ins clear. It is refl ected, for instance, in the geography of the dead (figure 4 .1). Both horizonta ll y and vertically, the space of the Ancestors lies in the inhabited house and village . Horizontally, Ancestor space is di stin gui shed on the land sca pe from Experie nce space as domestic to wild , or village to jungle. Vertically, it lies within the house and down towards the Underworld (kll1orai desa) , which is the normal abode of the dead wh en they are conceived as Ancestors. A rather pallid replica of social life among the living, complete with hou ses and villages, is supposed to exist there. The main passages from there to the world of the living pass through the grain-pounding mortar set into the floor of each house as well as through the collective cremation-sites and stone-planting sites of each lineage in the street outside. Experience space, by contrast, is mapped on to the landscape outside the village a t ground level and a bove, from clumps of trees, rocks and pa ths up to the sun. These locations are focusses of meaning whereby the concept of the Experiences
Turns them to shapes, and gives to airy nothing A local habitation and a name.
(A Midslllllmer Night's Dream, V.i. 16- 17)
The di stinction between Ancestor and Experience leads us to the heart of the ambivalence with which the Sora regard their dead . In both cases, it is

the sa me dead people who are involved. To a certa in ex te nt , the So ra dead resemble those in many parts of south a nd southeast As ia , where the recentl y decea sed are more dangerous than the lo ng-dead . W ithin H indu India , for exa mple, tills distinction ca n be seen as parallel to that between the pill" and the Mula or prela . But ma ny Sora features seem distinctive , in particular the simultaneity of the deceased's double na ture as a causa l agent, as well as the leng th of time over whi c h this ambivalent nature persists. A nd yet within this double nature, the empha sis does shift. A dead person und ergoes an evo lution over time, as the empha sis moves gradua lly from the dangerous Ex perience aspect of hi s being to the generall y benign Ancestor aspect. I shall analyse this in la ter chapters. For now , I shall di scuss the term s A nces tor and Experience in turn.
Allcesto,,-SOf//mIS alld the house: how the Ih'illg participate ill jill/erals
An Ancestor is a dead person. Each such person retains hi s or her own name and separate identity within the framework of the linea ge . An idai is not necessa rily one's ancestor in the literal , restricted sense of the English word : rather, one's idais are one's own dead . They therefore include numerous young people such as one's own dead children (cf. text 1. 1); wives of one's lineage, who are also Ancestors to their natal lineages; sisters who, ifmarried, will simultaneously be Ancesto rs to their husband's lineage too ; and to a certa in extent a male cross-cousin, if one has given him a duplicate funeral (as Sagalo did for Sunia in chapter 2) . There are thus varyin g degrees of Ancestorhood which may be recognised on a particular occasion by an entire lineage, a group of brothers, or a single household . In order to understa nd this, let us look more closely at the household and its relations with other households. A village usually contains several patrilineages (biJ·inda). The imagery of the lineage's interna l structure is arboreal, with 't runks' or 'branches' (aneb), ' twigs' (sipa) and ' leaves' (ala) . For the sake of simplicity I shall sometimes use the word 'line' to specify the descendants of anyone man under di scussion. The lineage's male core is descended from a single male ancestor who is listed by every member in the rites of their personal ancestor cult, since he is equally the ancestor of a ll . While this ancestor may be three, four or occasionally more genera tions away, more recent ancestors are listed on most occasions only by their own lines , that is by their own direct descendants . Spatially, the lineage is fixed. Each linea ge possesses its own cremation ground (kl11lalod) and group of upright memorial stones (g-ml-ll-ar, 's tone-planting site') to which a new stone is added at each funeral (gll-{I/', 'stone-planting') conducted by the lineage. One must marry a member of
/11 l erprel illg (flld perslladillg lli e dead 69
anot her lin eage and women usua ll y move into th eir hu sba nd 's hom e on marria ge, so that the male co re of the lin eage stays put while women co me and go . Since the villa ge conta in s seve ral lineages, one may marry equally inside or outside the vill age. T he re is constant contact among villa ges throu ghout the a rea . De legations vi sit ot her villa ges for fun eral s, whil e right s to the use of la nd a re widely scattered : a hou sehold may cultivate plots up to an ho ur's walk away from home a nd can expect hospitality from in-laws, cross-co usins or fri end s in many villages on the way. The final ' leaf of the lin eage is the hou sehold . Gene ra ll y it co nta in s a nuclea r family o f husba nd , wife (or, for som e, wives) a nd children. Each son as he marries fOllnd s a sepa ra te household , tak in g with him a complete rep li ca of hi s father 's a ncestor-cult (later to include hi s father a nd mother too when they die), while the yo un ges t son genera ll y brings hi s wife to hi s parents' hOll se and rema ins in it after their death.' Within the long lang, the part of the villa ge inhabited by one lin eage, several houses are often joined toge ther in rows , each with a sepa rate entrance giving on to a common verandah (pil1da). A house consists of one sin gle-storeyed room , constructed of so lid sto ne walls plasterd with reddi sh mud. These walls stop some way below the thatch roof, which does not rest on them but is supported on a framework of wooden pillars (sundallg) , beams and rafters. Conversations can be conducted between houses over the party wall , so that a row of hou ses in some ways resembles a longhouse. Just inside the door is an area in which one can stand upright and where the heavy wooden grain-pounding mortar (anal) is set flush into the cowdungfinished floor. Beyond this the remainder of the interior is divided horizo ntally in two by a loft (maran) set at chest height and made of stout planks. The loft contains large baskets of grain (unless the house is very poor a nd lives entirely in debt) . Apart from the door, light enters only through a gap between the thatch and the top of the wall. At the fa r end, under the loft , is the hearth (kuda), the smoke of which similarly pa sses through thi s ga p. Down here, often screened from the door area by a low interior wall , is the dark and shadowy area where the family ea ts and sleeps by the light of the fire . This area is for squatting or sitting, not standing, and one must walk crouching or like a crab. Women keep the floors swept clean and the walls are draped with tools , cloth , and gourd and clay bottles suspended on nails and hooks. On the advice of shamans, people may make wall-paintings (idlal) dedicated to sonums and hang up other offerings such as a sonum-pot (sonum-dang) containing grain. Many parts of the house are more or less direct symbols of the lineage or of its twigs and leaves. The main pillar (sllndang) supporting the roof



70 Dialog ues lFitli tlie dead
represents the continu ity of patrilineal kin ship in the past and future. For exa.mp le, before drinking a lcoho l indo ors, the man of the house may spnnkle a few drops at the foot of it with a few words of offering to his predecessors. For a man to say 'I have my main pillar' (iiellll1uda-sul1 den daku) is convent ion a l 'deep-la ng uage' Uaru-ber) for ' I have li ving so ns.' Anothe r important part of the house is the loft. The gra in stored here belongs to the male householder's patrilineal ancestors and though in -marrying women cook this gra in , they must not climb up to fetch it - a prohibition which is relaxed only after menopause, or earli er if a marriage IS very Il1tlmate. Until then , the g rain can be fetched only by males o r by daughters and sisters of the house. The mortar (ana!), which is set into the floor near the door, is the passage from the house to the land of the Ancestors in the Underworld . Here is pounded the grain which nourishes the members of the house and which as we shall see later co ntain s a vital element of those Ancestors' sou l. In addition, the pounding motion of the pestle (ol1dring) in the mortar has a thinly veiled connotation of sexual intercourse. The door (sanang) is also an entrance for Ancestors. At certain mO~lents of the funerary cycle great play is made of opening and barring their passage through both the mortar and the door. Outside the house, Ancestor space broadens to encompass larger groups of pa trilineal rela tives at different levels of segmenta tion of the kin-group: from the house for the nuclear family, to the terraces of three or four closely-related houses, to the cremation-site and stone-planting site for the lineage as a whole. Immediate Ancestors of the household are fyd inside the house a nd at threshing-floors out on the hillsides at various seasonal rites (abdur) which are associ a ted with stages of the growth and harvest ofcrops. In addition, they may be fed inside the house (s/'lI1gp/r, 'house-rite') when they cause a non-fatal fever (asu) in members of their own households
particularly in a small child to whom they wish to give their name. But th~ main context in which the dead are addressed as Ancestors is at the several stages of their own funerals.
Funerals bring together the entire lineage among both the living and the dead. The relationship between ancestor and descend a nt is one of succession in the widest sense . What is at stake here is not only material inheritance, important though this sometimes is, but also an element of transmitted social identity. The way a living person participates in a funeral is therefore a close reflection of the role he expects the deceased to play in his future life . A general outline of the stages of the funeral wa s given in chapter 3. Here, I shall focus on the central rite of the stone-planting in order to discuss various ways of rela ting to the deceased . For the moment, I

Men sacrifice ·a buffalo under one of three rubrics which ex press the degree both of th eir claim to be the heirs of th e deceased and of their obligation to incorporate him into their lives as an A nces tor. Just as in the hea lin g for an illness, this buffalo is a 'substitute' for the donor. This is vividly expressed in the Sora idiom for giv in g someth in g in one's own nam e. 'Planting tlie stone' (gu-m). Only the most direct heirs do this. For adults, these will be their sons, assisted by their unmarried daughters; for children , their parents . Except for the lineage's sto ne-plant in g site and cremation-ground, all property is conceived of as being owned by individu a ls. Immovab le property (land, hou se-s ites, usefu l trees) as well as catt le are owned and inherited largely by males. Other possessions like jewellery and tools a re usually inheri ted by children of the same sex as the previous owner, sharing equally. The most substantial property is usually a man 's paddy land or his rights to the use of a shiftingcultivation site. AU of these are divided equally among his sons, but they do this only after staging his stone-planting and taking on his uncleared debts (teng-, literally carrying them publicly on their head). As well as a buffalo such heirs provide the stone and in some villages they may also 'sacrifice' an egg or eat a 'crema ted ' rice-flour effigy of the deceased. This seems to signify their exclusive right to hi s name for one of their future babies (see chapters 7- 9). Through these actions they will 'induct' (amgan -) the deceased into their house or houses in which he has a right of residence as an Ancestor in this full sense. 'Hilling a buffalo' ( id-bong) . This is done by other linea ge mem bel'S 'out of pity because it 's our brother/sister (abasu)'lI11 bui1ang/ tonan lell gamle)' . They give a buffalo but do not sacrifice eggs or share in eating an effigy . They therefore do not carry the dead person's debts nor do they inherit a share of his property or the possibility of his name for one of their descendants . In this sense , the deceased is not inducted into their houses as a full Ancestor. But the continuing unity of the lineage as a whole is acknowledged, since all Ancestors of all its branches are

enumerated at the stone-planting when anyone of the lineage's members dies (chapter 6). Within this total li st there are degrees of co llatera lity , all of which participate by hitting a buffalo; if the line of direct heirs sho uld later die out, everyth in g should normally revert to the nearest of these. 'Dancing a bt!ffa/o' ( tongseng-bong). This is done by an affine (parent-in -law, brother-in -law, or child -in -Iaw). If they have come from another village the party of the affinal lineage camp and cook outside the village, then enter with a flamboyant war dance to a beat called gan-desa (,entering-territory', but also with overtones of ' invading'). Dancing a buffalo is the affinal act pal' excellence. I once witnessed a furious argument when an unwanted suitor of the dead man's daughter turned up from anot her village with his suppo rters a nd made ready to kill a buffalo on the outskirts of the stone-planting ' for hi s father-in-law to be'. Simi la rly , the affines do not take home the sonum of the deceased or induct him into their houses. They normally have no right to inh er it his belongings or his name, nor does the deceased have a corresponding right to make them ill and demand sacrifices from them . But this can change if someone decides to 'take the bones' of his male cross-cousin back to hi s own site and stage a seco nd funeral , thereby incorporat in g him for a while at least as an Ancestor. This is what Sagalo did for Sunia , and some of the implications will emerge in c hapter 8.
An Ancestor is in principle benign, protective and so licitous for the welfare of his descendants, who as hi s rep lacements are an entity whom it is in his interest to preserve - thou gh eve n here he is sensitive to his amour propre and can cause fever if annoyed. Ancestors are the source of all that has come to us by inheritance , and their claim on us is based on a piquant combination of moral obligation (even if often viewed as a nuisance) and sympathy for the pathetic condition which is the lot of the dead.
Experiellce SOlllmlS alld the lalldscape: fo"",s of i11lless alld death
It is not in the form of Ancestors that the dead kill their descendants, but as bearers of the symptoms by which they themselves died: they perpetuate in others the forms of their own deaths . They have this capacity by virtue of being representatives of one or other of a range of what I have called
Experience son um s. This kind of collective sonum is a cosmological concept yet it has an active consciousness of its own, animated, so to speak, by its individual members. Through making the living share in the experience for which it stands, it seeks to assimilate them by an annihilation of their own identity . According to the specific imagery of the sonum concerned, this attempt at assimila tion may be expressed by a verb of gra bbing, eating, swallowing, or even sexual embrace. However, all such words imply a total overwhelming by the a ttacker of the victim, who thereby' fuses' or ' merges' (l1Iaj-) in to the Experience. A full discussion of the vocabulary and grammar of these verbs is given towards the end of chapter 8, where it will form a crucial part of my interpretation of what sonums are. Eve ry location outside the villa ge which is distinctive enough to be referred to in conversation has a name, which can usually be etymologised . Thus any walk through the jungle can be specified as a sequence of points almost eve ry few yards: 'Sweet Mango', 'Cows' Co nfusion ', ' Peacock's DancingGround', 'Bees' Home', 'Wind in the Sa l Trees' (respectively Sindiul , Garegetangen, Tarongse ngmar , Borsll1g, Sargiaringen). The sa me kind s of descriptive, usually compound word s serve equally to designate villa ges (Alinsll1g, 'Wine Village', spelled throughout this book Alinsing; Manengul, 'Furthest Mango '; Sogad, 'dove'; Kumbt.llstng, 'Rat's Home'); sites of Experiences; and locations which have no such special significance. Thus any place which can be sufficiently isolated perceptually and conceptually to be labelled , has the potential to contain consciousness in the form of sonum. This potential is not always realised : for example, nothing actually happens at Garegetangen except that cows stray, or perhaps did on one particular occasion, but it is always possible for stronger meanings to build up at such sites. As Experience sites, they are then gathered into classes through some common attribute. For example, Sweet Mango and Wind in the Sal Trees are the sites of water-sources, and therefore (for reasons which will emerge in chapter 6 and beyond) of specific instances of the
category Earth-Sonum. The sites into which Experiences recruit their victims are analogies of the villages of the living and as well as their ordinary names are also called, e.g. ' Leopard -Village' or ' Leopa rd-House' (Ktna-sll1g) , ' Sun-House' (UyungSll1g), etc. (-slI1g serves as the compounding form of both SI 'lI1g, 'house', and go/jang, ' villa ge'). Since each such point represents a form of illness and death, the Sora landscape is a gigantic three-dimensional mnemonic device. This functions in a manner like the aI's memoriae ('art of memory ') by which classical Greek and Roman orators memorised the points to be made in their speeches by mentally walking round a landscape or building and retrieving ideas deposited there previously (Yates 1966: for an extraordinary parallel as a mental 'illness', see Luria 1968). The information yielded by this landscape is on the one hand a set of Experiences which explain

ce rtain kind s of eve nts, on the oth er hand li s ts of nam es of peop le known to have died in these ways a nd to have ' me rged ' or ' fu sed' (II/aj-) int o the sites of these Ex peri ences. Sora frequently refer to th e connect ions be tween Experience sites and person s in eve ryday conversat ion , as well as eve ry time they perform a rite in co nn ection with a site .
Encountering, welcoming and dismissing sonums While a person 's s uscept ibility to demands for fo od from an A nces to r pure and simple is cast clea rl y in terms of kin ship and is co unte rbalanced by the ima ge of nourishment , hi s susceptibility to attacks by an Experience is cas t largely in term s of even ts in his li fe and act io ns which brin g him within the range of those Experie nces. These include apparentl y random co ntac ts li ke pa ss in g nea r their reside nces or e nco unterin g one of their scouts who 'wander' (go rod-) across the terra in . Certainly , the son um 's connection with a new victim is ba sed not on any kind of kinship ri ght but on notions of encounter or moments of heightened susceptibi li ty . But even so, each Experience sonum contains a reshuffled selection of everybody's Ancestors from various lineages. The ringleader (l11uda) a t least of such attacks usually has a close kinship or other emotional connection with the current victim while the other Experience-members are 'shadow-men-at-his-back' (/ub-dung-marenji) who back him up and share in the spoils (if the victim dies, these are his own soul; if he recovers, then that of the sacrificed animal). Thus one generally communicates throughout one's life with the same ra nge of dead people who at one moment act as one's nurturing Ancestor, at another assa il one as representatives of an Experi ence. The idea of encounter is linked to the way in which people move on the landscape. Figure 4.3 gives the main sites connected with Experience sonum s for Alinsing, the village ill which Menga lu and Sagalo live . It will be seen that sites fall into three types, which I ha ve called residences (the inhabi ted 'houses' or 'villages ' [s/'/Ilg) of the Experiences, marked on the map by squares); outposts (triangles); and sacrificial sites (circles) . Most of these are referred to by their place names. Thus Sindiul, 'Sweet Man go ' , is the name ofa location , in which there is a spring of the same name, in which there is the residence of an Earth-Sonum, called in full Sindiul-a-Labo-slIl1, 'Sweet-Mango Earth-Sonum'; similarly, Sangkaroren (etymology uncertain) is a place in which happen to be located side-by-side two different residences . There is a spring containing an Earth-Sonum (called SangkarOl'en Earth-Sonum) and on the nearby path is the residential site of the Ra'tud-Sonum for the entire village of Alinsing. Residences, as we have seen, store the Experience's victims and in so doing harbour the potential for further recruitment. Susceptibility to an

Experience ari ses from a combination of predi sposition and 'encountering' (olong- ). Direct contac t with the site ma y precipitate an attack. But this is not the only possible means, since members of the Experience use th e site as a base from which they move out to seek new recruits among relatives and fri end s. Different categories of reside ntial Ex perience mayor ma y not have outposts to ass ist them in this. Son urn s do not actually resid e at outposts, but outposts are linked in various ways to a reside nce and recruit victim s on its behalf. As an example , let us look at Ra ' tud -Sonum (Ra'tud -s lm) , oneof whose conspicuou s properties is to a ttack people on paths. Every village has one Ra'tud -House at an important path -junction and a number of outposts on other paths all around , which are frequented by its members (the ' Ra ' tud -people ', Ra 'tud-lIIarallji) . These outposts act ra ther like booby-traps, a nd to succumb fatally to any of them will lead to residence at the main site . There is no separate term for outposts but in some cases they are called 'Police Station' , tana, because they are used to 'seize' or 'arrest' (i1am -) passers-by . . Encountering goes beyond fixed outposts and includes mobile patrols and other agents. Ra'tud-People wander continually along paths and may be encountered on anyone of these; Leopard-Sonum may use actual leopards to attack one; and so on. The idea of encounter shades into ideas of actions and forms of behaviour which attract the attentions of an Experience. Smallpox (Ruga-boj, 'Speckle-Woman') is a caste-Hindu sonum of the plains, identified by the Sora with Durga, and is attracted by the wearing of flowers or brightly coloured clothes, which is not normal Sora taste . Excreting near the spring of an Earth -House will offend the sonum's sense of cleanliness and cause blockage or swelling of one's excretory passage. Uttering the name of an Experience in vain is to invite trouble - though everyone does so constantly since 'Leopard' and 'Ra'tud' are among the commonest everyday swear-words: 'You Leopard-Man (lona-mar)!' , said either affectionately or in real anger, like 'You silly bugger!' in English; or 'Ra '- TUUUD!!', said most memorably when someone opened the door of his house after an absence of only a few hours to find a huge , heaving termite hill growing through the middle of his floor. Sacrificial sites are referred to as ' where we do the rite (lVante p trtebe), to heal a patient of such-and-such a sonum . These are the points at which the living aim to head off an encounter. Here, sonums are fed with alcohol and an animal's soul, but only in order to be dismissed . While residences and outposts are spread across the landscape at some distance from the village, that is in fully wild space, sacrificial sites are mostly strung out along the paths leading out of the village, taking over at roughly the outermost edge

'sub-Experiences', have no residential sites of their own and are fed at the edge of the villa ge at sites which seem to have no inherent significance but which are simply distinct from each other. The point here is to banish the sub-Experience from the villa ge to Ex perience space. This is part of the process by which the Sora continually try to hold apart the Ancestor and Experience aspects of their dead , by 'inducting' (al1l -gclI1-, '[causative particle) -enter') the former into the house and ' banishin g' (al1l -dllng-, '[causative particle) -leave') the latter by leading it out of the house. As well as the person of the pa tient , the space which is protected is tha t of the house. In one sense, each direct Ancestor was formally inducted at his or her stone-planting and indeed has a right of overlordship in the house. In another se nse , the Ancestor was passed through the stone into the Underworld and so does not so much reside in the house, as repeatedly re-enter it on various occasions. Even here, the Ancestor's direct presence makes the living uneasy and they hasten to restrict his movement. At the annual harvest-commemoration and at other seasonal rites, Ancestors are invited in for the festival and sent back afterwards through the mortar to the Underworld . On other occasions, Ancestors may enter a house or follow someone home demanding food by causing a fever. The residents feed them inside the house with a chicken and then similarly send them back. Sometimes an Ancestor is fed in the house and is then given a closed pot hung on the wall for months or years where he is fed with the soul of some grain which is put inside the pot and changed from time to time. He may also be given a small wall-painting. Thus though in one sense it owns the house, under no circumstances is any sonum, even an Ancestor, left there at large. Hit is there at all, it will make itself known in a way which will force the household to take action. They will then either make it go away, close it up in a pot, or frame it inside a wall-painting. In their Experience aspect, of course, the dead are not invited into the house at all, but it is known that they have entered or followed someone home when they are diagnosed in illness. They are then dismissed in a healing (pnplr) , in this context called a banishing rite (am -rlung-plr , '[causative particle) -Ieave-rite'). When the dead come like this as members of an Experience, they are initially gathered up inside the house with an invocatory chant and usually some alcohol, raw grain and the live animal. The participants then lead the sonum along with the materials out of the house to the sacrificial site where they will perform the main chants and kill , cook and eat the animal. The dismissal of the sonum is contained in the words and actions of the rite. In the case of highly contagious Experiences, such as Leopard or Convulsion, there are taboos about lingering at the site
Interpreting and persl/ading the dead 8 1
afterwards, taking mea t home, or even looking bac kwards. But otherwise the mea l is a leisurely picnic and meat is taken back for those who did not attend .
From medical history to biography
It should not be thought from aU this that the Sora rega rd their land scape or live their lives in ex treme fear. They go everywhere constantly, walking, sitting, laughing, drinking, much as people elsewhere will have a picnic within inches of the lorry lane of a motorway or a hi gh-tension cable. But an encounter should be seen as something much more than the equivalent of stumbling on an uninsulated electric cable or a dump of poisonous chemical waste. An Experience attacks someone through the personal rela tionship which exists between them. Even the most everyday affliction is therefore never an accident, with the English word's connotation of randomness. There is no discussion of symptoms in illness or death , without discussion of the motivation of members of an Experience which can be linked to those symptoms. Conversely, illness is one side of a coin stamped on the other side with the positive relationship of Ancestor to descendant. The landscape is one, not of fear, but of a keen awareness of mutual dependence.
The scope of the Experiences' action on the living is therefore more far-reaching than that of mere symptoms, in a narrow 'medical' sense. The Sora live hard. Illness and sudden death are a constant element in Sora life. External medical facilities are scarce and are used erratically and uncolllprehendingly. Infant mortality is high: many women have given birth about ten times and lost half of these children. I rarely met anyone who had lived beyond fifty-five or at the most sixty (so far as I could judge by working through genealogies, since no-one knows their age); and I have often seen even robust teenagers and young adults collapse unexpectedly and die within hours . Virtually all Sora appear to suffer frolll all of the following endemic conditions (in medical categories): hookworm with anaemia, roundworm, viral hepatitis, ringworm, amoebic dysentery, bacillary dysentery, vivax malaria , falciparum malaria (sometimes with blackwater fever) and tuberculosis: while wounds , received from working with axes and knives in the forest and from walking barefoot over thorns and sharp stones, sometimes turn septic. Fevers are frequent and people of all ages may continue to work strenuously and cheerfully in a state in which most of today's Westerners (and middle-class Indians) would not even attempt to sit up in bed. 2
Most people carry most of these 'diseases' in their bodies most of the



82 Dialog ues ll'ith the dead
time. Yet they are ' ill ' only sometimes. T he role of perception and interpretation is clea rly crucia l. In interpretin g illness and death, Sora thinking balances the se nsation s and mood s which we might ca ll symptoms against an exp lanatory framework which states that they are caused by the son um s of the dead, who ha ve them selves suffered from similar cond iti ons. In every case, not only shou ld the phenomenology of the illness be accounted for , it should also be convincin gly united with a motive ba sed on personal relations between the present sufferer and a predecessor who is now causin g this experience. The process of tracing this link is comp lex and can sometimes seem tortuous. Detailed case-stud ies of this during inquests will be given in chapters 5 and 8; but meanwhile I shall give some simple examp les of this kind of rea soning here:
- Shortly after an eld erly woman has died with Eart h-Sonum symptoms, her devoted husband also dies in a similar way. She took him out of love to join her because she was lonely without him .
- A man falls ill with Ra'tud symptoms. His dead father, who himse lf died ofRa'tud, says that as a sonum he met his son on the path yesterday and asked for some of the wine he was carrying, but was refused. He has caused the illness as a punishment. The son promises not to think mean and greedy (ranglw) thoughts next time he is carrying a pot of wine, makes the appropriate Ra 'tud sacrifice to his father and his Ra'tud cronies, and recovers.
- A boy and girl were in love, but her brother was jealous and made her life wretched, so she hanged herself. At her stone-planting, she asked who was drumming so beautifully in the background. It was her lover, so she called him to drink wine from the gourd from which she - in the body of the shaman - had just been drinking, saying ' I loved you desperately while I was alive, but now this is the only way I can occasionally be with you.' Shortly afterwards, the boy went out and hanged himself. She had taken him into the Sun, by the contagion of suicide which was in the drink.
How is it that the seven categories of landscape-based Experiences (figure 4.2), some of them referring to specific and even rare forms of dea th, are able between them to yield a convincing interpretation for every illness which occurs? The answer lies partly in the fact that a full interpretation must enrich mere phenomenology with conscious motivation. But it lies
Int erpretillg alld perslladillg the dead 83
a lso within the constitut ion of the Experiences them se lves. These Experiences are ultimately flexible enough so that between them they stand for a ll possible kinds of symptoms, signs and omens in death, as we ll as for complicated and conflict in g interpretations of motive in terms of personal relation s. Such a sma ll set of resid ential Expe riences do not do this as monolithic categor ies, but mould themselves to the particulars of lived experience through their command of subordinate levels of meanin g which match these particulars more closely. Each Experience contains a number of distinct ca usa l powers or agents. People see these as elements which on their own command only the specific symptoms or states which they ca use, but which combine with each other (and with the total li st of known victim s) to form the total cha ra cter of the superordinate Experience. For example , it will be remembered from chapter 3 that Sun -Sonum is primarily a female who commands the technique of the blacksmith in order to forge embryos out of molten metal. As such , however, the Sun is composed of several elements. Some of these are male, some female , some of indeterminate sex, and various relationships between them are brought to the fore in different rites and myths. Some of the most important aspects are:
Uyung-boj 'Sun-Woman ' The dominant aspect; in all ways she determines the actual formation of the baby, while the other aspects are her 'em issaries' (pisirian) or 'bonded labourers' (kambari).
Mo'mo'-yung 'Dumb-Sun'
A male, imbecile blacksmith who simply hammers embryos under her direction and has no initiative of his own; he announces his presence during a trance by grunting inarticulately.
Duri-sll11 'Lumbago-Son um' Identified when Sun-Woman uses Dumb-Sun to strike a person on the loins with his hammer to cause back pains (see case-study in Chapter 7).
Lur~jadan 'Python-Snake' The Sun and Moon were brothers ('Sun-Sonum', Uyung-slll1 and 'Moo n-Sonum ', Anggaj-slll1). But the Sun's hot little children filled the sky and scorched the earth. To help humans, the Moon hid his own children (the stars) in a chest and rubbed his mouth with the red gum of the ame tree. Then he told the Sun that he had swallowed his own children and urged the Sun to do likewise. When the Sun had swallowed



84 Dialogues Ivith the dead
all the baby Sun s, the Moon released the sta rs again. F uriou s at having been tricked , the Sun periodically send s a python to pursue and swa llow the Moon. This ca uses eclipses, but also menstrual disorders and miscarriages, in which the foetu s is similarly 'swa llowed ' by thi s python.
I shall call these subordinate ca tegor ies of the Experiences their sub-Experiences. The sub-Experiences do not contain any dead people, but are merely aspects of the superordinate Experiences which do. Appendix I gives a li st of all the so nums I saw people dealin g with in Alinsing (many more such so num s can be found in other villages and in the writings of Elwin and Sitapati). It will be seen there that there are over one hundred of these sub-Experiences and that I have bee n able to fit almost all of these easily under the seven superordinate headings of the Experiences. On the one hand, sub-Experiences correspond fairly directly to a medical or quasimedical symptom or other equivalent sign of their activity. On the other, the association of each sub-Experience with one or other of the residential Experiences, which is explicitly recognised by the Sora, is based on a continuity of imagery between these two levels. The Sun's overall imagery of heat, redness and the processes inside the womb leads through the blacksmith's hammer to the pains of lumbago, and through red tree-sap and eclipses to menstrual disorders and miscarriages. Another aspect of the Sun, for instance, focusses on leprosy and lesions as a kind of burn (Madu-slI11, 'Leprosy-Sonum'). Thus every superordinate Experience has a cluster of properties, each of which is taken up in a narrower, more specific way by one of its various sub-Experiences. So each one of these constituent sub-Experiences expresses only a small part of the Experience's full nature and mobilises only a small part of its entire armoury. The sub-Experiences thus face Janus-like in two directions. With one face they point towards the relatively abstract residential Experiences from which they derive their potential to act and to the identity of which they in turn contribute. With the other face they point towards the physical signs and symptoms in the realm of perception which they are deemed to cause. It is their relative descriptive accuracy at this level which gives them verisimilitude, while the continuity of imagery with the residential categories allows this conviction to be carried over to the realm of motiva tion among the Experience's dead residents. Sub-Experiences do not have the degree of complexity which would warrant their own space to store dead victims. The perpetuation of sub-Experiences across time comes not from having people as their carriers but only from the dependence ofeach one on a larger, more complex category which does do this.
Int erpreting and persuading the dead 85
In this li g ht the residential Expe ri ences appear as hi ghl y simplified a nd co nde nsed summari es: simplilled in the sense of carryin g out a concept ua l red uction of their primary perceptual data of sym ptom s, signs and moods ; condensed , in ' that in order to act they must ge nera lly unpack their compressed latent properties and set one specific aspect of these to work. The sub-Ex peri ences are not themselves summaries but co nstituen t elements of tho se summaries, who se re la tionships are conceptua li sed in te rms of ba sic human social relation ships: myth and kinship (the Sun a nd Moon were brothe rs) and ro ya l or burea ucratic authority (Sun-Woman's command over her slave Dumb-S un and her agent Python -Snake). Why then does a particular dead perso n affect a particular livin g person, on a particular occasion and in a particular form? An answer to thi s question will emerge gradually as this book proceeds. Though the a rena of sonUl11S includes wha t one might ca ll medical diagnosis, their interpreta tive power reaches far beyond what this would imply. This is nicely illustrated by Rumble-Tummy-Sonum (Kurkur-pung-slIl1), a minor condition caused by Leopard-Sonum (Kll1a-slIl1) . Following my terminology above for sonum sites, we can say that Leopard -So num is a residential Experience, composed of several subordinate, non-residential sub-Experiences. It has one residence outside each village, plus several more outposts and sacrificial sites, all located at termite hills . As a cause of death it can attack directly by sending an actual leopard against one (an agent or mobile patrol). Omens presaging this involve clawing or nibbling sensations and include finding one's clothes eaten by termites . Another sign is finding oneself in a sudden silence in the midday heat in the tniddle of the jungle, surrounded by biting gnats. LeopardSonum's sub-Experiences draw on specific narrower aspects of the totality of leopardhood . Prickling sensations on the skin may be caused by Bena , the original mythical human leopard-victim. He is associated with Grandfather-Man (lojo -mar), a person who is very aged and therefore causes blindness. This is like the blindness of the panic one experiences on suddenly coming face to face with a leopard . Rumble-Tummy-Sonum is the agent responsible when one's tummy rumbles like a leopard's growl. Healings for these Leopard sub-Experiences involve chanting the names of all known victims of the village's local Leopard residence. The sacrifice is generally performed not at the site of this residence itself but at a sacrificial site which is usually a termite hill, in accordance with the imagery of nibbling. The shaman also uses a grass which resembles the leopard's furry tail, as well as other objects, tunes and words which reinforce the Leopard theme by contrast with those used for the other residential Experiences, at

the same time as und erlinin g the narrower di stinction s between th e various named Leopa rd sub-Experiences. The deceased Leopard -victim who is interested in you can thu s choose from a range of ways of impinging upon you , as is clear from the following (text 4.1):

Despite its often close reference to symptoms, then, a sub-Experience is much more than a label for a medical condition. Even such a trivial symptom as a rumbling tummy may be a reminder of a relationship between the patient and an earlier leopard-victim. Perhaps it should be seen as a label for a wider psychic condition and we should say that it is the sonum which helps to define, clarify or articulate the symptom. Everyone sometimes has tummy-rumbles, but only for Kantino and others in a similar position are they a phenomenon which is likely to be noticed and responded to as a sonum (and perhaps even for him, not on every occasion). In giving Kantino a tummy-rumble, his father is setting up or confirming an analogy between them. A minor condition like this is a tentative advance by a dead person towards the victim, the first potential

step toward s the la tter's own death. Ka ntino should avoid an esca la tion o f hi s relation ship with hi s fath e r within the leopa rd idiom. 6 A divining and healing shama n in tra nce, acting in the persona of DlIl'i- SIIII (Lumba go-Sonum).
-"



88 Dialogues 1I'ith the dead
death may strike at any time. But many conditions are chronic, flaring up at some times and lying dormant at others; and people's tolerance also seems to vary. On mornings during some slack seasons for work , when the climate and diet are good , life is easy and there is pl enty of time to chat, processions of families from all over the village converge on the string of dismi ssal sites along the main path leadin g out of Alinsing. There, independently of each other but within earshot, their shamans chant over them the different signature tunes of various sub-Experiences . But waiting for the monsoon in the sweltering heat of May and June, just as the thirst-quenching and nourishing wine-trees begin to fail , people must hack down tangled vegetation, burn branches, hoe and sow the hillsides from dawn till nightfall. My impression then was that people were exhausted and I was often very alarmed at the poor health of people close to me as they were laid low with repeated attacks of malaria and dysentery. Yet few healings were held: people were too busy, either to consider themselves ill or else to follow this through with a healing.
A condition in an Experience mode is thus not simply an 'illness' but also a mood: it is a reminder from someone one has known, made at a moment when one is disposed to acknowledge it. A person will be subject throughout his life to more or less forceful reminders from the many people w~om he h.as known and who are now dead. He will experience many mmor b.odlly disorders for which he does not bother to seek any explanatIOn. But those for which he does seek an explanation form a history of these relationships and their shifting moods. In some cases the patient will have a predisposition, like Kantino's for forms of LeopardSonum, based on inheritance, kinship, emotion, physical weakness, etc. In other cases he will unintentionally set up a rela tionship, e.g. by trespassing near the site ofa sonum. All of these are constant potentials, each of which may. only occasionally be actualised. Most are shrugged off after a healing sacrIfice; some are memorable as a serious crisis survived (as Doimani told me a bout a serious illness in her teens, soon after both her parents had died : 'My soul resisted - No!, I said, Don't take me! [purada-i1en asarre - aii11 agamlai. pangdonging.1'); and others, Jjke Jamano's illness in chapter 5,
become part of a long-running saga of chronic ill-health leading ultima tely to death .
Thus the thrust of the Sora word plrplr, a healing rite, does not seem to lie only in the notion of healing the patient. Certainly, the patient and his supporters hope that the undesirable condition will cease as a direct result of the rite, and often urge the sonums to make the patient well again (text 7.1). But the object of its verb, plr-, is not the patient but the sonum itself:
Int erpreting and persuading the dead 89
kUf{lS11l1 plrtai, 'I pi1'- Leopard -Sonum' , in the active, said by the shaman; or lonaSl111 plrtellai, 'I get som eone to plr- Leopard -Sonum for me', in the middle, sa id by Ka ntino when heca lls a sham an (for the gramm a r of verbs, see chapter 8) . This suggests that the mea ning of plr- also includes a sense of 'acknowledging' , and that of the noun pnplr is not only a ' hea ling' but also an 'ac knowledgement' . This accords with a stron g imagery of feeding and nourishin g sonums, expressed by words like posi- and 10- which are also used of feedin g domestic pets (and , dispara gingly , uxorilocal husbands). I shall from time to time remind the reader that the So ra word for 'healing' the patient also implies 'acknowledging' the claims of the perso n who is causing the illness.) Thi s is desirable in order to help to shift our attention away from our own dominant medical idiom. Sora practice is not an inadequate form of medicine, nor is it some sort of pre-medicine which ha s not yet succeeded in becoming fully medicalised or scientised. Its premises and aims are different. Each illness is a reminder of a relationship and each is temporarily satisfied, or blocked off, by the performance of an acknowledgement which addresses itself to that relationship. A person's involvement with sonums is not merely a medical history because it is a constantly developing exploration of aspects and possibilities in his own life. His person is defined largely in terms of other persons, just as, circularly, they are defined in terms of others including him. Through acting these relationships out in dialogue he develops parts of his own personality, seen as the potential for experience and action. His 'medical history' is thus at the same time a measure of his genealogical status (for example, a woman's Ancestors would be 'ashamed' to attack her husband since he has not incorporated them into his own Ancestors) and a reflection of his shifting emotional states. Indeed, it may be called the core of his biography. Within this mosaic of relationships, however, it is the relationship which brings about that person's death which will come to dominate all others as a final assessment of his life. The process by which this assessment is reached will be analysed in detail in the following chapter, when Jamano finally succumbs to his ill-health, dies, and is interrogated at his inquest about the causes of his death.
Speaking for oneself: formal debates and ordinary conversations The mutual involvement of Sora persons clearly has a more intense theoretical basis than anything most readers are used to. This involvement is especially evident in the sense of a person's mortality and the interplay of the living and the dead. The concept of 'acknowledging' implies use of the discursive medium of language. Language is of course not the only medium



90 Dialogues Illith the dead
through whjch Sora people exp ress and exp lore their involvement with their dead. T hey use their bod ies in ma ny ways to acknow ledge , reflect or resist thi s involvement as they dance, weep or la ugh. A sick person 's body, lik e his so ul , is represented by th at of an an ima l which is then separated from hjm when it is sacrificed. From th e stone-pl a ntin g onward s, as the deceased begin s hi s long journey towa rd s a partial triumph over hi s death , the li ving accompany every stage of hi s funeral with a vigoro us stamping dance, with its mixed con notation s of war dance, sexua l interco urse and regrowth . On these occasions, men, women and older children dance with babies and toddlers tied in slin gs on their hips . With their little faces pressed sid eways against the older person's bare flesh as they are jigged up a nd down , thi s must be one of the most vivid early memories of every Sora person . Ancestor-Women a nd Ancestor-Men , and shamans in trance, are even more involved with the dead . Each time they perform , they ' beco me' the dead and sha re their sensu a I depriva tion by fasting a nd avoiding sex beforehand. If they do not do this they risk being trapped , una ble to return from the Underworld. But language has a special place as a medium which can be allusive and discursive, synthetic and analytic. When one party to any relationship dies, it is their conversation which is isolated from the tangle of their various interpersonal contacts and developed over the years which follow. But this conversation does not simply take place, casually, like any other. It is set within a framework of rites performed by an array of specialists in highly formalised verse. Before presenting the dialogue in the next chapter, I should like to guide the reader's understa ndin g of it by contrasting it with two significant properties of the verse which surrounds it. For each of these properties, I shall argue that verse conventionalises the situation of the moment by emphasising what it ha s in common with other, similar occasions in the past; while the prose of conversation allows speakers to dwell on what is unique. The first property of verse is that it ca rries to its extreme a pervasive tendency in all Sora speech, a tendency to speak in grammatica lly parallel phrases or doublets: edate-gudate, ' he weeps he wails'. Sora call these doublets takud-ber, 'finishing-off speech' and they can encompass either single words or lengthy phrases.4 The reader will notice numerous examples of doublets in the conversations in later chapters, though these a re not easy to translate elegantly. 'Leave abandon that house that home,' says a woman to Sagalo's wife who is in the wrong place among the dead , ' that place that location, that seat that site, leave it abandon it' (chapter 7, line 286). 1amano is urged after his death to strengthen his so n's forcefulness in
interpreting and persuading the dead 9 1
public affa irs: 'If you put the word s into his mo uth if yo u pour the word s in to his mouth , he won't be fri ghtened he wo n' t be nervous', and himself boasts about hi s own record while a live: 'The Po lice Officer-in-C harge may come the Inspector may come, important men may come bi g shots may come ... my fa ce is as good as his my visage is as good as hi s, even though he knows G overnment language Oriya lang uage' (chapter 5, lines 155-6). Verse (which is a lways sung or chanted) is composed entirely of such doublets, as in the invocation sung by the shaman in cha pter I (text 1. 2):
cla sping hand s, grandmothers ... clasping feet , grandmothers ...
your monkey's four-footed walk, your ape's four-footed walk ,
grandmother grandmother
The use of doublets here goes beyond a conversational device and a mounts to a total technique. ' Hands' is paired with ' feet' , 'monkey' with 'ape', a nd so on. 5 Though at first sight such juxtaposition s seem to enrich their images, on closer examination it can be seen that they constrict the semantic field and the scope of implicit reference of each of their elements and their potential to combine in other, less predictable ways. Finishing-off speech bundles together everything it touches . In verse, there is not a single word which is not contained within a parallel phrase or clause, while in ordinary speech these pairings form only small nuggets within a flow of language which has a looser, more flexible overall texture. The second property of verse is the way it is used in long stanzas to summarise the positions of various persons in opposed or complementary roles. Dialogues are often antagonistic and their subject matter is often judicial. Later chapters will show how among other things they serve to resolve certain kinds of what one might call legal di sputes, for example over inheritance. At tense moments when speakers are particularly anxious to impose their point of view, the dialogues can turn briefly into simultaneous monologues in which people talk past each other (e.g. chapter 7, lines 171 and 285-6). At the stone-planting and the kalj a, the ordinary dialogue betwee n living and dead laymen in everyday langua ge is paralleled by an exchange in verse sung by specialists. The Ancestor-Men form into two teams. For several hours they mime, dance and sing a song which is called both kata, 'myth', 'story' or 'pa ntomime' and rudi, 'debate' or 'a rgument' . One team impersonates the deceased while the other impersonates a succession of the main living mourners who are also present in person nearby. They then sing



92 Dialog ues Il'ilh Ihe dead
their way through a series of exchanges between, say, a dead woman and her husband and the dead woman and her children; or else between a dead child and its mother, its father a nd its siblin gs. These exchanges resemble not so much dialogues as lengthy speeches made by each sid e in turn. Rlidi is a lso the usual word for an argument among the livin g. It is a judicial term which borrows its imagery from the courts in the towns and from the bisa/'a (from Oriya and ultimately Sa nskrit) , the now-defunct village council. For examp le, a /'udi sometimes breaks out in the street with an allegation of theft of buffalos, money, go ld ornaments or women. Bystanders soon line up on two sides and then mak e indi gnant speeches, so metimes listening to each other, but often haranguing each other simultaneously. A rudi , then, can be either a public slanging match between the living, or else a formalised debate between the livin g and the dead in which what each side ha s to say is pre-empted and sung by speciali sts. In either case, the interaction is limited by the adversa rial format of set speeches. In the sung exchanges, the verse performances have a rhythmic and musical flow which precludes the replies or interruptions which are the essence of conversa tion, and cannot respond very directly to the contingent reactions of addressee or bystanders. In the same way, when ordinary speakers become excited, their speech tends to fall into a wider pattern of doublets which does not invite the intercalation of responses moment by moment but serves as a verbal battering-ram. While the song of the Ancestor-Men offers a debate between positions, dialogue between the living and the dead offers a conversation between persons. Stripped of the accoutrements of the law court, of the lining up of parties across the street, of opposed monologues, the format ofan ordinary dialogue allows it to reflect the specificity of each situation. After the special singing of the shaman's invocation and her rauda's response, after the Ancestor-Men's sung debate, amid all these specialists' bodily techniques of trance and fasting, in front of the bounded, consecrated space laid out on the ground for offerings, comes the essential core of the occasion. But this is set in a language which is utterly ordinary and even banal. In talking with the dead , one speaks for oneself, in the language of one's own everyday life. Unless it were clear from the content or one had background information, it would be difficult from a transcript alone to know whether a given dialogue was between the living and the dead or entirely among the living. What marks off conversation from sung verse, then , is the fact that it is less tightly bundled into doublets . It therefore allows a more specific and flexible discussion and allows, even compels, living and dead laypersons to respond to each other directly. It thus offers a deeper possibility of
Inl e/'p/'eling and pe/'sllading Ihe dead 93
comp ro mi se or resolution and it s psychological impli cat io ns are much more far-reaching. The relationship between sung exchange and spoken dialogue can be illu strated from one typical occas ion . The team of Ancestor-Men representing the dead man at l'li s funeral were singin g to the team representing his widow. For a lon g time the song passed throu gh th e convent ional sentim ents which a recently deceased man might express towards his wife. But sudden ly the singers started to comment on the widow's tears: how come she was crying now, when only a short time before her hu sband's death she had been neglecting him and her children and meet ing anot her man secre tl y in the forest?
