At this point the widow ran away and hid , while her lover became very angry with the singers and threatened to beat them up. 'But since it was a kala wha t could he do (do kalan asen ian lume)?'. The affair, of course, had long been an open secret. These lines were a public warning that when the song was finished and the trance began, the wife and her lover would not be able to avoid discussing their affair with the dead man himself in the discursive, penetrating format of ordinary conversation, where no specialist could do their work for them. Starting with the next chapter, we shall see how such reproaches and embarrassments lie at the heart of dialogues between the living and the dead. The formal songs sung by the Ancestor-Men do no more than sketch in the outlines of your situation. They sing their piece without reference to you and then move off elsewhere; the shaman invokes her rauda and her own persona then disappears; the rauda replies with her promise to lead on the dead and then her persona too disappears from the shaman's vacant body. All the supporting formal structures of verse and music are withdrawn and you are finally left face to face with your sonums to speak with them on your own behalf. It is the stages of this encounter which we shall witness and interpret throughout the remainder of this book.



PART II
RESPONDING TO A NEW DEATH



A family lived in isolation in the jungle. One day the mother died a nd since he did not understand what death wa s, the father just abandoned her corpse. But every day when he left his children and went out to find food, her ghost (leu/man) returned and swept the house and cooked . When the husband came home in the evening he asked his children who had done this work. They replied that it was their mother, but he said 'Ah children, how can it be, don't say that, it makes me sad .' But since they insisted that this was so, he stayed behind one day to see for himself. His wife did indeed come and start to work. He was overwhelmed with joy and rushed up to her and threw his arms around her. But as soon as he clasped her, she turned to ashes in his embrace . Only then did he understand that he had to cremate her and plant a stone for her.



5
Transcription of a dialogue from the inquest on Jamano
The dialogues in this chapter constitute text 5.1.
Deteriorating relations between Jamano and Mengalu
This chapter will analyse the inquest held on the day after Jamano died, in which the dead man is interrogated about the causes of his own death. The inquest after death represents a special, more elaborate form of the divination which seeks to find out the cause of a living person's illness . I showed in the last chapter how the interpretation of bodily, more or less 'medical', states among the living is bound up with their thoughts and feelings about dead people. Though many states of malaise cannot be ignored, people also use interpretation in deciding how far they regard themselves as ill. For example, Kantino's susceptibility to RumbleTummy-Sonum is related to his readiness to ascribe meaning to his internal rumblings . I thereFore suggested that the nature of sonums at least partly creates the symptoms by which they make their mark. By giving form to bodily states, sonums turn them into expressions of personal relations. Though people may say they get better as a result ofa healing, the same dead person is likely to attack again on another occasion anyway, or even another dead person using the same Experience and similar symptoms. In addition, second opinions for the 'same' condition, as well as new diagnoses for what are perceived as new conditions, may suggest further relationships between the patient and other dead persons. Following the implica tions of the Soras' own vocabulary for their response to an illness, I suggested tha t as well as 'healings' of the sick person, such ri tes should also be understood as 'acknowledgements', since they are also a response to an initiative originating with the dead. We can relate this to decisive moments in a person's biography (always remembering that the meaning of this word stretches far beyond the span of



100 Dialogues lI'illi Ill e dead
hi s ' lifetime'). I have tried to represent a person's hi story of illness and dea th in fi g ure 5. 1. The diagram is cycli ca l and fall s into three circuits: a sick (asu) perso n who recove rs quickly ret urns around circuit A to a state of being ' hea lthy' (suka) ; so meo ne who is chroni ca lly ill (bill/bil1l) passes ro und circ uit B, as he goes from shaman to sham a n a nd healin g to healing; finally , whe n al l hea lin gs fail , everyone mu st eventually pass once around circuit C. The two points where divination is needed correspond respectively to the two traditions of sham a ns ske tch ed in chapter 3: the lesser divina tion -and-healing tradition of Mascara -Eyed Ridi , and the main funeral tradition of Pubic-Haired Sompa , which includes the inques t in this chapter. Ju st as the dia gnosis of an illness chooses between a range of sub-Experiences, so the verdict of an inquest invo lves makin g a choice of one among the range of residential Experiences and linking this to motivation among the previous victims of that Experience. But since the dead person will now become a causative agent for the form of his own death , the verdict tells the living not only what happened to him but also what to expect from him in the future. T hi s verdict ca n be comp lica ted by the elaborate socia l life which the dead lead amo ng themselves, visitin g each other at their variou s residenti a l sites, havin g a ffairs a nd marryin g each other , ge tting drunk , and gangin g up aga in st the livin g. The res ult is an extremely wid e sco pe for interpretation , since in principle, a ny person living or dea d cou ld conceivably be judged respo nsible directly or indirectly for an y illness or dea th in a nyone known to them. In practice, of course, dia gnosis is not so wide- ra ngin g and ce rta inly never random . In chapter 2 I introduced the rea der to Jamano , hi s son Ranatang, his brother Uda and his more di stant lineage-brother M enga lu with whom Jam a no had a lon g- running di spute over land . Their genea logica l relationsh ips were shown in fi gure 2.2. In February 1979 I return ed to Alinsing valley (see figure 4.3) after nea rly two years' absence . I wa s taken directly to the house of Ranatang in Tongseng, the offshoot of Alinsing founded on the other side of the valley by a segment of the Headman ]jneage of the main village itself. On that day, Ranatang was hosting a double name-giving for two infants aged about three . One of these was hi s own chi ld , the other the child of his unmarried sister, one of Jamano's daughters who had stayed at home as described at the end of chapter 2. I was warmly greeted by all present. Among the people who packed the house making preparations were Ranatang himself, a strong, handsome and self-confident young man ; his father's brother Uda, a divination shaman who had recently also become a funeral shaman after the death of the old lady who had previously done this work; and Ranatang's father Jamano himself, now a scrawny and very sick man, coughing frequently and with a welcoming smile which seemed already tired of this world. The only exception to this cheerful mood wa s Mengalu, who seemed surly and withdrawn and very different from his ebu llient self as I remembered him two years earlier, especia lly before I watched the death by sorcery of Sagalo's little brother being laid at his door. In particular, I noted at the time that Mengalu seemed to have yielded the role of leader of the lineage's Ancestor-Men to Mejeru , the only other man whose knowledge of the movements and words approached Mengalu's, a playful but modest and retiring man who I felt would not normally seek to lead the group. Quite early in the proceedings, Mengalu left and crossed back to Alinsing to sing in another rite there on behalf of his nephew Sagalo with whom he was now close again. Later I learned that Mengalu had not contributed grain towards that day's name-giving feast. These moves by Mengalu over the name-giving in Tongseng are not to be
.,



102 Dialog ues Il'ith th e dead
interpreted as a denial of his membership of th e Headman lin eage as such , for he remains a devoted exponent of its id entity in rites; rather, it is a de nial that he shares thi s lineage membership with Jamano and Ranatan g. The feeUn g wa s mutual , as wa s shown on a ni ght a few weeks later in A pril when Ranatang's ten-da y-old baby died. Thi s baby had been refu sing the breast since birth and had undergone healing rites for various sonums (mo stly sub-Experiences of the Sun such as her python) whi ch attack infants. I wa s sleeping in Ranatan g's house at the time . Normally when someone di es a tree is cut down as near as possible to the lineage's cremation site, which for the people of Tongseng is right across the valley in the main village of Alinsing . But on this occa sion a tree at the edge of Tongseng itself wa s cut down and this wa s done only by the men of Tongseng. They then dragged it by the light of flaming torches alon g a twi stin g path (the 'funeral route', a special lon g detour to confuse the ghost [Iwllllan] and prevent it from finding its way home), down over the boulders of the stream, and up again on the other bank to the lineage's cremation site on the edge of Alinsing. I believe that this was calculated to exclude the main part of the lineage living in Alinsing, since normally as soon as the guns are fired and the women of the bereaved household start their lament , every man who will himself be burned on the same site is expected to get up and join in felling the tree and building the pyre. As we stumbled across the stream-bed carrying the huge tree and the tiny corpse, Ranatang remarked to me that it was high time they had their own cremation site back in Tongseng. Many people in Tongseng repeated this sentiment, both throughout the time surrounding the baby's death and a few months later when Jamano himself died. It was only when we reached the cremation site that we met up with the people of the main part of the lineage from AUnsing, along with the pyre-lighters (siga) who work for the entire village. As for Mengalu, I do not remember if he was there and can find no reference to it in my notes. Certainly if he had not been, it would have been conspicuous. The following morning, when the pyre had died down, the AncestorWomen of the lineage cooled the soul of the dead baby by dousing the ashes with water contributed by every household of the lineage, and as usual buried them in a pit on the site of the cremation . There was no talk of Mengalu's house having failed to contribute water, but he was conspicuous by his absence later in the day at the abbreviated, simplified version of the stone-planting which is performed for unnamed babies (jumkub , 'feeding the ashes' or maybe 'eating the ashes'). Mengalu was reported (by Ranatang) to have refused to lead the lineage's Ancestor-Men in their chant at the baby's funeral , with the words

J amano's death and the first inquest at his crema tion It was against this background , in August 1979 , that Jamano died. Ha ving been ill for a long time, he had passed through a succession of differin g diagnoses and healings which had failed to improve his condition . I was absent at the time of his death and cremation, and so missed the first inquest the following day. This was conducted by the funeral shaman Kumbri, an old woman who had married into Alinsing from another village but was not closely connected with the Uneage of Jamano and Mengalu. When I returned a fortnight later just in time for the stoneplanting, I learned from gossip that the preliminary inquest had revealed the cause of death as Ra'tud -Sonu.n. " .s an Experience, this wa s consistent with his conspicuous symptom of coughing blood , but as with any Experience this left open the question of motivation. This, according to gossip, had been supplied by Mengalu ' s sorcery . 1cannot be sure how much this view was articulated within the preliminary inquest which I missed , conducted through the shaman Kumbri. But since the later, stone-planting inquest to be analysed below was conducted through the dead man 's brother Uda , 1 suspect that the first inquest will have introduced the theme of Mengalu's guilt less definitively than we shall now see it developed.

Ranata ng later produced the following examp les of Mengalu 's suspicious and unfeeling behaviour at the time of 1amano's cremation a nd first inq uest: a t the time of cutting wood for the pyre he was very slow in com ing to join the others; while they were sta nding beside the burning pyre he turned to a noth er man and sa id: 'Let's go and prepare some wine' (yirai, agadsa/enai) and 'seemed to be lau ghin g' (mang/e amrid) ; at the inquest, the other membe rs of the lineage came and 'spoke weepingly' (eda/e %l/g/eji) with the dead 1amano. Though Mengalu did at first attend he did not wait to speak to the dead man but went and slept in a nea rby house 'as thou gh he
wasn't sorry' (a'sil/tae all1rid).
Some of these could be interpretations of innocent behaviour by Ranatang's overwrought imagination , while some, like going off to lie alone elsewhere, could be the product of Mengalu's own intense nervousness. His behaviour at the best of times is histrionic and unpredictable, and he clearly reinforces people's suspicions about him by doing the wrong thing in a crisis. Other reports were circulating about actions and words of Mengalu's which, if he had indeed done and said them, were extremely foolish. Though he is just the kind of man who might take a perverse pleasure in playing with fire in this way, the tape-recorded conversations transcribed below suggest that they may equally well have been invented or heavily embellished. It was also being said that only a short while before 1amano's death Mengalu had placed his hand upon 1amano's head (not a normal gesture) and said 'When you die J shall sing your stone-planting for you'; and that earlier, at some stage of the quarrel over the land , he had said 'I'll bind you and later in the middle of the night you'll squawk like a chicken' (rajingtall1 do tiki amen kansim en amrid tungar dina kekerblj gam/e u/ete). This was taken as an unmistakeable threat of sorcery. More was involved, however, than personal animosity or even a dispute over a plot of land. We have seen how three months earlier, at the baby's death, there had been much talk among Ranatang's people about the need for a separate cremation site and stone-planting site - in other words, to split off from the main Headman lineage in Alinsing. 1amano's funeral afforded the opportunity for a first move in this direction. A strange innovative compromise was reached, which I had never seen before: 1amano was cremated at the usual cremation site in Alinsing and a stone was planted at the lineage stone-planting site. But on the day of the

T he second inquest at Jamano's stone-planting Among other things, this divination will confirm and amplify the verdict reached earlier in the first inquest, after Jamano's cremat ion. Just inside the doorway of the darkened , crowded house, two shamans are sitting side by side with their legs stretched out over the mortar. Kumbri conducted the earlier inquest alone but will today play only a minor role . Most of the sonums will speak through Uda , the dead man's younger brother. As the sounds of drums and oboes advance and retreat outside with groups of dancers, the shamans prepare for trance. For several minutes they sing in unison, invoking a chain of helping rauda sonums stretching back to Pubic-Haired Sompa. Then, still in unison , their bodies lock rigid and are unclenched by bystanders.
The raudas implicale Mengalu as sorcerer (lines 22-32):
Post-mortem dialogue embodies a quest by the living for certainty from the lips of the dead . The dead can speak to the living only after the shaman's main rauda-predecessor has spoken as mistress of ceremonies and se t the tone for the conversations to follow . As former shamans, raudas supposedly stand impartially above deception and vested interest. Hence they will say, 'whatever is hidden , whatever is kept silent, whatever is buried [we
shall reveal it]' (line 23) . Where a serious case of sorcery is involved, those who speak may include 110t only the dead, but also the disembodied soul (puradan) or consciousness of the sorcerer himself. Where, as so often, sorcery accusa tions run between men who are lineage brothers and therefore participate in each

other's fun era ls, th ere is a stron g connection between a ttendin g the series of inques ts alon g with everyone else and speakin g with the dead man , and demonstra tin g one's innoce nce. Thi s is supposed to be enoug h to preve nt one's own di sembodi ed soul from a ppearin g in the mouth of the shaman the only occas ion whe n a living con sciou sness ca n do thi s. On thi s occasio n, however, M engalu was not prese nt.

The ' person' who has spoken, und e r the pressure of bein g led by the ra uda , na mes him se lf as the ma n wh o m everyone a lread y suspects. H e deni es hi s g uilt , but in a fee ble way . The fa ct that he had been brought at a ll is highl y incrinlina tin g, while hi s de nial is hardl y forceful eno ugh to co unte rac t thi s. Indeed , give n the a uthori ty of the ra ud a , Me nga lu 's dil emm a a bout whe the r or no t to a tte nd see ms to ha ve no possible solution . So me people sa y tha t even if yo u do at tend , it is still possibl e for your soul to speak up throu gh the sham a n. In this case you then have to fi ght it out in public with thi s refraction of your own person a lity whi ch obstinately in sists on drawing su spicion to , or even confessin g, what you have come alon g in the flesh expressly to deny. Whatever M engalu may have believed about the workin gs of thi s, he could not brin g himse lf to attend on thi s occas ion. In the same way, at the death of little Mando ex actly two years earlie r, M e ngalu had not attend ed the inquest the following day but had sent his four wives and all their children to work in the hills for the entire day and disappeared him self too. By the second inquest on the dead child, on the day of the stone-plantin g itself, he did attend but his task had become much more difficult. It was only with the dialogue at the first karja the following spring that the sense of his guilt began to fade in people's gossip . It seems already inevitable that the ensuing drama should be acted out in terms of the hostility between Mengalu and 1amano. But this was not enough for a total interpretation of Jamano's death. As explained in chapter 4, a verdict is ultimately explained also in terms of an Experience and of an Ancestor who has shared this Experience. Both of these are additional to Mengalu's involvement and we shall be able to follow the speakers step by step as they esta blish connections between the roles of sorcerer and of the various dead persons with whom he has colluded.
Jamano arrives allrl is lesled/or a Irue slale 0/ consciousness (lines 33- 64): The above Lines were followed by the voice of 1amano's long-dead father, who denied having had any hand in his son's death , but suggested that 1amano's deceased wife Onsam was implicated , a point which will be developed later. He was followed by Jamano himself, speaking through Uda . Jamano stayed for two or three hours, during which he discussed several topics with implica tions for the future lives of his interlocutors. But I shall here present only the first ten minutes during which he discusses the immediate causes of his death. In harmony with the truthfulness of the raudas, the dead too should be



110 Dialoglles with the dead
placed in a state of consciousness where they will speak the truth and will neither indulge in 'deceit' (bllkai or jolda , common among attackin g sOl1ums) nor , as is more usual with the recently dead , suffer from ' false awareness' or ' lack of self-awareness' (ondrullg). Thus Jamano wi ll be told to ' speak out' , oblillga (Ii nes 33, 91 , 105) and to do so 'stra ight' , rojtad (35 , 93) , 'honest ly' , sa 'kai (38 , 93) , 'properly' , bangsale (94) ; while possible reasons for hesitation are spe lt out only in order to be dismissed: 'don ' t be afra id ' , batongdongal11 (33). In line 93 they urge him not to 'concea l' (soso -) or 'cover up' (daltad-) the truth because he may be reluctant to st ir up trouble between two branches of the lineage. Jamal1o's response to these urgings a re not taken for granted but are tested: the word for this test is tungjillg (42). From lines 38 to 64 inclu sive, every remark addressed to the dead man is concerned with this: question s requiring specific answers are fired at him one after another , and hi s first , early bid for co nfirmation and approval is cut short with a response equivalent to 'we ask the questions , not you' (39,40): they require far more of him before they will allow the atmosphere of interrogation to relax. At line 56 he tries to expand into anecdotal detail, but they are still interested only in the question of truth, and force him to keep to the point.

The interrogation is sojienedonce autlien ticity has been established (lines 65- 104):
The tone of the conversation now starts to cha nge . Up till now, in their anxiety to test the authenticity of hi s presence, Jamano's interlocutors have been ruthlessly insisting that he should tell them facts about his last hours of the sort which are already known and agreed by them. The first sig n of softening comes at line 67, where a voice finishes off his sentence for him



11 2 Dialogues Ivith the dead
with a piece of information which he then echoes as hi s next remark (68) . They are now prepared to help him by supplyin g the answers to their own questions and he is at la st allowed to make anecdota l detours and to lead the conversat ion himself (70). So the question in line 72 echoes Jamano's remark, the indi sputable fact that he vomited ; as a question , it a llows him to repeat himse lf, which prompts further details from the audience (74, 75, 77). Where these are cast in the form ofa question, they are more rheto ri ca l than interrogat ive, and the tone of susp icio n in lines 38 to 64 ha s been softened (75 , 77) . From now on , the living will begin to s upply the facts abnut hi s last movements, words and even feelings (87). The very same kind of ques tion which ea rlier had been used as a test (,Whom did you meet?', 'What did you say to each other?'), ha s now become a vehicle for the living to tell their own versions: the questions, where they are st ill interrogative, tend to beco me lead in g questions . Thus the woman who speaks lines 81,83, 85 and 87 has presumably already been given a purportedly verbatil1l account by Sangganen of what Jamano had said to her just before he died, and is determined to go over the details, with him in agreement. Where he may have been at a loss in line 86, the way in which she cuts in with line 87 suggests that they regard the work of the test as complete and that adequate verisimilitude has been established.

The verdict is developed in an atmosphere of shared consciousness between victim and mou/'l1ers (lines 105-27) :
This roasting imagery , in which the victim is skewered through the anus ' like a squirrel', is conventional for Ra 'tud. 1amano's sarcastic reference to an earlier, failed diagnosis helps to authenticate this, the true one. This verdict so far contains two elements: Ra'tud-Sonum, explaining the symptoms, and Mengalu's sorcery, explaining the motive. Each of these is now progressively developed and a link, at first tenuous, is forged between
them. At the same time, the interlocutors' earlier mood of interrogation is
The inqllesl 011 Jail/OlIO I 15
finally replaced by one of pity and compassion. Through the graphic ima gery of the ex peri ence of being killed by Ra 'tud-Sonum, we a lso see the beg inning of a subtle conflation between 1amano's s upposed perception of the event and that of hi s interlocutors, between the 'subjective' and 'object ive' perspectives. In line 56, 1amano first mentioned hi s own feelings of di stress in th e mo st un specific terms, then in line 71 , with the symptom of vomiting, which the leadin g question in line 72 showed to be the publicly accepted fact that he had vOlllited blood. When the topic of sy mptom s was take n up again, in line 87 , it wa s in th e terms with which 1amano was credited with having described hi s own inner feelings. The symptoms were not referred to again in external terms , but the image wa s immediately developed in hi s own word s into the unmistakeabl e imagery of Ra ' tud which , though experienced by him , is also accepted by everyone as part of the world which they all share: sujlillgji 'they're prodding me' (102, 104), pada/'Ie 'is racked ' (102), 1I101lingji 'they're roasting me' (104). Lines 109 If will show tha t from now on the thread of continuity from the objective vomiting to the subjective experiencing of Ra'tud has become complete and beyond doubt: 1amano's interlocutors, too, feel his agony to the extent of supplying gratuitous details themselves (109 , 111 - 13). This shared consciousness , as it may perhaps best be called, of victimhood, corresponds to the dynamics of the situation. With 1amano's death by Ra'tud, his relatives themselves become vulnerable as potential victims of his new-found Ra'tud mode of being. Meanwhile the theme of sorcery remains bound up with all of this, especially for Ranatang. There is a tension between his implicit certainty about the sorcerer's identity and his quest for confirmation of this certainty from his father. 1amano's dogged refusal to be explicit (see line 16) only stokes up Ranatang's rage as his questioning becomes increasingly frantic (91, 105, 123 , 126) .

Extract ./i'om tlie remainder o.l tlie dialogue: tlie scope o.l tlie final verdict (lilies 154--6) :
The dialogue at an inquest does not confine itse lf to the causes of death. At this point the conversation moved on to a succession of topics. First, another potential quarrel (and potential so rcery suspicion), involving so meone in the room , was aired and smoothed ove r. Then Jama no dwelled for some time on his own prominent status and spent a whole hour on a delailed tally of the money which people owed him , with the story behind each loan. These debts, which will now be administered by Ranatang, were thereby made public knowledge and accepted or disputed by his interlocutors. For example, Raduno, who did not have regular dealings with Jamano and did not attend, seemed amazed to hear from me afterwards that he owed money to the dead man; but much later he told me that he had since remembered an old debt of precisely the sum mentioned . Finally the conversation returned to the means of Jamano's death, but this time focussing on the hitherto obscure role of Mo'mo', who had acted as ringleader of the Ra'tud-People. Mengalu's sorcery and Mo'mo"s Ra'tud symptoms had by now both been strongly developed. But why was Mo'mo' interested in causing Jamano's death? It now turned out that he had acted on the instruction of Jamano's dead wife Onsam. The division of responsibility between these people and Mengalu is complex and throws great light on the kind of links which the Sora make between the phenomenology of illness and its interpretation in terms of human motivation . The diagnosis shou ld combine both a motive and a shared experience of a form of death . Mengalu's sorcery provides the motive, while Mo'mo"s role as Ra'tud ringleader explains the symptoms. However, the link between Mengalu and Mo'mo' remains unexplained. IfMo'mo' had had his own motive for killing Jamano by means of the symptoms at his command, the case would have been a simple one. But in many cases, the symptoms surrounding a death do not correspond with those at the command of the dead person whom for other reasons the deceased will name as the agent of death. In such cases, as with Jamano's wife here, it emerges that the person with the motive has acted through the agency of an Experience which she herself has not undergone and whose symptoms she



118 Dialogues Il'ith the dead
there fore does not comm a nd direc tly: she 'se ll s' (t enl-) the vIc tIm to a member of th e other E xpe ri ence or ' hires ' (balii -) the Ex peri ence- member to attack her intended victim. Similarly, when a death is motivated by a living sorcerer, he 'ensorce ll s' (abtaj-) a nd ' bind s' (jing-) the vi ctim by ma kin g a pact with a dea d person. T he latte r then acts through her own Experi ence or incites som eone else a mon g the dead to do so. Jama no 's case is thu s one of th e more elaborate ones , brin ging into play a chain of severa l links. By the end of th e afternoon the asse mbled compan y had established the followin g ex pl a nation , clarifyin g elements whic h I suspect had bee n partially aired in the preliminary inquest. M e nga lu had obtained Jamano's exuviae and with them performed actions and uttered word s of sorcery. These induced or encouraged Jamano 's dead wife , On sam , to act. Thou gh she resides in an Earth -Sonum , her marria ge with her hu sband ha d not bee n very clo se (t//1IY//II) , so that she wa s unable to ' take' him unaided to join her. In thi s context I was told by way of contrast about other elderly people in close marriages, especially men, who pine to death rapidly after losing a beloved spouse. This is often diagnosed at their inquests as the result of the action of the predeceased wife who is lonely in the Underworld. So the implication of the reference to Onsam seems to be that her husband did not miss her enough, and that she found a way to force him to join her. To do this, she 'sold' him to Mo'mo ' , who is her lineage brother and thus still close to her in the Underworld . Through the circumstances of his own death, Mo'mo' is one of the ringleaders of Ra 'tud-Sonum. Perhaps addi tionally motiva ted (I could not be sure of this) by the common tension between a woman's husband and her brother, he then led his Ra ' tud companions in their fatal assault on Jamano. The symptoms and motive for Jamano's death were thus joined in a chain containing several links. The primary agent among the dead , Mo ' mo ', is most plausible in terms of Experience but least motivated . He is incited to act by Onsam , who thou gh not plausible in terms of Experience has a t least some degree of motiva tion . Finally, the impetus ori gina tes with a sorce rer , Mengalu , who since he is alive is outside the idiom of Experience but whose motive is very strong indeed. The motive of the first is superseded in explanatory power by that of the second , which in turn is superseded by that of the third. The verdict of Ra'tud-Sonum amounts to more than the mere fact of Jamano's membership ofRa 'tud , which is after all a very common cause of death . It takes account of many elements and amounts to a summary of his life and works. It acknowledges a lack of passion in his marri a ge, to the extent that his dead wife turns to her own lineage-brother for support.

All in all , the dead man and his living interlocutors have worked to gether to build up an intimate and subtle portrait of a personality, derived from the sum of his social interactions. Precisely because it is publicly composed , this portrait reaches into the future and so serves as a vehicle for working out the complex Sora idea s about continuity between persons. Ranatang is well equipped temperamentally to inherit not only Jamano's property and loans, but also his role as leader and protector. In such a strong household , too, the sisters and daughters are likely to continue to be reluctant to leave home for marriage, and will thus continue to contribute to its further prosperity. Uda , in my judgement a weaker personality, will now ride on



120 Dialogues with the dead
Ranatang's back as he previously did on Jamano's , but will be a stronger asset with his role as a funeral shaman now firmly estab li shed. He has long practised as a divination shaman and has only recently acquired the different set of raudas who now enable him to sit at a funeral. This is the first time he has actually performed. Several factors suggest why he may have become a funeral shaman and done so at this moment. His settlement, Tongseng, was aiming to become independent of the main village of Alinsing and every village should have its own funeral shaman; the on ly other funeral shaman in that settlement (an in-marrying old woman) had died recently; and Uda was particularly concerned about the interpretation of his brother's death , which had been expected for a lon g time. Meanwhile, Mengalu's tragedy continues to unfold as the consequences of an early moral error push him yet one step further a long the scapegoat's path to perdition. When T revisited him in 1984, Mengalu was seriously ill and people were saying behind his back that his sorcery sonums had turned on him because he could not feed them with an adequate stream of victims (yil'tudle, 'the fire has turned on him '). Every time tha t Jamano is diagnosed as the cause of a sickness among his descendants, it will be a reminder of any or all of these elements of his own biography and in the biographies of those around him.
6
Redeeming the dead and protecting the living
Jamano's funeral continued: cosmic rescue by his Ancestors At the conclusion of the divination which took place on the day of Jamano's stone-planting, a verdict was reached regarding the relationship between the deceased and the ultimate motivator of the attack, incorporating on the way all those through whom this relationship was considered to pass. The terms in which the dialogue is cast have differentiated Jamano into two aspects. In the first of these, he is a member of an Experience, in the second, an Ancestor to the descendants who perform his funeral. He has 'become Ra'tud' (Ra'tuden gaddle) yet he remains father, grandfather, husband or uncle to those he left behind. The former aspect is necessa rily unpleasant for him and hostile and aggressive to us; the latter is relatively pleasant for him and benign towards us. What does this hard -won distinction mean for those left a live? The present chapter will begin the examination of how his dual nature moulds future relations between the dead man and his survivors. It will become apparent that the long-term aim is to make him into a pure Ancestor and thereby eliminate entirely his Experience aspect. But the reader who remembers the discussion in chapter 4 of the mutual incompatibility of Ancestor space and Experience space will not be surprised to be told that this cannot be accomplished in one step. Establishing the causes of death is only the first of several moves made with Jamano's future condition in mind. Apart from the inquest and the actua l planting of the stone, a number of other important acts were performed on the day of his stone-planting. Twenty-one buffalos were sacrificed by various lineage members and affines, an unusually large number which reflected Jamano's importance; for severa l hours the Ancestor-Men formed two teams and sang a kata dialogue representing
121



122 Dialog ues lI'illi Ill e dead
respec tively the dea d Jamano a nd a success ion of livin g interl ocutors (chapte r 4: it wa s durin g thi s that Menga lu burst inlo tears) ; on eight occas ion s they laid out (ol/g-) variou s meanin g-laden objects and p lants indoors over the mortar or on the gro und outs ide and subjected them to a slo w monotone chant which I shall ca ll the ir song of redempt ion; and finally when th e buffa los were killed ce rta in cuts were served with rice in a feast while othe rs were exchanged o r returned to their donors to be take n home. Desp ite the tensions amo ng the central characters, those peopl e who were less close ly invo lved ate, drank a nd danced a lot and had a good time as a lways a t fun e ral s. So me of them p robab ly flirted and took a step towards future mar ri ages. For Jamano him se lf, the day was a c rucial rite of transition. Eve n whil e the inquest was go ing o n in side the ho use, o lltsid e the Ancestor-Men were struggling to free him from his membe rship of Ra ' tud -Sonum. The Sora word for this resc uin g is landi-. This is a n act ive, transitive verb and its object is not the Experi ence Ra ' tud but the dead person. The Experiences exist as given categories, as their mappi ng across the land sca pe shows: wha t matters is where people move among them. The root l(fl/- mean s 'change', while the mea ning of the root di- is uncerta in ; the word l(fndi- is used in a doublet with uJ'{/i-, in which the root ur- mea ns ' un tie '. The overall semantic c1ima te of the pair is thus clearly one of transfonna tion a nd relea se of the dead person. For reasons to be given in the co mmentary to lines 40- 6 below, I sha ll translate landi- as ' redeem'. Thi s redemption is effected by the words chanted by the Ancestor-Men in their solemn monotone. They sing in the collective person a of a ll the Ancestors of the entire linea ge and this is the only kind of song I have seen where the singers fuss about correct and careful performance. I shall give extracts frqm two of the eig ht such cha nts which as one of Jamano's Ancestor-Men I took part in sin gin g on that day . The first (text 6. 1) is in the persona of the ma le Ancestors, while the second (text 6.2) , which gives some additional imagery, is in the persona of the female Ancestors. Some poetic words have been left as untra nslata ble since my Sora friends themselves were unable to gloss them . Eve n more than with the prose texts in other chapters, one could here comment endless ly on obscure ima ge ry. However, I sha ll keep comments to the minimum necessary for a reasonable degree of comprehension in passing or in order to draw attention to points which will be taken up elsew here.

To what extent has the dead man been changed on the day of his stone-planting? At first sight the answer seems paradoxical. On the one hand, by words like these, sung throughout the day, he has been not just redeemed, but transformed. On the other hand, as with any Sora transition, much of the change which has taken place is not definitive, but reversible: despite his redemption, he will continue to afflict the living with the form of his own suffering. How is this so? We can perhaps approach this by first considering another, relatively simple aspect of today's transformation, one which by now is indeed irreversible. The two stages of the inquest on Jamano show the importance of the verbal articulacy of the deceased. Even on the day of the first inquest, but certainly from today, the day of the stone-planting, Jamano is no longer a ghost (fatlman). The story at the head of part II says that the dead woman remained a ghost until her husband understood the need for a proper funeral procedure of cremation and stone-planting. But she was unable to tell him this for herself. Fresh from the shock of dying, coated with ash from the pyre, a ghost does not understand what has happened to her and continues to return home where
Redeeming the dead alld protecting the living 129
she can sometimes be heard rummaging around the cooking pots looking for food. Out of doors, too , her rela tives sometimes catch glimpses of her in the forest. It is their sad duty to explain to her that she no longer belongs in her own home, or at least not in this form. Her confusion is associated by the Sora wi th the ondrung (false awareness) for which the last chapter shows Jamano was still being tested at his stone-planting inquest. The significance of the ghost stage becomes clearer from the way a ghost interacts with the living. Ghosts have bloodshot eyes which are not at the fron t bu tat the back of their heads, so tha t if you suspect tha t the person you have met is a ghost, the way to find out is to lift up their hair at the back and see if the real eyes are underneath . They can cause fits by 'swallowing up' (molesid-) people, and children in particular are terrified of walking past a cremation site in the days immediately following a cremation. The fits which a ghost causes can be treated, not through the ela bora te verbal format of a healing rite, but at best only with plant medicines. A ghost is terrifying and dangerous precisely because it is inarticulate and incapable of negotiation through dialogue. Perhaps Jamano had already ceased being a ghost when the first inquest was held the morning after he was cremated. Certainly, the inquest presupposes a high degree of articulacy, so that people sometimes say that the ghost state ceases immediately on cremation and that a dead person is incorporated from that moment into the Experience sonum responsible for his death. But at the first inquest there are some indications that the deceased is in some respects still a ghost: there is no dancing to accompany the drumming, and when he arrives he is given only water to drink (see chapter 8), rather than wine as befits a proper sonum. These uncertainties have been resolved by the day of his stone-planting. So today's song is more a declara tion of intent than a fully accomplished action. Rather than setting the seal on his transformation, the song of redemption initiates new possibilities in the evolution of the deceased and in living people's relationships with him. Ifwe consider how this had earlier worked for the dead people who caused Jamano's death, we see that they have long ceased to be ghosts . But despite the transformations and redemptions to which they were subjected at their own funerals, Onsam and Mo'mo' were still able and willing to use an Experience to cause Jamano's death. This will now be equally the case with Jamano's own potential to cause illness and death in others. For all the confident and assertive tone of the Ancestor-Men's chant, the transition which they aim to effect remains painfully provisional, sometimes for several years. This leaves the living constantly vulnerable. The newly-redeemed state of the



130 Dia/ogues Il'ilh Ihe dead
deceased is very precarious and he often returns to his original death Exper ience. T hi s is known because he is revealed in diagnoses for hi s sick descendants as having attacked them in this form . The word for doing this isgorod-, which is also the ordinary word for ' wander'. In the context of the movement of the dead between two cosmo logica l locations, the Sora illustrate this by a smooth waving of the hand from side to side, as though the word a lso meant ' oscillate'. This is perhaps the closest So ra comes to expressing in a sin gle word the paradox of the simultaneou s existence of a dead person in two incompatibl e places. In the context ofsonums, the verb gol'Oc/- cove rs the ent ire field of moveme nt by which so nums defy the wishes of the living. One ca n say equally tha t 'Jamano wanders [between states), (JaI11C/1/O gor odl e) or that 'Ra'tud -So num wanders [from one victim to th e next]' (Ra'lud-stlll gorodl e).
Let us first exa mine the latter usa ge, which shows the dynamic force of the state in which the dead person is caught up. This usage amounts to saying that the Experience sonums are infectious or contagious, in that they perpetuate themselves on to new victims. This is said not so much of Earth and Ra'tud as of the rarer or more shocking forms of death - Leopard, Convulsion, Sun (i.e. suicide and accidental death) , Smallpox and Sorcery (the last one is common, though it acts in collaboration with one of the others). These, the more acutely contagious Experience sonums, are capable of moving relentlessly from one member ofa family to another and often among friends, acquaintances and lovers. The contagion 'enters' (gclI1 -) the body of a potential victim and must be removed by a shaman. Sometimes, though not always, it takes what one might call a material or quasi-material form (anoki). For example, for Leopard and Sorcery, this is usually a black hairy caterpillar which people can see being extracted during healings; for Sun it is the blood of the previous victim seeking to enter a body, or sometimes jagged splinters of pig bone; while for Smallpox it is the speck led (rige) seeds of the gra m plant (rogo) which SmallpoxWoman (Ruga-boj) flings against the victim's body to sow them , where they sprout as pustules. However, Sora usage does not recognise a distinction between the 'material' and the 'immaterial'. Their imagery is sensory : whatever the cause, whether more orIess contagious, you may 'feel' (1/1'1 -) a 'power' (renabli) working upon yourself just as well as you can feel a caterpillar, as the attacking sonum 'strokes' (Sl/l1-) you as a preliminary to 'seizing' (Fiam-) or 'ta king' (pang-) you. This may be accompanied by a dramatic narrative experience, such as Jamano's of being pounced on and beaten up . Similarly, when someone lies seriously ill he is exhorted, ' If they're offering you food [i.e. of the dead], don't take it, don't eat it!' (cf.
Redeelllillg Ih e dead alld proleclillg Ih e lil'illg 131
chapter 5, lin e 77) and ifhe recovers he may recount his struggle to resi st the temptation to eat. Such dangerous, contagious movement shou ld be prevented. So parallel to the stone-plaliting with its redemption of the deceased , a rite may be performed to ' block' the Experience from perpetuating itse lf on to the living. This is not done for the majority of act ual deaths (which are ca used by Ra ' tud and Eart h) but only for those which occur through an Experience which is highly contagious, the insta nces of which (apart from the admixture of sorcery) are not numerically frequent. The word for this , dallg-, again a spat ial word , is that co mmonly used of obstructing a path , damming a strea m , etc. Here its gra mm at ica l object is not the deceased, as
8 Washing in pig's blood to block the co nt agion of an accidental death. Until thi s is done, the entire village is at ri sk. A pig has been dragged out of th e village and eve ryone now drinks the raw blood and pours it over their bodies .



13 2 Dialogues Ivith th e dead
in redeemin g, but the Experience itse lf. We may note the simila rity between these blockin g rites and the fending offin rites of healin g. In both cases, the dangerou s so num is led out of the house and discharged into Experience space. A hea ling rite is performed each time a person is stroked by a sonum, in order to forestall hi s being taken. A blocking rite is done once only , as a pre-emptive move immediately after a death . In anticipation of any future attacks, it covers the full range of potential livin g victims. For example, a dea th by Sun or Smallpox threatens a whole village, one by Leopard, Convulsion or Sorcery usually only the hou sehold and other intimates of the deceased. But the sense in which an Experience 'wanders' is a limited one. Ultima tely , Experiences act only through personal agents. The reason why an illness wa nders between people is that the deceased himself ha s wandered from his state of Ancestorhood back to his death -Ex perience: 'Jamano wanders', Jal11ano gorodte. The emphasis here is not on the property of contagiousness, which to a greater or lesser degree characterises all the Experiences since all are perpetuated and repeated . Rather, it is on the instability of the deceased person 's own redeemed state. This instability is regressive, so that the best translation of this second use of gorod- might be 'revert'. The Ancestor-Men have clearly stated the dead person's proper and desirable destination, yet the deceased has great difficulty in reaching it and staying there.
Social order among the dead: Earth-Sonum as a transitional stage between Experience and Ancestor Jamano remains confused, or perhaps uncommitted, regarding the transformation which has been wrought upon him today. In ontological terms, he seems to find it difficult to be changed from one kind of sonum to another and to hold himself in that new state because of the complexity of this transformation . Cosmologically, this uncertainty in his state of mind corresponds to the extent of the gulf between his starting point and his destination. The Sora express movement across this gulf as a journey over cosmological space, but the ultimate nature of his transforma tion is a social one. The Experience which killed Jamano sundered him from his kin . As will become increasingly clear in later chapters, the process of redemption in the funeral cycle undoes the work of the Experience only in order to reconstitute in the Underworld a continuation of the social order which existed among the living. In rescuing Jamano, the Ancestors are substituting themselves for the attacker. Just as the Ra ' tud-Men (Ra'tud-marenji), led by Mo 'mo ', had earlier taken Jamano into their own Experience, so the Ancestors now take
f
Redeeming th e dead and protecting the living 133
him into their own co llectivity. Regardless of the various forms of their ow n deaths , they are in this context Exper ience-free. Thus in the redemp tion so ng sun g in the persona of Jamano 's female Ances tors, his wife Onsam was listed even ' though she had incited her brother Mo'mo' to kill him . Sim il arly, the attackers' ringleader Mo'mo' would nonetheless have been en umerated among Jamano's Ancestors if he had been a member of the deceased's lineage (a s Mo'mo' just happens not to be). For them, as now for Jamano him se lf, the choice between being an Ancestor and being a member of a n Experience is only a choice between modes of being: each dead person can ex ist in different modes at different moments. Jamano is thus subject to two kinds of agency, each of which is linked to two quite different groups of persons. These two groups are analogous, both in their power of agency and in the fact that their members have their own mutual relationships and hi sto rical reasons for being with eac h other. But the mem bership of these two groups does not necessarily overlap and their inner social texture is quite different: the Experience group where Jamano was lonely among wretched company, and the comforting society of his rescuing Ancestors. The Ancestors, on whose behalf I sang along with three or four other Ancestor-Men , represent the lineage with its constant reservoir of male names which we listed exhaustively at each stage of the funeral sequence. Within this overall list, the ordering of names in the chant reflects the current articulation of the lineage into branches and twigs . These names are eventually returned to living descendants and their original holders die a second death and disappear. An Experience sonum, on the other hand, contains a number of people who have shared a similar form of death. These, too, are articulated into chains of predecessors and successors, according to the stories behind each person's recruitment. The current attacker at any moment is one of the most recent recruits who acts as a ringleader while the earlier victims support him as shadow-men. Over time, as the latest victims become ringleaders themselves, the oldest shadow-men eventually disappear without trace just like the oldest Ancestors. So in theory , if an observer returned at any time in the future, the residential Experiences would be the same except that the particular people involved (that is, the lists of names) would be quite different. Unlike a lineage, an Experience's list of names is non-repeatable because it contains an agglomeration of persons whose coming together is partly contingent. The residential Experience is thus a self-perpetuating corporate group like the lineage, except that rather than begetting new members, it recruits them on the grounds of a shared form of death . The Experiences are a crude perversion or parody of the lineages which they perpetually strive to subvert. Redemption aims to restore in the

realm of po st-mortem existence some thing o f the fin e-gra ined tex ture of socia l rela tions a bove ground . T he lea p betwee n the two modes of bein g a nd betwee n th e two kind s o f social group comes a bout throug h the interm ediary category or Ea rth So num. T hi s will require so me prelimina ry exa min a tion here, since thi s ca tegory will becolll e more and more promin ent in later cha pters. On the one ha nd , Ea rth -Sonum is an Ex peri ence cau sin g illness a nd death , jus t like Sun-Sonum , Leopa rd -Sonulll or Ra' tud -Sonulll. On the o ther ha nd , it brings togeth er groups of pa trilineal kin sfolk after their redemption , in a way which Illakes it close to the concept of A ncestorhood . It is in this sense of Earth -S on um tha t the dead nourish th eir descenda nts throu gh the soul-force which they put into growin g crops. There are thu s two ways of coming to resid e in Earth -S onul1l. One way is to die with appropriate symptoms, which for reason s to be ex plained below usually involve a swelling of the body or a blocka ge of its orifices. Thi s happen s only to some people. The othe r way, wl'lich ha ppen s to everyone , is to be led there by one's Ancestors as part of one's redemption. I Earth-Sonum has several properties which make possible this dual role. In the first place, it lies at the lowest point in the cosmos of any Experience and is the nearest to the Underworld where the dead reside in their capacity as Ancestors . It is thus the Experience furthest removed from the Sun and , indeed , it explicitly reverses the Sun's imagery of amorphousness and molten ma terial. The imagery of Earth-Sonum encompasses coolness, wholeness, integrity a nd the retention and concentration of form , an imagery which can be applied to both the person and the group . But to reside in Earth-Sonum is still not fully equivalent to being in the state of Ancestorhood. Earth-Sonum can also be an Experience causing illness and death. The swellings and blockages which it causes, such as constipation or death in childbirth , are part of that very imagery of wholeness and retention. Moreover, the groups of persons whom it reuni tes after dea th are not the entire lineages of Ancestors which were enumerated in chapter 6: there are many different Earth-Sonum sites spread out across the landscape and each one of them brings together only one of the many disjointed fragments of the lineage. So just as there are oppositions between kin-groups, so the hig hly articulated internal structure of the category Earth-Sonum contains within itself the potential for opposition and conflict between different exemplars of the category. Nonetheless, residence in Earth-Sonum is an essential staging-post on the journey to full Ancestorhood. This can be clearly seen if we contrast it to its polar opposite in cosmological terms , Sun-Sonum. In figure 6.1 I have



136 Dialogues with the dead
tried to show the full vertical range of so num space. It develops fi gure 4. 1, which showed how Ancestor space reaches from the village downwards while Experience space exte nd s from the jungle upwa rd s. Apart from th e Sun , a ll other Experience sites lie horizonta lly on the same level as we ourselves live (cf. fi gure 4.3). Some of these a re sli ghtly raised in rocks or trees, while others, such as Ra'tud sites on paths and cro ssroads, usually lie flat on the ground . The Eart h-So num sites, located in the springs and water so urces which a llow huma n settlement and cultiva tion , are the only sites which dip below ground level. They lie at the furthest ex treme from the Sun but reach down towards the Underworld. Jamano 's rescue from his Ra ' tud site involves a downward movement towards the Underworld a nd is just one phase of the downward journey made by all person s during the course of their existence, from th e Sun to the Underworld . This progression moves through three levels and stages: in the Sun before birth, on the ground durin g life, and underground after death . The polar opposition between Earth-Sonum and Sun-Sonum is clearly reflected in their imagery. Earth-Sonum consolidates and concentrates a person's being. Instead of the Sun's dissolution and amorphousness, Earth-Sonum builds on the imagery of integrity and wholeness. The location ofEarth-Sonum in springs is associated with coolness rather than heat, water as opposed to fire . Instead of Sun-Sonum's dirt and bodily leavings (chapter 3), Earth-Sonum emphasises cleanliness through the affirmation of the body's outline during washing. This was done for the first time at birth with turmeric (a cooling 'Earth' medicine) and remains an important, enjoyable and sociable operation carried out every day of one's life. Extending this imagery, we can note that unlike the low Blacksmith castes of the Sun, when Earth-People are conceived as Hindus they belong to the ksatriya Paik caste (in Sora, Aleng or Yoi) , whose women wash frequently , 'a lways look freshly groomed ' a nd a re bedecked with flowers in their hair. There is a similar contrast between Earth-Sonum and Sun-Sonum as causes of illness and dea th . As was explained in chapter 3, the foetus is forged in the heat of the Sun's smithy but must be cooled when it enters the realm of the living in order to separate it fully from the microcosm of Sun-Woman's forge in the womb of its mother. Sun-Woman's python retrieves the baby if this separation is inadequate. Throughout life, Sun illnesses cluster round a set of sub-Experiences to do with scorching and melting, such as open sores, while the Sun is also the residence for the victims of accidents, murder or suicide, as well as for blacksmiths and
Redeelllillg the dead and protecting the living 137
ma lformed persons such as the dumb and the lame (see chapter 4 to understand ho w for the Sora verbal articul acy is as important as wholeness of body for a fullne ss of being in the broadest sense) . All of these death s are associated with 'heat, uncerta inty of form or lack of articulacy. At the moment of dea th a perso n passes once more tlHo ugh a heat which di sso lves and reorders hi s person. It seems to me significa nt that, just as a baby is born unab le to speak, so the fire of the fune ra l pyre once aga in disorients the fres hl y deceased person and produces the in a rticulacy o f the ghost (/([{ll1Ian) a nd the confusion of ondrung (,false awareness').2 A person who dies a Slln -dea th reverses the norm a l downward trajectory and the gulf which opens up between hi s redeemed and unredeemed states is the wides t possibl e. This is reflected in the special procedures for a Slln-victim's stone-planting. The A ncesto r-Men 's chant of redemption is not enough to rescue him. They also create a path across the open space between earth and sky by firing a stream of a rrows into the sky to create a ladder. M ea nwhile , indoors, the funeral shaman ties a thread from the pitch of the roof to the mortar beneath and fastens a metal bracelet or swo rd to the bottom of this thread . Sitting on the mortar for her trance, she then coaxes the deceased down the thread, through the metal object, and into her body. An Ancestor-Woman sets the thread alight a t the bottom so that it burns upwards. Sora say that this destroys the dead person's return pa th and leaves him the path through the mortar to the Underworld as the only available route out of the house. As a cause of illness and death, Earth-Sonum's imagery of wholeness a nd retention explicitly reverses this imagery of dissolution and release. The sy mptoms of an Earth attack are the blockage of bodily orifices and the co nstriction of bodily emissions: a general swelling or bloating (pungpung) of the body, especially of the stomach or, for men , the scrotum; the ina bility to urinate or the pa ssing of blood in urine; constipation ; and for women, death in childbirth . Attractive male oboists who play with their puffed cheeks near springs are also susceptible to rape by female Earth-Sonum
dwellers. I shall give some examples of deaths caused directly by Earth -Sonum. The persons involved are all close relations ofMengalu and are shown on a genealogy in figure 6.2, where they are numbered for ease of reference . Though I shall refer to some of these people later, it is not necessary for the reader to note their identi ties in detail since what matters here is simply the sense of a chain of recruitment, analogous to the generations of a lineage, which is common to all the Experience sonums. Some victims of Hal/all' Water (Bungsengdan) Ea,.th-SOIl1l11l
Rumbana (I), a middle-aged man, bought the land containing thi s site from someone in another village and started to work it. He habitu a lly tethered his ca ttle near the spring and their dung and urine seeped into the water. As a consequence he died of an inflated scrotum caused by the insulted Earth-Sonum.
He was the first victim in Alinsing. Soon afterwards, his fellow lineage-member Bumbuden died , also in middl e-age. Bumbuden (5) did not own or work the site but often herded his cattle on a nearby hillside . Being thirsty there, he stole the wine from trees belonging to another village as it hung in pots waiting to be collected. So the men of that village put some earth from his footprints into a pot with some sorcery-medicine and sealed up the pot's mouth, thereby sealing in his breath, urine and faeces . 'It was as if they had tied up his penis and plugged up his anus' (a lo 'ojenjile}i amrid do a sambin tlldable}i amrid). In the inquest it emerged that they had hired (bail'i-) his recently-deceased lineage-brother Rumbana to produce these Earth symptoms and that Rumbana had then taken him to reside with him in Hollow Water Earth site.

On one occasion while I was there, Mengalu suffered pain in urinating. This was diagnosed as being caused by Ra'gi and he recovered after getting a shaman to lead her out of his house in a banishing-rite along the path towards Rere Earth site. It was the association of excretion with childbirth which allowed Mengalu's urinary problem to be explained by the action of his predeceased sister.
