Informants explained this and similar texts by saying that an EarthSonum blocks the birth of a child by pushing the baby back up the birth canal to prevent its emergence. This imagery contrasts strikingly with that of the Sun who as blacksmith-embryologist creates the conditions of heat whereby cores split, boundaries melt and one entity can separate out into two . In a normal birth the sealing of boundaries is achieved after the birth by washing and applying cool medicines and songs to both mother and child separately. Here , by contrast, the sealing seems to have been applied in advance to the mother-plus-child as a unit in order to prevent the birth.
Redeeming the dead {lnd protecting the lil'ing 14 1
The Ancestors residin g in the Ea rth -Sonum ca n be hostile to such bodily sepa ration s and seek to repress them. The residents of an Earth -Sonum will never attack the child bea ring wives of the men who cultivate its site, that is, the wives of their own kinsmen. They attack only their daughters and sisters, that is, women who were born into their own kin-group but who are noW producing babies on behalf of other lineages . A woman 's natal Ea rth -Sonum is at its most virulent during her first la bour, when the transfer of her loyalty to another lineage is a t its most delicate and provisional. Where they block this labour , her Ancestors seem to be fi ghting to prese rve the integrity of their own group through the analogy of the body of their daughter. The reader may remember the impulse of the male-centred household to accumulate wives and retain sisters and that thi s desire is fulfilled only slightly in life above ground but wholly in the shamans' incestuous marriages in the Underworld (chapter 3) . As part ofa woman's gradual separation from her father's house, the physical bond between herself and her chi ld changes into a lineal bond between the child and its father. The mother produces the child through the Sun's heat, while the father then converts this act of physical division into one of generational succession for himself and his group. He does this by means of grain containing something of his own Ancestors (and chapter 3 showed how a wife's natal kin often fight back in the same idiom , by continuing to give her some of their own grain). A person who is ' in' an Earth site is also in the 'soul' (puradan) of all grain which grows out of that Earth. That person enters (gclI1 -) his descendants' house every time a harvest is taken in from the threshing floor and stored in the loft. Like previous Ancestors, then, 1amano has now 'become' (gach/-) the grain of his descendants . This grain will be pounded in the mortar, cooked on the hearth and swallowed daily in each of their houses. At his stone-planting, the words of the Ancestor-Men at which Mengalu broke down in tears were (sung in 1amano's persona):
I have become lineage food I have become lineage grain I have become Underworld-journey rice
I have become branch food I have become branch grain I have become straight-path rice
1amano's redemption, then , has two stages or aspects. In the first place, he is rescued from his Experience, Ra'tud-Sonum, and led into the state of Ancestorhood. There, he will be united with all the Ancestors of his entire lineage. But in the second place and partly overlapping with this, as an Ancestor he resides in one Earth site among the several which are available to him, as a member of that Earth-Sonum. Every spring and water source contains a named Earth-Sonum which



142 Dialogues with the dead
accumulates groups of Ancestors associated with living persons who cu ltivate land near that site. To say that someone is in Eart h-So num is to invite the question 'Which one?', meaning 'Wh ich particular spring, which specific exemplar of the overall category?' Each of these sites represents rights to a range of pieces of land which lie in the site's vicinity or are watered from it. This may be the right either to return in later years to the same shifting-cultivation plot or else to convert the plot into terraced paddy-fields. Named Earth sites are thus relevant to the direct heirs of the man who first clears or claims a plot. This is why even among those few close relations of Mengalu in figure 6.2 above, we can already see two separate Earth-Sonums at work, Hollow Water and Rere. The song for the rite (pl/plr) which acknowledged Ra 'gi and healed Mengalu mentions two more and later chapters will show that there are several others within his branch of his lineage, for example Sweet Mango. The total range of a lineage's Earth sites accounts residentially for all its Ancestors and yields a topography of the Underworld which is probably unique to that lineage. Earth-Sonums, then, contain only fragments ofpatrilineages. So despite its imagery of wholeness and integrity, the category of Earth-Sonum is founded on differentiation and the potential for conflict. Its wholeness is still only partial because the dead make choices between Earth sites which can be seen as alternatives or rivals . The differentiation between subgroups of Ancestors even within one patrilineage, looks forward to the eventual splitting of that lineage. In addition, the extent of a married woman's assimilation to her husband's group can be judged only when she herself dies and announces after her death that she has gone to reside with her husband's Ancestors in one of their Earth sites, rather than in a site associated with her own brothers. These tensions will emerge ever more prominently in the following chapters, as we draw away from the immediate context of the stone-planting towards the long-term pattern of relationships of the living with the dead and with each other. In the early stages, the conflict is mainly between a dead person's Experience and Ancestor aspects, that is, it is primarily an ontological dilemma mapped on to a cosmological paradox. Later, the emphasis will move to a conflict between possible Earth sites, one which lies near, though as yet not quite at, the Ancestor aspect of his location and being.
The kaleidoscope of feelings between the living and the dead
There is a further dimension to the dead person's transformation, one which runs consistently throughout both the contrast between Experience and Ancestor and the clashes of interest between rival Earth sites. This is
Redeeming the dead and protecting the li l'ing 143
the psychological dimension, that of the feelings or states of mind of all the persons concerned . Sora appear almost to give primacy to this psychologi ca l dimension, in that feelings often seem to be the ultimate ar biters in conflicts which one might otherwise be tempted to ca ll struct ura l. In developing my discussion of feelings during the following chapters, I sha ll shift the emp hasis progressively from the feelings of the dead as these are co nveyed during trances, to those of the living mourners who speak with
them, in order ultimately to interpret the interdependence of these two voices.
Our first approach to analysing feelings has bee n within the interplay of the dead person 's Experience and Ancestor aspects. By the time of the stone-p lantin g, the So ra fun eral seq uence ha s a lrea dy differentiated the decea sed into these two aspects, with the intention of altering the balance between them a nd even tually eliminating the form er. The inquests on the day after cremation and on the day of the sto ne-planting arrive at a verdict about the Experience which the deceased now shares with his attackers. The song of redemption in the stone-planting rescues him from this Experience and leads him into the state of being an Ancestor in the Underworld as well as a resident of an Earth-Sonum site. For the more contagious of the Experiences, this redemption is further reinforced by the blocking rite which aims to prevent him from ' wandering' back to his Experience and thereby causing its repetition among the living. Since neither the redemption nor the blocking will be totally successful at once, the transformation begun at this time is gradually reinforced by healings, or acknowledgements, each time the deceased brings himself to the a ttention of the living by making them ill. After an attack in his undesirable Experience aspect, he is given a ba nishing-rite; at other times, in his relatively desirable Ancestor aspect, he is welcomed and fed inside the house - though always on a tight leash. Though the deceased comes mainly in one aspect at any given manifestation, both his aspects continue to exist for some time as simultaneous possibilities. Why does his redemption not stick at first and how does it eventually come to do so? The people who crowded round the shamans to learn the causes of lamano's death responded to this information with a mixture of pity (abasuYlm), grief (sinta) and outrage (the verb barabda-). However, they expressed these feelings not only for lamano's sake, but also for their own because they shared his sense of victimhood. This awareness of their own vulnerability applies equally to Ra'tud-Sonum as a means and sorcery as a motive. Regarding Mengalu's sorcery, they remark: 'If it's sorcery, if it's witchcraft, that explains why
1



144 Dialog ues witli tlie dead
your brother's throat is ill and does n' t get better however many sacrifices we do' (chapter 5, line 199), and 'Are we all going to be consumed by this sorcery?, (chapter 5, line 127). Regarding Ra'tud, they understand how Mo'mo' was earlier a victim ofRa'tud and has now become an agent of that Experience. They know that he wa s liable to act upon anyone who fell into his orbit through opportunity and motive and realise that their own Jamano is now himself in the same state. So their pity for Jamano is mingled with apprehension on their own behalf. To this extent they are not, yet, as much victims as he is; but they are all potentially his future victim s. Their vulnerability is only one side of their double susceptibility to the dead , who also nurture them. This impingement cannot be avoided: it is an intrinsic characteristic of people whom one knew while they were alive that they will have this effect on one after their death. As the double usage of the verb gorod-, 'wander', shows, the entities which perpetuate themselves are not so much the Experiences as dead people themselves. The Sora do not insist that every person who dies must of necessity cause further similar deaths. Though all are likely to do so and to make such attempts, some cause several deaths, others none . It is not in the transmission of his Experience that the deceased finds ultimate fulfilment, since a person who has caused one death may still feel the need to cause many more, as Ra'gi continues to do . Rather, it is through the gradual replacement of this destructive and hostile kind of self-perpetuation by another kind, that of protecting and nourishing one's descendants and heirs as their Ancestor . The concepts Experience and Ancestor are simply alternative vehicles for this perpetuation of the person. Their crucial difference is that as members of an Experience, persons perpetuate themselves by causing suffering, while as Ancestors they guarantee a complex social continuity between persons and across time, in which their inclination to attack eventually disappears. So what the living try to steer is the way in which the deceased will affect them, and this attempt is made collectively by those on whose heads his effect, whichever form it takes, will inevitably fall. Through their techniques of redemption and banishing, the survivors seek to modify the effect of Jamano's past over their own future . We are now in a better position to address the question, raised in chapter 4, of why a particular dead person afflicts a particular living person, on a particular occasion, in a particular form. A person's sonum is his mode of continuing to exist not only in death, but also among the living. Kantino's father reminds Kantino of their continuing closeness by sending him a tummy-rumble; Ra'gi brings herself to her brother Mengalu's attention by blocking his urine and thereby perpetuates something of herself in him,
Redeellling tlie dead and protecting tlie living 145
through the livin g out of hi s own life. Th e dead person moves around a chart of sonum space and send s back to the living messa ges, the nature a nd impact of which depend on his location of the moment. Wherever it is, the sonum has the power- indeed , in the Sora vi ew, the necessary property - of a ffecting th e living in a way which corresponds to hi s own stale. Thi s state in turn is knowable because he speaks with them as an autonomous con scious entity which , though materially insubstantial , is a personality of the same order as themselves. Any living person, or group of people who fall together in som e contex t, lives constantly with a repertoire of such sonums. The members of such a group will all experience each of these sonums and to some extent agree on its properties . Indeed , it is this trend towards consensus, so evident in the interrogation of Jamano , which makes the sonum real in a sense similar to that of any other recognised entity in the 'objective' world. As well as its cosmological location, each sonum has its own relations with other sonums, relations with other Living people and potential effects on each of them . These properties are subject to change and manipulation. The relationships expressed by means of sonums can be very diverse, and those revealed in Jamano's case are only one possible configuration . In that example, the potential for Jamano's self-perpetuation through his Experience ofRa'tud means that ifhe attacks his descendants in that form, what will be stirred up is the hostility between his son Ranatang, who has inherited the quarrel, and the supposed sorcerer Mengalu; while if Jamano later comes to reside in Sweet Mango Earth site (the ostensible pretext for his quarrel with Mengalu and a place which Mengalu loves) then his son Ranatang's claim to the disputed land there may be strengthened. Despite any consensus, the story is also likely to have many conflicting sub-plots. Among the dead, on the one hand, each person is sometimes a pure Ancestor, sometimes in an Earth site, sometimes in his original death-Experience, sometimes in an Experience with which he has no necessary connection but to which he has gone temporarily for some purpose of the moment: to join others in an attack, to 'hire' others to do his dirty work, for sex or simply for a drink or a dance. Among the living, on the other hand, there are the mutual relationships of the interlocutors, each affected by the death in a different way and brought during the dialogue into a unanimity which is temporary, artificial and probably never total. In addition, there are the possibly contradictory interpretations in rival inquests performed by people who have occasion to summon the same dead person, sometimes a cross-cousin but usually a married woman caught between two lineages. A case study in chapters 7 and 8 will combine both of



146 Dialogues Ivilh Ihe dead
these, as a courtesy stone-planting staged earlier by Sagalo for his distant cousin (chapter 2) provides a vital link in the interpretation of hi s youn g wife's sudden death . The existence of a progressive movement between various stages of the state of being dead suggests that for our interpretation of dialogue an apprecia tion of the Sora perception of time will be crucial. So far we have seen only the beginning ofth.is process around the moment ofdeath and the first stages of the funeral . Part III will develop a longer perspective over the years and generations following each death . It begins in the following chapter by transcribing a dialogue in which we hear nineteen sonums caught on one particular day in their movement across time. As we hear each sonum speaking to several living persons crowding around the shaman, we shall be able to note the variations in emotional tone between one sonum and another and even within one and the same sonum as it addresses different speakers. These variations will form the foundation of my analysis in the following chapters of time, continuity and feelings.
PART III
OPERATING THE CALCULUS OF ALL PREVIOUS DEATHS



7
Transcription of a dialogue with nineteen dead persons
The dialogues in this chapter constitute text 7. 1.
The transcription presented in this chapter and analysed in the following one took place the day after the dialogue presented in chapter 5, though the occasion is not related. It is taken from a rite staged to heal a small , unnamed baby in Sagalo's lineage . This baby was suffering from diarrhoea and backache caused by Lumbago-Sonum (Du/'i-slm), a sub-Experience of the Sun. Some of the personalities involved will be new. But so tightly woven are the relationships between members of different lineages, that the affairs of this lineage will quickly be seen to intermesh with those of others, especially of Mengalu's branch of the Headman lineage. It is mid-morning on an overcast day at the tail end of the rainy season. A number of people are scattered under a huge wild mango tree by the side of a dusty path leading out of the village. A preliminary summoning of the Ancestors, with a short trance, took place inside the baby's house last night, and this morning these Ancestors have been led out of the house in the banishing-rite format to the village's Lumbago-sacrifice site where the present, more substantial trance takes place. Now the shaman Rondang (an old woman whose nickname means 'Bag of Bones') is sitting on the ground in front of an old winnowing-fan made of basketry. On this are laid out the special apparatus appropriate to Lumbago-Sonum, such as the creeping waterside plant 'Lumbago-Medicine' (dUl'i-/'e) and the toy bow which will fire the caustic ('hot') kernels of the wild cashew (oloj) into the air and over the patient. The edges of the bare space under the tree are littered with remnants of old winnowing-fans from the one hundred or so Lumbago healings which I estimate are done at this site every year: since this is a banishing-rite, the

refuse would be contaminatin g if taken home again. The air is quiet , the people unhurried. F rom time to time a few drops of rain patter on to the leaves of the I~an go tree. People take sw igs from a little pot of aba, the distilled liquor which is the main drink of the cold rainy season and will be offered to each dead person on arr ival. Most of those present are women , draped as always with babies and young children. A few of these women hover around Rondang while the rest watch over the rice boiling above fire-trenches dug in the ground and wait for the men to bring the meat from where they are butchering the buffalo nea rby. The Ancestors of the lineage have ga nged up together to demand a sacrifice from the baby's hou sehold. In doing this, they are not perpetuating their own illnesses (which would of course be very varied) ; rather, they have 'hired ' the Sun to produce symptoms on their behalf. The aspect of the Sun which is available for hire in this way is called Duri-s/111 ('Lumbago /ho bbled/twisted/ cri ppled-Sonum '). The symptoms of Iumbago are ca used by Sun-Woman's slave, the blacksmith Dumb-S un (Mo 'mo'yul1g) , who strikes the patient with his hammer. This can be combined with other symptoms, too , and in fact the dialogue lays equal emphasis on the baby's diarrhoea. The relevant, simplified genealogy ofSagalo's lineage, which is small and impoverished, is given in figure 7.1. The two main branches are related through a distant common ancestor but the histories of their land holdings have now diverged to the point where each branch is associated with a different range of Earth sites. I have summarised the two branches respectively as being associated with the Bat's Nest (Kal1lursll1g) and the Sangkaroren groups of Earth sites. The conversations with the sonums are, as always, conducted largely by women. Most of the time I was the only man present, though there were always several more women present besides those who did most of the talking. However, whether male or female, the interlocutors are largely people who have come to meet ' their' sonums, that is, people close to them. Three households are closely involved today . The occasion is staged by Ikaram , the father of the sick baby and a member of the branch associated wth Sangkaroren Earth site. He is not actually present but spends his time with the men of the lineage who are killing, cutting up and sharing out the buffalo nearby . Those women who are not closely involved in the case are doing the cooking over there too. The mother of the baby, Ikaram's wife Rungkudi, remains with the shaman throughout but does not speak very much or very forcefully since she is in the village of her in-laws, speaking to their Ancestors. Being a young wife, she still feels constrained by



15 2 Dialog ues willt lite dead
'embarrrassment ' (garo}). The baby is still closely attached to her , so that the dialogue sometimes seems to imply that she herself is the patient (e. g. as 'daughter-in-law' in lines 2, 67, 87). Apart from the baby who is today's patient, Rungkudi has two other children, one alive and one dead . Her daughter Amboni (sonum No. 16 below) died of a scarring disease and it is their conversation here which was used to open chapter I of this book. H er teenage son Sarsuno, though alive, has just made a suicide attempt and we shall also meet the sonum of his lineage-brother Palda, the previous suicide victim who encouraged him in this attempt (sonum No. 10). The other two households involved, those of Sagalo and Sindi, both belong to the branch of the lineage associated with Bat's Nest Earth site . Sagalo is known to use from chapter 2. A good-natured young man , he lives with his widowed mother, who is Mengalu's sister. Two years ago , it will be remembered, Sagalo paid a terrible price for a teenage affair with the girl-friend ofMengalu's son Sargia. Mengalu had unleashed this sorcery on Sagalo in angry response to the latter's sexual poaching and it had ricochetted from him on to the defenceless soul of his little brother Mando, who was killed by it. The reader may also remember that the impoverished Sagalo then married the strong-willed and passionate Panderi. In choosing an 'elopement' (dari) with Sagalo, she defied both the restraints imposed by her distant cross-cousin relationship with Sagalo and her brothers' plans for an arranged marriage elsewhere. They were very much in love and by the time of my return in early 1979 they had a one-year-old baby boy. However, four months before today's dialogue, Sagalo suffered a further, most terrible loss. On 1 May 1979 Panderi carried their baby home from a journey and, within a short time, collapsed without warning and died. The verdict of the inquest was that her soul had been eaten by the Ra'tudSonum of one of the villages on the way. But at her inquest the following day, Panderi took great pains to emphasise that the intended victim was the baby himself and that she had been killed only by accident as she was bending over to protect him. We shall hear her repeating this message in the dialogue given here and her inquest will be analysed in the following chapter. Sindi, the last householder involved, is an elderly lady with an acerbic wit. After being widowed, she has stayed on with her own household among her husband's lineage, raising their five children. The eldest, Rutujen, is a grown man in love with a girl from a wealthy home in the Headman lineage whose father will not let her go to live with him. The youngest, Oindo, is still a little boy. Her three middle children have all died within the past few years. One of them, also named Oindo (sonum No . II),
A dialog ue Il'illt nineleen dead persons 153
died as a child and passed on hi s name to hi s younger brother who wa s born afterwa rd s; Maianti (No. 17) died as a n adol escent girl ; while her eldes t son Palda (No . 10) is the youth who committed suicide and who having himself 'gone into the' Sun ' has recently tried to persuade Rungkudi's son Sarsuno to kill himself and join him th ere. As a senior woman Sindi acts as the main interlocutor throu ghout the dialogue. It is common for older women to do this. Men tend to join in more on major occasions or if the situation touches them deeply. The male householder's linea ge Ancestors are generally greeted (olong-) by a group of his sisters and wives who offer them refreshment and emotional response. So just as she changes the source of her nourishment, a woman who marries changes from greeting the Ancestors of her father and brothers to greeting those of her husband. The tone in which sisters greet their own Ancestors is relaxed and familiar. That of young wives greeting their husband's Ancestors is reserved and respectful. But llluch of the talking, including that on behalf of a young wife like Rungkudi , is done by older women whose tone is fearless often to the point of being abusive. Such women may be either sisters of the lineage who have never left home or who have returned after being widowed or divorced, or else wives like Sindi who have borne children and gradually become closely identified with their husband's lineage. Interlocutors like Sindi speak only partly for the sake of others. Though much of the time she will speak on behalf of others with their sonums, for a long period we shall see her occupied with her own as the sonums of her husband and three children converse with her (sonums Nos. 10, II , 12 and 17). It never seemed to me that these women's role as interlocutor betrayed a position of weakness. It is not the more insecure or marginal women like the young wife Rungkudi who speak like this, but the women who are most firmly esta bbshed within their residential group. The selection of such women thus resembles that of the funeral shamans through whom the other side of the dialogue passes. Men as well as women say that women are better able to olong- the dead. This is a word which means 'greet', 'speak to' , 'interact with', as in the common inteljection olong-ing roi, 'hey, listen!', 'pay attention!' (literally 'olong- me a moment') . It seems they are saying that women are more articulate than men and that the practice of holding dialogues is largely a women's system. It may be that since women move between lineages and villages, their biographies and their vision of life involve a greater degree of adaptation and negotiation and therefore calls forth from them a greater degree of dialogic commentary. For some twenty minutes now Rondang has squatted alone on her



154 Dialogues with the dead
haunches and bea ten her drum steadily, summoning T umbago-Sonum and its collaborating Ancestors by singing in its characteristic signature tune. Now she sits for the trance, legs stretched out and eyes closed . Some women including Sindi leave off their other activities and move closer in anticipation . After a few mjnutes of sin gin g the tune which summons her rauda -familiar , Rondang's soul departs, her arms and legs are locked rigid and her fingers a nd toes are clenched tight. Wi th some effort, the bystanders unclench her , she sits at ease and the sonums are free to come. First the rauda , the sonum of her dead predecessor, a rrives and promises all assistance. Thereafter in succession over three hours with hardly a minute's break come nineteen dead people, followed by Herdswoman (Codu -boj) and Dumb-S un (Mo '1110 'yung) , both sub-Experiences of the Sun closely associated with Lumbago . Finally, to round off the trance the rauda comes again. The preliminary and closing speeches of the rauda, as well as those of Herdswoman and Dumb-Sun, are not included here as these would require copious extra commentary without furthering the present discussion . Instead, attention is focussed exclusively on the Ancestors themselves. Male sonums appear before female ones, as always, and to a greater genealogical depth . The order of their appearance is mapped on to the genealogy in figure 7.1. It can be seen that though not every Ancestor speaks, each twig or branch of the lineage is generally exhausted before the dialogue moves on to another - a pattern which echoes the ordering of names in the Ancestors' song of redemption in chapter 6. In translating, I have taken pains to preserve shifts in the tone of the original, since much of the significance of the text for later analysis will depend on just these distinctions between insult and tenderness, with all the mixed emotions which accompany familiarity. Conversation with the first nine sonums is largely banter about trivia, though in our analysis of moods in the next chapter we shall see that this is important in setting the scene for the very serious conversations which will follow from No . 10 onwards. For ease of reference, the sonums who speak are summarised in figure 7.2, along with their main living interlocutors. The sonums marked with an asterisk are particularly significant and their circumstances will be discussed at length in the following chapter:

The next four sonums continued to engage Sindi in sarcastic backchat. First Pansia (6a) returned to complain that he was insulted the previous time and to demand a drink. Then came Poiro (7), Gumesara (8) and Ourugad (9). Durugad boasted about all the rites he used to perform when he was alive. Sindi pointed out drily that it had still failed to save him in the end.
10 Palda
With the arrival of Palda, the mood of the conversation changes dramatically. The abusive tone in which he speaks to his mother has an intensity and viciousness which are far removed from the casual, familiar



162 Dialogues Il'ith the dead
rudeness of her previous exchanges with the other son urns . I had first heard him speak three years previously, through the funeral shaman Rijanti, a few months after his death. I did not know him when he was alive and it is not easy to distinguish his personali ty then from the insolence now req uired by his ro le as an unrepentant incestuous suicide who is sti ll trying to perpetuate the form of his own death in others. The Sora theory of the ca usality of suicides invo lves chains of earlier and subsequent victims and these are shown for Pa lda's case in figure 7.3. Earlier, Pa ld a had committed incest with a girl of the most unequivocally disapproved type of maronsel cousin , a mother's sister's daughter. Believing that she would tell everybody, he hanged himself out of embarrassment (garoj). But though this forms the precipitating cause of his suicide, on another leve l the cause will include incitement and assistance from a previous suicide victim. In Palda's case these were furnished by Rumbana. Rumbana was a young man of the Headman lineage. He was the only child of today's shaman Rondang and her husband Tabaro. He hanged himself shortly after returning from a year's migrant labour in Assam. The cause of his suicide on one level was that he had spent savi ngs entrusted to him by other Soras in Assam for safe carriage home. But on the other level, while in Assam he had been present at a murder and had brought the Sun-death contagion home to Alinsing, a contagion which was not successfully neutralised by the blocking rite which the entire village underwent after his death. His first victim was a little boy who hanged himself because his parents proposed to send him away to a school. His second was Palda. In between he brought about a failed suicide attempt by his lineage-brother Sargia (Mengalu's son and, incidentally, the youth on

whose beha lf Mengalu is said to have used sorcery against Saga lo in chapter 2). Now Pa lda himself has begun to take over the role of chief inciter (muda , ringleader) to suicide. The dialogue will discuss in detail hi s recent attempt o'n the life of Rungkudi's son, his lineage-brother Sarsuno. Many elements in Palda's messa ge are unwelcome to his mother, who resists them accordingly . As well as his attempt to kill Sarsuno , she resists reports of the marriage in the Underworld between his crony, the earlier suicide Rumbana, and her own daughter Maianti. Maianti and Rumbana have now begotten a sonum baby in the Underworld, another piece of news which Sindi does not appear to welcome. The implications of this marriage will be analysed in the next chapter. For the moment it can be said that it highlights and maybe prolongs Palda's largely unredeemed state as a resident of Sun-Sonum . Furthermore, though by his redemption song he was of course led into one of his own lineage's Earth sites (into Bat's Nest), he repeatedly makes the point that to the extent that he resides in Earth at all he does so in the company of his new 'brother- in-law' Rumbana, in the Earth site of the latter's lineage in Jagatumba . Thus, despite their separate redemptions by their respective lineage Ancestors into separate Earth sites, even in their redeemed moments their mutual bond as fellow Sun-People keeps them together. Palda's entry is a masterpiece of needling. Where other sonums ask for an alcoholic drink, he demands pan, a blood-red mixture which is not used by Soras but is chewn and spat out by the people of the plains, including the low-caste Blacksmiths associated with the Sun.

Palda now instructs them in the details of the rite by which he can be induced to leave Sarsuno alone. Here I think he is playing with the two levels of his own unresolved condition. The rite described is a banishing-rite (amdungp/r). This echoes the pre-emptive rite to block the spread of the contagion of a Sun-death, in which the animal is dragged out of the village on a rope to a random site, the blood is drunk raw and the rope cut into segments. But at the same time he combines this dismissal from the village of the bad, pent-up blood with the specific direction of his new residential Earth site Jagatumba (line I 74,.reinforced in line 180). Though she agrees to do this, Sindi still refuses to acknowledge his change of residence and amends this - consciously or unconsciously? - to Bat's Nest, the name of his own branch's site where she thinks he should be (181) .

The dynamics of Sindi's family life now begin to emerge more clearly. Having made himself thoroughly objectionable, Palda introduces his dead younger brother Oindo as the contrasting favourite son. Whatever their personalities may have been while alive, Oindo's role, like Palda's, is now set. He is portrayed as loyal and responsible and as the person who as a sonum saved Sarsuno from Palda's incitement to suicide. Attention is now focussed on the anticipation of Oindo's arrival and of Palda's hoped-for departure. In an attempt to hasten this, Sindi even acknowledges Palda's residence in Jagatumba Earth site (190) though with a bad grace and in a dismissive way which Palda will not allow to pass (191) .

But Panderi's pitiful and helpless tone throughout makes it clear that, despite her best intentions, this move is still beyond her ability .

8
Memories and rememberers: states of mind among the dead and the living
Emotional tone and degrees of redemption
The text given in chapter 7 is a typical Sora dialogue with th e dead. It is clear that some of the sonums who come have great emotional power over those who gather to meet them; and that , in formal terms , this power is somehow linked to the degree to which each dead person's redemption has been made to stick or is being resisted by the dead themselves. The deceased person may be a more or less will ing party to this resistance. While Panderi would willingly embrace redemption but is hindered by other sonums who now make up the Ra'tud side of her nature, Palda by contrast revels in his unredeemed state, as befits both his perverse personality and the more dangerous nature of his Sun Experience. Clearly, too, the increasing stability of a sonum's redeemed state is related to the passage of time itself. The past few chapters have shown how a dead person is perpetuated among the living. This perpetuation is seen in terms of a gradual progression between two idioms, Experience-membershi p and Ancestorhood. Ini tially, the dea th-Experience fills the entire scope of his being and leaves room for nothing else. Almost immediately, the Ancestors redeem him and there begins a struggle to hold illm to this new state. He reverts spontaneously to cause illness; healing rites send him back. Perhaps through the very act itself of causing dea ths (this is not quite clear) or else simply by the passing of time, the impulse to revert weakens and eventually fades away altogether. He is now a pure Ancestor residing in an Earth site. In the turmoil of actual events there can be an intricate play between the idioms of Ancestor and Experience. Thus Panderi's death-Experience itself hinders her attempts to reach her husband's Earth site and become an Ancestor in his house, willie Palda's camaraderie with his fellow Sun
lVlemo l'ies (lnd I'emembel'el's 177
res ident Rumbana even perverts hi s choice of Ea rth -site resid ence . As we shall see, Ihi s makes him effectively a n 'A ncestor' to anot her lin eage a nd actua ll y disturbs the regul ar movement of names and property within and between lineages. This kind of play is made possible by the fl ex ibility of the correspondence between structural time a nd li ved time. A ll changes in the Sora life-cycle start from a moment. If the initi a l moment is not given by nature, as in birth or death, it is created as in the stone-p lanting a nd la ter stages of the funeral sequence. But this moment is not the point at which the transition occurs definitively, but that at which the possibility for its fulfilment begins. Each stage must reckon with reversions to the previous state . While these a re ever-decrea sing, they can not be disrega rded . The decisiveness of the created moments gives a clea r framework of structura l time across which the eddies and counter-currents of fee lin g flow , with a different kind of time-pa ttern of its own . So num s like Palda are problem atic precisely because their recalcitrance creates such a n extreme discrepancy between these two kinds of time. As a heuristic measure, the sonums we have met in the dialogue above may be distinguished broad ly into Old, Middle-Distance and Rece nt sonums. These categories are not a bsolute, nor are they directly labelled in Sora terminology. They reflect no more than predominant tendencies: just as the dead wander back a nd forth between states, so our categories and dead people's membership of them cannot be hard and fast. These categories correspond not so much to time alone, as to shifts in emotional a ttitudes which correspond somewhat loosely to the passage of time, so that these categories could also be called 'Undisturbing' , 'Neutral' and 'Distressing' sonums. Those in the first category have been dea d many years. They no longer attack in the form of their original death-Ex perience; though they are known to be in Earth sites, they visit us only in the form of pure Ancestors or in ways closely associated with this such as through Lumbago-Sonum (Dul'i-SII11 , as in this dialogue). They no longer have any close personal emotional involvement with the living. The second category, who in general have died some years ago, have lost much of their power to distress though not yet their personalities. These are inclined sometimes to attack through their originaldeath-Experience and sometimes to behave as Ancestors. Finally, Distressing sO;lums may ca~se grief a mong the living and attack frequently in the form of their original death-Experience. The first sonums to arrive in any trance are always unequivocally Old sonums (of whom the males survive for many more years than the females). In text 7.1. this category may be said to include sonums Nos . I to 9. In cosmological terms Old sonums are Ancestors in the fullest sense. They



178 Dialogues IFith the dead
have taken up residence definitively in a particular Earth site and are very unljkely to revert to their original dea th-Experience (indeed , these are often already forgo tten by th e living). They show a striking uniformity in the tone and content of their conversation and a re barely distingui shable in personality . The circumstances of their death s are no longer discussed and they converse on the same kind of non -topics which Sindi, for all her accomplishment in backchat, often appears to find fundamentally uninteresting. These so nums are treated facetiously , even rudely, and dismissed quickly . They turn up ostensibly to receive what is theirs by right and to oversee their own legacies (see e.g. Indupur's claim on Sandi, lines 141 If). They justify this relationship by the fact of having passed on their names, and through their role in promoting crop growth. As Ancestors, the Old sonums are fundamental to the structure of Sora society. Their appearance early in the trance sets the scene in several ways for what will follow . Like the rauda-familiars, they can point to the direction which the trance will take when the serious discussion begins (cf. lamano's father's speech, before lamano's own appearance, in chapter 5). Like the raudas, too, they also serve to establish the otherness of the world from which they have all come. lust before their arrival, the shaman has sung an invoca tion describing her journey to the Underworld . Her imagery has established this otherness in terms of spatial inaccessibility. Now the Old sonums play further with spatial paradox by reminding us that even on the surface of the earth, their space overlaps with ours and yet is different (previous chapter, lines 70 ff),just as in terms of time, within this very same space, the seasons in their world are out of step with ours (lines 5- 11,73-4). This portrayal of the difference between the worlds not only validates the journey between them but also contributes to the transcendent authority of the dialogue which this journey has made possible, an authority which will be so important when the more recent sonums follow. Middle-Distance and Recent (or, respectively, Neutral and Distressing) sonums provide the substance of the conversations for which the Old sonums have merely set the scene. These sonums are still at various stages between a frequent reversion to their original death-Experience and the state of irreversible residence in an Earth site which will make them into Old sonums. Though these stages are not absolute and a sonum's behaviour may vary with the occasion and the identity of the interlocutors present, we may distinguish Middle-Distance from Recent sonums in that the former no longer cause the living the same intense anguish. Thus we can say of Middle-Distance sonums that (as with Old sonums) the living mourners have no intense unfinished business with them. With the more
Memories and rell7elllberers 179
di stant among these sonums (Nos. 13- 15, 18), their personalities have beco me reduced through conventionalisation and they co me to be identified only by superficia: attributes which lead ultimately to caricature. Thus No. 15 (Sara'ka) acts out a di stinctive speech habit and in addition repeats (a s he does every time he appears) hi s ludicrous personal hi sto ry. These Middle-Distance sonums are already not much more than Old sonums with a little garnish of stylised local colour still clinging to them. Sonums Nos. 10-12 and 17, on the other hand , while still being Middle-Distance, lie much closer to the heart of their current inte rlocutor Sindi since they are her own husband and children. In themselves they do not seem to distress her now. But her insulting banter with her husband , for instance, is not distant as it often wa s with the earlier Old sonums but perhaps tinged with some affection and at least highly specific in tone and content. She is here facing the sonums of personalities who cannot be fully conventionalised away until she herself is dead and her conversations with them give a convincing picture of what those relationships were probably like when they were all alive. Three sonums can be placed unhesitatingly in the Recent, Distressing category: the suicide Palda, the scar-ravaged little girl Amboni, and the young wife Panderi. In terms of time, Panderi (No . 19) has been dead only three months and has not yet received the first of her three annual harvest-commemorations; Palda (No. 10) has been dead for around three years; while Amboni (No. 16) has died within the previous year. Emotionally, they come in direct response to the presence of a mourner whom they have the power to disturb . In such cases the bereaved person may sometimes try to greet the deceased and even to embrace the shaman through whom the sonum speaks; but often he or she will weep silently on the outskirts of the group and it is left to a more confident and collected interlocutor to speak with the sonum on the mourner's behalf. These three conversations stand out sharply in the intensity of their impact. The topics discussed are highly varied and specific, and they plumb emotional depths of a quite different order from the other sonums. Indeed I still find it difficult, several years later, to listen to their tapes with a dry eye. All are unsettled not only cosmologically but emotionally: Palda deliberately nasty, little Amboni pathetic, though her dangerousness is just an unavoidable part of her condition, and Panderi who though she does not speak in a threatening tone is still trapped against her will inside the form of her own death. I do not have enough information to examine Amboni'scase, but shall examine the background to Panderi's and Palda's appearances.



180 Dialogues Ivitlt the dead
Conversations with two partially redeemed sonums
Pallde";'s message to Saga /o : lo ve agaillst all odds
On the ni ght that Pa nderi died, I wa s sleepi ng in Ra na tang's hou se in T on gseng, ha lf a mile across the va lley from where she lived in Alin sin g. During the ni ght there came the sound of drumming a nd lamentin g from A linsing a nd the sky was red with the fl a re o f a pyre . I wa s consciou s of th e others' tense wakefuln ess in the da rk for some tim e before someone broke the silence to ask , 'Are th ey si nging langga or langgi?' (i.e. ' beloved ' in the ma sculine or feminine form). However, before first light they knew from gossip not only who had died but were also saying that she had hanged he rself after a quarrel with Sagalo - and , moreover, that she had tried to do so once before but had been cut down in time. At first light we crossed over to Alinsing, where the pyre on Sa galo 's cremation site had not yet died down . A friend from Alinsing told me, 'She had been going around with only her external (bayira) soul , her real soul had been lost since she went to Pangrung yesterday . Yesterday a sonum grabbed her, Ra'tud .' Others added specifically tha t this was the Ra'tud -Sonum of Rungrungba village, on the way to Pangrung. But members of her natal lineage in Lower Village, who had always resented her marriage to Sagalo, were standing round the pyre muttering that it must have been suicide or murder to be so sudden. It turned out that they had already raided Sagalo's house and sna tched back Panderi's personal jewellery, grain and clothing (her keruru) with a promise to return it to her child if it should survive . This was a calculated spite since the baby was only a year old and such unweaned children usually perish since no other woman will wet-nurse them. Unlike Jamano 's case, there were here two preconceived verdicts in circulation before the first inquest. Neither of these verdicts involved sorcery but each did reflect one of two conflicting views of what the cause and even the perceptible symptoms might have been. These views directly reflected the opposed interests of Panderi's husband and brothers, both of whom were to perform a separate funeral and inquest. That involving Ra ' tud was based on the fact that the dead woman had returned from a long journey carrying her baby along a path which passed through many known Ra ' tud sites; characteristically of Ra ' tud she had then collapsed suddenly during the evening, in addition making horrible noises reminiscent of the dog Kambutung with which Ra'tud-Sonum hunts down its victims. Besides, it was argued , she had initially collapsed outside the house and called out for help, whereupon certain named witnesses had carried her inside, so how could it have been a concealed murder or suicide? The
Memories alld rel11emberers 181
second verdict, on the other hand , refl ected her bro thers' ho stilit y towards her ma rria ge. Their view was suppo rted in addition by her supposed previous suicide a ttempt a fter an earlier qua rrel wi th Saga lo . When the Ancestor-W omen of Saga lo 's lineage had cooled her ashes with wa ter, her brothers' group too k some o f these in a bone-carrying process ion to their own cremation site a nd buried them there, just as the remai nder had been buried on Sagalo 's cremation site. The inquests in her husband 's and her bro thers' houses were to be performed in that order on successive days by the same old woman , Rijanti , who thou gh now blind and barely able to walk acted as the ma in funeral shaman for both Alinsin g and Lower Villa ge. She had ma rried into the Headman linea ge of Alinsin g from another vill age bu t had stayed on after being widowed . She wa s not obviousl y involved with either of the opposed households for whom she was to perform. The following morning the first of these inquests took place in Sagalo 's house. Some representatives of Panderi's brothers came and sat stonyfaced on the verandah outside, from where they could hear through the open door everything that was said within. Raw rice grains were contributed by all the households in Sagalo's lineage, with the addition of his mother's brother Mengalu. This was in May 1979, during Mengalu's period of great closeness to Sagalo, whom he protected as his 'orphaned nephew' . Three months earlier, it will be recalled from the beginning of chapter 5, Mengalu had chosen to sing at a separate event with the Ancestor-Men of Sagalo's lineage rather than with his own at the naming ofRanatang's baby, even though that baby was ofMengalu 's own lineage. On the present occasion Mengalu had likewise lent his seniority to support Sagalo vehemently in his unsuccessful battle to prevent Panderi's brothers from confiscating her personal effects . But I could not help remembering the last time I had attended an inquest in this same house two years earlier (chapter 2). Then Mengalu had been revealed as the one who had murdered Sagalo's little brother by sorcery - a verdict which we saw in chapter 5 was to be echoed a few months after today's proceedings, a t the inquest on Jamano. The format and tone of the inquest on the morning after death are similar to those of the later stone-planting inquest, as presented in chapter 5. There are the same injunctions to the deceased not to conceal the truth and the same techniques for building from a shared perception of the symptoms towards a goal in terms of motive. Yet at the same time, the first inquest proceeds with a more elaborate display of tentativeness. The dead woman was fed with water rather than alcohol since rather than a true sonum she is



18 2 Dialogues with th e dead
still partly a co nfu sed ghost (/wlman) hot from the pyre and consequently at even greater ri sk of ondrung (,false awareness'). In the first inquest, therefore, an additional preliminary test is done before the actual trance in order to give the company a lea d towards the likely ca use of death , a lthough the verdict of this need not be bindingon the dialogues which follow. The shaman places two gra in s of rice between two leaves from a bael tree and passes them through the fl ame of her la mp. This lamp is associated with the idea of clarity of perception in the quest for truth in the shadowy Underworld. A verse is sun g nam ing one so num a t a time which may be either a specific dead person or an Experience and the leaves are opened and examined to see whether the two grains have stuck to the same lea f (indicating a negative proba bility) or to separate lea ves (positive) . On the present occasion, after working systematically through the names of various dead perso ns, the shaman moved on to specific Ra ' tud sites . After this procedure had been followed with a grim determination for about an hour, she came at the thirty-second test to the Ra'tud -Sonum of Rungrungba village. On separating the leaves it was found that the two grains had disappeared completely, which I was told indicated a very high probability indeed that this sonum was responsible. At this point the tests with leaves ceased and Panderi's brothers' people entered the house and took part in the remainder of the proceedings. Once the trance began, there came at first a succession of eight dead people who stayed only long enough to name themselves and enjoy a drink of wine. These included some Ra'tud -People who were from Rungrungba village but were not related in any way to Panderi or her husband. Then came the suicide Palda , who confusingly lent support to the alternative verdict by claiming to have induced Panderi to follow his own example and hang herself. He was followed , however, by the slavering hunting dog of Ra'tud , whose behaviour Panderi was supposed to have imitated in her death-throes. In the body of the frail old shaman I found this a chilling performance. Then came the Ra'tud-Man from Rungrungba who claimed responsibility as the ringleader in the attack . This was none other than Sunia, who as Sagalo's distant cross-cousin had been commemorated two years earlier in the same double stone-planting ceremony as had Sagalo's little brother Mando. Mengalu, despite his own role in Mando's death , asked him indignantly why he had done this terrible thing when they ('we', used without a trace of irony despite Mengalu's position at that earlier time) had treated him well by giving him an honorary stone-planting, even though this was not strictly required by his cross-cousin status.

F in a ll y the dead gi rl herse lf came. After bein g given a drink of water, rather than wi ne, she wa s made to reco unt every detail of her movements as she came a lony a long the path with her baby, where she had stopped to rest and whether she had felt anything at the time. She repeatedly empha sised how she had saved the baby who was the rea l ta rget of the attack. Her husband wep t at the thou ght that at the very moment when she wa s bein g ea ten a live, he had been havin g a good tim e drinking with hi s friends in the forest ; he reca lled how when she had recentl y been ill he had sacrifi ced an an im a l for her and she had imm ed iate ly recovered. All these points are still being echoed very closely in the previous chapter's text , recorded fo ur months la ter. There was also a lon g discussion of a ll her belon gin gs in order to es ta bli sh their precise quantiti es . Though they had been snatched by her brothers, the day mi ght come when they would be demanded back if the baby survived. At one point she was accused by her brothers' people of concealing the fact tha t she had bee n driven to suicide, and there were threa ts of calling another shaman to force her to corrobora te thi s view. But Panderi herself supplied the unmi stakable imagery of Ra ' tud , for exa mple, 'They prick me with a goad like a buffalo' (cf. cha pter 5, lines 102, 104). She confirmed that the ringleader in the attack had been Sunia and as a parting shot advised her younger sister, who was present, to marry Sagalo in her stead. The following day, Rijanti sat for the duplicate first inquest in Panderi 's brothers' hou se. I was unable to attend, and so cannot give a detailed account. Anything said in the earlier inquest could have been contested and it was not inevitable that she would either confirm her cause of dea th or repeat the advice to her sister. In thi s light I am inclined to interpret the appearance in the first inquest of the suicide Palda as being just enough to keep suicide a live issue. In the event, even though she was surrounded the second time by interlocutors from the other side of the dispute, the original verdict ofRa'tud was upheld and all her messages were repea ted. This was confirmed aga in at her first stone-planting given by Sagalo a month later and in the su bsequent stone-planting given by her brothers. All talk of suicide seems then to have ceased . The rationale for this verdict is so mewhat different from that for Jamano's case, where the role of the attacker was fragmented into three: the unmotivated person who shared Jam ano's Experience; the wife who did not share hi s Experience but was at least partially motivated; and the highly motiva ted sorcerer who was not yet dead and so was associated with a particula r Experience only to the extent that he had made a pact. In the present case there is no such fragmentation: the ultimate impetus for the



184 Dialogues Illith the dead
attack lies with someone who is him self a member of the Experi ence through which the attack is routed. Why did Sunia use his Experience, Ra'tud -Sonum, to attack Sagalo's baby and in the process kill Panderi? The So ra interpre tation goes beyond a simple conjunction of his own Expe ri ence and motive. It uses two analogies or displacements which I was able to discern in Sora discussions at the time. One of these concerns the attacker: Sunia was acting on behalf ofPanderi's own brothe rs. The other co ncerns the victim: it was the little baby boy who was the inte nded victim and Panderijust go t in the way while protect ing her baby . In the process, she was killed and the baby survived . Let us take the first di splacement first, in which the attacker Sunia is see n as standing for the victim's own brothers. Sunia resides in the Ra'tud site of his own villa ge Rungrungba . Each villa ge has its own Ra'tud residenti a l site, which ca uses the dea ths of a large number of people from tha t vill age (such as 1amano in Alinsing) as well as of a few stray travellers from other villages, like Panderi today. In recent memory, the Ra'tud site of Rungrungba has absorbed five other Alinsing people. In every case, some sort of personal connection was found to justify the attack. However, none of these victims from her own village was said to be implicated in Panderi's death. Instead, attention was focussed entirely on the connection between her husband Sagalo and his cross-cousin Sunia. What endowed Sunia with the potential to cause a death in Sagalo's household was the fact that Sagalo had given him a stone·planting. The ambiguity of this action can be understood if we refer back to the ways in which a man can participate in another man 's funeral. In chapter 4 it was shown tha t lineage members and affines (in-laws) are clearly distinguished by their behaviour; and that the basic way of participating in the funeral of a cross-cousin is to treat him as an affine by 'dancing a buffalo', in other words, to act as an outsider to his lineage. Yet if properly kept up a cross-cousin relationship can be very close, especially if cousins live near each other and frequently exchange labour and hospitality. My mother's brother's or father's sister's people were indeed in -laws to my parents; but their descendants, my cross-cousins, are discouraged as marriage partners for the next three generations and are often addressed as 'brother' and 'sister'. A cross-cousin's death therefore faces me with a choice: it is only if! am less close that I treat him as an in-law and so 'dance a buffalo' to his lineage's stone-planting; if closer, I may ' take his bones' and give him a duplicate stone-planting on my site, as Sagalo has done for Sunia. Even if! give my cross-cousin a second stone-planting he will not , like a married woman , be enumerated later among my Ancestors. Nor normally
Mel1lories alld rel1lel1lberers 185
will his name be given to one of my desce nd a nts. A male cross-cousin will simpl y res ide in one of hi s own lineage's Eart h-Sonum sites and give hi s name to one of their descendants . But though optional second sto neplantings are genera ll y given to cross-cousins for the sake of affection , here too there may be a strategic intention. To stage the primary stone-p lantin g ra ther than mere ly to participa te in one staged by someone else, is of course to assert one's right to be the heir of the deceased and to retrieve hi s name for one's own children . To take so me of his bones home in order to plant a second sto ne is the next best thin g: it brings within the range of quasi-consanguinity someo ne who would otherwise remain outside it as a n affine. If my cousin 's closest brothe rs la ter die out, I may ma ke a bid to be his heir aga inst hi s more distant co llatera ls. In this case I must first induce him in dialogues to change hi s res idence to one of my own Earth -So num sites, as the first stage in the transferral of what we may call more ma terial rights. Thus, though a decea sed ma le is not at first listed among the Ancestors of two lineages, by revealing in dialogues that he has changed the site of his Earth residence, he can effectively change his lineage : the rights first to his name and eventually to hi s property are thereby gradually transferred. The case discussed in the next section will include precisely such a move, as a more recent link between affines overrides an older collateral one within the lineage - largely on the grounds that it is more persuasive in terms of personal affection. The Sora interpretation of the present case takes advantage of Sagalo's relationship to Sunia in order to sa y something about Sagalo's marriage to Panderi. Sagalo's cross-cousin relationship to Sunia is analogous to the relationship between Sagalo and Panderi's brothers (figure 8.1). It was the latter relationship , as much as Sagalo's poverty , which her brothers cited to justify their hostility to the ma rriage . A cross-cousin relationship is supposed to last for three generations following the marriage which brings it into being and to lapse (masuna -) in the fourth . Tn the present case, Sagalo belon gs to the last generation in his line to whom this relationship will apply. Panderi's brothers do not wish to see this relationship ignored. The case thus emerges as one of a cross-cousin relationship which refuses to fade away before its time despite the anxiety of Sagalo and Panderi to discontinue it prematurely. As Panderi herself was to put it later, quoting what she had said at the moment of her own death, ' ... Ah husband Ah spouse, ... now that we've stopped being sisters, now that we've given up being cousins .. .' (chapter 7, line 285). What I have called the second displacement develops this through the substitution of the victim: it was not Panderi whom Sunia intended to



186 Dialogues with the dead
destroy , but her baby . Panderi insists repeatedly that the baby wa s the targe t and tha t she sacrificed her life to save him. Similarly, while her pyre was still bl azing, I hea rd that her brothers had already plundered her personal belongin gs from the wretched Sagalo 's house in the scarcely veiled expec tation that the baby would not be nursed by a nother woman and would not survive to claim them back. The bi rth of this baby, and of a boy at that , for Sagalo 's lineage represents the vindication of Panderi's marriage to him. So long as that ba by remains alive, he will continue to vindicate it. Sa galo has been quick to understand this and has already taken the very unusual step of taking him to a Christian missionary orphanage in order to make sure that he will be kept alive on baby food. This is two days ' journey away on the coast, far out of Sora territory, but he visits his baby frequently and thinks about him constantly . We have also heard Panderi encourage her younger sister to marry Sagalo . If there is to be polygyny, the first wife's normal companion as her co-wife is her younger sister (aliboj) and so this marriage would assert the continuing validity of Sagalo's first marriage to Panderi herself. It would also repeat Panderi's defiance of her brothers; provide another

(When I return ed in 1984, all these thin gs had ind eed come a bout. Saga lo had ma rried Panderi 's sister and retrieved hi s son , now five years old, from the orphana ge. He and the sister also now had a ba by daughter - called , as I expected , Panderi.) What emotiona l state, so soon after he r death , do the words which appear to come from Pa nderi aim to induce in th e two riva l living pa rties? Both are suffering from the loss of a woma n to whom they were strongly attached. But wrule her husband is bein g comforted, her brothers are being rebuked fo r thei r possessiveness. It is as if Panderi were saying to them , ' You thought tha t by wishing the ba by dead you could get your sister back. But you were defeated by my devotion to the marriage. By your wish , you have now lost me utterly - and you will lose your other sister to the same man.' For Sagalo, by contrast, the message points towards a certain continuing companionship. Sagalo's growing confidence about the survival of his son, or his satisfaction at Panderi's replacement by her sister, will be reflected in the growing permanence of Panderi's redemption as she ceases to wander as a Recent, Distressing sonum and remains mo re and more an Ancestor residing definitively in Sagalo's particular Earth site. Four months after her death, this positive future is still not yet assured . Panderi still finds it difficult to escape from her Ra'tud Experience (lines 286- 94) . But she is a determined woman and the passion with which she juxtaposes her protectiveness towards the baby with her love for Sagalo (e.g. line 285) suggests to me that her act of self-sacrifice for their baby's sake is intended to be taken by the bereaved Sagalo as the supreme expression of that love.
A complex a"d lI1/1velcome message for Si"di from her lIasty SOli Palda
While Panderi's redemption remains unstable against her will, Palda's case is quite the opposite: he revels in his Experience rather than seeking to escape from it and he abandons his 'correct' (rojtad) Earth site for a perverse one. This is despite the fact that he has been dead for three years to Panderi's four months. If his mother Sindi resists a message which he continues to push, we must ask who would welcome it. The number of interested parties is large and the occasions over the years when their views could be expressed are numerous. But so far as I was able to understand it, Palda and his mother are caught up in a bigger game, in which their affairs are merely a sideshow. This ramifies out from the ambiguous inheritance of Rumbana , the earlier suicide who induced Palda to take his own life. Figure
