8. 2 shows the main peopl e involved. Rumbana' s central role can be clea rl y see n. As he often tells us, Palda has aba ndoned Bat's Nest (one of hi s own lineage's Earth sites) and gone to res id e with hi s fri end Rumbana in Jagatumba Earth site. This choice of res id ence build s on a previou s connection , the fact that as a previous suicide it wa s Rumbana who induced Palda to kill him self. The connection ha s now been further reinforced in several ways. All the dead members of Paid a 's hou sehold have moved to Rumbana 's Earth site; while in the Underworld Rumbana has even married Palda's dea d sister Maianti and through her begotten a sonUl11 baby. Clearly , then , Palda 's irregular movements follow in the wake of Rumbana' s. After his redemption , Rumbana had ori ginally gone to Hollow Water Earth-site, as was appropriate. But as the genealogy shows in figure 8.2, the site in which Rumbana now resides, Jagatumba , is not one cultivated by his own Ancestors, but one belonging to his grandmother's brothers. Thus, through their crops, Rumbana's soul is now feeding households not in his father 's lineage but in the natal lineage of his grandmother, an in-marrying woman. To do this is to imply that the latter may claim to be his heirs. Why did Rumbana move? Let us examine the circumstances surrounding his death. It seems that Rumbana was widely loved in the village a nd that everybody had been deeply shocked by his death. For a long time his girl-friend could not bring herself to leave his parents' home or to find another man; his father Tabaro has given him four of the annual karjas instead of the usual three and still walks around 'like an empty shell (ku/taben, an empty grain basket made of stitched leaves and floppier than one made of basket-weave); his soul has already died (a puradan andrellg keid/e)' . Rumbana died unmarried and with him an entire branch of the Headman lineage died back (tad-) as far as Old Tabaro (generation - 3). Of the present Tabaro's three brothers and half-brothers ( - I), two died childless while the third when he dies will leave only a daughter, Gadi . All their considerable land was therefore due to come to Rumbana who indeed was already farming it with his aged parents. Where a man has sons , they will always be the ones to perform his stone-planting, so that inheritance presents no problems in principle. Where for some reason the stone-planter is in no position to inherit, uncertainties arise. The stone of the suicide Rumbana was planted by his father Tabaro but his inheritance will pass to other collateral relatives whose role in the stone-planting was only secondary. The ultimate



190 Dialogues lVith the dead
resolution of rival claims between these parties must wait upon Tabaro's own funeral; but meanwhile the claims and their justifications are being advanced and enhanced in numerous dialogues like the present one. There are three claims on Rumbana's estate. Two of these are from collateral branches within his lineage and one is from hi s cross-cousins, the descendants of Indiri's brothers. His fellow lineage-members participated in his stone-planting by each ' hitting a buffalo' . But though he is a linea ge Ancestor to all, they did not incorporate him as a full household Ancestor whom , for instance, they would expect to accompany them in the event of segmentation. Meanwhile his cross-cousins (who also live in the same village) 'danced a buffalo' from their part of the village to the stoneplanting site of his lineage. Each of these claims will be considered in turn. The reader will need to refer frequently to figure 8.2, where it will be seen that there are three bearers of the name Rumbana . As well as the young man whose suicide is at the centre of the present discussion, there is old Rumbana, the long dead man who gave him his name; and baby Rumbana , the current living holder of the name. It should also not be forgotten that although the living and dead characters involved converse on many occasions and through many shamans, the dialogue analysed here has passed through the mouth of Rondang who is the suicide Rumbana's mother.
I. Rumbana's closest relations are Tabaro's half-brother lani, his daughter Gadi who lives with him and through her his grandson, baby Rumbana . Gadi is a daughter who has stayed at home. Her lover, the impoverished Rutujen, is Sindi's son and the brother of Rumbana's fellow-suicide Palda . Their baby sons (of whom baby Rumbana is the only one still alive) were born in lani's house and received the names of lani's, not Rutujen's, Ancestors (cf. the naming of Ranatang's sister's baby at the beginning of chapter 5). The surviving baby is the closest heir genealogically and his claim has at one point been endorsed by the dead Rumbana when he passed on his name. On the other hand, the baby's claim is weakened by the fact that it passes through a woman and that he is a mere baby in a branch which apart from her contains only two old, tired men to fight his case.
2. The descendants of Andaraj and Mu'tuku: if lani's claim on behalf of his daughter and baby grandson were to fail, these more distant lineage members (led by Mengalu) would be the obvious heirs. Indeed, given the weakness of the link through Gadi, some of them may regard this as a foregone conclusion. Nonetheless, these claims have been fortified by the placing of some of their own Ancestors in Hollow Water Earth site. Old
•
I'v!ellJories alld remelllberers 191
Rumbana (generation -2) was the first person in the village to work land at Hollow Water, so that the rights to those plots belong only to those deemed to be hi.s heirs. Thus it was appropriate that he should reside there after death (for the story, see chapter 6). But since then , one Ancestor in eac h of these other two branches, (respeclively T itino and Bumbuden) has also come to resid e in Hollow Water (I do not know Titino's story; Bumbuden's story was also given in chapter 6). The web of claims is tightened by yet other connections. For exa mpl e, when Indomoro died childless, he also moved into Hollow Water, hi s widow married Mengalu and their next child was named Indomoro after him. The way is thus already open in principle for the claims of men in these two branches to the land at Hollow Water , claims which wh en the time comes will probably be supported by the intervention of their own Ancestors who already reside there .
3. Rumbana's cross-cousins, the descendants of Indiri's brothers: Rumbana's ostensible reason for moving to lagatumba Earth was that he wished to be with his grandmother Indiri. As a married woman with sons, Indiri's residence in her brothers' Earth site is itself irregular. As figure 8.2 shows, she is the destination of this entire migration among the dead. She was a woman from a fairly poor line which had numerous descendants, married into a line rich in land but short of descendants. So far as I can reconstruct it from this distance in time, the story appears to be as follows:
Indiri was Old Rumbana's second wife and the mother of his two younger sons. She died some time after all four of his sons had ma tured and the course of their lives appeared to be set. According to custom, her husband organised her first, main stone-planting while her brothers took her bones to their own site for her second funeral. But at some point after her death, she announced that she had gone to reside, not in one of her husband's Earth sites, but in one of her brothers', at lagatumba. This must have been soon after her death, since such moves seem to be made only then. I do not know what her sonum is supposed to have said at the time. But in making her move, she probably capitalised on the following points:
- firstly , she had no female descendants in her husband's line to whom she could transmit her name (Gadi was already too old to be named); - and secondly, it was already clear that the future of her husband's line was in jeopardy since his four sons between them had produced only one surviving boy (who was later to kill himself: the problematic Rumbana) .



192 Dialogues lI'ilh Ihe dead
We cannot now know whether additional factors were involved , for example a lack of intimacy such as was diagnosed between Jamano and his wife.
When her son Pandia subsequently died without an heir, his full brother Tabaro arranged his stone-planting, to which Indiri's lineage 'danced a buffalo ' as his cross-cousins. Because of the stone-plantin g, Pandia's property reverted partly to his half-brother Jani but mostly to his full brother Tabaro and through the latter was due to pass exclusively to Rumbana. Nevertheless, Indiri somehow recruited Pandia to join her in Jagatumba and the seeds were sown of a possible future situation: if Tabaro and Rumbana failed to produce further heirs (as in fact has happened), a strong case could be made out to those of his land-holdings which had originally been Pandia 's property.
Meanwhile the dead Indiri has been consolidating her position in Jagatumba . She offered her name to her own brother's great-granddaughter. I was present at this name-promising ceremony (abjien) in 1977. When I returned in 1979 the baby had died before the final name-giving (abFi/l11On) and I heard Indiri in dialogues insistently demanding another child to name and being told, much to her annoyance, that no woman in that lineage was pregnant. When Rumbana committed suicide in the summer of 1975 and went into the Sun, it was of course his paternal Ancestors, not his grandmother's, who went up there brandishing their weapons and brought him back. From there he accordingly entered Hollow Water Earth site where he remained for a while. Nevertheless, he too subsequently went to reside in Jagatumba. At the time of the present text, in September 1979, his move to this new residence remains his main message.
It is this message which is reinforced by his friend Palda in the dialogue and is associated with some bizarre-seeming goings-on in the domestic affairs of the Underworld. These goings-on are an underground parallel to a struggle between people above ground. They effectively provide the main forum for the working through of the latter. What we see in Sindi's encounter with her own sonums, for all that it touches her deeply, is little more than a sideshow. Gadi is the lover of Sindi's son Rutujen and the mother of his baby son. The messages coming from the various sonums through Rondang's mouth seem to pull the disputed land away from Gadi and Rutujen in favour ofIndiri's natal lineage. This is a further meaning of the prolongation of Palda's state of suicide and a reason why Palda's message is greeted so sourly by his and Rutujen's mother, Sindi .
Memories and remel11berers 193
In such a complex web of motive and feelin g, implica ting so ma ny people and spa nning generations, one could never be sure of all the stra nd s of one's interpretation , nor could one seriously 'verify' them with the people concerned. But the overall process seems to me to be similar to Panderi's case: here too analogies are created betwee n the situation s of different people. In the prese nt case the process of makin g analogies works in two stages: the first parallels the love-a ffair above ground between Gadi and Rutujen with a marriage in the Underworld between Rumbana and Maianti; the second goes on to parallel th e babies born of each of these unIons. The love of Gadi and Rutujen presents a serious obstacle to the claim of Indiri 's lineage. In most respects, the latter's hold on Rumbana's branch seems already secure, as this has been developed through shifts in Earth -site residence. Pandia and Rumbana have left their nonnative sites and moved to Jagatumba . Tabaro adores his dead son so much that it is almost beyond doubt that he too will go after his death to be with him in Jagatumba (as probably will Tabaro's wife, today's shaman Rondang, who was born into a distant lineage which is now extinct and so has nowhere else to go). However, there is a leak in Indiri's otherwise watertight case. By producing sons who are arguably the proper heirs, Gadi is making Rumbana's branch 're-sprout' (tambob-) and is threatening to take his entire inheritance with her. Serious attempts have already been made to legitimate these children as descendants within the lineage. The Kartia who died childless in generation -2 gave his name to one child who has since died; while his brother Old Rumbana took the rather uncommon step of giving his name to Gadi's other child even though he had one namesake already living, the Rumbana who was later to kill himself. (His intention, I understand, was to transmit his fields at Hollow Water, opened up with his first wife, exclusively to his descendants by that wife to the exclusion of his children by Indiri . This is a common distinction made between sets of half-siblings , here unhappily overtaken by his subsequent lack of descendants through Indiri.) Gadi is determined to set up house with Rutujen, and is thus likely to bear him more children, increasing the strength of their competition. If she moves to his house as his wife, the children become 'his' heirs and her chance of taking any substantial wealth with her would be very slim against her more obviously legitimate lineage-brothers led by Mengalu . Though her personal inclination is to do this, she is aware of these implications. On the other hand, Rutujen could move into her house as a 'kept husband' (lo-Iab). This is a common arrangement by which wealthy houses without



194 Dialogues Ivith the dead
male heirs welcome suito rs with poor prospects at home. H owever, Rutujen is still hes it a nt to do thi s sin ce it is demea ning a nd would leave him vulnera ble to sarca stic rema rks from the men of hi s wife's linea ge who would have been passed ove r as coll a tera ls. Ga di 's reluctance to press hi m to do thi s is promp ted by a des ire not to hu mili a te him, so th at they continue to live apa rt in an unresol ved an d un sa ti sfyin g domestic limbo . We ca n summari se the line-up for and aga inst Rutujen's ma rria ge to Gadi as follows: On Gadi 's sid e, her fath er Jani supports it , though to protect her cla im to Rumbana 's property he insists th a t Rutujen should move in with Gadi a nd not vice versa. Rutujen's ta rdiness in doing so is reinforced by the awkwa rdness of Jani 's presence, however beni gn, in the same house: the presence of parents- in -law evokes a range of embarrassments and avoid ances . Though most married women have no choice but to endure this, men generally avoid living with it. It is expected that Rutujen will be able to move in more easily after Jani 's death . Old Rumbana , by virtue of their baby's name, should be committed to lending his support from the Underworld to this move (though on another occasion, as part of another manoeuvre, he gave his name to yet another Rumbana, a son of Mengalu who is now dead .) Similarly, on Rutujen's side, his mother Sindi is pleased that his children may grow up in a wealthy house and prosper there . The other branches of Rutujen's lineage (and no doubt their Ancestors) are probably happy with this since, if his children should gain control of the land and subsequently die out, they could themselves be in a position to make a claim of the cross-cousin sort. The marriage is opposed by Mengalu and his lineage-brothers and by Indiri's lineage. It is the latter who are involved in the present discussion. The only way they can try to safeguard their hold on the inheritance is to ensure that even if it goes to Gadi and Rutujen , it will still come to themselves if there is a subsequent dying back among Gadi 's descendants. To do this, they must cut Rutujen's branch off from its collaterals (see figure 7.1) who would otherwise oppose them. This is best done by somehow infiltrating Rutujen's immediate family. It is Palda's suicide which has provided the opportunity to do this. This death had causes on two levels: his embarrassment over his incest, and incitement by Rumbana as a previous suicide. Rumbana was the village's Sun-death ringleader at the time and so the most likely inciter who could be diagnosed. But he can also now play the role of agent-provocateur: the fact that his new recruit is Rutujen's brother allows an edifice of interpretation to be built upon this recruit's death which reaches right into the heart of Rutujen's affairs.
Memories and remel11berers 19 5
F irstl y, as we have seen, their perverse ca maraderie ba sed on a sha red residence in Sun-Sonum ha s been ex tend ed even into their redeemed sta te in an Ea rth-Sonum , so that Pald a has a bandoned his own a nces tra l site and become the first member of his lineage to join the mi gration to R umba na's site Jagatumba . Subsequently , thi s fellowship is converted into a brotherin-law rela tionship . Palda 's younge r sister M aianti is al read y dead , a n unwed tee na ge girl alone in the Underworld. She then ma rri es Rumbana a nd becomes the second member of her family to reside with him in Jagatumba. Her dead brother Oindo and father Borjanu follow her. Borjanu's move cuts off the entire family from the rest of their lineage. Thi s marria ge is an analogy of that between Gadi and Rutujen a bove ground , but it is more than just this: it competes with it. The culmination of this analogy is the birth in the Underworld of a sonum-ba by who rival s Gadi's and Rutujen's own living son. The family in the Underworld not only rivals the one a bove ground , it actually disputes its claims and argues in the opposite direction . The message which comes from all Sindi's sonums who confirm Maianti 's marriage and baby, is that the suicide Rumbana himself does not wish his property to pass through his lineage-sister to Rutujen and their children . Rather, he will take Rutujen's dead sister and use her to create heirs for himself. The only child he has so far begotten in the Underworld is a girl , which I would perhaps interpret as meaning that this move is still tentative; but as with Gadi and Rutujen, more children are expected to follow . The fact that these sonum-babies cannot inherit land in the world of the living does not detract from the force of this message. The total patterning of such moves amounts to a system of law which is based on declared intention and is thus comparable to the making of wills. Of the possible living heirs, Gadi's children by Rutujen are being explicitly excluded. By his own residence and his recruitment of all of Rutujen's close dead relatives, Rumbana is firmly supporting the case of Indiri's brothers' lineage. However unwelcome , this message is no surprise to Sindi since it is currently being echoed or contradicted elsewhere in the village , in other dialogues staged by various households using the same or different shamans. The formidable opposition ranged against her can also be seen in terms of a poor family 's struggle as they try to marry into a wealthy one .
A metaphysical crux: translating sonums as private 'Memories' in a public arena
All three of the sonums in text 7.1 which I have classed as Recent are the focus of a clash of interest among the living for which the 'rules' provide no definitive answer. These clashes are based in each case on unresolved
i



196 Dialogues Ivith the dead
amb iguiti es in their relations with other people. I can not assert that this must always be the case for everyone who dies: many are inherited in a n unproblematic way, enter the most appropriate Eart h-Sonum site and stay there. Yet our three ambiguous cases are not likely to be merely because of the youth of the people concerned, or because one of them is a married woman . Owing to the potentially fissile nature of the lineage, even men like Jamano who die as elderly adults with substantial descent lines can be involved in such st ruct ural wrangles. However , what is so striking about Sora life is the way in which these ambiguities about property and structure are linked to emotional ambivalences and in which the two are so intimately interwoven that they can be discussed and resolved only together. In the present three instances, Palda's nastiness is linked to a complex series of strategies of inheritance; Amboni accuses her desolate mother of having transmitted to her a hereditary contagion from her own natal lineage (line 258) from which the girl's father's lineage have power to redeem her; while Panderi declares her love for Sagalo by belittling their cross-cousin relationship and trying to enter her father-in-law's Earth site rather than remaining in the Ra'tud site which has come to stand for her own brothers (line 286). In one and the same idiom there is a blend of intensely private feeling and of a negotia ted collective acknowledgement of how certain things stand in the disposition of the material and social world. How can sonums be at one and the same time so intimate and so public? Can we coordinate our account of a system of psychotherapy with an account of law and of the structural processes of the lineage system within which it operates? We can see this public aspect most clearly from a glance at the legal functions of dialogue. Perhaps its simplest such function is to produce a tally of the assets of the deceased . In chapter 5 Jamano listed in anecdotal detail all the debts which people owed him and which Ranatang would now inherit. Similarly, Panderi listed the jewellery which her brothers had snatched. This is common at funerals . Knowledge which otherwise would die with the person is returned to the public realm - public in the sense both that there are witnesses and that any disagreement should be aired there and then. More complicated are the matters which arise from the vulnenlbility of the descent line to demographic extinction. Genealogies contain many adult men without descendants, so that situations of Rumbana's type are not uncommon . At the same time, houses or lines with thriving descendants also want land on which to set their labour to work. The result is a speCUlative and opportunistic style of interpretation . The fact of Palda's
Melllories alld rClllembcrers 197
death , or the fact that Sagalo once gave SUllia a stone-planting, provides an openin g for the manipulation of further situa tions as they arise . Thi s is like a game of chess in which you are a lways working out possibilities several moves ahead , bLit in which your spec ulation s may be rendered null and void not simply by someone else's move but a lso by the sexua lity, the anger or the sudden death of any of the chesspieces. This opportunism is similar to that in political and lega l games played among literate populations in India (and elsewhere) with documentary weapons. It also resembles those grea t psychological art-forms, the frame-up or the confession under duress, in that it seizes upon a small and indi sp utable detail and builds from it an entire case which mayor may not be true , but which is prillwfacie plausible and persuasive. One remembers' Mengalu's difficulty in denying accusa tions of murder by so rcery: after Jamano 's death , I sometimes wondered whether Mengalu any longer knew himself whether he had killed him or not. As in any other legalistic forum, the discussion moves over time from discord to consensus. But people can at first behave with a bad grace when they do not hear from the sonums exactly what they want to hear: Sindi reacts sourly every time she hears about her daughter's marriage in the Underworld, and both Ranatang and Panderi's brothers threatened to call another shaman when an inquest was not going their way. Thus we can say that competing and incompatible private perceptions are exercised in the public arena until they arrive at a point of compromise such that the dead person's residence of the moment is agreed, and eventually to the point at which further moves no longer occur at all . So far the discussion has followed the lead of the Sora themselves and considered the state of mind mainly of the sonums as they move around space and send us corresponding messages. But clearly this is intimately related to the state of mind of their living interlocutors. Can we now be more specific about the relationship between moods of the dead and moods of the living? From the moment of a death and perhaps even during the terminal illness, the mourner's reaction to the event is moulded through the conventional attributes of the Experience into which the dying person is being assimilated . It is not possible for survivors to contemplate the death of someone who was close to them without being influenced by the terms which themselves provide the framework for that contemplation. The extreme distress which dead people sometimes arouse in those who contemplate them is linked to their being seen as still wholly or largely trapped in their original death-Experience. As Panderi put it (chapter 7,




198 Dialogues Il'ill1 Ih e dead
lines 287- 93), th ough Ances tors strive to redeem her, the Ra'tud -People will no t yet release her to go with them. W hile the Old sonums receive routine disrespect, Recent sonum s may be gree ted with the utmost tend erness. Yet along with thi s, Sindi 's speech to Amboni conce ntra tes ex plicitly on the fea r that she may perpe tu a te her Experience, just as the conversation with Palda , thou gh hardly tend er, fo cusses on his rol e in p recipita tin g Sa rsuno 's recent attempted suicide. T he empathy which their shared state o f mind ma kes the survivors feel with the patient, goes ha nd in ha nd with fear for themselves: often those whom one loved the most while they were alive, o ne fears the most once they are dead . Yet though it causes us pain to recall the Experiences from which our loved ones died, their personalities are not obliterated by this Experience but reta ined as they deliver their messages ea ch in his own styl e. Sindi 's son urns Borj anu , Maianti and the sterling Oindo all corroborate Palda's unwelcome message about his move, his marri age and his baby; but in the tone of their respective exchanges with Sindi they remain noticeably different from each other. This distinctiveness is accentuated by the way in which the sonums dwell repetitively on the details of their stories, such as Panderi 's route home and how she drank her wine, or Sara'ka's encounter with a blacksmith-sorcerer (sonurn No . 15). Indeed the consta nt rehearsal of a story can become the main part of a dead person's presence. The moments at which significant new information can emerge about the circumstances of the dead are usually at the various stages of the funeral sequence, passing through the funeral shaman. In between these landmarks, the dead reiterate their current position and use it to play repea tedly on our feelings in their present form before they a re ready to progress to their next one. Consider the devastating effect on Rungkudi of Amboni's son urn. Though I could not stay in the field long enough to check thi s, on the analogy with other cases I have followed I would expect Amboni some years later to be saying that Rungkudi was a good mother and that no grudges remained . Because of the veri similitude achieved through the cruelty of these early conversations, this later modification will be equally convincing. It will be matched by little Amboni's growing disinclination to perpetua te her sca rs and a greater tendency to make herself known only as an Ancestor and resident of an acceptable Earth site. Sonums respond to us, then, according to the way we feel about them . To Sindi at the moment, her son Palda is a bumptious oaf. But to the living descendants of Indiri's brothers, he is a strategica lly convenient and welcome new 'Ancestor' . Panderi is loving to her husband , reproachful to her brothers. Sonums' moods vary with the person they are speaking to and they are diagnosed as appearing in the form in which one thinks of them.

But the ra te at which the deceased moves away from hi s o ri ginal death-Experience is rela ted , not to the pa ssage of time as such, but to the develo pment o f the fee lings which the so num a nd the mourner ha ve for each other. T here are two stages in which we ca n develop our und ers ta nding of this. T he first, whi ch is mo re immedi ately accessible to us, is to keep my own perspective as a living subj ect. I shall discuss each of these perspectives a t length , in the present and the following sections respectively. The causal connection, firstly , between my own past and my own present mental sta tes suggests that we are dealing with an aspect of memory. The sonum's new state can take over only gradually because the memory persists of what things were like before the tra nsition. Reversions then appea r as throwbacks or recrudescences of these old memories so that it requires frequent confirmation of our newer feelings to repress them . A rite of acknowledgement reaffirms the redemption in the funeral : 'Don ' t go and stay with Leopard-Sonum', says Kantino to his father (i dong dakune KlI1aslIJ1 amang) .



200 Dialogues with the dead
Who is it, the sonum or the mourner , who still remembers their own previous state of mind? In his work on Dinka religion, Divinity and Experience , Lienhardt has written (1961: 149) about the
difficult question of differences between Dinka and European self-knowledge which I can discu ss only inadequately. The Dinka have no conception which at all closely corresponds to our popular modern conception of the 'mind ', as mediating and, as it were, storing up the experiences of the self. There is for them no such interior entity to appear, on reflection, to stand between the experiencing selfat any given moment and what is or has been an ex terior influence upon the self. So it seems that what we should call in some cases the 'memories' of experiences, and regard therefore as in some way intrinsic and interior to the remembering person and modified in their effect upon him by that interiority, appear to the Dinka as exteriorly acting upon him, as were the sources frol11 which they derived.
Where Lienhardt uses the word 'memory' only in passing, it seems to me that this is perhaps the closest single-word gloss available in English for 'sonum' . This term should be understood in the sense of ' a recollection' (as in the French un souvenir), not in that of the mental function 'memory' (la l1u!l11oire). I shall write 'a Memory' with a capital 'M' in order to keep the possibility of using the word in other, more ordinary senses. It is normal both in Sora and English to say that one 'remembers' (aY-II11-, compounded from an unknown element plus -1111, 'feel') one's dead. It is this very normality which forces our epistemological problem into the open. Though the idea of remembering other, dead people is commonplace in both English and Sora its significance in the two is very different. For us, both popularly and as a technical term in psychology, this kind of memory, like others, is held to be located in the mind of a rememberer and to have no autonomous existence outside. Consider, for example, the following:
Undoubtedly I was made aware of my friendship by the grief that Jean's death was causing me, and little by little I became terribly afraid that since the friendship would have no external object on which to expand itself it might consume me by its fervour and cause my death. Its fire (the rims of my eyel ids were already burning) would, I thought, turn against me, who contain and detain Jean's image and allow it to merge with myself within me. (Genet , Funeral Riles, tl'. B. Frechtman, 1969: 28)
The narrator is being eaten alive by his Memory of a dead person. But with the lack of any 'external object' this sonum, here called an 'image', is explicitly 'contained and detained' within the narrator's own person. Even ifit 'turns against' him, it will do so from within. A similar view, written out by Freud as a technical analysis, will be discussed in detail in the final chapter. For those of us to whom this kind of idiom rings true, calling a sonum a
AIelllories alld rel1lel11berers 20 I
Memory while it roams at large outside the mind of any rememberer engende rs a piquant sense of contradiction, even of oxymoron, since it is clear th at this is no mere literary conceit as for instance in the title of Nabokov's autobiography Speak, Memory (1967). To the extent that we accept this gloss for sonum , we are obliged also to accept that when a So ra goes to a shaman to have a trance performed, she does indeed confront him with the speech of hi s Memories . Notwithstanding his awareness of a theatrical element (see chapter 10), then , there is no meaning to be had in denying his sense of the reality of this encounter. Thus 'the Memory ofPanderi ' refers to her sonum as it converses with her living rememberers, such as Sagalo and Sindi - or her brothers, as she snubs them and expresses her yearning for Sagalo who embraces her through the shaman and feeds her with wine. Tllis is of course not the same as to talk about these people's 'memories' (with a small 'm') (Sora: an-aYlll1-en) of her. This transla tion can be read back through this book to yield propositions like 'Palda is a nasty Memory to his mother'; 'Kantino's father resides in Leopard-Memory'; 'All illnesses and deaths are caused by Memories'; and 'The rauda leads the Memories one by one to speak with their rememberers. ' It is through this play between their interior and exterior properties that Memories are able to be both deeply intimate and publicly negotiable. A sonum is my Memory of a person whom I loved (or not, as the case may be); it is also someone who can turn in conversation to address anyone of a dozen assembled rememberers in a manner specific to each of them and at the same time common to all; in addition, it is someone who resides in a feature of the public landscape. Because Western memories of the dead remain within the mind of the rememberer and have a small om', they are seen as 'subjective' phenomena and therefore have a degree of plurality and social irresponsibility which is quite opposed to Sora thinking. There, on the contrary, a Memory constitutes part of what corresponds for them to our 'objective reality'. Subjective and objective (for which Sora has no words) do not form a dichotomy separated by a gulf, but rather lie along a continuum or perhaps overlap, so that several degrees can be distinguished. There are those phenomena such as dreams which are perceptible only to their experiencer (though even here, the dreams of shamans have a public dimension); and those such as omens which are perceptible to others too but of concern primarily to their main experiencer. The former category perhaps corresponds most fully to our 'subjective', the latter already rather less so even though they are still applicable only to certain people. Encounters with Memories lie near our 'o bjective' pole. By the very act of perceiving a
•



202 Dialogues IPilh Ihe dead
Memory you are forced to act out in public your mutual relationship and to coordinate this with other people's similar relationships. When you talk with your Memory, this relationship is a complementary one to which you both come, simultaneously, each from your own perspective. But as your relationship moves on in time, you do not simply playa complementary role but become a successor to your Memory. If it is an Ancestor-Memory, you become his descendant; if an Experience, you relive in your own right, from the same perspective, the first person's original experience of death (figure 8.4, above). There is an analogy between this and the succession of inheritance and naming through Ancestor-Memories. Like inheritance, this transference too is made possible by the encounter of two persons within the flow of time. The two perceptions take place not simultaneously, as with complementary roles, but in succession. The event which is perceived the second time is a repetition of the previous event, but the fil'St sufferer's subjective perception has been transferred to you. Your present state is now the outcome not of your own, but of your attacker's past.
Verb forms: how the dead transfer their own states of mind to the living
Voices ;11 the Sora verb
One's property and name, just like one's mode of death, are inherited through the medium of inheriting a state of mind. All these transfers are mediated through dialogues with Memories. The transfer itself takes place by a principle of repetition or succession in which a new person comes to replace a previous one. Yet within the structure of time revealed by this transferral, there is a further shift: as the victim of an Experience is redeemed into Ancestorhood, what he comes to transfer to his descendants is modified from a form of death to other, more desira ble parts of his social person. The first kind of succession, that of illness and death, is discussed in a distinctive language of violent encounter. Panderi's first remark on her arrival in chapter 7 was 'I got eaten up I got drunk up (literally 'It ate me up it drank me up, ajumingle againgle)' (line 269, repeated at 273);' and later 'they ate me up fresh-and-alive (ronglapada jumlingji)' (285) and '[the Ra'tud-People] won't release me won't let me go! (a'nomdaing iienji len a 'nomdrengingji len)' (293). Similarly, Jamano said 'they're prodding me ... they're roasting me (sujlingji ... mollingji)' (chapter 5, line 104). In all these expressions, the experiencer is a passive victim on whom other, active persons are perpetrating their violence. Even when being redeemed, the person remains passive: for example, in chapter 7, line 287,
Memories and remell1berers 203
Panderi says '[Ancesto rs] redeem me (Iandilillgji)" just as in chapter 6 the Ancestors had sung about Jama no ' Let us lead him toward s us let us bring him by the arm towards us (aorungnaiba alonglongnaiba)' (line 53 and elsewhere). How does 0ne make the transition from the pa ssive to the active mode of ex periencin g, from being eaten to eating, from being redeemed to becoming oneself one of the redeemers? One kind of answer can be sought within the grammar itself of these verbs. Here what grammarians would call passivity and activity are represented in a distinctive and powerful way . By calling it powerful , I am not proposing the kind of extreme Whorfian view (Whorf 1956) which would suggest that the thinking of Sora theology and psychology is entirely dependent on the grammar of the verbs through which it is expressed. Indeed, as the quotation from Lienhardt showed, in some fundamental respect Sora thinking resembles that of the Dinka of the Sudan, who presumably have a very different language structure. Likewise, the mutual permeation of Austroasiatic (i.e. Sora) with Dravidian and Aryan linguistic influences is extensive and the Sora use their language to think thoughts which are sometimes quite 'Hindu'. Rather, the argument of this section aims to demonstrate that Sora practice exploits opportunities in the grammar they have in the service of objectifying their Memories and making them public. By analysing this use, I hope that we shall be better able to grasp the dynamics of the transference of different aspects of
personhood . There is a strong sense in which all Sora verbs look forward to a predicate for their fulfilment. The impulse of a verb is either taken up in a construction of motion or destination which I would call intransitive but still active; or else turned back on itself (intransitive because reflexive or middle); or if transitive, it comes to rest or embed itself in an object. Sora grammar is not so alien here from Indo-European. Three voices can be distinguished which I shall gloss as 'active', 'middle' and 'passive'.2 Of these, active verbs are mostly transitive (except for verbs of motion), middle verbs intransitive or reflexive, while passive verbs are formed from the active with a suffix derived from the personal pronoun which represents the object. In what follows, I shall show how the active and passive voices are associated with the Experience Memories and their modes of death, while . the middle voice is used to express the central act of the redeemed Ancestors, that of giving their name to a descendant.

Since this sense of transitivity generalises experience by spreading it around many experiencers, it raises problems for our grammatical categories of 'subject' and 'object' . For the Indo-European languages at least, and widely elsewhere, it is conventional to speak of the 'subject' and 'object' of a transitive verb. When the verb is shifted from the 'active' to the 'passive' voice, the object becomes the subject: thus 'the Memory eats the person' becomes 'the person is eaten by the Memory'. However, the terms 'subject' and 'object' are misleading and the situation is better represented by the terms 'agent' and 'patient'. In this, the agent is the person who initiates or performs the action, the patient the one on whom it is performed. The advantage for our present purposes of this formulation over one in terms of subject and object, is that the patient remains the patient of the action regardless of whether he is the 'object' of an active verb or the 'subject' of a passive one. A simple transitive sentence thus contains the elements shown in figure 8.9.
Figure 8.9 Sentence about Memory containing agent, verb and patient.

The Memory eats its rememberer

To explain the transmission of an id entical Experience from victim to attacker , T shall show how the perception of the transitive verb is transformed within the patient's own consciousness so that he becomes the new agent of that same verb and seeks to transfer his former passivity on to a new patient. The lin guistic expression of this process is given a di stinctive twist by the absence of a genuinely passive form despite the need for a passive meaning: there is no construction equiva lent to 'I am being eaten by so-and-so'. As we sha ll see, this gives a certa in property to the sense of victimhood: morphologically, the patient can never be the grammatica l subject of a passive verb but only the object of an act ive one. This means that someone else - an agent - must be the subject. What makes this active verb equiva lent to a passive is that the patient is emphasised largely at the expense of the agent who is played down or omitted .) Words in this morphological class come in three semantic types:
1. verbs with no agent, but a possible patient, such as words of weather, e.g. logel-I -e, 'it nights', i.e. 'night falls ', ganur- I-e, 'it rains'. These a re formed from an active third person singular which may be called 'impersonal' , since it is impossible to specify any agent by asking who or what is raining or becoming night. Such verbs can either stand alone without a patient (since the events they denote take place regardless of any perceivers), or else they can take as a patient the person who perceives them: logel-I-ing, 'It benights me, 1 am overtaken by night' , ganur-I-ing, 'it rains me, 1 get rained on'. In the latter case they amount to type 2 below. 2. verbs with no agent where a patient is essentia l, such as words of personal sensation, e.g. asu-I-ing, 'I am ill, it hurts me .' These are similarly formed and it is similarly impossible to specify any agent. However, they cannot stand without a patient since the event denoted is dependent on the perceiver in a way which is significantly different from the type above. 3. active verbs with a patient where an agent may also be specified: as in 2, these actions are discussed through the perspective of the patient. But in addition, an agent is implicit, e.g.jum-I-ing , '(someone) eats me', i.e . 'I am eaten' (cf. figure 8.6 above). This type is extremely common in Sora, with a singular or plural agent. In some forms, especially in the singular, a third-person agent marker may be entirely absent4 but the patient is always c1ear:jum-l-ing, '(it) eats me' (where the agent marker -e is elided);jum-I-ing-ji (where the -ji signifies a plural agent) , 't hey eat me' (cf. Jamano's mol-I-ing-ji, 'they roast me') .

What we have here is a transiti ve ver b in which the age nt is present but is left unclea r or unspecifi ed in favour of a hi ghly specific ·patient. This patient is the perso n who reports the even t. A more accurate trans la tion ofjum-l-ing would therefore be '(a gent un specified , suppressed or implied) ea ts me'. It is this avoidance of specifying the agent which allows technically active verbs to give a passive sense. The function of divination is to unma sk this latent agent. At first, the sick person says jum-I-ing or jum-l-ing-ji, '(agent or agents unspecified) are eating me', which is the only possible Sora equivalent to 'I am being eaten.' It is in the very act of confirming this when it saysjum-I-ai, 'I am eating' (or i1am-l-ai, 'I am seizing' and many other aggressive verbs), that the attacking Memory identifies itself. Only now does the patient have the knowledge to say, for instance, ' Ra'tud-Memory is ea ting me.'

The roles of agent and patient implied in active transitive verbs lend themselves to the aggressive vocabulary of encounters between livin g persons a nd the dangerous Memories which we have called Experiences. But the Ancestor-Memories show that there is another way of linking predecessors and successors, one in which a Memory creates an analogy without aggression. I shall discuss the expression of this through the verb abi1l1nlenai, 'I name (someone) after myself.' This is what 1 have called a ' middle' verb. Verbs in this class cover a range of what may be called middle and reflexive uses, meaning to do something for oneself, to oneself, etc. The form of such verbs is shown in figure 8.11 (using so-, 'hide', since 'I eat myself is not normal Sora usage) .

Middle verbs of this sort have a double patient. 6 The first of these is fully internal or reflexive, i.e. 'myself'. It can be seen here how the Sora middle construction gives a similar kind of identification which in many IndoEuropean languages is provided by a 'complement' in the same 'case' as the 'subject'. The second patient is more external. It may be represented by an adverbial phrase of position in space (s/ 'mg Imgen dakutenai, 'I stay in the house', Uyung-slll1en amang majlenai, (said by, e.g. a suicide) 'I merge within Sun-Memory'. It is thus not so external after all. Such verbs create an analogy between the agent and something outside himself, in such a way that they become somehow less separate from each other. In the context of relations between the living and their Memories, the key verb here is abi'illnlenai, 'I cause to bear my name.' The root al1l1n ' name' plus the causative particle ab- or -b- give the active form abfillnlai (a-b -l1l1n-l-ai), 'I name (i.e. cause to bear a name)' , used for example with the patient /emsoden, 'a dog'. In the sentence 'I name a child after myself ' the child , though an 'object' in conventional grammar, is not a patient in our sense of suffering or undergoing an experience . Rather, he has been identified with the agent in a way which makes him like the grammarian 's 'complement'. Our grammatical review of the way in which a dead person perpetuates himself among the living may be summarised as follows: there are two modes of transmission of properties from dead to living persons. Both operate by creating an analogy between the first person and the second and both have an impetus which in principle continues ad infinitum . But in the two cases there is a difference in the kind of analogy and in the way that it operates:
I. The categories of the Experiences are rooted in human frailty and their classification is based on a phenomenology of suffering. Analogy here serves as a principle of causality. It is seen as a negative principle because of the unhappy events and conditions which it causes, yet an unavoidable one. It is counter-social and its choice ofpaths is summed up by the verbgorod-, 'wander' or 'leap'. This transmission of suffering through activity, as its grammar implies, is aggressive and involves a transitivity of events. 2. The category Ancestor is rooted in social continuity. It esta blishes itself as a transformation of the Experiences by means ofa redemption in the funeral and a transition through Earth-Memory. This restores something of the social element which was destroyed by each person's death-Experience. Analogy here serves as a principle of succession, a principle which is seen as positive because it maintains and perpetuates



2 10 Dialog ues Ivith th e dead
the kin -group . Its path is disciplined, as a judici ously select ive movement along the a va ilable routes of kinship . This form of tra nsm ission is equiva lent gra mmatica lly no t to transitivity but to a middle with a co mplement , sin ce the repetition is one not of action but o f bein g: it is a transference not of events but o f some degree of identity.
T he gra mmatica l difference between these two forms of tra nsmission is that with transitive verbs, the Memory is caught up in the swing of the active/ passive pendulum . One ca nnot normally transmit one's name until one has settled permanently into a redeemed state, which involves freeing oneself altogether from active transitive verbs, with their aggressive vocabulary , and remaining instea d entirely within the scope of verbs having a middle form . (This does not of course mean that fully redeemed Ancestors do not 'ea t' sacrifices, 'see' their descendants, etc., only that this kind of active verb is no longer the vehicle for their self-perpetua tion) . We are now able to locate the precise point at which a dead person is able to switch from being passive victim to active attacker, as well as to describe the very process of this switching. As one might expect from the elaborate cosmological background , this point turns out not only to be one of mood and time but also to have its locus in space. The residential sites on the landscape are the very points at which the dead change their moods and switch back and forth between desirable and undesirable ways of
perpetuating themselves. Figure 8.13 gives the most important verbs prevailing at various stages in the relationship between Memories and their living rememberers. These cover the moments of attack, of recovery or else of death and absorption, and of redemption and eventual renaming. I have laid the verbs out so that we can follow this process across time, while holding constant the point of view of one and the same consciousness as it passes through the various stages of being a Memory. Chronologically, the person moves from top to bottom of the diagram; in term s of the evolution of hi s con sciousness or sta te of mind , from right to left. These sequences show a clear progression from the fully pa ss ive, in the sense that the agent can be specified (co lumn 5), to the ' imperson a l' (column 4) which is ' passive' in form but with no possibility of specifying the agent, to the middle (column 3). At this point the Memory rests, being neither patient nor agent. These are verbs of being, of state. It seems no accident, therefore, that it is at this stage that he is conceptualised as residing in a site on the land scape. The Memory 's location here in a fixed point in space summarises both his own past and his potential to act on others over time and thereby to make their future resemble this past. His first move in this direction is through the root gorod-, 'wander, revert', an active verb (column 2) which, though like all verbs it seeks a predicate, does not need a patient because it is intransitive and so is satisfied with a construction of motion. But this is only a means to a state of full transitive activity (column I), in which he will necessarily seek a patient. This aggressivity is only the other side of the coin of the Memory's passivity, since he is inclined to attack only because he is still suffering from his original death-Ex perience. The passive verbs which will lead him out of this aggressive perpetuation are tandi- and urdi- , 'redeem' (chapter 6). When redemption is finally accepted and he remains an Ancestor, his only activity will then be to redeem others; while at the same time in the middle voice, he will progress beyond a mere state of shared existence with fellow Experience-members towards the positive gesture of renaming. We earlier replaced the concept of grammatical subject by an emphasis on agent and patient. Now, as we see a single person move from patient to agent, we may return to the term 'subject' and through it to the question of 'subjectivity' . Each person moving across the diagram is the speaker of all the verbs: he is, throughout, a constant perceiving subject. In the early stages, while he is a patient slipping from life to death, the living are concerned to know about his perceptions. Whether in a divination for

illness or in an inquest in which the 'pat ien t' ha s ju st died, it is for hi s sake that the agent is made exp licit. But by the time he him self becomes an agent, people's attent ion is already shiftin g to the perceptions of his new victim. The questioners want to know about the old patient's identity, thoughts and feelings largely to the extent that this will enab le them to act on behalf of the new patient whom he is attacking. The latter, in turn , is now the new perceiving subject. However, within this passive state the divination is also co ncerned with a shared state of consciousness. The patient is not a lone and it is the very possibility of specifying the agent which ensures that this is so. Let us return to the semantic range of the three kinds of verbs which can appear in the 'passive' form (see p.206). Our types I and 2 gave us a vocabulary of sensations and perceptions in which an agent can never be specified, e.g. g[[nul'ling, 'I am rained on'; {lSuling, 'I am ill '; m[[lIggaling, 'I am tired'; ken idling, 'I die'; ai1U1l1daling, ' I need to urinate'; etc. Rather than calling these ' impersonal', it may perhaps be better said that as verbs of sensation they put their emphasis on the patient (the perceiving subject: 'it's happening to me') rather than on the agent (the grammatical subject: 'it's happening') which does not need to be specified. On the other hand, the passive type 3, jumling, 'I am eaten'; Hamling, 'I am grabbed', keeps the same perceiver but has the potential to externalise the agent. I would characterise this distinction as one between a 'closed' and an 'open' passive. A closed passive focusses purely on the perceiver and his perception and ignores or downplays the question of agency. An open passive, by contrast, is prepared to specify its agent under interrogation as part of a quest for knowledge about causality. Thi s causality is uncovered through dialogue, a procedure which at once brings the patient's perception into the public realm . During a divination the vocabulary of the open passive sort allows the impersonal aspect of transitive verbs to be personalised: 'Who is grabbing me?'. The shared consciousness which is created in the dialogue places the patient and his supporters in a relationship of close identification as fellow victims, actual and potential. By drawing together to identify the attacking agent, the patient and his living supporters locate it outside the circle of their gathering. Whether in a healing or in a funeral (where the 'patient' is already dead but has not yet become an agent), they attempt to confirm this exteriority. But at the same time, this location of the source of his suffering outside the sufferer himself converts his priva te perception into a public one. Since many people have their own separate relations with the Memory in question, this process requires a compromise, certainly, but also yields a
J\1elllOries alld relll elllberers 215
co nfirmation of the reality of their experience and of its calise. It is the lise of la nd scape wh ich allows Memories to ex ist both as shared perceptions a nd - between the times when they are currently act ive in the life of any rememberer - independent ly. It is this, too, which allows the Memory to a lter the dynamics of his involvement with ot hers by making the change from passive to active while holding constant the idiom ofRa ' tud, Leopard or Sun . The land scape is not merely a repository for the storage of names of people, nor even of the knowledge of what happened to them. It also contains the potential for the repetition over time of the events which that knowledge is about . Thus the difference between points in space not only allows the Sora to conceptualise the differences between kinds of eve nts by ho ldin g them apart, but also se rves to perpetuate those differences indefinitely into the future. Much of modern Western metaphysics, from social science to quantum physics a nd catastrophe theory (Thom: 197 5), is preoccupied with explaining changes in the form of entities or events, on the assumption that this is a problem . Sora thinking seems concerned on the contrary to account for the problem of their constancy. This constancy is explained through chains of human carriers whose feelings move in and out of tune with each other and who are thereby constitutive ofeach other's biographies.



9
Forgetting the dead
The progressive dissolution of the person after death
Through this book 1 have argued that the So ra person should be understood , not so much as having an essence of personhood, but as being a changeable confluence of attributes and traces of events. His formation is gradual and it would be hard to specify the point at which the person matures to his most complete or individuated state. The decHne and disappearance of the person, which will be traced to their conclusion in this chapter, form an equally gradual and complex process. While the Experience categories like Leopard-Memory and Sun-Memory presum ably exist indefinitely, the persons who pass through them on their way to becoming Ancestor-Memories are very much creatures of time. So if sonums are Memories and their mourners rememberers, what is the nature of forgetting? The formal stages ofthe funeral process appear to take three years, but this is not the end of the person. Memories are sa id to 'fade' (masuna-). But just as their nature wa s initially formulated in the public arena , so they will fade in the same way. In tracing this process, we sha ll move from the more intimate atmosphere which was so important in establishing thedead person's nature as a Memory , towards the socia l, economic and legal processes whose outcomes persist lon g after all emotional involvement has ceased. The dead inhabit a world which is real but.wh ich is different from ours. The dialogues in chapters 5 and 7 give an elaborate picture of the continuation of normal social life after death . Further marriages and changes of residence occur among the dead for the same kind of reasons as they do among the living and are reported to the living and discussed with them . This discussion covers who among the dead is drinking with whom, who is Hving with or visiting whom, even the banalities of hoeing,
Fo rge llillg th e dead 2 17
harvest ing a nd baby-sittin g. At the sa me time this wo rld of the dea d is not sim ply a continu ation of the world of the living, but a topsy-turvy parody of it. T he seasons are reversed, as well as ni ght and day ; people also say that the dea d keep doves as ch ickens and pythons as cows, and that they hun t living humans as game an ima ls. It is because the two world s are no t only ana logous but also so much at cross-purposes that they impin ge on each other in a way that is a lwa ys problematic for the living. The trees 1 need to fell to clear the gro und at my hoeing season are at the same tim e my Ancestor's bea npoles during hi s ripening seaso n (chapter 7, lines 70 ff); the dead drink the soul of our alcohol and leave it tasteless (chapter 7, lines 136~7) ; they have their own agriculture, yet are perpetually hun gry and demanding; a nd above all , they feed on us and 'take' us to join them. Yet if we in turn impin ge too intimately on their world , we a re unable to ret urn: a sick man who accepts the food of the dead is bound to die (chapter 5, line 77); by working or drinking in the vicinity of an Earth site or wa shing in its water we lay ourselves open to absorption by that Earth (cf. Old Rumbana in chapter 6, see p.138). As the previous chapter has shown, the very process of remembering is itself slanted to our disadvantage from the beginning, since by their own activity the dead always place us in the passive. Yet the living are not obliged to suffer all this as victims without hope. The internal structure of the concept Memory itself offers us the way out. Our task in facing the Experience aspect of our dead is to deflect the impetus of this aggressive form of their self-perpetua tion into their benign self-perpetuation as Ancestors , to persuade them to convert their active verbs of eating into the middle verb of renaming. This aggressive impetus does not encompass the whole of the dead perso n, but is more a temporary mode of his being. A dead person is composed of several elements, attributes or qualities (1 am not sure which to call them) , so th a t in his passa ge across time he describes a multiple trajectory, like a burst of stars from a firework . After his death , various of these attributes are remem bered with a different intensity and forgotten at different rates. Thi s corresponds to a variation in the way in which their impetus, which can never be simply annihilated, is transmitted to new persons . I can identify at least six such elements:
(i) his membership of the Experience which originally caused hi s death: this amounts to an initial , immediate assessment of his biography, coloured by the suffering of his dea tho To the extent that this is nega tive, it is transformed during the stone-planting by redeeming the deceased



2 18 Dialog ues Ivith the dead
and turning him into an Ancestor. The transformation is not completely successful at first. The dea d person co ntinues to revert and to tran smit hi s original co ndition to others. His rede mption is reinforced each time he is bani shed in a hea ling rite. Over time, these reversions become fewer and less virulent, but by the time he is full y redeemed he is likely to have caused the death of further persons. The impetus of the Experience is transmitted to other person s as shown in fi gures 8.7 and 8.8.
(ii) his state of bein g an Ancestor: this is a positive analogy to hi s dea th-Experi ence. A n Ancestor has a privileged a nd fairly beni gn relationship with his direct descendants. The impetus of his Ancestor role is spread ove r an ever-widening circle of descendants who share thi s Ancestor in common . It thus governs (iii)- (v) below.
The tension between Experience and Ancestor, which is so prominent in the early days following a dea th, expresses the power of dea th to sunder its victim from the society of the living. Yet the goal of redemption has already been declared at the stone-planting and from the point of view of the deceased, the rites of acknowledgement addressed to him when he causes an illness amount to a reassertion and reinforcement of tllis redemption . !his redemption reintegrates him into a wider lineal society which Incorporates the living and the dead into one continuous flow. By the time he has become an Old Memory many years later, a dead person 's de.ath-Experience is of Ijttle interest or is even unknown. But long before thIS, as chapters 7 and 8 showed, there is a further concern with another opposition, that between Earth sites which are viewed as mutually exclusive alternatives. Even where redemption is taken for granted, this can remain an area of keen dispute . The disputed or ambiguous Earth affiliations in those chapters also involved the inheritance of material property and the renaming of descendants, which were seen to be closely intertwined . Within the state of Ancestorhood, further attributes of the deceased also include the folJowing, which will be discussed late r in tills chapter.
(iii) his residence in an Earth-Memory: here the deceased is united with a sub-group of fellow-kinsmen in the ground from which they feed their descendants: which Earth-Memory a person chooses to reside in is problematic for all married women and sometimes for men . The impetus of Earth-Memory membership can still a lso be expressed in the Experience idiom, if the dead person causes death with symptoms of swelling and blockage; otherwise, this is another positive analogy to his death-Experience which comes to approximate closely to Ancestorhood as the impetus is shifted to the transmission of property and name. In
Forge lling the dead 2 19
either case, the impetu s is weakened over time as the Memory becomes diffu sed over a n ever-widening ra nge of descendants.
(iv) hi s material property: this is similarly transmitted and diluted in an eve r-w idening circle with each generat ion . Its relevance is overtaken each time by the death of its new owner, when it forms part of a newly con stituted body of inheritabl e material and effectively ofa new person. The material dispersed in this way includes the land which was associated with hi s res idence in an Earth site.
(v) hi s name; this emerges as the outcome of the previous two attributes, though the problems with Rumbana's inheritance in chapter 8 show how it can a lso be ma nipulated in an attempt to force that outcome. The emergence of the name suggests that the transmi ssion of the previous two a ttributes has been acco mpLished. But in addition, since its impetus is supposedly transmitted to one single person, it denies the dispersion implicit in the inheritance of Earth sites and property. In some sense, then, it re-concentrates something of the scattered personhood of the deceased.
Is this a ll there is to a person after he dies? As we trace the destination of each of these aspects, we shall still be left with an irreducible residue:
(vi) his presumed subjective consciousness: this is the aspect of the deceased which eventually dies a second death in the Underworld and turns into a butterfly (discussed at the end of this chapter).
The completion of inheritance and the waning of Earth-Memory sites The complex dual nature of Earth-Memory was first explored in chapter 6. There I presented it as a cosmological intermediary which brings together the cross-cutting categories of Experience and Ancestor. Although it can act as an Experience to cause death, it does so through an imagery of retention and wholeness, the same imagery which also allows it to become a rallying point for redeemed kinsmen who have otherwise been dispersed by their various death-Experiences. It is thus an essential staging post on the way to the state of Ancestorhood. Earth sites are focusses of agricultural activity, partly domesticated clearings in jungle space. It is this mutual superimposition of the two kinds of space (chapter 4) which may be sa id to de-Experientialise the concept of Earth and to Ancestorise it. In the short run Earth-Memory functions as a full Experience: in this sense it focusses its unwelcome attention particularly on childbearing sisters who through the normal workings of exogamy



220 Dialogues Ivith the dead
have give n themselves elsewhere. In the lon g run , however, this imagery co ncerns itse lf more with the placing of males in the progressive evo lution of the lineage. Eart h used in this way becomes an idiom for the perpetuation ofa man's property. In this sense, it can only draw away from any reference to the symptoms and ci rcumstances of the deceased while alive or around the time of his death. Earth-Memory thus becomes a concept which is cut loose from the moorings of the dead person's actual experience an d thus ultimately not an 'Experience' at all. However, the lineal unity which Earth-Memory helps to create will not la st forever. From an even longer perspective, an Ea rth site will be overtaken by the natural breaking up both of the lineage and of the sites associated with it. Co nsid er first of all the historica l pattern of Sora land use. The stockpiling of Ancestors in sites would seem to suggest that those sites are precious, as indeed they are under today's conditions of acute land shortage and declining fertility. But at the same time, the stories of migrations and expansions which I collected for several lineages all talk of moving steadily uphill under press ure from Oriya and Telugu settlers in the plains. The latter stories seem to imply a frontier into which one expands wherever possible. Land once claimed has not always been abandoned afterwards, especially where it can be converted into paddy fields, but the role of Ancestors is strongest where that land is recently claimed. I For example in the middle of the valley-floor at Alinsing, which has been completely cultivated as permanent paddy-fields for six or more generations, is the Earth site in a spring called Barang. Though it is said to have belonged once to a lineage who are considered the original inhabitants of Alinsing, today it contains no dead people and attacks no living people. The field in which the spring actually rises now belongs to Mengalu and Raduno, but they disclaim any connection with the Earth-Memory in the spring. It has simply 'faded ' (masulIa-) or become irrelevant as the frontier of cultivation was pushed uphill towards the edge of the valley where (as a glance at figure 4.3 will show) the active Earth sites are now concentrated. Though it can be used as a ' Police Station' (lana) by members of any Earth site at the edge of the valley in order to 'arrest' (i1am-) bathers, they act in the name of their own sites and nothing can happen in the name of Barang itself. In the terminology of chapter 4, it was once a residence but is now just an outpost. While the named site remains, 'now there's no Memory there' (nami sonumen agasa) . We may say that like an old volcano, the site exists but is extinct. There are as many Earth sites dotted across the landscape as there are springs and water holes, and many more than I have been able to show on figure 4.3. Any of these may become a residence after death for any man
'P
Forgellillg the dead 22 1
who works a paddy-field which is watered from that site or a sh iftin gcu ltivation slope in the vicinity , so that they can serve as title-deeds for his descendants to substan tiate disputed claims to such plots. Every man who works land near the site has residing in each of those sites one or more direct male Ancestors, as well as wives and sisters . The names of all of these (though married sisters may be disputed by others) shou ld emerge only among his own descendants or among those of a lineage-brother whose share, and structura l position, is equ ival ent to his own. The particular exemplars or Earth sites are opposed to each other. Or rather, it would be better to say at first simply that they are distinguished from each other since it is the events in peoples' lives which pitch Earth sites against each other as rivals. A range of Eart h sites can be spoken of as synonymo us when referring broadly to a linea ge or branch, but they will also be distingui shed at closer quarters by the particular members who are placed in each site. They are often referred to in speec h in doublets (chapter 4) and like any pair of terms in a doublet they will be seen in some contexts as synonyms, in others as alternatives between which a choice is to be made (cf. chapter 4, footnote 4). The ways in which these sites are grouped together or opposed to each other ma tch the na ture of current ba ttles and alliances. For a married woman the issue is generally between alternative Earth sites, that of her brothers versus that of her husband , as in Panderi's case. For most men, at least if they are not caught up like Palda in complicated cases across lineages, it is between sites which are at first grouped closely together as pertaining to the same branch of a lineage but which gradually become more separate as these branches grow apart. For example, figure 7.1 showed how the two branches ofSagalo's lineage are already associated with separate clusters of Earth sites. But when Sindi, representing one branch , speaks to Panderi on behalf of Sagalo from the other, we see her using these sites indiscriminately. She urges Panderi to go to her father-in -Iaw's Earth, but gives this as 'Bat's Nest, Bodigan, lelabbab, Kupa' (chapter 7, line 286). These are four separate si tes (not all of them marked on figure 4.3) which are actually worked not by Sagalo's branch but by the branch of Sindi's own husband . However, although the verbal technique of doublets here makes these sites look like synonyms, they need not be close together on the map, and furthermore each individual Ancestor resides in only one of them: in line 131 of chapter 7 Sindi says she has forgotten whether Indupur resides in Sangkaroren or Sal-Tree Water, and he explains that he wOltld have gone to Sal-Tree Water, which he used to cultivate, but that two of his lineage-brothers invited him to drink (and reside) with them over in Sangkaroren. We can see something of the wider pattern which this creates by looking



222 Dialogues lvilll IIIe dead
at the ro le of just one Earth site, Sangkaroren. This site happens to be relevant for both Sagalo's and Mengalu's people, even though they belong to different lineages. Just as each kin -group, and even household, may work land in the vicinity of several Earth sites, so representatives of more than one unrelated kin-group may assemble independently after death in the same Earth site. The effect of this on the ground is shown in simplified form in figure 9.1. There it can be seen that Mengalu works paddy-fields around the spring itself at Sangkaroren while Sagalo's uncle lkaram works a part of the hillside above. The Earth-Memory at the Sangkaroren spring thus contains Ancestors of both of them . But the significance of this Memory is different in each case. This difference is related not to the amount or type of land they work there, but to the timespan over which they have been working it and the associated history of persons. For Ikaram, Sangkaroren is still an active recruiter, either as a cause of death or by redemption from other Experiences. Thus, along with the other Earth sites of his branch such as Laiba, it is one of the residences of his Memories Nos. 1- 6 and 16 in chapter 7. For Mengalu, by contrast, Sangkaroren no longer recruits new members and has not done so since the generation of his grandfather. From his father's generation onwards, his Memories now go to reside in Hollow Water, Rere and Sweet Mango. Why is this? If we look closely at those ofMengalu's male Ancestors who
