I was on ly partially able to confirm the Earth residence of people in generations -3 and -4, from the memories of Mengalu's generation about who was enumerated in rites of healing when they were children. I think this means that in those earlier days it was still possible in their households to think that their dead in Sangkaroren might attack them, because not all their inheriting and renaming had yet been completed . The next generation, now young adults and below, know these names only as Ancestors and expect them to visit their houses and be fed only in this form . For younger people, these Memories are passing out of living memory. For Mengalu's line, Sangkaroren has become obsolete. One of the Ancestors' original motives for residing in the Earth site, to guarantee their descendants' heirship, is no longer necessary since the inheritance is now complete. There is no further point to be made by any new recruits in entering Sangkaroren after death. Instead , the site which now recruits members ofMengalu's family most actively is Sweet Mango. What is now his plot was cleared later and it was only then that this site began to be active as a Memory for his family. Of the first round of its residents only a second Andaraj (generation - I) and Poke have so far renamed, while even these namesakes are still very small children whose chances of surv ival are uncertain. Here, we see what a feeling of commitment to an Earth site can mean. Mengalu's son Rumbana died young in 1979. The verdict was that he had been taken by Ancestors who had ganged up with Ra'tud-Memory. The song of redemption was



224 Dialog ues lVith th e dead
designed to send him to Sweet Mango , into th e ca re of Poke a nd Jonu. Mengalu loves the a rea around Sweet Mango a nd spe nd s much of his time working there in so litud e. It is here that he expects to reside after hi s own dea th and it wa s because of a disputed plot by this sp rin g that Mengalu was accused of Jamano's death. However, Mengalu's dea d child was not allowed to reside there. The suicide Rumbana later cut into the dialogues with remarks like 'He's my namesake, I'm taking him to stay with me in Jagatumba '. We are now in a position to unders tand what a well-aimed blow this change of residence wa s to Mengalu's feelings about both hi s child and his land sca pe. The placin g of the dead has implica tion s not only for relations between affinal lineages. It also affects future lines of development within the lineage itself through the divi sion between these lines of the property for which the named Earth sites stand. Earth-Memories function as potential title-deeds for possible inheritors in a collateral position, should the entire range of front -line inheritors die out. So what matters is not simply to keep each site populated, on behalf of the lineage as a whole, with representatives from any of its branches. Behind the apparent synonymy of a group of Earth-site names lie the seeds of separation and rivalry: where segments begin to split, as Jamano's is in the process of separating from Mengalu's, the quasi-synonymy of their Earth sites will develop into an opposition betweel; alternatives. At that stage, these segments will have become separate lineages. They will then be able to intermarry and their women will be caught between the two groups of sites which will have become incompatible alternatives. Within its imagery of indivisible bounded solidarity , then, the concept of Earth-Memory is already preparing for the lineage's segmentation . Even where a linea ge does not split, an association with an Earth site cannot la st for ever. Sooner or later the actual piece of land and the group for whom it stands are likely to become separated. An Earth site serves to support the share-out of inheritance down all lines until this seems assured through renaming, most commonly after i;lbout two generations . But it is this very renaming which will destroy the increasingly artificial unity for which the Earth site has stood: through accomplishing the renaming, the EarthMemory 'fades' because it has abolished the relevance of its own imagery .
The joy of naming a baby: retrieving a crucial attribute of the deceased What one might at first sight call the funeral rites last for three years. But it is now clear that a dead person's ' funeral' is really closed only with the name-giving ceremony of his successor. The naming ceremony completes a
Forgelling the dead 225
sequence which bega n with the first inquest the morning afte r the predecessor's cremation, anything between three years and a hundred years earlier. T he naming ceremony (abiilll1-oll, form ed from the eleme nts 'ca use-to-bear-a-name' and 'chil d') uses the same funeral shamans , the same Ancestor-Men and Ancestor-Women , the same tunes and forms as do the stone-planting and harvest-commemoration (/(C/lja). T he gist of the prose dialogue during the trance is simil arly paralleled by the AncestorMen who sin g kata dialogues, as well as other songs almost identica l to the redemption cha nts in which all the lineage's A ncesto rs a re once more li sted . But the wording encapsulated in these form s is of joy and fulfilment , a message which one ca nnot escape as one hea rs this set into the tunes and verse forms of the earlier occasions. Ad mittedly , for many the sto neplanting a nd especially the karja were a wild party . They drank a nd da nced for three ni ghts while an orchestra of oboes and syncopated drum s passed through a succession of tunes and beats associated with categories and stages of Memory : the cremation-beat, the Underworld-tune, the Sun-tune when the Memory of a suicide arrived . But at the centre of the swirl of revellers, clustered around the shamans, there had always been a still group of weeping rememberers.
9 Dancing in the street at the a nnual karia . The stuffed peacock on a pole is a ' royal' offering to the dead.



226 Dialogues IFith the dead
Now, at the name-giving, nobody weeps. The songs and dances take place inside the packed house not amidst laments but amidst shrieks of helpless laughter and obscene horseplay (gatarsi, literally ' hand-scratching' , also meaning 'tickling'). Throughout, the theme is prosperity in the future and joy here and now. A prominent theme is that the Ancestor-Men representing the dead namesake enter the house bringing a branch of a particular thorn (kumba/i) which they hang over the door inside the house to block the entry of attacking Memories. This is the protection whjch the dead namegiver is supposed to provide for the new na mesake against even Ius own death -Ex perience. When I sang this once in a team headed by Mengalu for a baby of our lineage, we claimed to have picked the thorn and brought it all the way from Tungganrutung, the original start of their migration six grandfathers ago. There are numerous subsidiary themes: the mock battle during the day in which people throw pots of turmeric water over each other (the cooling medicine used at birth); the evening of pantomime which includes danced and sung enactments of the baby's future marriage, copulation, cultivation, full harvests , and successful battles with moneylenders and officials. Every image in the earl ier stone-planting and harvest-commemorations now appears to have been a promise of this moment. Four examples will illustrate this.
10 The turmeric fight at a toddler's name-giving ceremony.
Forgett ing th e dead 227
1. RemOl·illg the rillg alld fulfillillg a promise
One of the central acts of the name-giving is the removal of the brass finger-ring which was tied onto a bracelet around the infant's wrist at the name-promising rite (ab-ji-en, 'ca using the ring to be tied') where the newborn chi ld was singled out by the Ancestor soo n after its birth about three years previously. This is supposedly one of the rings which each participating household had given to the previous holder of the name along with their buffalo , grain and wine, and whjch had been used at the dead person's stone-planting and harvest-commemorations. Despite its promi nence, the Sora are able to say very little about the meaning of the ring, and the texts sung do not elaborate. Given the gynaeco logical associations of metal and the fact that the name-giving coincides, at least in structural time, with the end of breastfeeding and the supposed resumption of normal sexual relations between the parents, I suspect that the ring represents a completely closed and solid form for the soft material from which the child has been moulded. This seems to be echoed in the iron plough-tip on which the baby steps when entering the house (chapter 6, line 212).
2. Callf/iba/isil/g all effigy of the decease£/
In some villages at the stone-planting and harvest-commemoration, each participating household contributes a handful of grain which is ground
II Ancestor-Men untying the Ancestors' rings at a name-giving ceremony. Two children are being named on the same occasion.



22 8 Dialogues Illith the dead
into flour by the Ancestor-Women or Ancestor-Men. The flour is then mi xed with water into a paste and fashioned , in Alinsing into a lump or cake, in some villages into a human effigy. T his is then charred on a fire (perhaps representing the pyre) and eaten by the males of the same household s. C hildren may al so eat it, but on no account should it be inges ted by the lineage's unma rried daughters or sisters p ast puberty , as this would make them sterile. No -one was able to give any explana tion of this although the taboo was obviously felt deeply by the girls. But it seems }o me to mean that once a girl has swallowed a portion of the soul of her own patrilineal Ancestor, she is held fast by this incestuous act and will be unable to produce children for the lineage of another man. In other words, this representation of cannibalism is strictly confined among the sexually mature to those through one of whom the perpetuation of the deceased person must pass. The grain which was contributed by all inheriting households had been ground into flour in order to be reintegrated into a single ' person'. What the men are eating is not so much a fragmented share in that person, as when they divide his property, but rather an equal share in the potential for another aspect of the person to emerge on the name-giving day, whole and undivided in the line of just one of them .
3. Making references to ,'egetation which branches ji·011l a single rootstock
In the redemption chants at the stone-planting, much vegetation imagery was used and all of it is brought up again and developed today at the name-giving. The reader may remember that riadi and purpuri (chapter 6, line 208) are two kinds of frequently-branching grass which form mats as they spread out in a circle from a centre to which the stems remain traceable. Similarly, banana plants (chapter 6, line 46), as well as being a symbol for fleshy, sappy vigour, are also reproduced by suckers and offshoots which form around the base. Butid (line 201) is a bristly tuber with long filament roots radiating out. Tarab, laia and singkung (lines 43--4) are climbing creepers which wander from tree to tree, far from their original rootstock. They embody many other properties which make them apposite for the context: the bark of singkung is used as a cooling medicine in childbirth and helps easy delivery because the ripe seed cases spring open and the seed pops out. A further related word which happens not to occur in that text is 'tambob-' (from bob, 'head, apex') which is used of trees and shrubs to mean to sprout again after being cut down to the rootstock. When used of a man after his death, it means to have male heirs. This kind of imagery confirms that the dead person's Earth site was not
Forgetting the dead 229
completely equival ent to the state of Ances torhood but was only a mean s towards that state. Despite their empha sis on whol eness and retention , Earth sites still fragm ent the lineage into sub -groupings . Earth-Memory ha s a somewhat inward -turning, ungenerous, inces tuou s imagery and in terms of p la nt life is associated onl y with waterside pl a nts a nd weeds of rice fi elds (cf. text 6.3) , never with the dry land and mostly aerial bra nching creepers and pinnate-lea fed trees which a re used to represent the lineage. The latter express an outward-going vi gou r as they ramify indefinitely at the tips without losing the unity of their rootstock (cf. chapter 6, text 6. 1, lines 201~2, 2 0 8~9 and the Sora 's own use of ,branch ', ' twig' and ' leaf ' for sections of the linea ge) . I am inclined to see this as an ima gery of the reproduction of the group which spreads free of the divisiveness inherent in the sexual reproduction of humans. Here are no separate consciousnesses and identities with their problems of diffused cores and weak boundaries, no exogamy, no experience or Experiences, no passions or grammatical passivity. The new baby stands at the head of a line which should ramify for ever ~ except that unknown to us , Sun-Woman has already prepared a fatal Experience for it, and ifit is a girl the additional wrench of exogamy is probably lying in wait for her. Both exogamy for women and death-Experience for all people tear the person away from the patrilineage. It is probably here that the analogy between them, which seems to me implicit in Sora thinking throughout, comes nearest to being made explicit.
4. Entering the house
From the time of the stone-planting, an Ancestor both is and is not constantly present in the house of his or her descendants. In one sense, Ancestors are always there by virtue of the stone-planting and the passing on of their inheritance. They are in the grain which is stored and eaten there and which recently in -married wives a re not allowed to fetch down from the loft, and they receive little libations of drink at the foot of the main pillar. In another sense, the Ancestor was led out to the stone-planting and passed through the stone into the Underworld. From there his Memory will sometimes wander back of its own accord and make its presence known by following someone home or otherwise entering the house. If it has done this in the form of his death-Experience (or of one of its sub-Experiences), it receives a banishing-rite; if as an Ancestor, it is fed inside the house and allowed to find its own way home or else kept for a while in a pot or wall painting. Thus the terms of residence in the house granted to the Ancestor in these contexts are conditional.



23 0 Dialogues lVith the dead
At the name-givin g, however, the Ancestor is exp licitly inducted into the hou se by opening a closed door to the Ancestor-Men who impersonate him . Or rather, they pretend to break down the door, but do so to the great joy of all those inside. The force of this action a t the name-giving ca n best be appreciated by contras ting it with the other occasion on which the dead knock at a closed door, at the kalj a which commemorates the village's dead collectively.2 Towards nightfall on the first eve ning of the kalja the funeral shama ns sit on the cremation-ground of the lineage who are providing the year's representative house. They go into a preliminary trance in which each person who has died in the previou s three years (in Alinsing in 1979 there were twenty-two of these) announces his or her arrival and is greeted with tears and embraces. Everybody then returns to the villa ge a nd the Memories who have been raised come with them. One group of AncestorMen (for the kalja , those of all the village's lineages are pooled) barricade themselves inside the locked house while the others stand outside in the darkness armed with axes, swords and arrows, and sing demanding to be let in. The Ancestor-Men inside sing their reply on behalf of the living householders, widows, children and others. They raise doubts and objections, especially about the authenticity of the petitioner. The whole business takes several hours, since it must be gone through with appropriate wording for each Memory's family. Finally, the Ancestors break down the door and force their way in. They are followed by a riotous crowd of dancers and musicians who have already been drinking for some time elsewhere. At this point the pyre-lighters start dancing on the pitch of the roof outside, shaking the house and tearing a hole in the thatch in order to lower the bamboo ladder into the hole of the mortar. The ladder reaches through the mortar into the Underworld and a pile of grain and pea seeds is poured around its base. The ladder duplicates the Ancestors' path earlier in the evening when they were led from the cremation-ground to the door. Now the Ancestors are able to climb directly up it and enter the bodies of the shamans who sit on the mortar. The main trance takes place here at the foot of the ladder a t intervals during the rest of this and the two successive nights. Yet towards the end of the thii'd night the Ancestors are sent back down the ladder to the Underworld and told not to return until the following year. Outside, their buffalos are being massacred in the dawn light and their souls sent down with the Ancestors. Then the Ancestor-Men chop up the ladder with axes to the accompaniment of frenzied dancing on the shattered fragments. They will destroy this path a second time later in the day when they take these fragments to a cremation site and burn them . The name-giving echoes the kalja by replicating this motif of the
Forgelling the dead 23 1
Ancestors' forced entry, but gives it a new and optimistic twist. T hough there is no ladder, the two parties of Ancestor-Men sing in exactly the same way on either side of the closed door and event ua ll y break in . But this time the entry is perma nent, in the form of the name whi ch during the trance on the mortar will be attached definitively by the Ancestor to the child , through the act of un ty ing the ring . To the extent that the Ancestor is in the name, he or she now resides in the hou se as one co mponent of the baby's person. It is not so much a personal attribute as a social one, that aspect of the person which identifies something of the child 's ancestral origins a nd which the living ca n best apprehend a nd manipulate as a la bel. It contributes towards the formation of so meo ne who, while he is a new person, is defined and circumscribed as such by an elaborate socia l co ntext which enco mpa sses both himse lf a nd hi s predecessor.
The ultimate residue of the person and the final death of Memories Most of the strands which went to make up the deceased have by now been dispersed and diffused among many living persons, while his name has bee n returned undiluted, normally to a single living successor. The return of the Ancestor's name is no reincarnation , nor is the continuity from one namesake to the next cumulative, as in the idea of karma. It is a repetition of the name, implying perhaps a repetition of the person's lineal role but not of his biography or destiny, which would have included the repetition of his encounters with Experiences. Meanwhile the Ancestor continues to exist as a separate person , a Memory who can talk to and protect the second namesake . It is this aspect which will eventually die again in the Underworld , be cremated a second time a nd turn into a butterfly. Who or wha t is the dead person by this last stage? In order to understand the significance of this, a complete phase of time should be seen as embracing not two but three holde rs of a na me. There are a lways two concurrent holders of the name at the centre of the stage, either one living and one dead , or else both dead and with the more recent one seeking a baby to name while the older one waits in the wings only long enough to see him successful. The work of the first holder is done only with the appearance of the third. I have tried to represent this in figure 9.2. The stages shown are not necessarily of equal duration, and it can be see n that the state of being 'alive' (am eng) is only the first out offive stages which I have identified in the person's total known existence. In each of these progressive separa tions, one of his attributes returns to opera te among other living perso ns whjle his other attributes remain fully dead. F irst he passes on hi s Experience as pa rt of becoming an Ancestor ; as an Ancestor, he then hand s on his property; finally another attribute, this time ILis name, is separated off and returned to the rea lm of the ljvin g. Yet the existence of a second dea th in the Und erworld shows that there remains some ultimate residue which continues its onward trajectory. This residue appears to be the dead person 's subjective consciousness which persists while being progressively stripped of attributes. If there is any resemblance here to the wider Indian religious idea of mo/qa, liberation, this resemblance comes with a quite different valuation. For the dead , their condition is not so much a purification and release as a deprivation and progressive attenuation. It is undoubtedly better to be alive than dead , and life is not a preparation for death . The ordeals ofa shaman's initiation a nd practice involve daily journeys into a state of temporary death . But this is done not as the personal voyage of a mystic or ascetic but as part of the shaman's participation in the affa irs of the living, in their emotions, hea lth and social relations. The living have food, warmth, sex, friendship, a productive economy, and laughter (I have heard the dead crack wry jokes but never heard them laugh). The Underworld contains a bleak pastiche of ljfe above ground. The agriculture of the Underworld fails to feed and the only sustenance which satisfies the dead is the soul (puradan) of people, animals, grain and alcoholic drinks from this world. It is this valuation of death which gives the recently dead their intense desire to interfere in our affairs and leads them to ask repeatedly to handle and wear the personal belongings, tools, clothes and ornaments which they used in life (cf. chapter 5, line 43; chapter 7, lines 251-8). This valuation of death constitutes the essence ofloss both for the living and for the dead , which lies in the very fact that the former will forget the latter. The dead have their own feelings but it is the living from whose perspective the whole system is ultimately conceptualised. It is in their first ,
Fo rgetting the dead 235
unbearably pain ful Experience aspect that Memories are at their most personable. Everythin g a bout the deceased which has character is retrieved so far as possible and brought back to the realm of the livin g. As the dead drift inexorab ly away from us towards butterflyhood they also become more inaccessible and unknowabl e. At times, the shady lower branches overhanging a stream are sett led with swarms of butterflies. Sora people say that butterfl ies are indestructible, since when they flock to a pot of sweet wine they apparently drown but when pulled out they come to life again a nd flyaway. But they are a lso inarticulate and characterless . Butterfl ies are beyond the rea ch of di a logue a nd they receive no cu lt. A person 's l-Iltimate residue, his lonely co re, cannot even be spoken to. It can never find its way back into the society of the living and must ultima tely die again and lose even the society of the dead . From the subjective viewpoint of the deceased, by far the greater part of his consciousness if not a ll of it recedes beyond the range of the awareness of the living while remaining in a state of self-awareness - except that it no lon ger ha s any experiences of which to be aware. A butterfly is a consciousness which cannot cease to exist, but which has no attributes because notrung can be known about it. Incapable of activity and action, liberated from passivity and passion but also deprived for ever of company, butterflies are Memories without rememberers. This, at last, is the final dea th of the person.



10
Dialogues with the self? Sora bereavement and the presuppositions of contemporary psychotherapy
How can we understand Sora dialogues with the dead in terms of our own lives, our own memories and forgetting? The feelings oflove, apprehension, jealousy, loneliness and companionable cheer seem recognisable, at times piercingly so . But the way in which these are put together seems strange. In particular, many readers may have difficulty in believing in, or even conceiving, the intense two-way traffic which the Sora say passes constantly between the realms of life and death. The deaths of those around us foreshadow our own and are something which we must all face. But different societies give people very varying means with which to do this. Gorer, in an examination of mourning in Britain, traces a radical change between the Victorian era and his own, a change which he pinpoints around the period of the First World War. After his brother died, he says, people treated him kindly for a while, but soon expected him to resume normal life. Then, he writes,
I refused invitations to cocktail parties, explaining that I was mourning; the people who invited me responded to this statement with shocked embarrassment, as if I had voiced some appalling obscenity .. . educated and sophisticated though they were, [they] mumbled and hurried away. They clearly no longer had any guidance from ritual as to the way to treat a self-confessed mourner; and , I suspect, they were frightened lest I give way Lo my grief, and involve them in a distasteful upsurge of emotion. (GoreI' 1965: 14)
Gorer's sister-in-law was similarly unsupported socially and 'let herself be, almost literally, eaten up with grief' (Gorer 1965: 15). Gorer concludes that 'this lack of accepted ritual and guidance is accompanied by a very considerable amount of maladaptive behaviour' (Gorer 1965: 110). What could constitute 'adaptive' behaviour and how could we recognise it? It is perhaps impossible to say for certain when, if ever, someone has
9'
Dialog ues with th e self'? 23 7
recovered from loss. Certainly , as a system of psychotherapy , Sora dia logues with the dead a re not inevitably successful. F or example, T a baro ha s already prolonged the funeral cycle of his son Rumbana from the usual three annual kalJa s to four , but still remain s a numbed , empty 'grain ba sket' (kliltaben) . Again , the Sora reli a nce on the a bility of the dea d to express themselves articulately in lan guage is of littl e help to Ra na tang when ILi s baby dies in chapter 5, or indeed to the famil y of any infant (from the Latin in ~ral1s, ' not-speaking'). This problem is compli cated by the difficulty of di stingui shing grief as a feeling from mourning as a process and an activity , since it is the latter which has the more outward, public form and has attracted more attention from anthropologists. The greatest cultural elaboration may not always coincide with the point of deepest feeling (cf. R. Rosaldo 1984: 185ff, 192). Thus, a mere formal description of the funeral of Ranatang's unnamed infant, attenuated as it was on the grounds that the baby did not yet have a full soul , could not do justice to what we saw of the depth of the parents ' feelings. But the evidence presented throughout this book should be more than enough to challenge the popular view still often heard in the West, that people suffer grief less acutely in societies where the mortality rate is higher. This view is also widespread among historians of the Western past (e.g. Aries 1962: 38- 9; Stone 1977: 70, 420, 680, both cited and opposed by Macfarlane, 1979: 106-7; cf. Macfarlane 1981: 250- 1) . But in most cases, the order of the Sora funeral sequence does link public procedures and supposed inner feelings. So long as the dead keep reverting to their Experience aspect, it is clear that their rememberers have not yet been released from the most intense phase of their grief. Subsequently the dead and their mourners enter less intense phases . The pacing of the three-year funeral sequence does not suit all deaths and all mourners, and we have seen cases which remain highly problematic even after their third karja. Indeed, the fact that Memories do not ultimately die their second deaths until around the time that their living rememberers are undergoing their own first deaths, is an implicit statement that one remains affected by all one's Memories for the rest of one's life. The healing of the mourner, whatever this is, lies in the Memory's changes of form but not in the Memory's annihilation. We can advance our view of the adequacy of mourning procedures only if we enlarge our horizon to examine their entire scope within the society they serve. It is precisely because the Sora ideas of the person, of memory, of loss and healing, invite comparison with concepts from the relatively asocial disciplines of psychology and psychiatry, that the need for this



238 Dialogues with the dead
approach becomes so apparent. In proposing the gloss 'Memory' for 'sonum' in chapter 8, I sketched in a contrast between the exteriority of a sonum and the interiority of a Memory as the latter must appear in such secular Western fields of discourse . What kind of bereavement therapy will be genera ted by interpretations of the interior sort in which the Sora style of dialogue is not possible? The modern industrial society of which anthropol ogy is itself a part is extremely varied. Its approaches to death include various interpretations of Christianity; adaptations of Buddhist eschatology (Fremantle and Trungpa 1987); spiritualism and parapsychology (Thouless 1984); secularised death rites (Lane 1981: 82-6; Huntington and Metcalf 1979: 184-211); widely cast theological and anthropological comparisons (respectively Hick 1976; Bloch and Parry 1982); and the sociology of clinical necessity (Hockey 1990). Despite this variety, the upsurge during recent years of interest in death and mourning is certainly related to a widespread sense that procedures in our own society have become inadequate. (Or perhaps this variety is itself a symptom of this inadequacy, which extends to the sheer physical encounter with death. For example, as a British adult I handled and even saw my first corpses only in Sora land.) The concern which underlies Gorer's remark about 'maladaptive' mourning has since grown into a significant movement. The professions of 'bereavement counselling' and 'thanatology' arose through such pioneers as Kubler-Ross in the United States (1969) and Saunders in Britain (Saunders 1959; Saunders and Baines 1983). Numerous professional and self-help books have appeared with titles like Death and the family: the importance of mourning (Pincus, 1976), The courage to grieve (Tate1baum, 1981), Grief counselling and grief therapy: a handbookfor the mental health practitioner (Worden 1982). The emphasis in this literature on 'facing' or 'coping' clearly supports Gorer's point (1965: 110) about a 'lack of accepted ritual and guidance': Facing death (Bertman 1991), Coping with cot death (Murphy 1990), Bere{/\lement: a guide to coping (Golding 1991). This movement has been linked with an interest in death in other cultures, in the hope that these less 'maladaptive' forms of behaviour can somehow be integrated into our own in order to reform the latter. I But our analysis of Sora mourning suggests that, to the extent that we judge them successful, such practices cannot easily be broken up for eclectic appropriation. Nor can our own practice easily be stretched to accommodate them. For a possible reason why this may be so, I should like to turn to a very finely written paper by Freud called Mourning and melancholia (1957, first published in 1917). This paper, whether acknowledged or not, seems to be the source of most subsequent secular Western treatments of bereavement
Dialoglles ll'ith the self'? 239
within the idiom of psychotherapy. It is a theoretical statement from which a model ca n be extracted and laid side by side with one derived from So ra practice. An initial comparison of these models in formal terms will lead us to an enhanced understa nding of the scope of dialogue within Sora society as a whole. My choice of classic Freudian psychoanalysis does not gra nt it any absolute interpretative status (a s in much psychoanalytic anthropology) but merely reflects its position as arguably the Western world's lea ding blueprint for practical intervention in the mourning process. Indeed , the point of comparison would be lost if psychoanalysis were taken as anything more than an ethnoscience from modern industrial society. The fact that this hegemonic literature derives mainly from Freud rather than from, say, lung (e.g. 1959 [1934]), whose collective unconscious is hardly ever mentioned , has its own consequences and merely serves to sharpen the contrast. 2 Freud's model depends on a contrast between 'mourning' and 'melancholia' as two possible responses to bereavement. He presents mourning as a normal condition which is resolved with the passage of time as the mourner's 'libido' withdraws itself from the deceased, who is referred to as the 'loved object'. Melancholia, on the other hand, is a pathological disturbance of this process in which this healing fails to occur: the bereaved person's 'ego' turns in on itself, 'identifies' with the lost object and loses interest in stayin!! alive. In either case the involvement between the two persons is so intense that 'the existence of the lost object is psychically prolonged' (Freud 1957: 245):
this withdrawal oflibido is not a process that can be accomplished in a moment, but must certainly ... be one in which progress is long-drawn-out and gradual ... [FJirst one and then another memory is activated ... If the object does not possess this great significance for the ego - a significance reinforced by a thousand links - then , too, its loss will not be of a kind to cause either mourning or melancholia. (Freud 1957: 256)
In normal mourning,
[rJeality-testing has shown that the loved object no longer exists, and it proceeds to demand that all libido shall be withdrawn from its attachments to that object ... Normally, respect for reality gains the day . Nevertheless its order cannot be obeyed at once .. . [W]hen the work of mourning is completed the ego becomes free and uninhibited again. (Freud 1957: 244-5)
The ego accepts this 'verdict of reality' (Freud 1957: 255) because it gains a narcissistic satisfaction from being alive and does not wish to share the fate of the object that has been 'abolished'. It therefore severs its attachment to that object (Freud 1957: 255).



240 Dialogues lVilh Ihe dead
In melancholia , on the other hand ,
[a]n object-choice, an a llachment of the libido to a particular perso n, had a t one time existed; then, owing to a real slight or disappo intment co min g from this loved person , the object-relationship was shattered ... [T]he Free libido was not displaced on to another object; it was withdrawn into th e ego. There, however, it ... served to establish an identification [em phasis original] of the ego with the abandoned object. Thus the shadow of the object fell upon th e ego, and th e latte r co uld henceFo rth be judged by a special agency, as thou gh it were an object, the fo rsa ken object .. . This substitution of ide ntifica tion for object-love is a n important mechanism in the narci ssistic a ffections. . . (Freud 1957: 248- 9)
Now 'one pa rt of the ego sets itself over aga inst the other, judges it critically and, as it were, takes it as its object' (Freud 1957: 247) . 'The patient . .. reproaches himself, vilifies himse lf and expects to be cast out and punished' (Freud 1957: 246) since '[i]n mourning, it is the world wllich ha s become poor and empty; in melancholia it is the ego itself ' (Freud 1957: 246). The final result is a loss of self-regard so severe that the patient even loses the will to live (Freud 1957: 246). This is related to the bereaved person 's identification with the deceased in a particularly interesting way:
Hostile impulses against parents (a wish that they should die) are repressed at times when compassion for the parents is active - at times of their illness or death. At such times it is a manifestation [of melancholia] to reproach oneself for their death ... or to punish oneself in a hysterical fashion . .. with the same states [of illness] that they have had . (Freud 1957: 240)
The resonances between this and Sora thinking on bereavement are remarkable. Freud's formulation reads almost like a direct translation of that of the Sora into an alternative set of metaphors. There is a comparable intense attachment, based on memory, between the bereaved and the deceased; a comparable gradual, painful withdrawal which eventually leaves the successful mourner ' free' ; an ambivalence between compassion and hostility; a risk of a shared fate through an identification between the two parties; and, where the entire process fails, a comparable loss of the will or ability to live. But underlying this, there is a crucial difference. Freud has no doubt about the nature of reality. It can be 'tested' (Freud 1957: 244) in such a way tha t it returns a 'verdict' (Freud 1957: 255) which has the power' to dema nd that all libido shall be withdrawn from its attachments to that object' (Freud 1957: 255). This verdict is that 'the object no longer exists' (Freud 1957: 255) and the bereaved can either show a ' respect for reality' (Freud 1957: 244) or turn 'away from reality' into 'hallucinatory wishful psychosis' (Freud 1957: 244). The Sora are also sure about the nature of reality and the
Dialogues lvilII (h e self'? 24 1
possibility of testing it. However, their form of reality-testing (lul1gjil/g, chap ter 5, lin e 42) returns the opposite verdict: the deceased does continue to exist. But he does so in a way which modifies the dynamics of hi s relationship to the living since these dynamics are not the sa me as when both parties were still alive. By denying the Mem ory its a uton omo us ex iste nce, Fre ud reverses the Sora model and takes the ini tia tive out of the hand s of the dead. Fo r according to the So ra , the power of the livin g to modify their own psycllic states depends on their own selective respo nse to this initi ative, so as to modify its impact. Thus it is not the Sora mourner who find s it difficult to 'a bandon a libidinal position' (Freud 1957: 244) but the deceased . Indeed , the dead may even be more articulate than the berea ved about their sense of loss (e.g. Panderi in chapter 7, line 185). Similarly, where Freud talks of the mourner 's ' hostile impulses aga in st parents' (or against the deceased generally), the Sora talk of the ho stility of parents and other ancestors towards their descendants. Again, Freud talks of the melancholic's self-reproaches (Freud 1957: 248) and 'insistent communicativeness which finds satisfaction in self-exposure' (Freud 1957: 247). These self-reproaches and this self-exposure similarly invert the Sora process in which the reproaches and exposure come from the dead, as when little Amboni accuses her Mummy of not caring about her enough or when adulterous lovers are faced with the woman 's dead husband at the end of chapter 4. The living respond by self-justification, as Sindi does to Amboni (chapter 7, lines 255, 257) or by counter-reproach (e.g. Sindi to Palda, chapter 7, line 198 and elsewhere). To summarise, then, and using the word 'remember' to covel' all relevant mental processes, we may represent the Freudian and the Sora schemata of bereavement as in figure 10.1. In Freud's model , the barrier between the living and the dead is quite impermeable because reality-testing shows that the dead do not exist. The bereaved person's libido has feelings of involvement, such as love 01' reproach , towards the deceased . In successful mourning which respects reality, this impulse bounces off the barrier and is deflected elsewhere, perhaps onto another loved object. In melancholia , this diversion goes wrong and the libido is withdrawn into the ego through an identification with the deceased , that is, with someone who does not exist. In the Sora model, despite their ontological differences, the entities on both sides of the barrier are real. The impulse which is equivalent to Freud's libido emanates, not from the bereaved person , but from the decea sed. It is with the latter that the initiative lies, too, to set the tone of his relationship with the bereaved. This impulse also has dynamic properties. But since the
