barr ier betwee n them is permeable, this dynamism is not such that the impulse is subject to deflection. Rather, throu gh di a logue it receives a response; and both the initial impul se and its answering respon se are made up of two contrastin g strands, corresponding to the Experience and the Ancestor aspects of the deceased. At first, hi s Experience aspect predomi nates as he attacks the living rememberer, who respond s in the sa me idiom of Leopard , Sun , etc., by performing an appropriate healing rite. But even at this stage, the dead person 's Ancestor aspect is la te nt and all'ead y waiting to emerge. As time goes by and his redemption takes gradual effect, hi s Experience aspect fades and this Ancestor aspect comes to take over completely . But all this takes place aga in st an evolving reciproca l relationship between living and dea d . Thou gh the Experience aspect fad es first, in the end even the Ancestor aspect fades as their mutual bond weakens in preparation for the Ancestor's second death and final
disappearance. In Freud's view, dead people are objects existing only in the minds of those who remember them and are manipulated by them passively. Their acknowledged power over their rememberer therefore stems from properties of the latter's own mind. In the Sora view , the dead make the living into passive objects of their own activity. They are autonomously existent and sentient, with the power not only to initiate relationships with living people but also to dominate the tone of such relationships. Any analytical distinction which Freud makes to ease the situation lies within the bereaved person's process of remembering the deceased, not in the properties of the deceased himself. Hence to plunge into melancholia , and perhaps to die of grief or by suicide, is the result of the bereaved person's failure to redirect his own attachment to the deceased. For the Sora, by contrast, the distinction which allows the dynamic development of the situation is a property of the dead themselves, not of the living. So to die of grief or by suicide is the result of the bereaved person's failure to ward off or modify the dead person's attachment to himself. This kind of attachment was illustrated by the role played in Jamano's death by his aggrieved wife Onsam (chapter 5); while for suicide it can be seen in Palda's attempts on the life ofSargia (chapter 7, introduction to Palda's appearance as sonum/ Memory No . 10) and on the life of Sarsuno (chapter 7, lines 169 ff) , as well as in chapter 4, where a boy kills himself at the beckoning of his predeceased girl-friend. Can the contrast between these models explain why Freud's patients suffer from disorders such as narcissism, schizophrenia and psychosis
Dialog/les Illith th e sel/? 245
rather tha n from the effects of huma n co nsciou sness stored in the Sun , the Ea rth, rocks and trees? C lea rly , the 'co nstitu tion of the human ego ' (Freud 1957: 247) revea led by the two syste ms is not the same: in Sora thinking it is not 'one part of the ego' which 'sets itself against the other, judges it critica ll y, and , as it were takes it as its object' (Freud 1957: 247) but a separate entity externa l to the person. Both systems perceive a tension between two states of mind. Fre ud 'abo li shes' the dead , then analyses the livin g and find s this tension entirely within them; the Sora analyse the dead, who experience a tension in their own selves and communicate this tension to the living through dialogue. The discussion in chapter 8 makes it clear that it would be difficult in Sora eve n to frame the proposition that Memories are loca ted within the mind of the rememberer, since it is these sonum s themse lves which show us how the Sora concept of ,mind ' itse lfi s radically different from ours. The difference in the two systems' loca tions of Memories means that the specific Freudian experience is isolated and , as such, must inevitably be judged 'subjective'. As we have seen, the Sora do not impose upon experience the dichotomy of subjective and objective. But if we insist on applying it to living Soras' experiences of the dead, it is clear that whatever their 'subjective' origins and continued sustenance in the inner feelings of individual s, they are at the same time made 'objective'. This is achieved both by the inter-subjective consensus which is established through dialogue (chapter 5) and also through the cognitive map to which experience is referred (chapter 4). This map is one and the same as that of the physical landscape over wnich groups of people walk, work and dispute every day of their lives and which is equally a map of the social order in the broadest sense. It is this common landsca pe which furnishes the means by which both personal experience and the social order are regulated and perpetuated . Thus for Freud , as for the Western secular tradition generally, the structure of experience is ba sed largely on the structure of the experiencing mind ; wnile for the Sora, it is based on that of the outside world - itself also conscious - which that mind experiences. The Sora's outside world of ordered space is set in a time which likewi se has its own ordering - or maybe more than one ordering. There is seasonal time, which determines agriculture, the annual collective stages of the funeral cycle and thus ultimately the ontological evolution of the deceased . And then there is the inner time-scale of the mourner, with its more uneven , jerky rhythm and backward counter-flows. The healing time presupposed in psychotherapy is more shapeless, since it recognises the latter kind of



246 Dialog ues Ivith the dead
time but not the former. The reasons for this , I suggest, lie not only in psychotherapy's contrast between the normal and the pathologica l, but also in its public, con sensu a l context. The kind of mournin g which the psychoanalyst Vo lkan calls ' un complicated' (i .e . analogous to Freud's normal mournin g) goes through recogni sa ble sta ges (Volka n 1981: 25 ) which Volka n ca lls 'self-limited sequenti a l patterns of behaviour' (Volkan 1981 : 37). Here he follows Pollock (1961) , who distingui shed between the acute and chronic stages of reaction , a nd whose 'acute' sta ge is further subdivided into 'shock reaction' , 'acute affective reaction' and 'separation reaction ' (Volkan 198 1: 41-4). Bowlby and Parkes (1970) , despi te minor differences , had similarly distingui shed an initial numbness , a sta ge of yearning for the lost fi gure, a period of di sorganisation, and finally one of greater or lesser reorga nisa tion (discussed in Volka n 1981 : 45- 6). In reviewing these, Volkan accepts Pollock's division into two general stages, which he calls the 'initial stage' and the 'work of mourning, which is, in effect, a longer second stage' (Volkan 1981: 46). The initial stage includes features such as numbness, shock, denial of the death, anger and ' the splitting of ego functions with regard to the perception of the death' (Volkan 1981 : 46) . 'Ifno complications supervene,' he says, 'one can expect the manifestations of grief to disappear within two to six months . . . During this period, signs of the initial stage ... may suddenly disappear and re-appear [my emphasis] again, until the mourner, after acknowledging his anger, settles into the next stage, the prolonged work of mourning with its piecemeal advances.' (Volkan 1981: 46- 7) We can see that, however uncertainly the stages of time are defined, there is a sense of an overarching progression, and within this and against it, a defiant backward-flowing strain of're-appearing' (in Sora, gorod- 'wandering' or 'reverting'). It seems to me that these writers are trying to convey a similar message to that of the Sora, with their progression from 'Experience' to 'Ancestor' and from Recent, Distressing Memories to Old , Undisturbing ones. Both Freud's model and that of the Sora rely for their dynamic element on a distinction, in the one case between mourning and melancholia (or for Volkan 1981, Worden 1982 and others, 'uncomplicated' and 'complicated' reactions) , in the other between Ancestor and Experience. The patient (respectively living or dead) is directed towards one of these to the exclusion of the other. In both cases it may be said that aspects of these two terms can coexist and overlap, as if the seeds of melancholia were latent in every mourner, just as the Ancestor and Experience aspects are present in every dead
T
Dialogues with the self? 247
person . However , where Fre ud appears to regard these as paths which are at least in principle alternative and mutually excl usive , Sora thinking treats them rather as successive stages . T he transition between these stages is made possible by the structure of the funera l rites and takes place within thi s structure. W hile Sora certainly make a distinction between the desirable and the undesirable and this distinction has normative implica tions, there appears to be nothing which corresponds to the Freudian distinction between the normal and the pathological. It is not simply that Freud assigns terms like self-exposure and the splittin g of roles to the pathological : th ey co ntribute towards the very definition of pathology. By co ntrast, the Sora integrate both their terms, Ancestor and Expe rience, in to a chronologica l process by whi ch the mourner is led straight into the more dangerous half of the dist inct ion a nd eventually out the other side. This difference may be summarised as one between alternative paths (the pa th of mournin g versus that of melancholia) and coexistent, parallel path s (Experience versus Ancestor) , with a progressive shift in emphasis over time . Both sides of the Sora di stinction are prese nt from the beginning. The intensity of the initial grief is allowed its expression and any necessary adjustment in private fee lings takes place within, and by means of, the balancing of these . Yet it is a ma tter of inexorable necessi ty that this should be modified not only over time but through the very ordering of that time, because this ordering is a public one. The rea son why the ordering in the Western model is so relatively undeveloped is that a patient's emotional time is not closely tied in with anyone else's. Starting from this contrast in presuppositions about space, time and the existence of the dead, one can point to significant contrasts in the scope claimed by each sys tem . For both of them , dea th is more than it seems at first sight. Freud's understanding of melancholia in bereavement ramifies into a wider field of loss and neurosis, an association which is made more explicit in Klein' s development of his thought, in which being weaned amounts to an early bereavement in infancy (Klein 1935; 1940). Yet however widely the field of dea th is defined , what Freud offers us is still no more than a psychology of death , while the Sora offer a psychology, a sociology, a cosmology, indeed an everything of death. Why do Sora people find dea th so important, so interesting, so powerfully explanatory? Death is not an absence or negation of life: both are phases of existence. It is this which makes dialogue between living and dead possible and important, and makes its format like a continuation of conversation among the living. Among many other clues, a great part of my evidence has been from word s spoken by Soras to each other and to me.



248 Dialog ues IFith th e dead
The doings and say in gs of others form the main theme of most conversation s; and So ras confess their feelings in phrases a nd motifs which are partly conventional but which at the same time I believe are often relatively un guarded attempts at se lf-revelation. Inama's recounting of hi s dreams about me (chapter 2), whether o r not he 'rea lly ' had them, I take to be a way of saying that he loved me and missed me durin g my a bsence. During one part of Jamano's stone-planting, I overheard Mengalu saying to Tabaro in a weary voice:
Tabaro, [pa use) I've grown old. For us, it 's this journey, it's this stone pulled upright, it 's this merging .. . This stone is what we become , this rite is what we become, and that's it. Tbe liquor we drink , th e rice we ea t - where are we going to go? We just go down that path .. .
These are things which can be said without embarrassment and in front of others, though there are always veils of context and a shared past. Thus , Mengalu is an 'age-mate' (tojleud, 'near-born') of Tabaro who has grown up with him since childhood. Tabaro is the husband of the shaman Rondang (chapter 7) and father of the suicide Rumbana who also tried recently to make Mengalu's son kill himself. Meanwhile, Mengalu and his sons are among the claimants to the dead Rumbana's estate (chapter 8); while at that day's funeral for Jamano Mengalu was under scrutiny because the dead man had already accused him of sorcery. A shared past leads to a fondness for reminiscing. Whenever my first Sora friend Inama hugged me after a long separation, he would spend several minutes recounting our shared experiences, repeating our old conversations as if verbatim: 'Do you remember how we lay in the river in the sunshine, and you said . .. and then I said .. .' Not only affection but where they 'are appropriate' (tam-) anger and hostility, too, can be openly expressed. Aggrieved conversations easily slide into the rudi format of debate or mutual haranguing. But there are many situations in which one cannot speak as one feels. Here, the speech that is uttered may not so much reveal as conceal (soso -). Sora talk of 'inner' (i11l1gen) and 'outer' (bay ira) soul or feeling and are alert for clues to the implicit to an extent which elsewhere might be considered a rare gift. Once, a group of friends asked to hear some white people's music. I had been among them for more than a year and had never once played the tapes which I had brought with me and had expected to be my constant companions. I had made the mistake of bringing intense pieces rather than something lighter: after the first few bars ofa Schubert quartet, during which I was not aware that I had betrayed any emotion, several of them started saying 'It's upsetting you, isn ' t it - why don't you turn it off?'
Dialogues with th e sele 249
So me of what is known or sensed implicitly , becomes part of the flow of goss ip. But some of it cannot be sa id openly in front of the parties who are most concerned. Shama ns do not simply hea l people's illnesses, or even their griefs. The shaman provides a means through which the impli cit can be made exp licit and the un speakab le be bro ught to the point of ha vin g been spoken. F rom time to time, in an attempt to return hospitality , I used to invite gro ups of Soras for an exped ition to the coastal town of Visakhapa tn a m, some 200 miles away , where I had the use of a hostel. On one occasion, half of the group waited in the hos tel while I took the other half to the post office to phone them up. The result wa s even more of an entertainment than I had expected, since they reduced themse lves to helpless laughter by confronting each other with illicit affairs and other embarrassments and indiscretion s (in the end the postmaster politely asked us to lea ve, as we were so noisy) . These were matters which were known to all but which could never before have been raised so directly with the person concerned. Afterwards , they agreed that this had been possible because the phone resembled a shaman in that you do not see the other person's face while you talk with them. The use of telephones was unprecedented , offering a protective screen of a kind not otherwise availa ble or even conceiva ble between living speakers. It is precisely a screen of this sort which lifts inhibitions once one of the speakers is dead . As in the case of the psychoanalyst's patient, the affecting person, or Memory, must be absent. But here this absence is not simply a consequence of the privacy of the consulting room, as when a domineering parent has been left outside in the waiting room . Since dialogues are public, the only way the affecting person can be absent is by being dead. Though the person with whom one is concerned or obsessed still speaks for himself, he has embarked on a path of progressive conventionalisation and simplification. Whatever his new ca usal power as a Memory , he ha s at the same time become diminished psychically: he has become manipulable to a degree to which no living person would consent. So to what extent is a dead person really there when he speaks? Once, when I missed a divination I asked a friend afterwards which Memory had claimed responsibility for the patient's illness. 'It was so-and-so,' he said . When I sounded surprised, he added, 'So he said, but how can we know? It's only words (Edte gamete, do Ivan galambe? Berna sadtang) .' There were many amusing stories circulating which played on the theatricality of dialogue. Once, a young man disappeared in Assam and was presumed dead . Some years after his funeral had been performed back home he turned up, much to the embarrassment of the funeral shaman. In another story an old man doubted that he would receive his buffalos after death and



250 Dialog ues Il'ith the dead
insisted on staging his own funeral while he was still alive. The shaman somehow obliged. Later, this man also insisted on passing on his name to his baby grandson. But since a woman is forbidden to utter her fa ther-in-law's name while he is still alive, the child's mother was reduced to calling her own baby simply 'the old man' (talajba). Another story concerned a meteorite which crashed into a paddy-field near Alinsing. The villagers gave it a stone-planting, at which the meteorite's Ancestors spoke through the shaman and thanked them nicely for lookin g after their child when it had died so far away from its family and friends. People roar with laughter when they recount these events. Yet such stories do not seem to provide grounds for scepticism, nor do they diminish the intensity of the majority of dialogues , which are meant to be taken serious ly . What makes these stories so bizarre is only that they push dialogue up against the limits of context. But where the context is unexceptional, as the remark ' it's only words' shows, this ambiguity is an essential part of dialogue. In asking who it is who speaks, we are also asking who or what a shaman is. Napier, writing about masks, talks of our
conflicting views that resu lt, at least in part, from a need to see mask wearers exclusively as either true incarnators or mere dramatists. Neither our critical faculties nor our modes of description a llow us to envision a context in which both of these dispositions might function at one and the same time. (Napier 1986: xxiv)
This comment may help us to recognise the extent of the enigma which the shaman presents. In the first place, whether she is seen as incarnator or dramatist, the kind of multiple-personality interpretation sometimes applied to a shaman is clearly inadequate. Apart from this approach's implication of pathology, multiple personalities belong to the world of private fantasies and concern largely the person who contains them . But Sora dialogue, with its legalistic overtones and its central regulating role in society, discusses the concerns of others at least as much as those of the shaman. Again, the shaman is more active than any blank, reflective screen or mirror (Lacan 1949; Winnicott 1980a). Even with their partial and guarded emotional involvement with their clients through 'countertransference', the psychoanalyst and psychotherapist have been left behind. Another possible way of understanding the Sora shaman is to compare her with the dramatist or the creator of dramatic novels . An author who has been interpreted in a way which makes him sound somewhat like a Sora shaman is Dostoyevsky. His novels have been called 'polyphonic', since they unfold through a 'plurality of voices' (Bakhtin 1984 [1929]). Dos
Dialogues with the self'? 25 1
toyevsky's characters, writes Bakhtin, are themselves subjects rather than objects wi thin the consciousness of the a uthor: within this work there is no outside viewpoint and so no 'finalising' authorial voice (Bakhtin 1984 [1929]: especially chapter I). The characters arefree from the usual author's synthesis 'which would then cancel out all preceding links as . .. totally superseded' (Bakhtin 1984 [1929]: 26). Lunacharsky vividly summarises this part of Bakhtin's argument:
Dostoyevsky's novels are superbly staged dialogu es ... Dostoyevsky deliberately puts certain vital problems up for discussion before these highly individual 'voices' , trembling with passion and flickering with the flame of fanaticism, while he himself remains a mere spectator of the convu lsive disputes which ensue, a curious lookeron. . . (Lunacharsky 1973: 82)
Here there is a clear parallel with Sora dialogue in the legalistic and moral debate between positions embodied in persons. But is either Dostoyevsky or the Sora shaman really as absent as this? Bakhtin writes that Dostoyevsky (in this translation spelt Dostoevsky)
is profoundly active, but his activity is of a special dialogic sort. It is one thing to be active in relation to a dead thing, to voiceless material that can be molded and formed as one wishes, and another thing to be active ill relalioll 10 someolle else's tillillg, aulonomous cOllsciouslless . .. Dostoevsky frequently interrupts, but he never drowns out the other's voice, never finishes it off 'from himself , that is, out of his own and alien consciousness. (Bakhtin 1984 [1929): 285, italics o rigin al)
The Sora shaman is similarly present, in the sense that she is represented by her rauda-familiar, who likewise intervenes without using her authority to foreclose the discussion. The rauda is a previous shaman, now dead, and it is often possible to detect a continuity between the words of comfort spoken to her clients by the shaman herself before going into trance and those spoken by the rauda on her arrival afterwards. Though the role of shaman is supposedly disinterested, shamans are often deeply involved in the affairs of their clients. One has only to think of Uda in chapter 5, the dead man's brother who leads the grief-stricken Ranatang on to further heights of rage as he incarnates both the boy's dead father and the living Mengalu's confession to his murder by sorcery. And in chapter 7 one can only guess what the old shaman Rondang is feeling as she acts out the nastiness of the youth who has joined her beloved only child Rumbana in suicide, and as her characters frustrate the living Sindi with their unfavourable view of where Rumbana's inheritance is heading. However, to the extent that the Sora shaman can be compared to Dostoyevsky as an author of autonomous creatures, her situation is far more complex.



252 Dialog ues with the dead
Dostoyevsky works primarily in space, ra the r tha n in time: his mode was ' not evolution, but coexistence and interaction '; he had a ' passion for ma ss sce nes' because of hi s impulse to 'concentrate in a single moment the greates t possible qualitative diversity ' . Hence, at such key moments, his drama s unfold with a 'ca ta strophic swi ftness of action ' (Bakhtin 1984 [1929]: 28- 9) . Sora dialogue does not work by means of such decisive moments and denouements, but through reiteration and a form of chan ge which is hesitant and partially reversible. Yet in fa ct it moves forward in a way which Dostoyevsky does not: ' none of[Dostoyevsky's] contradiction s ... ever became dialectical, they were never set in motion along a tempora l path or an evolving sequence' (Bakhtin 1984 [1 929] : 30) . However, this is precisely what does happen with the shaman. She does not , of course, resolve proble ms of the world in a general or existential sense. Indeed, the Sora concept of 'contagion' and of the passing on by the dead of their unhappy experiences, shows a perception that each particular resolution is bought only at the price of other, subsequent unhappiness elsewhere. The difference in the shaman's position in time is related to the contrast between her medium and that of the novelist. However open an author's dialogues may be, the reader will nonetheless reach the last page of the book. The author himself dies and to say that his characters live on in some sense is not to equate them with the Sora dead. The characters in a shaman's dialogue will outlive her more literally because they are not fictional , but real people. Moreover, the living half of these are still able to speak for themselves through their own mouths. As if that were not enough, the separation of the divination and funeral traditions ensures that the same dead person must speak on various occasions through several different shamans. We have heard Panderi speak twice through Rijanti at rival inquests and again through Rondang at a healing rite. The verdict on 1amano's death was developed in two stages through the mouths of the funeral shamans Kumbri and Uda respectively; the terms on which he will attack in the future will become known through yet other shamans of the divination-and-healing traditions, of whom there are several likely to act as his mouthpiece. Similarly, each of the dead persons who speak through Rondang at the healing in chapter 7, is currently somewhere on a trajectory which began with a verdict through a funeral shaman and which may be reinforced or modified at everyone of their appearances through Rondang and the many other divination shamans inside the village and maybe beyond. Here one can see the ground of the difference between the novelist and the shaman. Like Lunacharsky and other Soviet critics, Bakhtin relates the
Dialog ues with th e self"? 253
new-found polyphony of Dostoyevsky to th e contradiction s of ca pi ta li sm, especia lly in Ru ss ia where it 'se t in a lmost ca tas trophicall y . .. upon a n untouched multitude of diverse world s a nd socia l groups whi ch had not been wea kened in their individual isolation , as in the Wes t, by [its] gradual encroachment' . (Bakhtin 1984 [1929]: 20). T he position s, th a t is, whi ch a re embodi ed in Dostoyevsky 's characters are ultimately ideological ones. It is true that the struggles we see in Sora di a logues are not simply betwee n person s, but also between positions and sometimes prin ciples . Thi s view is supported by the formul aic sun g debates whi ch pa ra llel the most important of the dialogues . But what kind of positions are these? The politi cal e nvironment of the Sora is full of conflicts, especially in their relation to the state and the other ethnic groups who at times seem to be its allies or agents. But these positions do not become voices in the dialogue which the Sora hold with their dead . Ra ther , through the shaman s' high-caste familiars, ethnic struggle provides a means which ena bles a very intimate dialogue to take place. The Sora lineage system, procedures of inheritance and sense of personhood are all relatively uncodified a nd provisional. Without case-law and precedent, the way to judge who a re Rumbana 's appropriate heirs or to assess the validity ofPanderi's marriage is to try to persuade these dead persons themselves to support one's own view of the matter: the disposition of their property is related to the resolution of ambiguities in their feelings of attachment to other persons. Yet the forms of Sora consensus show that to contest another person's position is not to dispute that person's sociological or metaphysical presuppositions. The struggles in Sora dialogues are not between ideologies but between persons, cast at least partly as litigants. So if the Sora dead are fictions at all, they are the negotiated and shifting joint fictions of many authors. There are the various living interlocutors, coming together in different combinations and all urging their own viewpoints upon the dead and upon each other. The dead in turn appear to give concordant or discordant responses. What an observer may be inclined to call cases, are merely episodes or moments in the lives of everyone concerned. These bear upon each other either through direct causality or else opportunistically through various relationships of implication and analogy, as in the subordina tion of Palda's affairs and those of his entire family to the exigencies of Rumbana's. But in this respect , the dead are perhaps not much more of a fiction than the living. It might be truer to the Sora's own experience to say that their society makes demands to a greater or lesser extent upon the dramatic creativity and interpersonal alertness of everyone. All persons are both self-authoring and authors of



254 Dialogues with th e dead
each other's lives. The shamans and their raudas may have their own interests, but the widely dispersed process of reaching consensus diffuses authority to the point where the verdicts of dialogues are ultimately au thorless. It is surely this fact which allows the livin g to speak with the dead so easily and in the same kind of ordinary prose which they use with each other. It is perhaps for this rea so n too that the ' objective' existence of Memories depends so little on any representation in the restricted world of material objects. Thus the psychoanalyst Volkan, following Winnicott's discussion of children's teddy bea rs and blankets (Winnicott 1980b), describes what he calls 'linking objects' (e.g. stones, personal fetishes, talismans) in the context of mourning:
[The mourner] invests certain inanimate objects of his own selection with magical qualities. Such ' linking objects' furnish an external locus at which the selfrepresentation can meet with the representation of the lost object [in a form of communication over which the mourner] feels himself to have absolute control. (Volkan 1981: 84--5, cf. 20,106)
Certainly, living Sora do use material objects, such as trees and rocks, memorial stones and wall-paintings, to represent their dead . The dead, in turn , show a great desire to wear their old clothes and jewellery or to handle their old tools. However, the communion between living and dead Sora moves very rapidly from this towards a non-material kind of exteriority. In the Christian and in many other traditions, the upright gravestone with its label is a longstanding focal objectification of the deceased. But for all the attention which it receives at the time, the stone planted at a Sora funeral is a more short-term objectification . It is bunched up against many other similar stones and I found again and again that after a few years, nobody remembers which is which. A psychoanalyst's mourner cannot let go of an object so easily because the consensual world which he inhabits gives little or no validation to any non-material representation: since the latter cannot be pointed to, it is that much harder to insist to others that it exists. But the Sora dead have their existence among the living not so much in material objects but as speakers. The particularity of any material representation of them is lost long before their particularity in speech . Volkan does also identify non-material exterior links, such as a song (Volkan 1981: 106, though he discusses these only briefly). These he calls 'linking phenomena' and they function like a non-material counterpart to his linking objects. It is the fact that one can recognise phenomena like this which enabled me in chapter 8 to gloss the term sonum as 'a Memory', But if what the Sora are doing is using 'linking phenomena', then they are doing
Dialogues Il'ith the self'? 255
so to a deg ree far beyond anything Volkan suggests. Where does th e Sora linkin g phenomenon end: at the autonomous, sentient Memory, who without being a physical obj ect is nonetheless objectively rea l? Or perhaps a t the act of holding a dialogue with tha t Memory? The So ra experience of illness itse lf seems like a linkin g phenomenon , as too does the expe ri ence (which after chapter 8 must be ca lled a passion rather than a n act) of dying itself. These seem more than simply ' linking phenomena' and more like constituents of a total linking, and linked , universe. There is thus a unity between realm s of discourse which in the world-view of industrial society are not easily reconciled. For instance, the 'social sciences' are bedevilled with the incompatibility between the sociological explanation of a phenomenon and its explanation in terms of individual psychology . The material which has been presented suggests strongly that the sociological and psychological aspects of the Sora interpretation of a death, the realms of material inheritance and of emotion, run so closely in parallel as to be indistinguishable. Schieffelin has observed that for the Kaluli of New Guinea , one laments the dead partly in terms of what they would have done for one if they had still been alive. But '[w]here human relationships are stated so strongly in terms of material reciprocity, [they] are not so morally dissociated as they are in the West ... and to state one's desires in terms of sentiment is not the hypocrisy it would be for us' (Schieffelin 1976: 150). Similarly, though in their own language the Sora ' think', 'feel' and 'know' (respectively ogandi-. 1111- and galam-), nevertheless what we call 'intellect', 'emotion' and 'cognition' are for them not necessarily distinct experiential or epistemological realms. In this sense Sora epistemology appears less compartmentalised than some others: the realms which in the West are called theology, aesthetics, psychology, sociology, politics, medicine and law are articulated and regulated in large part tlU'ough the medium of dialogues with sonums, or 'Memories'. Sonums present an interpretative problem for anthropological analysis precisely because for so many questions the Sora regard them as a solution. This entire book can be seen as an extended gloss on the word 'sonum'. Sonums have their own 'logic' which I have tried to tease out. But I have also tried, however inadequately, to enable the reader to imagine what it may be like to feel as a Sora does. I have tried to look beyond the idea of a 'translation of culture', based as it is on analysing categories and concepts, towards what may perhaps be called a 'trans-sentience'. Feeling and thinking are closely, but mysteriously, intertwined . As Michelle Rosaldo put it, ', .. feeling is forever given shape through thought and ... thought is laden with emotional meaning'. (1984: 143). They are not



256 Dialog ues Ivi/h the dead
opposed to each other: rather, emotions can be seen as 'cognitions implicating the immediate, carnal " me" - as thou ghts embodied ' (M. Z . Rosaldo 1984: 138). We can interpret Kantino 's rumbling tummy in chapter 4 in these term s: Leopard -Memory is a conceptual category through which K antino can think a bout hi s dea d fath er. Th e symptom of a rumbling tummy is the embodiment of these thou ghts which articulates hi s feelin gs for hi s father and gives them form. My preferred translation of ' Rumble-Tummy-Sonum ' as ' Rumble-Tum my-M emory' , perhaps preserves the sen se tha t thought and feelin g are somehow both at work here . How ca n a n anthropologist or a reader ma ke the move from a gra sp of patterns of thought to the involvement of his or her 'carnal " me" '? A total trans-sentience seems even more unattainable than a perfect tran sla tion . My own involvement as a fieldwork er led me along what are only th e first steps towards what T should like to call a bi-sentience. This path seems analogous to that in the growth of bilingualism , where one starts to think and even to dream in another language but does so patchily and while referring back frequently to one's first language. This leads to a play of true and false parallels between the two languages, of illusions and insights about those languages. So it is with feelings . Sometimes, during absorbing moments in the field, I seemed to forget that I was not a Sora . I took part in work parties, or cooked, drank and gossiped. While staying with a friend whose child was sick I had a dream which allowed my host to diagnose the Memory which was attacking his little girl. In dreams at other times rauda-familiars, lamp in hand , would beckon me to go with them to the Underworld in order to become a shaman: after one of these dreams I even woke up to find that I had sleep-walked and was standing in the darkened street outside the house. In a hazy mixture of good faith and make-believe which suited both me and my hosts, I fasted , sang and danced as Ranatang's Ancestor-Man. But among all the many details of my activity and talk with them, something would soon arise to remind us that I wa s not actually one of them and to emphasise for both of us the sense of my own otherness. I felt this sense most acutely when a Sora friend died and , like everyone else, I was called over to speak with her or him and continue our friendship. For the reader, any degree of trans-sentience must rely on an even more limited fluency in an unfamiliar way of life, with a correspondingly weaker sense of involvement, than it does for the anthropologist. Yet in practice, it can sometimes seem surprisingly strong and direct. Carrithers cites an account by Firth of the actions and words of a group of people who are
Dialogues Il'i/h the self? 257
trying to ca lm a youn g man whom F irth describes as ' hi ghly agita ted ' (F irth 1985: 39) . Carrith ers ex pla ins the easy intelligibility of this pa ssage on the ground s that it
remind[sJ us of other occa sions o n whic h we have met such a pa tt ern , throu gh personal expe ri e nce or throu gh represe nt a ti o ns o f emotion ... Pa R a ngifuri 's tea rs, th e incohe re nce of his word s, and his ge ne ra l demeanour are di stin ct, vi vid , a nd di scriminable from other patterns such as, sa y, ' riotou s jollity'. Moreover, the pa ttern is not merely visual or a uditor y. Other, constitutivel y social compone nts th e co ntrast be l\veen Pa R a ngifuri 's ordinar y gree ting and this ' perfunc tory' one, the soothing ges tures and enquiri es of th e othe r me n - also contribute to th e passage's conse nsibility. (Carrithers 1990: 266)
The example is skilfully chosen, because at this point in Firth 's narration the a nthropologist and the other actors prese nt are as mystified as the reader a bout the causes of the young man's agitation . This allows Carrithers to point out that 'there is something peculiarly pure about our apprehension of Pa Rangifuri's state: along with the other Tikopia then present . .. we can perceive that something has happened and very roughly identify the character of that something' (Carrithers 1990: 267 , italics original). Such patterns, he says, are 'consensible and intelligible' even whjle being 'unique and irreproducible' (Carrithers 1990: 268). I would add that there can be a varying intensity to this 'consensibility', according to what each person brings to the encounter. Trans-sentience does not depend wholly on a translation of terms, concepts and models, or on the explication of their context and mutual relations. It requires that we should position ourselves more or less wholeheartedly somewhere within the arena of translation through a leap of imagination . In doing this we sense an analogy , actual or potential, between another person's situa tion and our own. We find such analogies constantly whenever we empathise with or 'feel for' other people, however partial these analogies may be and however labile our empathy. Thus an English friend , who knew nothing of Sora ethnography, wept when I showed her the dialogue between Rungkudi and her little girl which opens this book. Anyone mjght find that dialogue moving, but the fact that my friend had young children herself, I believe, allowed her to position herself very clearly. This kind of overlap suggests that the imaginative leap we make need not be a disinterested one indeed, it will not be so if that imagination is to have much force to it. My friend's consensibility was a (mercifully) smaller version of the overlap felt by Renato Rosaldo when his wife Michelle was suddenly killed in a fieldwork accident and in his grief he found himself 'repositioned'

258 Dialogues I\li/h the dead
(R. Rosaldo 1984: 182) in a way which wa s not just a temporary fea t of the imagination: he now understood the rage felt by bereaved Ilongot men with an intensity of understanding which had previously eluded him. Thi s approach , with its emphasis on relative intensity (in R. Rosaldo , 'force') leads in a different direction from the discussion of terms in different languages for 'love' , 'a nger', and so on .3 Sora talk little a bout feelin gs as noun s, though some such words do exist, e.g. l1I([noien, sin/an , mea ning res pectively ' happiness', 'sorrow'. What in English are word s of feelings, are used most in a verba l form: sin/a/ai, l11oii'i/ai, bambda/illg, l/1a/sida/illg , aY"11/enai, ' I feel sorry, 1 feel happy , I fee l angry, 1 like, I remember'. The reference of these is interpersonal , rather th a n interna l, since they generally take a construction involving a further person or interpersonal situation: sin/a/am , '1 feel sorry for you', ay ll11/al11 , '1 remember you', bal'abda/ing gal11lellden , 'I am angry because ' . So rather than discuss ' love' or 'anger' as such, 1 have followed the Soras' own lead and considered persons as the agents and patients of verbs, who act and suffer in ways which suggest that they are passing through a gamut of different feelings or emotions. Above all , they talk, in a way which confirms unmistakably that for them, feelings are not simply internal states since the feelings of each person implicate many other persons. 1 have presented these verb roots and forms as they succeed each other in a progression across the sweep of time. A person's supposed feelings after his death move from those in which he acts most aggressively, through verbs of diminished aggression and finally to verbs of nurturing and protecting. To the extent that 1 have been obliged in English to talk at all of feelings as nouns, my use of terms like 'mood' or 'state of mind' is intended to suggest that the Sora seem to regard these feelings as fleeting episodes or modes of being. As we have seen, the Sora do not genera lly attach a label to these moods as such. Yet at the same time we can see how M emories (sonums) constitute a key set of nouns in the rhetoric of feeling , a set which is highly elaborated and frequently invoked and which functions in some senses as an alternative vocabulary of feelings . Every category of Memory also implies certain moods associated with emotional attachment. All of these moods belong somewhere within the orbit of the English word 'love' as well as of other, more or less opposed English words like 'hatred', 'resentment' or 'anger'. For the Sora, such moods are not just states but stages in a dynamic sequence. Any given form of Memory presupposes other forms of Memory from which it has arisen or into which it will metamorphose. As forms of Memory flow , change and blur into each other, so in the course of their dialogues the living and the dead echo,
Dialogues IPi/h the self? 259
reciprocate and modify each others' feelings. T he di scussions of how a livin g person' s biography evo lves through his illnesses (chapter 4), or of the relationship between memory and what I have ca lled Memory (chapter 8), ex plore the nature of the traces which these feelin gs lea ve. T hese traces can be cumul ative: they change the person who feels them and ultimately
perha ps even const itu te that person. If the interpreta tion of dialogue offered in this book is co nvincin g, this should not be taken as an elega nt achi evement of the anthropo logist, nor eve n of the shamans through whom the dialogues pa ss. Rather, it revea ls a who le way of being human in the company of other humans, all of whom are creatures of time. For a deceased person in the West today who is mourned by more than one rememberer, there is comparatively little provision for the mutual interplay of their memories of him . In the case ofa psychoanalyst' s or psychotherapist's patient there may be none at all, whi le for members of a religious congrega tion there may be rather more , thou gh only so far as they are encouraged to talk about the dead on limited occasions. By contrast, each Sora who dies becomes yet another Memory , common to many . If figure 10. 1were to reflect the full scope of this, it would show numerous living rememberers linked in simi lar ways with the same dead person, while each rememberer would be similarly linked to numerous dead people. The rememberers are at the same time witnessing and being affected by the details of each other's relationship to that dead person. The dead, similarly, have their own mutual relationships. Every Sora Memory is thus one node in an endlessly extendible social web which encompasses both the living and the dead . The patterning of this web is continually carried forward and reproduced with the flow of time, while the potential existence of each node pre-exists the dea th of the person around whom it will grow. This is implicit in the Soras' awareness of their own mortality as they talk , whether seriously or banteringly, about the future continuation of their relationships once some of the present company have died ('Will you give me a decent buffalo at my stoneplanting when I'm dead?') . In this book I have tried to show that it is the autonomous and publicly shared nature of sonums which informs both Sora society and the Sora person's awareness of who he or she is within that society . Dialogues with the dead are not about death , but about life: it is only by having a vision of what it is to be dead , that one can have an understanding of what it is to be alive.



Appendix 1
List of sonums recorded in Alinsing (excluding 'Ancestor', idai)
Categories given here were recorded in Alinsing village. In the terminology of chapter 4, the main headings numbered from I to 7 are residential Experiences which cause death and then store their victims in sites on the landscape. The sonums listed under each of these headings are their sub-Experiences which cause illness but do not in themselves cause death or store victims (though in usage these names sometimes serve as synonyms for the main categories under which I have grouped them). The main Experiences remain constant throughout the area studied by me, though other villages would give a slightly different repertoire of sub-Experiences. A discussion of the symptoms associated with each of these categories would make this appendix unnecessarily long and complex.

Preface I More complicated, a nd perhaps not essential for th e rea der to remember, is th e fact that in certain cases wbere two different consonants meet side by side, th e first is pronounced almost as a glottal stop. Thus Kidlllllg, a kind of creator-being and village guardian, is pronounced more like Ki'llIlIg; garoj lid, 'there's no embarrassment' , is pronounced garoi' lid. Unless other forms of the word reveal it, I am not always confident tbat I can reconstruct the missing consonant and so have sometimes left these glottal stops as they so und .

3 Fo r some views of the relat ionships betwee n 'tribal' a nd 'Hindu' , see Bailey 1957, 1959, 1960, 196 1; D umont 1962; Ga rdn er 1982; Sinh a 1962. 4 Among 'co usin s' , tb e Sora distingui sh members of one's ow n lineage from members of other lineages. To take my own generation as the simples t example, my father's brother's child is my ' bro tb er' o r 'sister' . But my fath er's sister has married out into a nother lineage and her children will be members o f that lineage . Similarly, the children of my mother's brother co ntinue to be members o f th e lineage into which she was originally born. All these people a re my l1Iarollgger (male) or lIIarollse! (female). Since we are descended from siblin gs of opposite sexes, this usage co rresponds to the E nglish 'cross-cousin'. The term s lIIarollgger and lIIarollse! a lso cove r the child of my mother's sister, since (unless the two women have ma rri ed into the sa me lineage) this person, too, is a member of a nother lineage . Thus lIIarollgger a nd lIIarollse! are used to cove r a wider field than 'cross-co usin ', since they include what anthropologists sometimes pondero usly call the 'matrilateral para llel cousi n' . These cousin rela ti o nships generally continue down to the third generation. Though, as we shall see, there is a tension between conflicting patterns of behaviour, marria ge and sexual rel a tionships between the cross-cousin kind s of lIIarollgger and marollse! are widely forbidden or discouraged . Relatio ns with the mother's sister's child a re always forbidd e n. All these cousins can a lso be addressed as ' brother' and 'sister'. Throughout most of this book, howeve r, I shall use not the Sora , but the English term 'cross-cousin' wherever this applies. This is because any discussion of cousins largely centres around tensions between a woma n's relationship with her brothers on the one hand , and with her husband on tbe other. 5 Though Elwin was fluent in some India n languages, he did not claim to know Sora more than slighlly. This is similar in its essentia ls to Oriya or Bengali (Inden a nd Nicholas 1977) in its use of uncle and a unt terms a nd its presupposition that cross-cousin marriage is not practised ; while Suryanarayana's thesis (1977) on kinship a nd marriage among non-Lanjia Sora in the plains of Andhra Pradesh presents a 'south Indian ' system in whic h the terms are not so different but a re arranged so as to support this practice. We shall see in chapter 3 that the La njia Sora play with both these possibilities. Cross-cousin marriage is seen as a privilege, bordering on brother-sister incest, which living Sora mostly fore go but which is common a mong Telugu a nd Oriya high castes as well as among Sora shamans in the Underworld . ·
7 Apart from th~ small kitchen gardens (kolla) immediatefy outside the house, the Sora classify la nd into two ma in categories . One is ba/'llll ('hill', 'slope'), a clea ring in ' fores t' (l1I!ab) of the sort called in English slash-and-burn , shifting cultivation or short-allow (for technical agricultural aspects in various parts of Asia, see e.g. Ruthenberg 1980; Spencer 1966; Conklin 1957, 1963; Vitebsky 1984, 1992; Hagen 1987). Each household clears the trees a nd bushes on a site, burns th em and sows seeds as the rains begin. This is moslly worked by exchange la bour



266 Noles 10 pages 35- 60
between house holds a nd is essentially a subsistence agri culture, producing mille t grains with little or no surplus (see Vitebsky 1978a, 1978 b). The other kind of land is saroball , ' pad d y-land ', so metimes strea m-fed but usua ll y rain-fed. This is the form ofland which can yield substantial surpluses . W ea lthier house holds thus have rice as th eir sta ple diet, while the selling or lending of surplus padd y both a mon g Soras an d to th e outside world is probably the bigges t single econo mi c activity.
The supposed ownership of permanent paddy-la nd is lega lly recog ni sed and entered in the Revenue Department's Reco rd of Rights; a n annual tax ('cist') is due on these to th e gove rnment. While an a verage village will contain no more th a n a max imum of 300 ac res of paddy fields, th ere may be as much as 2,000 to 3,000 acres of fores t whic h a re subject to shifting cultivation, of which at a nyo ne time a large proportion is not being c ultiva ted but is lying fallow. All fores t is legally owned by the go vernment, so that all shiftin g cultivation is considered a n illegal encroachment. Howeve r, des pite th e occasional punitive fines a nd increasing general official pressure, the Sora consider shifting cultivation to be indispensable on ideological , recreational, dietetic and economic grounds, so that I do not know of a single household, however wealthy, which does not practise it extensively, while the many poorer households depend on it entirely. 8 The alill (fishtail palm) dries up during the hot seaso n a nd the monsoon which follows. Its place as a source of alcohol is taken by the aba (mohua, Madhllca illdica), the fleshy flowe rs of which drop from their trees during the hot season and are distilled into a spirit which warms the body during the chilly rains. The drink provided by these two trees is an essential part of the daily diet, of social gatherings and of almost all offerings to sonums. Sora conviviality takes for granted a plentiful supply of drink a nd this will often be emphasised in future chapters . Milk is never drunk and water rarely, except as the juice of boiled gruel. As the word 'gruel' would suggest, Sora food is bland . The staple diet consists of millet or, for wealthier people, rice, boiled into this watery, unspiced gruel and eaten with only salt and sometimes chillies. Meat is likewise boiled without seasoning. Other methods of cooking, including the oil-based techniques like frying common elsewhere in India , are not used . 9 As in much of south Asia, 'Where are you going?' is not an innocent request for information. It may sometimes be as bland as the English question ' How are you?' On the other hand, it may engage the other person and draw the encounter into quite different areas. I carried my child on a Buddhist pilgrimage one full-moon night up Adam's Peak in Sri La nka. The steep stone steps up the mountain led to only one place: the summit. Nevertheless, every pilgrim who passed us coming down , as ked one after another 'Where are you going?' For a subtle and amusing discussion of the implications of this question in a T a mil village setting, see Daniel 1984: 64-7 . In Soraland, this is only one of several possible opening remarks: 'Sukka po?', 'Are you well?' (reply: 'SlIkka' , 'well' or 'ASII', 'ill'); 'Lemlam' , 'I regard/ respect you' (reply: ' Lemlam'); or with strangers, 'Wall a gO/jallg 1I(//11?', 'What's your village?' (reply: the village name, or if the other person is attractive, maybe a made-up village name with a suggestive etymology, like Tulemba, which sounds close to 'Let's have sex').

3 The formation of the Sora person Lineages do not ha ve surn a mes, thou gh for th eir dea lings with the administration Sora may adopt titles like 'Head man ' (Gomango, the Oriya form of Gomang) or 'Pries t' (Buya) if these heredit a ry offices a re held within their lineage, otherwise simply 'Sora' . I was a lso told during my brief return visit to Soraland in 1984 that , although I was presumed to be still alive, several babies had bee n named in memory of me since I had left in 1979.
4 Interpreting and persuading the dead I There is ev idence to suggest that villages ma y originally have co nta ined only one lineage. The funera l shaman acts for a n entire village, yet at the sa me time her cross-cousin marri age in the Underworld sugges ts a n exclusive re lationship with her na tal lineage. Similarly, at the karja, the Ancestor-Men of all the lineages, who act sepa rat ely for other stages of th e fun e ra l sequence, are pooled a nd one bereaved house is chosen as the setting for th e entire proceedings (see chapter 9). If this interpreta tion is correct, then the m os t likely ori ginal settlers of the village site are the Buya lineage, who tend the village Kidtung sonum in seasonal growth a nd harvest rites (abdllr) at a site called j ellallg-Io (probably meaning 'Earthbone-place') . 2 Though I am not trained in medicine my assessment is based on the following: avid reading while in the field of Mallsoll 's Tropical Diseases (Manson-Bahr 1972, latest edition 1982); accompanying ma ny sick Soras to hospitals in towns and interpreting for them; and my own experience of contracting many of the conditions named and spending a long time in hospital myself. 3 What could be taken as the literal translation of ' healing', ab-sllka-, 'cause to be healthy', is used only of the rite to reintegra te shamans, Ancestor-Men and Ancestor-Women among the living after they have 'become' the dead Ancestors during their duties at a funeral. 4 These doublets were called ' tag-words' by R amamurti (1931) . There have been studies of comparable 'echo' words in Gta' (Mahapatra 1976; Zide 1976) and of parallelism in traditional Munda ri poetry (Munda 1976) which between them pose but lea ve unanswered the ques tion of whether this is a phenomenon native to the Munda language family , or something characteristic of much ofIndia a nd embracing Dravidian and Indo-Aryan la nguages as well (Zide 1976: 1335; M a hapatra 1976: 830; cf. Trawick 1990: 245). A similar pattern is also widespread in the islands of southeast Asia (Fox 1974; Fox 1988; M etcalf 1989; some literature on a similar phenomenon in other parts of the world is summarised in Jakobson 1966). I have discussed the structure and semantics of this Sora speech pattern in detail elsewhere (Vitebsky 1978c). In other ways, too, the material presented in later chapters will suggest that it is a characteristic pattern in Sora thinking to bundle elements together as synonyms at one time and at others to separate them by discursive means. For example, paired aspects of Sun-Sonum appear synonymous when listed in invocations but are discriminated in myth in terms of kinship or political hierarchy, or in illness in terms of symptom. In the same way, the various Earth-Sonums associated with different branches of a lineage are treated as synonymous when the emphasis is



268 NO les 10 pages 9/- 13 7
on th e lineage's unity a nd as opposed wh en it is on th e lineage's fragmentation (chapters 6, 9). 5 In each couplet , only ce rtain e lemen ts a re paired in this way, while th eir surroundin g fram ewo rk ('clasping', 'grandmothers', 'four-foot ed walk ') is held co nstant a nd unchan ged . Each of th e two elements to the pairs brin gs with it its own se mantic fie ld . Whe n the elements are considered se parately, they offer a co ntrast: hand s are difrerent from , even opposite to, feet; monkeys are di stin ct from apes. But the fact that they are now juxtaposed also invites us to see their similarity, to see them as two limbs or two simians. The po int now is not to di stin gui sh eac h of th e two from each other, but to recognise that simians can climb down to the Underworld , a nd that to do so they hold on to each other hand and foot in a chain which represe nts the unbroken continuity of shamanic succession . In a study of Chinese poetry, Boodberg (1954) called this a 'binocular vision, the superimposition of two sy nta ctical images in order to endow them with so lidity and depth ' (cited in Ja kobso n 1966: 402 and Fox 1974: 482; I have not seen this work). But as I a rgue here, this quality is bought at the price of th e se mantic fre edo m of each element taken separately.
6 Redeeming the dead and protecting the living I Earth-Sonum is a lso a common verdict on those who die in old age without dramatic symptoms . Here, it is considered the most benign possible form of death , suggesting a fulfi lled life (in contrast to a ll ot her Experiences , but particularly the sudden, premature death of a Sun-victim). Those who die by the action of Earth-Sonum receive a full funeral, including redemption songs with appropriate wording. I interpret this as meaning that Earth is not yet a happy loca ti on because one's membership of it is based primarily on a form ofsufrering and death, rather than on the transformation of these. Hence the imagery of rescue, wandering and banishing rites remains the same. However, Sora say that this transforma tion is a relatively easy one, meaning I think that the distance is not great between Ea rth as an Experience and Earth as redemption from Experience. 2 Unlike children who die in infancy or before birth , adult Sun-victims were perhaps fully separated from their mothers but somehow faultily cast and forged. This may be obvious early on, as with crippling; or it may be revealed on ly later in sudden deaths, by the rapid dissolution of their form . I have examined the logic of this imagery elsewhere (Vitebsky 1980) and shall here do no more than summarise. Blacksmiths are associated with the very process of metalworking and are conventionally expected to be lame (lablab), imbecile or otherwise defective, as in Sun-Woma n's blacksmith slaves 'Dumb-Sun' (Mo '1/10 '-Yllllg) and ' Moron-Sun ' (Kika-yullg). Though there are a few Sora blacksmiths, the majority belong to a low Hindu caste ca lled Gansi who visit Sora territory and are considered unclean, dangerous sorcerers (cf. chapter 7, sonum No. 15, lines 247- 50). For suicides (which are without exception by ha nging) and those who die in falls (the majority of accidenta l deaths) , the rope or tree involved is said to bridge the gulf, which is otherwise 'pathless' (ka'ja) , between earth and sky. In addition, for murders and those accidents where blood is actually shed , it is said th at the sudden loss of blood releases the soul so th a t it 'ca nnot find the way' to
+

th e Underworld but in stead ri ses to th e Sun ' like smoke' . (It is to avo id a simila r dispersal of the sou l th a t sac rifi cia l a nimals a re kill ed by a blow on th e head before the throat ca n be slit: the blood should not be released while the animal is a live, otherwi se 'we ca n' t send its soul' to the relevant sonum. This is a lso the reason why wild a nim a ls a re not general ly ofrered to so nums, since they ca nn ot be killed without wounding th em: exceptionally, wh ere a wi ld peacock is ofrered as a sy mbol of roy a l stat us to the dead at the kwja or to the Hindu sma llpox goddess in a seaso nal blocking rite aga inst epidemics, it is chased to ex haustion across the mountains a nd then hit on the he ad with a special club.) As a fin a l jud gment on a pe rso n's life-s tor y, a pos t-mort em Sun verdict is a decidedly nega tive one. This is clea res t in th e case of suicid e, whic h is ge nerally motivated by embarrassment over public knowledge of one's own misdemeanour o r humiliation (usually sex ua l): in line 192 of chapter 7, the moth e r of an unpleasant youth who comm itted sui cide for just this reaso n, ta unts him with th e questions 'Are you telling yourself you just died of Ra ' tud-SonumT But whatever the reasons , a ll Sun-deaths are highly contagious. 8 Memories and rememberers: states of mind among the dead and the living I A-jlllll-illg-te is an alternative form to jlllll-/-ing, ' he/she/ it ate me' . 2 Linguists have been wary of identifying a passive in Sora. Ramamurti dismissed it in a few words, writing, '[t]here are only two voices - active and middle. There is no passive voice in So:ra: [sic].' (1931 : 26; see also his footnote on tha t page). Pinnow (1966), using only Ramamurti as his source, writes as if there were no passive either in form or in function. Biligiri (1965), though he nowhere mentions the word 'passive', nevertheless translates the form in -ing like a passive, in this case blb-/-ing (in his orthography blb-/-ili) , from blb-, 'submerge', here translated as 'I was drowned' . The initi a l sepa ra te pronoun o f th e agent is ge ne ra lly omitted unl ess required for empha sis, so th a t if no noun is used to indica te the age nt , th e end ing o r co nt ex t must suffi ce to make clea r who this is. Whe n the patient o f the verb is a noun , this may either be separate (Sl '/Ilgell YIIIl-I-ai, ' I th a tch the ho use', or else infi xed (YIlII-slIlg-l -ai , 1 thatc h-house') . So lon g as noun s a re used for both agent and patient, th ere is no amb iguity. But where th e ve rb links two pronouns, not both of which are in all cases expressed, thi s gives ri se to two kind s of paradigm. The fir st shows a co nstant pa tient with a changing agen t, while th e second keeps the agent constant and changes the pa tient.
Constant patient, changing agent
(alllell) jlllll-I-illg you (singul a r) eat me ( al1ill ) jlllll -I-illg he eats me
( Oll1bell ) O~illlll- I- illg you (plural) ea t me (//lill~ii jlllll -I-illg-ji th ey ea t me
Constant agent, changing patient
(all ill) jlllll-I-illg he (she, it) eats me jum-I-{//Il he eats you (singula r) jlllll-I-e he eats him (her, it) jUIl1-I-ai he eats us (including listener) jlllll-I-e/ell he eats us (excluding listener) jUIl7-I-ebell he eats you (plural) jUln-l-e~ii he ea ts them
It will be remarked that without additional freestanding (and usually omitted) pronouns it is not always possible to express both agent and patient unambiguously: for example,jllll1-l-illg can mean 'you (singular) are eating me' or ' he/ she/it is eating me '. Sometimes both of these can be expressed, but only where suitable markers exist and can be combined (very few such combinations are possible).
Some forms combining both agent and patient markers
(NB Agent markers are in plain type, patient markers in bold) (Iiell) jUII1-I-ai-ji I eat them (allin-ji) jUII7-I-i"g-ji they eat me (all/ell) a-jllll7-l-ai-ji we eat them
(anin-ji) jllll1-l-elell-ji they eat us (excluding listener) (all1en/allill) jlllll-I-illg you (singular)/ he/she/ it eats me
In the first and third of these examples, the suffix of third-person plurality -ji refers to the patient, in the second and fourth example, to the agent. Where ma rkers cannot be combined, as in the fifth example, the patient will always be expressed even at the price of suppressing the agent. So where the agent is in the third person but is not made explicit by either the context or a noun, this can lead to a complete anonymity of the agent and thereby into the passive: it is only one step from (allin)jllll1-l-illg, 'he eats me', tojul1I-l-illg, 'I am eaten'. I shall show at the end of this chapter how divination for sickness starts from the point of view of the patient and moves towards revealing this anonymous agent.

9 Forgetting the dead It may be that the relationship betwee n the ca tegories of Eart h and Ancestor has changed (neither Elwin nor Sitapat i di scusses this relationship a t a ll. Few serious attempts have bee n made to study the long-te rm history of shifting cultivation in Indi a: a notable exception is Pratap 1987). Under the conditions which I obser ved , the acu te shorta ge of land now, co upl ed with the absence of any form of official land registration which corres ponded to how land was act ua ll y used, may have encouraged the stock piling of Memories as title-deeds . On th e other han d , the demographic inev itability that th e role of an Earth site must decline for reaso ns of lineage evo lution, is accelerated by ex ternal factors in the state of th e land marke t. Theoretically a given site can be subdivided indefinit ely among heirs, but in pract ice, especially on uneven terrain , it will not tolerate much fragmentation. As we have seen, a household 's association with a site can be eithe r throu gh permanent paddy-fields which are watered from it or throu gh surroundin g shifting-c ultivation sites, eve n thou gh these do not use th e water for irriga tion . Though the government recognises ri ghts only to paddy-fi eld s, the Sora also recognise rights of ownership or usufruct to shifting-cultivation plots, rights which vary from village to village according to local pressure on land . They realise that continued fragment a tion of both kinds of plot is wasteful a nd inconvenient and continually attempt to rationalise their holdings by buying and selling. In addition the generally low margin of surplus means that most households are very vulnerable to vagaries of demography, climate or health. Both to pay for sacrificial buffalos and as a legacy from bad periods they are enmeshed in various forms of long-term debt to each other, to Oriya Panos, and increasingly to money-lenders of the Telugu-spea king Komiti caste in the towns (Vitebsky 1978a, 1978b). Though legally no non-Soras are allowed to buy land in the area, in practice much paddy-land is effectively owned by outside moneylenders through mortgage arrangements which do not show up on paper and are impossible to redeem. Thus rights to land change hands frequently, voluntarily or through fraud, in a variety of forms of outright sale, mortgaging and share-cropping. 2 I saw this festival over several years, each year in several different villages. Specific details can be highly variable . For example, in one village the ladder is replaced by a thread tied from the roof and hanging down to the mortar beneath. The ladder's destruction with an axe is paralleled by the setting alight of the thread so that it burns from the bottom upwards .
10 Dialogues with the self? Sora bereavement and the presuppositions of contemporary psychotherapy It seems tha t we can point to moments in other societies, too, when mourning behaviour apparently becomes less 'adaptive'. Geertz (1959) gives a vivid account of the emotional confusion of the mourners a t a funeral of a child in Java when a breakdown occurred in the expected structure of the proceedings because somebody was trying, for political reasons, to modernise the style. 2 Jung is entirely absent from the index and the bibliography of, for example, Worden 1982, Volkan 1981, Bowlby 1980 and Bollas 1987 (incidentally, the title of Bollas' book, The shadow 0/ fh e objecf, is taken from page 249 of Freud's Molll'lling and melancholia). Jung's archetypes could of course offer a distinctive

and fruitful way of thinking about Sora son ums o r Memories, especially the Experiences. 3 Certa in la nguages a nd th e contexts of their usage may e nco ura ge an acco unt of feeling whic h hove rs around nouns and continually comes back to res t on th em.
